About the Author
Dan Heisman
is a United States Chess Federation National Master, author and instructor. He is well regarded for his practical advice on a wide range of key subjects including general improvement, thought processes, planning, strategy, tactics, endgame play and technique.
Also by the Author:

Introduction
In 2009 I was hearing more and more about the concept of social media. To me it seemed a way of using the World Wide Web to communicate quickly, and simultaneously, with many people all over the planet with similar interests. As a chess instructor and author, I asked myself: ‘How can I best make use of social media to help as many prospective improving chess players as possible?’ I came across Twitter and decided that every day I could publish a chess ‘tip’ and everyone could read it, benefit, and/or comment.
I joined Twitter in 2009 and, since 2011, have been publishing a Chess Tip of the Day at www.twitter.com/danheisman. If you go to that URL on a browser and scroll down, you can find about 4,000 chess ‘tips’, and growing by one every day. I do tweet about other things on Twitter, so I differentiate my tips by starting them with an abbreviated month/day, e.g. M17 = May 17 or N8 = November 8. In this book the dates are irrelevant, so they have been removed.
In 2021, the Chess Journalists of America voted my Chess Tip of the Day the ‘Best Twitter Feed’ award. As I write this, I am awaiting to see if my Twitter tips win another award.
What is a chess ‘tip’? It is a suggestion; something I think might help a person play chess better, think more efficiently, refer to a website or media for some recommendation, draw attention to something interesting, publicize events, or just enjoy the world of chess better.
In that sense my tips – and thus the contents of this book – differ from similar works such as Alburt and Lawrence’s
Chess Rules of Thumb
, which contains primarily principles/rules or Soltis’s
The Wisest Things Ever Said About Chess,
which are ‘wise’, but not necessarily all suggestions. There is some overlap, of course, between this book and those predecessors. I can (and will!) offer some principles as suggestions to improve play, or suggest that a wise statement might contain good advice to follow.
As readers might know, Twitter was originally limited to 140 characters, but a few years ago, expanded to 280 per tweet. However, publishers were not interested in a book of ‘The Best of Chess Tip of the Day’ that were just hundreds if not thousands of copied and pasted short tips from my Twitter page – especially since they were available online for free! For this reason, I have expanded each tip to 1-4 pages of additional material not found on Twitter.
Therefore, this book is much more than just some of my best tips. I have selected tips that were not just the most helpful, but also ones that could be expanded with additional text and/or examples. I have been teaching chess for over 50(!) years and full-time since 1996, so many of these tips represent ways to highlight and address the most common problems I have found in students of all levels, offering suggested ways they can help mitigate or maybe even avoid these problems and/or improve their play and study. While many players at the same level may have similar problems in a skill-like analysis, they can also differ greatly in other areas, such as playing too fast or slow, or in their knowledge of openings, exact endgames, or principles.
As an instructor, the tips I offer often lean toward general improvement advice, rather than how to play very specific positions. So it is unlikely you will find too many tips comparable to: “When playing Black in the 6 Bg5 lines in the Najdorf variation of the Sicilian Defense, you will more often develop your queen’s knight with ...Nb8-d7 than you would ...Nb8-c6.” Or: “In the Lucena endgame if the defending king moves on to the sixth rank, consider using it as a shield for the offensive king to possibly move safely along the eighth rank to threaten promotion.” For these types of ‘position-specific’ tips, perhaps it would be better to consult reference material about how to play the Najdorf or one about ‘exact’ endgame technique.
When expanding the included tips into a page or more, I was able to turn this manuscript into much more than just suggestions; the expanded tips contain additional helpful material, including illustrative stories and many diagrams with instructive play. Much of this expanded material is either typical of, or verbatim, what I would use as part of a lesson, so in that sense many of the tips can be considered ‘Best Mini-Lessons of an Old Coach’!
On that note, the first book I wrote for Everyman was
A Guide to Chess Improvement: the Best of Novice Nook
. I think in every sense a reader will find this book to be a great companion to that one, and consistent in my messaging to players wishing to improve.
Occasionally I hear someone say they initially judge a chess book by flipping through it and glancing at the diagrams to get a feel for the book; I am not sure this is the optimum way to evaluate a new book, but I hope this one at least meets the minimum requirements!
As with all books of this nature, most readers will find some tips more helpful than others. This is only natural, based on each player’s playing strength, perceived and actual weaknesses, and areas of interest. In that sense, the book does not have to be read from start to finish, as one might read a novel or, for that matter, many of my other chess books. Instead, you can peruse the book at your leisure, looking for tips that you find interesting or helpful. And it is likely that a tip you find unappealing today might be the key tip in a year or two that will get you out of a rut or slump! In that sense this book can be used either as a reference book or a straight read.
With this many tips, there will naturally be some overlap between tips that address the same subject like good time management, careful analysis, creating active pieces, or trying your best on every move. I have tried to provide adequate cross-references between tips, but there is going to be some purposeful synergism even between tips without a cross-reference which address similar topics. It is important to note that the text is written so that the great majority of the cross-references are there to be helpful to correlate or augment the suggestions, but it is not necessary to follow those cross-references in order to understand the referencing tip.
Occasionally one tip would lead directly or as a corollary to another tip. If there was sufficient material for the additional tip, I would make this a separate tip but, if not, I sometimes included additional related tips, usually with the note *Tip Alert!*.
Unlike my
The Improving Chess Thinker
or
Silman’s Endgame Course
, the material in this book is not meant to be arranged in any ascending order of difficulty. That is mostly due to the nature or tips and suggestions – they are not normally ‘you need to know this tip before you can read the next one.’ There are, however, a few tips that do flow naturally from a previous tip; in those few cases I tried to put the tips in consecutive order or at least reference the other tip. Similarly, many of the tips are general or involve overall improvement, so I decided to assign each tip, as best possible, to one of the following chapters:
• General Improvement
• Thought Process
• Psychology
• Tactics/Safety
• Positional Concepts and Strategy
• Openings
• Endgames
Middlegame issues are addressed primarily though Chapters Four (Tactics/Safety) and Five (Positional Concepts/Strategy). Further, because the tips are in subject order and not in order of priority, I have put the label
*Top-10 Tip*
after the ten tips I think might be most helpful to the average reader. However, each reader is invited to pick out their own ‘most helpful’ set, which will likely differ greatly from mine! Drop me an email via my website www.danheisman.com to let me know your ‘top ten’.
It almost goes without saying in modern chess books that, when I use a term like ‘best move’, it is not my opinion, but the result of engine analysis. For almost all instances where the quality of a move is mentioned, the engine was
Stockfish
.
This is my 13th chess book, so I am trying not to be superstitious and am hoping 13 will turn out to be a lucky number! At my age, I am not sure I will be writing additional books representing a luckier number, so I wanted this book to be instructive, entertaining, and of high quality. I hope the reader agrees!
Acknowledgements
I would like to thank Byron Jacobs of Popular Chess for agreeing to publish this book and laying out the general groundwork of how a tip should be expanded. I would also not only like to thank all my Twitter followers, but especially those that took the time to suggest which tips were either the most helpful to them or those they felt would be most helpful to include in the book. I would also like to thank my editors, Byron and Richard Palliser; it requires a good editor to make a chess book more readable and instructive!
Dan Heisman,
May 2025
Chapter One
General Improvement
This chapter covers a variety of areas of personal chess improvement, like which areas to concentrate upon, types of games to play or books to read. Included are some ‘meta-tips’ dealing with how to best use advice or how to learn/deal with chess principles. The ‘General’ heading also serves as an area for tips that do not fall neatly into the topics covered in the other chapters.
Tip 1
When you first begin to play chess and improve, concentrate primarily on just three things: the safety of moves (both yours and your opponent’s), using all your pieces all the time, and good time management: not playing too fast or too slow for the game and allocating more time to critical moves.
Here are two stories to illustrate this suggestion:
The Internet Chess Club has several weakened engines to play against amateur players. One is designed to only look four ply (two moves) ahead; after that it is shut off and completely blind. Within two moves it will play safely; it will not lose material and will win material if it can see the certainty within that window. It otherwise has only rudimentary strategy. What do you think its FIDE/USCF equivalent rating is?
Amazingly, the answer is 1650. That is higher than the average rating of an adult tournament player! This result shows how important the most simple, basic tactics are, not to mention consistency. If you can master the three items in the tip, you can play competitively with most tournament players!
When I first started coaching radio personality Howard Stern, he was little more than a beginner; his early online long time-control rating was about 900 (equivalent to about 750 USCF/FIDE). After six months he was up to 1000. Around that time, we began a lesson and Howard said, “Dan, I think I have gone about as far as I can go. I keep playing those 1100 players and they are just too good for me. I can hardly ever beat them.”
I replied, “Howard, we review your games, and what is happening is that you are making basic tactical mistakes and giving them lots of free material and they are seeing some. Those 1100 players are also making many tactical mistakes, but you are seeing a little bit less and getting less. If you would just stop giving them free material and also win the material they are offering you, you would beat them every game.”
“But Dan,” Howard protested, “That’s easy for you to say. It’s not that easy at my level.”
“Howard, you are correct, of course, but that’s basically what is happening.”
Now fast-forward after four years of additional coaching and Howard’s online rating has risen to 1700+ (about 1550+ USCF/FIDE).
Normally Howard prearranges his online games with friends (they are his regular chess friends, since he played under an online handle and they did not know who he really was). But one day he got on too early – he gets up early for his radio show – and none of his friends were available. So, he did what he did not usually do, hit a button to seek a random opponent. The computer paired him with an 1100 player.
Later that day we had a lesson. I began the lesson as I always do: “Do you have any questions or anything special you would like to do today?”
Howard replied, “Well, I played a game this morning, but it is not worth reviewing. I hit the button and the computer paired me with an 1100 player and he just gave me all his pieces and resigned on move 19.”
Howard’s comment triggered in my mind what he had said four years earlier, so I replied, “You mean one of those same 1100 players that four years ago you told me you would never beat?”
There was silence on the phone for only a short time and then Howard, in good humor as he almost always was during lessons, retorted, “Wise guy!”
Moral of the story: When you are a low-rated player, studying basic tactics, playing carefully every move, and getting rid of your giant tactical mistakes will help you more than memorizing 10 opening books or learning the Lucena and Philidor endgames. Minimizing giant mistakes is far more helpful than learning many strategic nuances. Once you can keep your pieces safe each move and can recognize when your opponent has not and can take advantage of it, then studying lots of opening theory and advanced strategy will become far more useful.
Tip 2
*Top-10 Tip*
The first step in improvement is trying your best on every move, which requires at a minimum good time management (not playing too fast or too slow). You want to look yourself in the mirror after each game and truthfully state “I could not have tried better on any move!”
Suppose you could try your best 95% of the time. Sounds pretty good, right?
Let’s assume the average chess game lasts about 40 moves. 95% of 40 is 38, so that means two moves each game you would *not* try your best. This observation leads to a little thought experiment:
Suppose we have two players, virtual clones. One is rated 1800 and tries his/her best all the time. The other player is identical with that one exception: twice a game he/she suffers a ‘lapse’ and cannot put in the effort that the position and time on the clock would allow, essentially not even trying. What would be the rating of the second player?
I have had several players make estimates for me, and I agree with the consensus answers. These two lapses a game could easily make player no.2 1400-1500 instead of 1800, a loss of 300-400 rating points! That is likely an order of magnitude more rating points than a 1400 could get from memorizing a book on a favorite opening, for sure (even if that were possible).
This experiment shows that consistency in effort and concentration is worth quite a bit. It is no coincidence that if you go to a big tournament the ones who consistently take the most time are the better players.
What might cause lapses from trying your best? There could be many factors, such as:
• Forgetting to do all the things you know you should do, like making sure the opponent does not have a check, capture, or threat (forcing move) which might defeat your candidate move.
• Fatigue or some sort of tiredness due to internal or external conditions, such as not getting enough liquids during the game or not having enough sleep.
• Some sort of distraction, like worrying about what happened earlier in the game, or where you are going to eat dinner.
• The inability to roll up the sleeves and take time to carefully analyze complex positions, whereas following general principles on more simple positions requires less effort.
• Quite a few other reasons are possible, such as getting over-confident or playing with too much fear.
Whatever the cause, these lapses can be disastrous. Learning how to minimize or prevent them can make a big difference in your game. The first step is awareness.
• Many of my students offer a variety of reasons why they did not play their best on every move. For example, they might state: “I thought I was winning easily and it did not matter what I did, so I was just quickly making attacking moves without paying attention to what my opponent was doing.”
• Luckily, I never fell victim to these types of psychological issues. Instead, I always said that I had only two gears when playing: Try your best, given the board and time conditions, or resign.
I tell my new students “You want to be able to look yourself in the mirror after each game and truthfully state “I could not have tried better on any move.”
That does not mean you should play so slowly that you get into unreasonable time pressure. Playing too slowly is not doing your best; quite the opposite. If you play too slowly you are likely giving the game to your opponent, rather than making them win it.
You want to play the best move you can find in a reasonable amount of time
, as noted above, given the board and time conditions. If you can use almost all your time without getting into unnecessary time pressure and properly allocate more time to complicated and critical moves, that is about all you can reasonably be asked to do. Therefore, that is the best measure of whether you are trying your best.
Tip 3
*Top-10 Tip*
The best thing you can do to improve is to consistently analyze with strong players.
Why analyze with strong players? They can:
• Help you determine which moves are strong candidates, which are not, and why.
• Show you when to stop analyzing and chose the move.
• Show you how deep to analyze, and how to avoid quiescence errors.
• Help you evaluate positions better.
• Answer questions you have about analysis and evaluation.
Many students wonder how to access these players. There are a variety of ways:
• Have friends who are strong players who will analyze with you.
• Analyze your games with opponents, especially at over-the-board (OTB) events, where some of your opponents will be stronger.
• Hire a chess coach to analyze games and positions with you during lessons and improve your skills, like analysis, evaluation, and time management (and not just teach you openings and how to play the Lucena endgame).
• Go to a local chess club, make friends with the stronger players, and watch them analyze their games and others (hopefully including yours).
• Join an online analysis/improvement group run by strong players – most are paid, but occasionally some are free.
I was lucky enough to be invited to join the Germantown Chess Club when I was a beginner. Many of the best players from the Philadelphia area went there, and most were willing to analyze their games and others once they finished their weekly slow game.
I would watch them analyze and occasionally throw in (dumb) questions like “Why didn’t you play/consider that move?” and they would answer “Because of so-and-so”, such as “In this type of position I am trying to do X and Y, and that move doesn’t help me get toward that goal”, or “I looked at that briefly, but what do I do if he replies Z, and then I don’t have any good answers,” or “That would be a good idea if such-and-such were true, but in this case it’s not, so it doesn’t do as much.”
I remember one time I was analyzing a position from an early middlegame with a strong player, likely Don Latzel, and I had pawns next to each other on the same rank, something like (I will show only the two pawns, but most of the pieces were still on the board; these were not passed pawns):
White to play
In this position I played 1.f4-f5 and after the game Don said to me “Why did you play that move?” and I replied “Because both pawns are undefended and after 1.f4-f5 one of them is guarded.”
Don then told me something I never forgot (but it has been so long that I am paraphrasing):
“OK, Dan, I understand, but in your position neither of the pawns was in danger. By spending a tempo to play 1.f4-f5, you fix your pawns on light squares, which weakens the dark squares around them. So later your opponent can safely place pieces on f6 or g5 to blockade the pawns, which also, in the long run, might make your pawns more vulnerable. On the other hand, if you can safely leave them on g4 and f4, you are controlling all the fifth rank squares in front of the pawns, so that no opposing pieces can sit on those squares. Therefore, you might want to push one of those pawns only if you can get an attack or you need to do it to keep them safe.”
Good advice! It is also similar to the reason why, if you study a positional text on hanging pawns (which are also abreast like this on semi-open files with no adjacent supporting pawns; mine were not on semi-open files), it will usually advise you something like “When your opponent has hanging pawns, try to attack them to entice one of them to move forward, when you can blockade them and make them more vulnerable.”
Of course, I am a full-time chess coach and this is the kind of instruction we like to supply, but my point was that Don was not a coach; he was just a friendly stronger player who was willing to help a young player understand the game better.
These days when I go to our local chess club, I try to ‘pay it forward’ by doing the same things for the improving players (of all ages) at our club. Although I do not play much anymore, I wait until games are finished and offer to review them with the players. It is very much giving them free ‘’mini-lessons’ like the ones I was so lucky to have received many years ago.
It is for reasons like this that I encourage players to join a local club. Sure, the internet is great and you can get a game 24/7, but if there is a convenient club within a reasonable distance, the benefits to going there and meeting players ‘in person’ is often quite helpful, both socially and for your chess improvement.
Tip 4
Do not ‘lock yourself in a closet’ waiting to get good enough to compete. Get out to clubs and tournaments, and start playing and learning.
As a full-time instructor for many years, I occasionally run into the mentality of a student who feels they are “not good enough” to go to clubs and tournaments. They want to first take lessons until they are strong enough.
I sometimes compare this to an aspiring golfer who takes golf lessons and goes to the driving range, but doesn’t want to yet play golf on a golf course. Such a player may develop a good, or at least adequate, golf swing, but they would not be developing any of their course management skills like how to handle the wind, when to play safe, or dealing with competitive pressures from opponents or other situations.
In a similar manner, playing in chess club and tournaments requires learning how to deal with any number of situations that don’t come up from just studying openings or tactics.
I like to tell the following story to illustrate one aspect: I had an adult student who mostly played online and was eager to improve. At my suggestion, he decided to play in his first over-the-board tournament and in his first game was paired with the young son of the tournament director. After they had played a few moves – enough to take my student out of his opening book – his young opponent got up and started to run out of the room, apparently to use the restroom.
His father, the tournament director (TD), naturally intercepted his son on the way out to talk to him. As a TD and father myself, his interception was undoubtedly to ask his son if anything was wrong. But my student immediately suspected the worst, that the TD possibly was trying to illegally help his opponent! However, he felt there was nothing he could do – the person he should ask for help in such a situation was that very same TD. Therefore, by the time his opponent returned from the restroom, my student was a nervous wreck and could not concentrate. He said he soon put his queen en prise and resigned. So far as I know, he never played another tournament.
Obviously, my student greatly overreacted to an otherwise fairly innocuous situation. But it does illustrate the point that there are many situations in a real event which just ‘locking yourself in a closet’ and studying does not prepare you for.
I suggest to nervous students that there are two types of nervousness:
• The nerves from competition – This is natural, somewhat fight or flight adrenaline, and it never fully goes away. It does diminish somewhat for most players when they compete regularly.
• The “newness” nerves – This happens when doing something for the initial time(s) whether it is attending a new school or job, a first date, or first visit to a club or first tournament(s). This is often a greater nervousness, but the good news is it not only fades, but often disappears entirely once you have enough experience. Therefore, if you play in tournaments often enough (let’s say at least four per year), then eventually playing in those events becomes ‘old hat’ and the newness nervousness dissipates.
In tip #70 we will address the fear of losing, which creates a different reason why some lock themselves in the closet – they fear losing so badly that they only want to play once they are strong enough to lose less often. This fear not only prevents them from losing and learning, but it also is misplaced, because the two practices of sectioning events into levels of playing strength plus the Swiss system both tend to push players of all levels score towards 50%. In other words, players rated around 1200 mostly play opponents near their level, and so do players rated around 1700 (or pretty much pick a level) play opponents near their level.
Ironically, what this means is that if you are 1000 strength and try to wait until you are 1500 to play, you will only win slightly more often! This logic breaks down if you are extremely good or bad, or play in completely open sections, but the bottom line is that ‘waiting until you are good’ rarely accomplishes very much, except delay getting valuable experience.
While it is true that on the average, you learn more from your losses than your wins, there is usually much to be learned from your wins as well. I tell my students you can learn from 100% of your losses, but maybe only 99% of your wins – the exceptions being if your opponent falls into an opening trap (or blunders right away badly) and resigns. Otherwise, your wins are probably full of meaningful learning situations as well. In any case, play and learn!
An important final note: locking yourself in the closet to study is inefficient. I suggest something called the improvement feedback loop, a learning ‘triangle’ where you play-study-get feedback. While you can study at home efficiently, playing requires at least an opponent, but, moreover,
good feedback is much more than just having an engine analyze your game.
Feedback from opponents, strong players, databases, and other sources is a key to efficient improvement.
Tip 5
If your goal is to be good against humans, there is some benefit playing low-rated engines, but much more benefit playing humans. Humans play much differently than engines: psychology, time management, style, etc, so playing humans is better practice.
By teaching so many students one to one privately, I get a pretty good representation of what players aspiring to improve are doing (right and wrong; strengths and weaknesses). One of the common issues some students have, especially those that play primarily online, is that they do not want to play against humans.
A few who do not play against humans do so mostly because engine opponents are much more convenient; they will play any time 24/7, at any time control, with any color and, for the most part, at any level of desired opponent strength. Those are certainly excellent reasons to play computer opponents, and it is beneficial to play engines if the choice is to do so or not play at all.
But when I ask students what their goal is for lessons, almost everyone – naturally – says they want to improve their chess game. But if my follow-up questions is “Against whom do you want to play better?”, the overwhelming majority indicate some type of human opponents: tournaments, clubs, friends, online players. Only a minority state that they play engines and just want to do better against engines.
So as an instructor, I now have two crucial pieces of information: my student wants to be a better player, and most want to play better against humans.
If playing engines
will
help their game, shouldn’t that be a recommendation? They answer is a qualified yes, because it would be much more helpful to play against humans. As noted in the tip, playing humans has major advantages if your final goal is to play against them:
• Psychology – Most humans opponents are friendly and there are no psychological issues in playing them. Unfortunately, not everyone (especially on the internet where some hide behind their anonymous presence) is a friendly foe and no one can become a strong player without learning how to deal with opponents of all types, including the annoying ones.
• Time management – Most (but not all) weakened engines play immediately, since a good engine can play very well if only given a few milliseconds. So, to minimize waiting and total game time, engines are programmed to play quickly no matter what the circumstances. But humans not only play much more slowly, they play at different speeds depending on the criticality of the position and some have poor time management, playing much too fast or too slow. Therefore, when playing against humans, you have time to think or do other things during the opponent’s move (Tip #48), and learn how to deal with opponents who play too quickly or too slowly (Tip #84).
• Style – Engines that are programmed to play weakly don’t play in the same style nor make the same type of mistakes that humans at that same rating do. As just mentioned, a human could have a much weaker rating than their overall strength if they play too slowly and consistently give games away. An engine playing at the same strength would undoubtedly play weaker moves, but time trouble would never be an issue. There are a variety of ways to make an engine play weakly; some I have seen include only allowing the engine to look so far ahead (four ply was one example), or having the engine not develop pieces for the first 10 moves before playing more strongly, etc. None of these artificial weakening methods resulted in that engine playing like a human of identical strength. I am sure some do much better than others, but an ‘debilitated’ engine’s consistent weakening tendencies generally are different than an amateur human’s natural inconsistent thought process and analytical issues.
Therefore, if someone has access to human opponents, as they very conveniently almost always do through tournaments, clubs, and most of all online servers, then it makes sense to select a human opponent for ‘most optimum’ improvement aspects toward their goal.
Tip 6
Just because the leading servers do not have many ‘buttons’ to find slower games with increments, it is still an excellent idea to play slow games, online or otherwise, with an increment (or time delay).
When sudden death (all moves in a specified amount of time) games are played without an increment or time delay, it is very possible to reach a position where neither side can checkmate before one or both players runs out of time. This is called a ‘race’ condition because in those circumstances, the quality of the moves no longer matters; often just who moves fastest wins.
Understandably, the Rules Committee wants to avoid those situations in serious games. When I attended the US Chess Federation Rules Committee meeting that was one of the subjects and they wanted to do everything possible to ‘encourage’ organizers, tournament directors, and players to always augment slow sudden death time controls with increments or time delays.
This brings us to the curious modern-day situation where the leading online servers, who have many times more members than over-the-board federations, for the most part do not offer ‘automatic’ buttons to request slow games with increments. I worked for Chess.com (my TV show was “Q&A with Coach Heisman”) and had several conversations about how to make it easier for those who want to play serious slow games find others of like mind.
Although, or maybe because, those conversations were ultimately fruitless, I have had complaints from around 500 students (or chess friends) that they cannot find anyone online who will play slow games with them – or at least have difficulty doing so. Since I know or chat with an extremely small percentage of the players on the planet who play online, the actual number who are eager or would be willing to play slow games with an increment must be enormously higher.
There is some good news – pretty much all the servers have a ‘custom’ time control capability where a user can set up almost any time control and then offer it on some sort of map to prospective random opponents who can select their game. But slow games, although popular, are not nearly as popular, nor get the turnover, of speed games, so those creating the slow games offers may wait an inordinate amount of time to get someone to look for and choose their game. They often give up before that happens. On the other hand, if they hit a ‘30 0’ button and get a 30-minute game with no increment, they are paired with the first reasonably rated opponent who also hits that button. Therefore, it is much easier to get a game through a button, but alas, there currently is no button with a slow game and an increment.
The other good news is that several servers have slow leagues or events that offer time limits of 45 45 (i.e. 45 minutes for the game with a 45-second delay or increment per move), 60 30, or even 90 30. There the players are almost always paired and they negotiate a time to play, when they can set a custom time control at the appropriate speed and challenge their known opponent.
Unfortunately, sometimes these leagues are not prominently advertised by the servers. Since the server gets most of the clientele through faster time controls, a prospective slow player either must do a search or have been told by a friend or colleague that such a league or event exists so that they can join and find the slow, serious games they desire.
On my website danheisman.com I do have an alternative suggestion for those that want to find slow time controls with increments, but feel like they are relegated to hitting 30 0 buttons if they want to find a game within a reasonable amount of time. My method is based on the ‘networking’ method of job hunting and, in practice, it has worked very well for those willing to invest a little time up front to get it up and running. Here are the steps:
Make daily generic posts for obtaining friends to play slow games at clubs that feature slow play, such as:
• Clubs on Chess.com, like the Dan Heisman Learning Center, Slow-Chess-League, nm-coach bills-free video-lessons, or G30.
• On LiChess join the Hemmingford Chess Club.
• Someone told me: “@chess_dojo on Discord is fast and organized for players looking for slow play and postmortems.”
• There may be other good places, such as the chess forums on Reddit.
A post can read something like:
“Hi! I am looking for members who would like to play long time control games with me. I am looking for players in the rating range xxxx-yyyy. If you are interested, please message me and I will get back to you as soon as possible. Thanks!”
Change or modify this message to meet your needs. For example, if you live in Europe, you might want to say so if that affects what time zones allow you to play at a reasonable hour.
Post often enough that many can see your posts (do not just post once and give up if no one sends you a message). Check your messages on your server frequently so that you can respond in a timely manner. Once you have enough friends to play games any time you wish, you can stop posting. Also, check others’ posts to see if they are looking for slow games and respond with a message – they may have even gotten that idea here!
When you do find players who will play you slowly, it is important to ask them “Do you have a list of friends around our rating who also might play me some slow games?” If so, add that list to your friends’ list and it will grow quickly!
When you and your friend meet, you can go to the server and challenge your friend, setting the custom time control at the agreed time. If you are looking for a game on short notice and had not scheduled a game earlier, contact your list of friends (text message, email, etc), and see who is available.
It may take a few weeks or even a few months before you have enough online friends to make this work for games at any time you wish, but, once you have, you are set for life.
Final note: this method worked well for radio personality Howard Stern, a long-time student. Of course, Howard plays anonymously on the internet, so he was not able to attract a large set of friends just due to reputation; he had to do it one friend at a time, but it worked. If it worked for the anonymous Howard, it can work for you!
Tip 7
Learn by playing ‘What if?’ games with a strong engine.
Many online players find their game automatically reviewed by the chess server’s engines. These annotations are great for figuring out the opening book recommendations, which wins were missed, or what was the losing move. However, these brief annotations are not a great substitute for how much a player can learn when reviewing games (their games and others as well) and playing “What if?” games – or just position play-outs – with the engine.
During any chess study, whether it is reviewing your own games, looking at others’ games, doing puzzles, or learning openings, you naturally will arrive at positions that either did not occur in the game, were not part of the puzzle solution, not covered in the opening material, etc. Sometimes these alternatives lines, especially if reasonable, are covered in the side notes by the author. But especially if you are a weaker player (and often for stronger, too), there may be moves where you think ‘But what about this move?’
Another situation covered by this tip would be ‘How would I (or anyone) win or draw this position if X were played?’ This issue is especially common in the endgame. Students often tell me something like “Yes, the engine says this endgame is winning, but I am too weak to win it.”
Unless this is a late endgame ‘exact’ position with a known solution, you likely will not find the technique to win that position in an endgame manual. But you also do not get better by osmosis; if you do not have a strong player available to help (a common situation!), then playing out the endgame with an engine will provide valuable experience. You can play the side trying to win or draw, let the engine play that side and you defend, or even let the engine play both sides.
When running an engine, you can usually set the number of variations (‘best moves’) you want to view. The number one variation, starting with the player to move’s best move, is called the #principal variation’ (PV). If you show three variations, it will look something like:
Here’s how to read this output: The name of the engine,
Stockfish 15
, is the header. The “1:” on the next line means that what follows is the PV, the suggested best moves for both sides. The “(7.38)” in parentheses is the engine’s evaluation of the position for the PV; if it is positive, it means White is better; negative indicates Black is better. Here White is ahead by 7.38 pawns. The “[29]” is the number of ply that the engine is looking ahead (it may also be doing an expanded search beyond that number of base ply, depending upon the engine). The rest of the line is the best moves for both sides, starting with White’s best 32nd move. On the next line “2:” indicates the line that follows will start with White’s second best move, followed by the best moves thereafter for both sides.
When playing ‘What if?’ games, it is often a good idea to show at least the best two variations. That way, if the evaluation between the best move and the second best move is sufficiently great (especially when the value of the best evaluation is in the competitive -1 to +1 range), the engine is telling you that the no.1 move is clearly best or even forced.
This is where the fun begins, because if you don’t know why the second best move is weaker or why the best move is better (which is almost the same!), you can force the engine down one or both the lines to see what is happening on the board in the various continuations. Often this will clarify why the evaluations differed so much, but other times may still be unclear so, if you are really curious, you can mark that position as a discussion point for your next lesson or chat with the strong chess friend.
If you are replaying one of your games and the evaluation of the PV drops dramatically, that means the move just played was a big mistake (by the way, the evaluation cannot go
up
dramatically; Zermelo proved in 1913 that your position cannot get ‘better’ when you make a good move, but that’s a different issue; see danheisman.com/steinitz-zermelo-elkies.html).
Whenever the evaluation drops that much, you want to know why; sometimes the variation is so complex that no human could find it and you will not learn much, but often it’s a common tactical error that you should learn to recognize and analyze better (assuming it was either your mistake or an opponent’s for which you did not take proper advantage).
If the engine is playing out the rest of the game for both sides, try to discern any strategic patterns that can be applied to future similar situations. Perhaps it is activating a pawn majority or piling up on a weakness, or cleverly using its king in the endgame.
Modern engines give us tools we did not have when I was improving. Playing ‘what if’ games with engines augments your learning and likely speeds up your development.
Tip 8
When considering an equal move versus an unclear move, unless you are in an important event where a draw is very beneficial (e.g. needing a draw to win $10,000 at the World Open), it is likely better to play the move that is unclear so that you can learn something. In doing so, next time a similar situation occurs you are less likely to feel it is unclear.
Some situations are always going to remain unclear, but there are positions where sacrifices are often available, such as the Classical Bishop Sacrifice (‘Greek Gift’) or where you can sacrifice a piece for a pawn, but expose the opponent’s king.
When you first start playing chess, these common patterns will seem a mystery, but as you see them in annotated games or try them in your own games, the resulting positions that at first seem completely unclear sometimes slowly resolve themselves into ‘good-looking’ or ‘poor-looking’ possibilities.
Therefore, it is good to try things that require a little risk. If you see a speculative sacrifice and to you it is not clearly good or bad, if the alternative is a dull game with roughly equal chances, and the situation is a good learning one (no big money or titles at stake), take a chance and try it. Here is an example:
Kubacki – Heisman
LaSalle Invitational 1967
White to play after 15...
Q
xd3
This game was played in my first year of tournament play and I believe my opponent was the highest-rated high school player in the state (Kubacki was 1900; I was rated just 1467).
In this game Michael, White, seemed to be influenced by Misha Tal, the former world champion who loved what seemed to most to be unclear sacrifices. Michael unleashed:
16.Rxe6+?! fxe6
I have to accept or I am exposed with little to show for it.
17.Qxe6+ Be7?
Much better is 17...Ne7, and if 18.Qxf6 Rf8 regains the piece through the skewer to f3.
18.Ne5! Nxe5! 19.Qxe5?
Better is 19.Bxb7 with equal chances.
19...Bxg2! 20.Re1
If 20.Qxh8+ Kd7 and Black is just winning.
Black to play after 20.
R
e1
I thought for a long while here and, among other candidates, considered the desperate 20...Be4?, but then I realized I had a perfectly good answer. I played:
20...0-0-0!
Amazingly, this is a very rare position where the only two winning moves are 20...0-0-0 and 20...0-0! I won easily after:
21.Qxe7 Qxd2 22.Qxc5+ Kb8 23.Re7 Qd1+! 24.Kxg2 Qd5+ 0-1
When they asked Misha Tal, famous for sacrifices, if he always worked them out to the end before making them, he said he did not. He did what most of us do – he got to a certain point and said to himself ‘That looks good, let’s go for it!”
Here is some special advice to the many who analyze superficially: The common beginner mistake to sacrifice a piece just to get a pawn and two checks is not worth it – you need a continuing attack!
A final warning:
taking the unclear path only makes sense if the alternative is equal or worse
. Unless it is a really meaningless game, I strongly advise against going into an unclear line if the alternative is playing ‘clearly’ and having a very good if not winning position!
Tip 9
The three types of chess “’visions’ are visualization, tactical vision, and board vision. Develop all of them!
Let’s define what I mean by each:
Visualization: The ability to keep track of where the pieces would have moved during lines of analysis. Humans use their imagination to visualize.
Tactical vision: The ability to spot basic safety issues (motifs like pins, double attacks, removal of the guard, and Counting) when starting to analyze a position.
Board vision: The ability to look at a chess board and quickly see what all the pieces are doing (statically). For example, seeing that a bishop on a1 is attacking h8. This name is not standard throughout the industry; for example, in his book
Rapid Chess Improvement
Michael de la Maza calls this “chess vision”.
Visualization is a root skill in analysis. If you can’t keep track of where the pieces are in your imagination of possible move sequences, you can’t analyze or evaluate them properly. There are many theories on how to develop better visualization, but one is surely practice! Play hundreds if not thousands of long time control games and every time you analyze, try to carefully move the pieces around in your imagination, keeping track of where they go. Practice may not make perfect, but it should make you a lot better! See Tip #11 for more about visualization.
Your tactical vision helps determine the areas where you will analyze potential tactics and determine the safety of both sides’ pieces. Your tactical vision is the one that recognizes both the Seeds of Tactical Destruction (Tip #100) that tell you where potential tactics might occur, and then which tactical motifs (double attack, pin, back-rank mate, removal of the guard) might be attempted in analysis to execute the tactic and win material or checkmate.
What are some examples of board vision?
• Counting the material balance.
• Judging relative space.
• Judging piece activity.
• Judging central control.
• Recognizing weak squares, weak/strong pawns, and potential outposts.
• Judging piece coordination.
• Seeing the pawn structure, open and semi-open files, etc.
• Seeing if anyone possesses the bishop-pair.
• Judging relative king safety.
What board vision does not include would be possible tactically opportunities (tactical vision) or what the pieces can do if they move (visualization).
In his interesting book
Genius in Chess
, IM Levitt discusses this skill at length, but interestingly does not give it a separate name from visualization. Levitt devised a series of board vision puzzles and gave them to international players of various strengths. Even though board vision may not involve ‘goodness’ of moves – it may only require something like counting the number of legal moves in a position – Levitt came up with an interesting conclusion: even at the international level, there is still a correlation between rating and board vision skill.
I have found that this correlation extends to the lower levels as well. This brings up the ‘chicken and the egg’ question: Does better board vision make you a better player, or do you simply have better board vision because you are a better player? I suspect most of the cause and effect is in the former: developing your board vision helps make you a better player.
Let’s provide an example of Levitt’s board vision exercises. He created a scoring system that the faster you answer and the more accurate, the higher your score (time counts). In the following position, as quickly as you can, provide two numbers: 1) the number of legal moves White has, and 2) the number of those moves that are checkmate:
How many legal white moves? How many are mate?
The correct answers are 29 and 29. Only four pieces can move: queen (4 moves), rook on d2 (10), knight on e3 (8), and bishop b3 (7). Even though the knight on c6 is absolutely pinned to the king, 1.Qb4# is mate because chess rules allow an absolutely pinned piece to still guard another piece against a king capture (think of the rule as being designed to not allow 1...Kxb4 because 2.Nxb4 would capture the black king before the white king could be captured).
How does one improve board vision? In most ways that you do chess work: play long time control games slowly and view the board, do many chess puzzles, play over many annotated games, analyze with other players, watch chess videos, follow online grandmaster games, and do non-traditional puzzles aimed at specifically developing different types of skills.
In the next tip #10 we give some examples of how non-traditional puzzles may be a good way to improve both your board vision and the other visions as well.
Tip 10
You can improve your board vision by working on “non-traditional” puzzles that require goals different than win/mate/draw.
Just as a football player can improve his skills by going to the weight room and lifting weights even though there are no barbells on a football field during a game, a chess player can exercise his brain and chess logic by doing exercises and puzzles that require him to do things with pieces that do not normally occur in actual games.
Most non-traditional puzzles help you improve your board vision (Tip #9) and, depending on their type, possibly the other visions as well. For example, in his books
Chess Puzzles for Kids Vol I and II
, Jeff Coakley uses several types, including:
•
Double Whammy
(I believe these are also ‘Series mates in 2’) – White gets two moves in a row to checkmate Black who, unlike in traditional mate-in-two problems, doesn’t get a move. In double whammies, the first move cannot be check. (When a student objects that this cannot occur in a game, I change it to “Find a move that’s not check that threatens mate-in-one”, which is the same as the Double Whammy’s first move!)
•
Switcheroo
– Switch
any
two pieces on the board with each other to deliver a legal checkmate (one that could occur in a game) to the black king, The only switching restriction is that pawns cannot be switched with pieces on the first or eighth ranks.
Let’s give an example of each from Coakley’s books:
Double Whammy – White makes 2 moves in a row to mate Black
Several of my students start by suggesting the answer is 1.Na3 and then 2.Bf3#, which would be great if the bishop were not pinned by the knight move!
Solution:
1.Qa1
and
2.
Q
h1#
– A fantastic board vision problem as the queen has to travel ‘long distance’ to go from Black’s kingside to White’s queenside to White’s kingside to mate a king on Black’s queenside.
Next is a switcheroo: switch
any
two pieces on the board to leave Black’s king in a legal checkmate; pawns may not be switched to the first or eighth rank.
Switcheroo
Solution: Switch the white queen with the b3-pawn. There are many other switches that lead to mate, but none of them could legally occur in a game. For example, switching the white queen with the f7-pawn is mate, but that type of double check with the queen and rook on g6 is not possible in a game. Or switching the knight on c3 with the bishop on e7 would checkmate the black king, but that’s also not a legal position because both kings would be in check.
In my experience, most strong players find these types of non-traditional problems fun. I think they help improve your chess logic, board vision, tactical vision, and just your understanding of what pieces can do when coordinating together.
Tip 11
When you are visualizing, only a few pieces have moved (at five ply only five maximum, plus captures or castling). So you don’t need to ‘see’ the entire board, just be aware of which pieces have moved where and the rest are still as seen in the position in front of you.
Sometimes I run into the misconception that in order to visualize a possible move sequence, one needs to be able to visualize the entire position that could occur, comparable to what you would do if you were playing blindfold.
While the ability to visualize an entire board is helpful and those who can do it are at an advantage, for beginners and players trying to improve remedial visualization, it is a good idea to understand that such a feat is not necessary.
When you visualize one ply (a ‘ply’ is a half move; when a game begins 1.e4 e5 that is one move but two ply) ahead, then usually only one piece has moved from the position you see in front of you. There are some exceptions, like captures (which move one piece and remove another at the same time) and castling, which ‘displaces’ two pieces. So, in order to visualize the board, you only need to keep track of which pieces moved; the rest of the pieces, if uncaptured, are still exactly where you see them in front of you.
Admittedly, this is easier said than done, but understanding this simplifies the goal of being able to visualize what happens if a certain sequence were played.
Let’s take a position from my book
Back to Basics: Tactics
as an example (which you can also try to solve if you wish):
Black to play and win material
First I will give the answer and then we will do step-by step visualization:
1...Rxe4 2.Qxe4
(2.dxe4 Qxd1+ 3.Qxd1 Rxd1 wins a knight)
2...Qxe4 3.dxe4 Rxd1+
. Black’s first move is an example of a “sneaky pin” (Tip #105), since the white d-pawn is not easily guarding e4 due to the resultant pin on the rook on d1 by the black queen and rook on d8. Let’s visualize the main line:
1...Rxe4
– The rook that was on e5 is now on e4 and the knight that was on e4 is now off the board (permanently, so don’t bring it back in your imagination!). Everything else on the original position you see in front of you is the same, such as the white king on b1 and black king on g7, etc.
2.Qxe4
– The queen that was on e2 is now on e4 and the rook that originally was on e5 and captured on e4 is off the board. We now have the position we started with, but the white queen is on e4, and the knight and black rook on e5 have disappeared. Again, everything else is the same.
2...Qxe4
– The queen that was on d4 is now on e4 and the white queen, the white knight, and the black rook that was on e5 are all gone.
3.dxe4
– The pawn that was on d3 is now on e4 and both queens, the white knight, and the black rook that was on e5 are all gone.
3...Rxd1+
– But now the black rook that was on d8 is now on d1 and the white rook that was on d1 is gone. As previously, the pawn that was on d3 is now on e4 and both queens, the white knight, and the black rook that was on e5 are all gone. What final position should we visualize/imagine?
The final position
Tip 12
Augment your slow chess skills by playing speed chess/blitz. Speed chess is an excellent way to practice/learn openings, increase your tactical vision and board vision, and improve time management.
My students who don’t play speed chess, or not much of it, tend to suffer from the following problems:
• They don’t know their openings very well, or at least as well as they could. It takes a lot of practice with an opening and speed games provide roughly the same amount of experience per unit game as slow games (assuming you look up your openings after each series of games; Tip #134).
• They tend to panic when short on time even when they have plenty of time to make reasonable moves, because they are not used to playing with relatively less time on their clock.
• It generally takes them longer to develop quick tactical vision (helpful in slow chess too), since they don’t have to consistently find safe moves or winning tactics in a short time, as is required in speed chess.
I don’t usually suggest regular speed chess play until a player is around 1100 USCF/FIDE. Playing below that level is often frustrating because players don’t have the board vision to consistently make basic safe moves. But once you are strong enough to play speed games and have fun, it is a great way to develop the skills and knowledge listed above.
Having said that, there is a related issue (Tip Alert!):
If you want to be good at speed chess, first become really strong at slow chess.
I sometimes get students who exclusively play speed chess, but want to become a really good player overall. You certainly can get much better at chess by playing exclusively speed chess, but in doing so there is a limit to picking up many of the slow chess skills like visualization, chess logic, endgame experience, and others that are not developed as well in speed games.
Did you ever notice in World Blitz/Rapid events that although some grandmasters are much better at speed chess than others, there are no speed chess ‘savants’ who are rated c.2000 in slow chess but c.2800 in speed chess and can compete with Nakamura and Carlsen. How did all those world-class speed players get so proficient at speed play? By playing a ton of slow chess and developing excellent slow skills, which in turn allow one to play very well when given much less time to think. The best speed players are generally the best slow players.
Tip 13
Knowledge may only be about one third of chess ability. The rest are skills/traits like analysis, evaluation, time management, visualization, stamina, deductive logic, determination, perseverance, consistency, the ability to learn from mistakes, the ability to take instruction, and other skills.
The five main parts of chess knowledge are:
• Opening patterns and sequences.
• Endgame patterns and sequences.
• Positional patterns like weak pawns and squares, pawn majorities, etc.
• Tactical patterns and sequences.
• Principles (like the ones in many of these tips!).
This sounds like quite a bit, and it is. However, my belief is that the skills and traits mentioned in the tip, along with all the other applicable skills and traits, may make up even more, possibly something like two-thirds of chess ability.
What other skills and traits were not listed in the tip? You can probably name a few I omitted, such as ability to take instruction, concentration, consistency, ability to not let mistakes affect your play, and a favorite of many, ‘willpower’.
In talking with many aspiring players, quite a few have the paradigm that getting better at chess simply involves gathering more and more knowledge. But unless you are in your opening ‘book’ or in a deep exact endgame, you are probably in a position where you don’t know what to do, and must rely on your analytical and evaluation skills to pick the best move you can in a reasonable amount of time.
Without question, your knowledge of tactical and positional patterns, along with the proper allocation of principles, all play a big part in your analysis and evaluation. But in most ‘non-book’ positions, this knowledge must augment, not replace your ability to calculate and select moves.
In some very quiet, but unfamiliar positions it may be possible to select a move entirely on experience and principles. But to do so in tactical or complex positions that require careful analysis, I call the ‘hand-waving’ error. This thought process error is common in many low-rated players that either don’t realize what careful analysis requires or play mostly fast time controls which don’t allow them to develop careful analytical skills.
Developing skills is more difficult than simply acquiring knowledge. Some skills and traits are more innate. For example, some are born with incredible willpower, perseverance, or concentration, while other skills are easier to develop – one can improve stamina through physical workouts or time management through good coaching and awareness.
Tip 14
Hire a coach who will help you develop skills, and not just feed you knowledge.
Like anything else, there are good coaches and bad coaches. To make it more complicated, interaction between coach and student is personal, so someone who can be a great coach for one student may not be so good for another.
However, there is one precaution that I want to present: in general, good coaches not only provide knowledge, but also help the student develop skills. As noted in the previous tip, I believe chess is about two-thirds skills and one-third knowledge, and the one thing that most correlates with your overall chess playing strength is your analytical ability, a skill. Some coaches, however, primarily instruct by disseminating knowledge: they teach students opening sequences, ‘exact’ endgames like the Philidor and Lucena rook and pawn endgames, and even position-specific patterns like how to get a pawn majority rolling or conduct a typical kingside attack.
Don’t get me wrong – this knowledge they are teaching is valuable and even necessary information toward becoming a good player. The problem is if that is all that’s being taught, the important chess skills of analysis, evaluation, time management, how to study, how to tolerate and learn from losses, visualization, and so on, will not necessarily be addressed.
I once was discussing this on Twitter when someone tweeted at me (I paraphrase):
“Dan, I have gotten to 1500 primarily by enhancing my chess knowledge: I have learned more openings, endgames, tactical patterns, and principles. But I have no idea how to analyze an unfamiliar position and you have made me realize that if I want to continue development and play with much stronger opponents, I need to develop my analytical skills.”
Well said. When I was a developing player, I found that I could beat far more knowledgeable, older players just by ‘hanging around’ until the position got critical or complicated, and then using superior analytical skills to outplay them in those critical moments. Many players who just increase their knowledge and don’t work on increasing their skills can get ‘stuck’ faster than those with increasing skills.
As a chess coach who specializes in skills like thought process and time management, I often run into prospective students who have had prior coaches who only instruct by providing chess knowledge. This is understandable because there is not as much money in big-time chess as there is in other sports, so many strong players cannot make a living just with prize winnings, and need to augment their income with coaching. But some strong players, as in all sports, are not natural teachers, so providing their excellent chess knowledge is their main method of coaching. This also makes sense in that the main skills involved in being a strong player, like those under discussion, are quite different than some of the main skills in being a good teacher: communication, empathy, patience, listening, and understanding.
Students are sometimes surprised when I tell them that although I can teach them openings and ‘exact’ endgames too (although a strong player who knows the Grünfeld can probably teach it a lot better than I can!), my forte is trying to help students learn how to think correctly and consistently, manage their time better, understand and mitigate their mistakes and weaknesses, and learn how to evaluate more accurately. There are many excellent coaches out there who also impart these skills and traits to their students, as well as possessing that ‘strong player’ knowledge that is also so important.
So next time you interview a potential coach, listen carefully to his pitch to see what he is offering with regards to improving your skills. If you only hear something about increasing your knowledge (openings, endgames, tactical and positional patterns), you might want to think twice about your long-term prospects.
Tip 15
Chess knowledge is extremely important. But the corollary is that unnecessarily reinventing the wheel, especially in openings, is very inefficient.
Among my new, lower-rated students, about 99% admitted that after each game they did not religiously look up their openings in a book, database, engine, or video. Instead, they tended to either just do it sporadically or ‘guess’ through trial and error what might be a better move next time.
But when I was a beginner and exposed to books like Horowitz’s
Chess Openings Theory and Practice
or Evans and Korn’s
Modern Chess Openings 10
(
MCO
), I found it impossible not to look up my openings after each game. No one told me to do this; it would have been excruciatingly painful to keep making the same mistakes over and over when I could just consult a book and see what the grandmasters recommend in the opening positions I reached! Even when I played speed games, which was often, I tried to remember as many openings as possible after each session so I could look them up, too (this was around 30 years before online play was possible; for online play the chess server will save all your games, fast or slow, in a database, often with comments from an opening book or engine).
In the Netflix show
The Queen’s Gambit
, Beth Harmon learned openings in the early 1960s by perusing
MCO-9
and visualizing on the ceiling while she was getting ready to sleep. I am only a little younger than the fictitious Beth, so young Dan was doing the same thing with
MCO-10
in the late 60s, except I did not use the ceiling; I had a handy-dandy pocket chess set!
*Tip Alert!* After each game, ask the question ‘If I had to play this game again and my opponent made all the same moves, on what move would I improve by playing a different move?’ If you won when your opponent fell into a book trap and resigned, the answer may be ‘none!’, but that is rarely the case. In almost all games there comes an opening move where you could have improved on your play.
You can find the answer in a book on that opening, an opening database, or maybe a video about that opening. But in any case, if those fail for any reason, you can give the position to a strong engine to see its preference. If the move you played is evaluated as more than about 0.1 pawns worse than the engine’s move, there is a good chance that next time you can do better with the suggested move.
I tell the following story to illustrate this point:
I once had a new student, rated in the low 1000s, who said he had hundreds of chess books, but he had been playing for about 20 years and had not improved much. One of the things I do to evaluate students is to review a few recent slow games with them, preferably games they played against a human, and even better if the game is time-stamped so I can judge their time management. This student showed me a game where he was Black and he played the dubious Marshall Defense
1.d4 d5 2.c4 Nf6?!
, a common opening inaccuracy among club players:
White to play after 2...
N
f6?!
Black gets an inferior position after
3.cxd5 Nxd5
(3...Qxd5 4.Nc3 is also an effective ‘Attack with Something Worth Less’ AWL threat)
4.Nf3
. More accurate than the commonly played 4.e4 when the AWL threat of 5.e4 gives White a very large, almost theoretically winning, position.
When I saw my student made this move, I remarked something like “You shouldn’t do that; it’s not much of an opening. If White plays correctly you get a bad game.”
My student was surprised and a little taken aback. He replied, “What do you mean? I have been playing this defense for 20 years!”
So I questioned, “That may be so, but did you ever look up this opening in any of those books that you have?”
There was a brief silence when he finally admitted, “No.” Then I revealed, “If you did, you might have trouble finding it because it’s not a regular opening; more likely a footnote that suggests you shouldn’t do it.”
I just spent five minutes trying to find this line in
MCO-15
and could not, even when I looked under irregular defenses to 1.d4. Therefore, if my student had always followed this tip, he would have saved himself 20 years of playing an inferior defense to 1.d4 and 2.c4!
Tip 16
Play and study things that help you learn, not necessarily ideas that maximize short-term results.
I often hear a new student, let us assume rated in the 1000-1400 range, tell me “My previous instructor felt that I am better at positional play than I am in complications, so he felt I should play the Caro-Kann versus 1.e4”.
To most, this suggested approach seems logical since the instructor seems to be playing toward the student’s strengths. And indeed, if this student was about to play in the World Open U1400 section with the aspiration to win money, this ‘maximize your strengths and hide your weaknesses’ would likely be the best approach. Or if the student was at his lifetime peak rating of 2300+ and was playing in a FIDE event to try to obtain an International Master norm, again taking maximum advantage of the player’s strengths is the best way to go.
However, what is usually the case is that the 1000-1400 player’s long-term goals are not to win the World Open. They want to get to the next level and become a stronger player.
This long-term goal is the basis of why I disagree that the ‘Give a 1200 player some openings and strategies that maximize his short-term results’ is a good idea, unless that 1200 has given up on trying to become a 1400 or 1500. And since almost all my students have long-term goals to become much better players, I think this ‘hide the weakness’ approach could be more harmful than helpful in the long run.
I often run into students who don’t know how to analyze or evaluate very well. So, when they need to find a move and are asked to think out loud for me during a lesson, they ‘hand-wave’ a bunch of generalities and move on general principles rather than concrete lines. And indeed, playing openings that avoid early complications can aid them in playing this way, but if they really want to become better players, they should not avoid what they can’t do very well, but indeed meet these limitations head on by practicing careful analysis and evaluation, not taking steps to avoid it.
One gets better at something (say a weakness), by learning how to do it correctly and practicing it, not by masking that weakness. Masking works great for players who have already peaked and are trying to get maximum results; not so much for players aspiring to improve who are strongly held back by their weaknesses.
I call this approach ‘Playing/learning to maximize learn-term learning rather than playing/learning to maximize short-term results.’
Of course, my suggestion assumes you can practice your weaknesses – let’s say it includes visualization during analysis – in a positive way. But that is one of the things instructors and exercises are supposed to be for – improving a student’s skills. The instructor can’t just say “That was a mistake!” – he/she has to show the student how to analyze different types of positions, ways to practice/improve visualization, etc.
When trying to improve visualization, everyone does not benefit from the same approach. Most strong players became excellent at visualization just be playing thousands of long time control games slowly and carefully visualizing on every move. Practice may not make perfect, but it can make ‘much better’. In the age of the internet, some players attempt shortcuts via visualization exercises, and there are dozens of websites that use different methods. I suggest to my students “Try some of those websites and the ones that are most fun have the best chance of helping.”
With openings, beginners are often taught to open 1.e4 to learn how to fight from the first move. I agree with this approach! Varying this when you get to 1000 because you are still weak at tactics seems like going down the wrong path...
Tip 17
It is not how much you learn per unit game or book or puzzle that matters most; it is how much you learn per unit time. Therefore, if you read annotated games faster you get less from each game but read more games, so it becomes a trade-off. I chose to read as many games and authors as possible.
One of my basic homeworks for students is to read as many annotated games as possible. It is the main source that – if the author does a good job – covers all aspects of chess: openings, middlegames, endgames, principles, analysis, evaluation, time management, positional features. Because of all these benefits, I think most chess coaches suggest their students (except young scholastics) read annotated games, However, their advice on how to do so varies quite extensively.
Some coaches suggest that each game be read multiple times to get the most understanding out of the positions and annotations. Others suggest that the students ‘guess’ every move beforehand, playing one or both sides along with the players. Many coaches don’t differentiate much between various sub-genres of game books, putting excellent but non-instructive books like Fischer’s
My 60 Memorable Games
and
Karpov’s Best Games
alongside instructive anthologies like Chernev’s
The Most Instructive Games of Chess Ever Played
or Nunn’s more advanced
Understanding Chess Move by Move
.
There is no doubt you learn more playing over a game three times than you do reading it only once. And if there were a very restricted number of books and games, this approach might make sense. But economists have a concept (used quite a bit outside of economics) called diminishing returns. It means that the more you repeat something, the less you get out of it each time.
To make a rough (and admittedly simplified) model with some basic math, suppose each time you read a game, you get a benefit of 50% of what you did not know before that reading. Therefore, the first time you read a game, you only learn 50% of what you maximally could have learned. The second time you also get 50%, but only 50% of the 50% you did not already learn in the first pass, a total of 25% more. That means the second time through you have learned 50%+25% = 75% total new information. A third time through would get 50% of the unlearned 25%, for an additional 12.5% and a total gain of 87.5%.
Now 87.5% is certainly more than 50%, but suppose instead of reading one game three times, you read three games one time and got 50% out of each. If we make the plausible but only vaguely approximate suggestion that the benefit of reading each game, in the long run, is roughly the same, it is better to get 50% from each of three games in the same time it takes to get 87.5% from one game thrice. Of course, this model is a simplification and some games are more instructive than others, but in the long run this idea holds.
When I started out, I did not have a chess coach, but I did talk to many stronger players. In my first two years I went from unrated to 1900 USCF, and got to 2000 in my third year. In those days ratings were much lower so I actually made the USCF ‘Top-25 junior’ list by my fourth year of tournament play.
One method that helped me greatly was to read as many annotated games by as many authors as quickly as possible. When I first started out, this was a little difficult because I did not have the ‘board vision’ to absorb an entire position at a glance, but once I could, I simply got out a board (usually a pocket chess set for convenience so I could do it anywhere, like at my grandmother’s house while she and my mother were making dinner), made the move, read what the author had to say about the viewed position, got to the next move, the next, and then to the subsequent game and finally to the next book.
My estimate is that I read about 2,000 annotated games in three years, roughly two per day. People ask me “Did you play over all the side variations?” My answer is “No, only the ones that either looked fun or I was curious about!” Then they say, “But if you played over the games that fast, didn’t you learn a lot less than if you had studied them more slowly?” My answer to that is always “Only if you measure learning, by how much you learn per unit game. But that is, in many senses, a false measurement; I wanted to get better as fast as I could, so the correct unit of measurement is how much I learned per unit time.”
If you asked me to replay any of those games, I could not (well, maybe Morphy against Count Isouard and the Duke of Brunswick). But I had seen the same important lessons many times – how and when to use pawn majorities, good and bad pieces, how to play almost every opening imaginable, where the pieces flowed at various phases of the game, etc. These tactical and positional aspects, especially the important ones, tended to occur frequently in the most instructive games. Even though I was skipping many sidelines and read games quickly, I spent many, many hours going through all those games, in a sense learning by gestalt.
Would this work for everyone? No – first, it must be fun! Why did I do this? Was it just to become a better player? No, it was because it was so much fun that every time I finished a game I hoped I had time to do the next one. If reading those games is work, you won’t do it for long.
This idea of getting the most per unit time rather than per unit game, puzzle, or anything is, I think, important, especially with finite time but many resources.
Tip 18
One of the most important things you can do is review your slow games with your opponent.
I was surprised when I found how many players either don’t review their games with their opponent or failed to understand how helpful that is. Of course, it is more difficult to review your game with your opponent when you are online than it is over-the-board, but in each case if you have the time, you should offer to do so.
One obvious benefit of reviewing the game with the opponent, as opposed to anybody or anything (in the case of engines), is that they are the only ones that can explain why they did or did not do things, like make certain moves or carry out ideas and plans. Without that information the complete story (and thus opportunities to learn) cannot be formed.
Some players only want to review games with stronger opponents, feeling this gives them somewhat of a free lesson. It is true that they are the best opponent for which a game review would be most helpful, but it is also beneficial to do it with equal or lesser opponents. It is easily seen that the reason you should want to do it with lesser opponents is that, from their viewpoint, they are the ones reviewing it with the stronger opponent (you!), and if you feel you benefit from reviewing with stronger opponents, you should grant your weaker opponents the same benefit! Play it forward...
What about opponents of equal strength? The good news is that equal strength does not mean equal knowledge and understanding of all areas. Your opponent may be of equal strength, but may know the opening much better, or understand the pawn structure better. In that sense two equal players can ‘bootstrap’ each other, much in the same way a ‘study buddy’ of equal strength can help a study session.
What are some of the main things to review after the game?
• Opening – where could each player improve.
• Complications and critical moments – move the pieces around and analyze to see what could have happened.
