• Find the losing move – if someone won, identify the moment where the losing side could have played a better move to save the game. Sometimes identifying the losing move is trivial, but other times finding it takes a great deal of analytical work!
Tip 19
*Top-10 Tip*
Think of a draw offer as an offer to remain ignorant of what you would have learned the remainder of the game.
As a professional chess instructor, I get a biased sample of the chess world – almost 100% of my students take lessons so they can improve. Not everyone who plays chess has a main goal of improvement, but a large percentage do.
If your goals include improvement then, when you are playing a game, you want to not just try to win, but also do things that will help you become better in future games. That may include a variety of suggestions that I have discussed in this book and other places, like looking up your openings after the game to avoid repeating the same mistakes or reviewing your game with your opponent, strong players, and possibly a chess engine.
Just as playing more tournaments will give you more experience, so will playing more moves! If you play long time control games and practice good time management, you will often have opportunities to play a variety of challenging endgames.
However, many players try to shortcut the game and avoid this learning experience by offering premature draws. This could be for a variety of reasons. They are:
• Wish to gain rating points so they offer draws to higher-rated players,
• Are afraid of their opponent,
• Feel that they are not very good at endgames, and/or
• Are afraid of losing so bail out even in clearly winning positions.
I see these premature draw offers quite often, and advise my students according to the following axiom – play and learn! The worst thing that will happen to you is that you make a mistake and lose, but then you learn something and are a better player for future games. Sure, there are many ‘dead-drawn’ positions, such as those with bishops of opposite colors, where you won’t learn anything by continuing and a offering and/or taking a draw is wise. And sometimes there are external circumstances: not feeling well/tired, a prize at stake, or a family emergency that necessitate otherwise unwise draw offers.
Best is to emulate Bobby Fischer, who was famous for refusing draw offers before his opponent could finish the sentence: “Do you want a...” – “No!”
Tip 20
It is easy to obtain, study, and understand material that is inappropriate for what you need to get to the next level, so beware and try to avoid this trap.
Assume you are supposed to be taking a class in Russian 2, but accidentally walk into a Russian 5 class. Would you quickly notice that the other students and the material are beyond your current abilities and realize that you are in the wrong class? I bet you would!
Similarly, assume you are studying math and are up to Algebra I, but someone gives you a Calculus II book to read. Would that be an easy read for you? I think you would probably look at a couple of pages, realize the issue, and put it aside for a few years.
But unfortunately, chess is much different. Suppose you know most of the basic definitions, like what is the difference between a capture and an attack, winning the exchange versus coming out ahead in a trade, what an isolated, doubled, or backward pawn is (well, maybe not backward pawn; most of my students think they know, but are usually incorrect). If you know about open and semi-open files and can recognize the Queen’s Gambit Declined, as most players rated 1200+ can, then you can understand 95%+ of most chess books ever written.
And that is the problem! It would be much better if chess were like Russian or math, and players could instantly recognize a book that is inappropriate for what they need to learn to get to the next level. But chess is not like those subjects.
So now we have the worst of all possible worlds:
• Most players can read almost all the material, no matter how inappropriate.
• The material is usually not presented in a classroom situation. If it were in a classroom, material would not be introduced until it is next in the logical progression of what the students know and need. Instead, purchasing chess material is more often in a fashion of the Old West in the US, where anything goes.
• Publishers and authors of advanced material want to sell that material and make money, but the overwhelming potential audience to purchase that material are players under average tournament strength (including non-tournament players who may purchase), so the temptation is to find ways to market the material as being good for everyone, or at least the average player.
The result of the situation is that when I get a new student, there is a very strong chance that at least some of the material they are currently reading is inappropriately ‘too high’ for them. To make a further analogy with schoolwork, it’s as if my third grade student currently is using the textbooks
Read with Dick and Jane
,
War and Peace
,
Plane Geometry
,
Introduction to Gene Therapy
,
Advanced Thermodynamics
, and
Fuzzy Wuzzy learns to Count
!
When I try to explain to new students why some of their texts are inappropriate, many get defensive. They tell me how much they enjoy the book or how much they are getting out of it. It doesn’t matter that they are making unsafe moves; they want to know more about the Caro-Kann. And yes, by the way, they are stuck at a 1200 rating; how should they get out of that? (Tip #89)
I was having this conversation with Michael de la Maza, who wrote
Rapid Chess Improvement
. Michael related the story that when he was stuck at 1400 and could not figure out how to rise above that level, someone suggested he read Silman’s
How To Reassess Your Chess
. Michael read the book, especially the early edition’s emphasis on good knight versus bad bishops and vice versa, and thought “Aha, I didn’t know this stuff! This is just what I need!” So he went out and played an endgame with a knight versus a bishop and spent time thinking about how to get a good knight against a bad bishop. Then he said, “I dropped my knight!”
The moral of Michael’s story was to back up what he and I both believed, the content of this tip. Michael was still at the level where he was not consistently making safe moves and, when you are still at that level, you get diminishing returns (if not negative) on most issues other than those that help you more consistently make safe moves.
Those issues would include good time management and a good thought process, which in turn allow you the time to consistently check to see if your candidate moves are safe, along with good ‘board vision’ to see what is happening all over the board quickly and accurately, and an excellent grasp on basic tactical motifs, not just so you can solve them, but so you can recognize them quickly and accurately (Tip #91).
In most disciplines, if you study advanced material before you master the basics, you likely will not be able to understand it well anyway. If you are learning basic arithmetic and someone hands you a calculus book, you won’t be able to use it. But chess is not like that: any experienced adult chess player who understands basic concepts like weak pawns or tactical motifs, can understand almost anything written in the most advanced chess books. But therein lies the problem: when they read those books, instead of putting them down for more appropriate material, they think like de la Maza “Aha!, this is just what I needed...”
Tip 21
There are many good books of all levels and what may work for one person at one level may not work for another.
I often find adults under the ‘Hope Chess’ level (1600-1700 USCF/FIDE, 2000 LiChess; Tip #65) who don’t consistently make safe moves, working too much on openings, rare exact endgames or advanced positional concepts when that’s not what’s preventing them from improving.
Sometimes new students ask something along the lines of “What is your program for getting a player, like me, from 1400 to 1600?” It is a reasonable question, but the underlying assumption that all 1400s have the same strengths and weaknesses, and thus need to do the same work to improve, is not generally true.
For example, some players move much too quickly and some players move much too slowly. Some players are addicted to fast games and others – at the same level – hate them because they get frustrated they can’t consistently make good moves.
Some class players like to memorize openings and others think openings should never be studied, and end up making the same mistakes early in the game over and over, year after year. When I first started working with NM Josh Bowman, when he was about 11, he knew an inordinate amount of opening theory for someone his rating, which was then about 1100. I told Josh, “Put away your opening books for a year or two; what you need to become the next level has nothing to do with openings; you know openings better than many players rated 1600, so that’s not your problem.”
Even the same exercises may not work for two players of identical strengths and weaknesses. There are many visualization websites and exercises; yet I have found that what works for one person may be completely useless for someone else. As an example, more than one website uses blindfold identification of the color of the squares as a visualization aid and tool; that never worked for me and some of my students, but apparently it has helped quite a few others (I get asked about it every now and then). I got most of my visualization skills from playing very many long time control games slowly and trying to visualize the pieces when doing analysis on every analytical move. In this sense practice may not make perfect, but it can sure make you better.
I loved Kmoch’s
Pawn Power in Chess
, but others do not because of its arcane nomenclature (Tip #78). Chess work helps more when it is fun (Tip #77).
Tip 22
When you purchase a chess book electronically, an ebook, consider buying it on an app that makes the book ‘come alive’ like Forward Chess.
When you purchase a chess book ‘hard copy’, you are paying for the printing and the distribution. If you purchase it on Kindle, you are bypassing some of the cost, but the problem is that Kindle is primarily an adjustable electronic picture of the book. That has some advantages like you can read it on your mobile device or you can change the size of the font. But to take full advantage of the electronic version of a chess book, much better to purchase it on Forward Chess (or a competing app if there is one), The advantages of using Forward Chess are:
• It is an ebook, so like Kindle you save publishing costs (it is less expensive) and can read it on your mobile device.
• Forward Chess connects the moves in a chess book with a chess board. So when you click on the moves, you get a diagram of that chess position. This diagram is not just for the main moves of a game in a game book, but also the side variations, puzzle answers, etc. Therefore, unlike Kindle or a hard copy, you get an automatic electronic board and don’t need a real board or a third party electronic board – it is all built into Forward Chess!
Because they have most of the mobile ‘chess reader’ market, Forward Chess offers an extensive library of current and classic titles to potential buyers. At the time of this writing, their website forwardchess.com/products lists over 1,000 titles, and is growing by the week.
A curious final note: Forward Chess publishes several of my titles, so I asked Forward Chess why they did not publish one of my best books,
The Improving Chess Thinker
. Their answer was interesting: because the de Groot ‘think out loud’ exercise requires students to give ‘stream of conscious’ chatter about everything going through their head when they make a move, the disconnected nature of the moves they mention does not go well with the Forward Chess benefit of showing a diagram, since there often is not the flow of moves out of a position that you see in a game, puzzle, or side variation. Therefore, it was difficult, if not impossible, for Forward Chess to transfer the thoughts of the players into meaningful diagrams using the app’s main benefit, their ‘live’ board.
Tip 23
Just as a chain is only as strong as its weakest link, you are only as good as your weakest moves.
I cannot tell you how many times I have heard from both students and lower-rated club players “I was playing a higher-rated player and I had a good game for 20-25-30 moves but then x happened and I threw it all away.”
Many of these players therefore deceive themselves into thinking that they are just as good, or at least on the doorstep, of being as good as the player with whom they had an even position until the game got complicated or critical. But the time when it is most obvious that the better player outplays the weaker one is not early with opening knowledge or principles (although that too happens); it’s much more likely to occur when the position is complicated, a critical strategic choice has to be made, careful endgame analysis must be done, and/or when time is running short and critical decisions need to be made quickly.
For example, if you play like a 1600 for 40 moves and then play like a 700 for the next move, that is usually enough to lose to most 1600s. There was a recent game at our club between a 2000 player and a 1500 player. The 2000 player made a mistake early in the game and lost a pawn for just fair compensation. But he was able to hang in and eventually win the pawn back to achieve a fairly equal but dynamic endgame. However, once the time for both players started to run short, the 1500 player fell apart and eventually resigned as he put his rook en prise trying to stop the 2000 player from promoting a pawn.
This tip is one of the biggest reasons why lower-rated players’ infatuation with memorizing opening theory is so misguided. It is not that knowing more about the opening is wrong or unhelpful; it is much more that the ability to quickly and accurately analyze and evaluate positions, especially critical ones, is far more likely to decide the game.
Admittedly, it is easier to learn a new line in the Nimzo-Indian than it is to become a clearly better player when time is short and critical decisions must be made. But that is why good coaches emphasize homework and competitive slow play to help students slowly develop a better recognition of critical positions with more knowledge, skills, and abilities that can deal with those situations better. Like in all sports, there are very few panaceas or verbal epiphanies that instantly allow a weaker player to become magically much better.
I improved greatly when I realized I could not just make a move, wait for my opponent to reply, and if they made a check, capture, or threat hope that I had an answer. My epiphany was when I understood that I had to look at least one move ahead to make sure I had a safe answer next move to any dangerous reply from the opponent. But although that one enormous realization made a big difference in my play, I did not jump from 1400 to 1900 overnight – it still took me about 18 months of consistent tournament play, going to the strong Germantown Chess Club and picking the brains of some of the best players, and reading about 2,000 annotated master games. It was a lot of fun work!
During that time, I also realized that even though my older and more experienced players had better knowledge, such as in the openings, if I could just follow good opening principles, make safe moves, and ‘hang in there’ until the game got complicated, I had good chances. All I needed to do was to recognize when the game got critical (‘criticality assessment’), take my time on the most critical moves, and try to ‘out-analyze’ my opponent during those crucial situations. This strategy worked amazingly well as it took me up to 1900 quite rapidly by outplaying players rated 1600-1800. It was not until I got to 1900 and was being outplayed in even material endgames by equally analytical 2000 players that I found I had to learn much more about subtle positional strategy.
My experience was comparable to the reason why Michael de la Maza, author of
Rapid Chess Improvement
, gave up chess; he also found he could move up among class players with a similar strategy of mastering the most important basics in safety, board vision, time management, and activity. But when he got his rating up to USCF expert level (2000), he told me he would have to do some real work to learn about chess in order to improve further, and he felt it was not worth it! I had gone through a similar path, so I understood, but unlike Michael, I had decided to do that extra work to reach one more level before ‘real life’ intervened.
Tip 24
Don’t try to hide your weaknesses or downplay your worst moves; find out what you need to improve most and concentrate on those areas.
Suppose your rating is 1400 and, on the average, you know book moves for about six moves into your openings. Your playing strength for those moves is maybe 3500. If an average game is about 40 moves, what is your playing strength for the other 34 moves if your final rating is 1400? I think most would agree it is about 1400.
Now suppose further that you lock yourself in a closet for many months and try to memorize enough opening lines that you know all your openings to about eight moves deep (it could take longer...). What is your playing strength for the other 32 moves? 1400, of course. But now what impact has learning all those new opening lines had on your overall rating? It is still going to be about 1400, maybe a little over.
Let us go further and give you a pill that will enable you to memorize an entire book about one of your major openings (White, Black vs 1.e4, or Black vs 1.d4). That is more than most humans could likely learn in a lifetime. What’s your rating now? 1400? If you take three pills and learn all your major openings that deeply, what could that do to an otherwise 1400 player? Maybe if he/she is very lucky, make them a 1500? If so, what does that say about studying openings if you are 1400 and aspire to be 2000?
To become a much better player you cannot just learn a bunch of knowledge (Tip #13). That is very helpful, of course, but what you also must do is improve your skills, such as analysis, evaluation, and time management.
Sometimes I have a new student who tells me they have recent games, but they want to show me their subtle endgame losses, not the games where they put their queen en prise. That is understandable, but if it turns out they put pieces en prise every few games, those horrible mistakes are going to have a much bigger effect on their playing strength than the subtle mistakes they make in the endgame.
These players often say “I know what I did wrong; I didn’t see the bishop on the diagonal” or something similar. But from my instructor standpoint, I want to know
why
they did not see the bishop. Did they:
• Play too quickly.
• Lack the ‘board vision’ to see the bishop on the other side of the board.
• Execute a poor thought process that never checked to see if the queen move was safe?
Once we can identify the source of these important issues, then we can begin to take steps that will lessen the probability that this player will continue to put pieces
en prise. If we can eliminate, or at least minimize, the big mistakes first, then we can work on the next most egregious mistakes or misconceptions. In this manner we get the biggest ‘bang for the buck’ for our lesson and study time.
It is natural for players who find out about advanced ideas like how to win a Lucena endgame to then want to learn those advanced ideas and, on the surface, doing so may not seem really harmful. But just as you want to learn the multiplication tables before doing interesting geometry, chess is best learned from filling in the basics first.
I tell the story that I was already in the Top-25 juniors in the US when I lost a rook and pawn endgame because I did not know the Philidor endgame draw well enough. Yet my lack of that ‘basic’ knowledge did not stop me from becoming stronger than 95% of tournament players ever get. This experience told me it was important to learn the Philidor, but that type of specific knowledge, while very helpful, is not that necessary to become a very strong player.
Similarly, I read the story of how Victor Korchnoi, when a young grandmaster, did not properly know the rules for castling. His opponent had castled queenside and the rook had passed through an attacked square (b1 or b8); Korchnoi thought that was illegal, but his amused opponent told him it was legal and that he should check with the arbiter. The arbiter further explained that it was only the king that could not pass through an attacked square when castling. The moral is the same: you can become a very strong player without getting some isolated basic ideas correct, so long as you are very proficient with the most important aspects of the game like analysis and evaluation, especially with regards to tactical/safety issues, and have a good grasp on strategy.
Tip 25
Chess is a mental game, so anything that affects your concentration or stamina can have a large effect: outside worries, conditioning, illness/headache, concerns about chess factors not involving the current move, etc
.
Good players are able to concentrate on almost every move of every game, but weaker players are often distracted by issues that probably should be resolved before or after the game. When I review games with students, quite often lower-rated students will answer queries about their questionable decisions with answers something like:
• “I was still upset about not seeing his pawn was hanging two moves ago.”
• “I knew I was losing so I was not paying attention when he blundered.”
• “My girlfriend came into the room so I was not paying as much attention as I should.”
• “I was getting tired, so instead of getting up and stretching or getting a glass of water, I kept playing and moved too fast.”
There are many other similar issues that crop up to cause mistakes. Some distractions are unavoidable, but it goes without saying that weaker players are much more likely to fall victim to these types of problems.
The way I often state this issue is “Anything that you are thinking about during your move which is not related to finding the best move you can in a reasonable amount of time is likely counter-productive.”
If you made a mistake, ‘there’s no crying over spilt milk’ – it is over and done with, and worrying about it when you should be concentrating on the current move is not helpful.
Therefore, when playing, keep in mind that you want to concentrate on the current move, or maybe the near-term plan. But worrying about things that happened earlier in the game, or wondering what you are going to do after the game should be kept to a minimum, if not eliminated.
Tip 26
Don’t stop your opponent from making bad moves.
I think this tip speaks for itself! Here is a recent example from a student’s game:
Black to play
In this position Black has the advantage because his minor pieces are more active and White’s queenside pawns are a little vulnerable. Black can keep his king in the center and connect rooks with 1...Kd7 or try to open a second line for his rooks with 1...a6.
The move he chose in the game was not bad, but his reason for it was! He played
1...d5
, which fixes the white central pawns and removes a black pawn from the dark squares for his bishop’s later mobility. But his primary reason was to prevent White from ‘gaining space’ and attacking his knight with d4-d5.
However, if Black
knew
White was going to play d4-d5 he should let him do it! That move greatly increases the scope of the bishop on the long diagonal (it is no longer hitting a pawn guarded by a pawn), and forces the knight to a occupy White’s weak square, ...Ne6-c5. If after 1...Kd7 if White plays 2.Kd2 Black’s advantage is about ‘+1’, but if 2.d5? Nc5 Black’s advantage is ‘+5.3’!
Here’s another example from our chess club last night:
1.e4 c5 2.Nc3 Nc6 3.f4 d6 4.Nf3 Bg4 5.Be2
.
Black to play
Here Black played
5...a6
. Later, I asked him why and he responded that he didn’t like the look of 6.Bb5. But White has just played 5.Be2; he could have already played Bb5 on his fifth move, and he didn’t! So if we told Black to make another fifth move and White would respond 6.Bb5, Black should be happy that White is moving a piece twice, especially to a square he could have moved to originally in one move, and which makes no serious threat. In other words, Black is spending a tempo playing 5...a6 to prevent a move he should be happy to see!
If Black had given another reason such as “I wanted to expand on the queenside with ...b7-b5” or “I was planning to put my queen on c7, so I need to shield myself from a later Nc3-b5 after also playing ...e7-e6 to stop Nc3-Nd5” then this example would not have made it into this book. But as soon as he said he wanted to stop a move he should have welcomed, I thought of this tip! Here is a third example from a recent student game:
Black to play
In this position Black should just play something like 1...0-0, while the engine prefers 1...Nd4.
But instead, Black played
1...a6
. When I asked my student why he played 1...a6, he said he wanted to stop 2.Nb5. But when I asked him what 2.Nb5 did that made it worth stopping, he said he did not know.
Instead if Black plays, say, 1...0-0 and White does play 2.Nb5, the knight is not threatening anything. Therefore, if Black then plays 2...a6, that makes some sense since it costs one tempo to play 2...a6, but costs White two tempi to play 2.Nb5 and then retreat the knight next move. On the other hand, playing 1...a6 to stop 2.Nb5 just costs Black a tempo he could have used constructively elsewhere.
One final example from a well-known ‘exact’ endgame:
Black to play
I call this the ‘dance around maneuver’ because Black is going to win the d-pawn and White needs to ‘dance around’ the d2-square, not going there until after Black captures on d4, so White can get the opposition and draw: for example,
1...Kc4 2.Ke2 Kc3
(or 2...Kxd4 3.Kd2)
3.Ke1
(or any square that touches d2), and if
3...Kxd4 4.Kd2
with an opposition draw (this well-known exact endgame could finish 4...Ke4 5.Ke2 d4 6.Kd2 d3 7.Kd1! Ke3 8.Ke1 d2+ 9.Kd1 Kd3 stalemate).
Notice that 1...Kc4 is the most accurate because an unsuspecting white player might play 2.Kd2?? and after 2...Kxd4 Black has the opposition and wins.
It therefore would be clearly less practical for Black to play 1...Kc3, even though this also draws because it would give White no chance to play 2.Kd2 (don’t stop your opponent from making bad moves!). After 1...Kc3 2.Ke2 Kxd4 even unsuspecting players would probably stumble into 3.Kd2.
From this analysis, we can conclude that while 1...Kc4 and 1...Kc3 are both draws, only 1...Kc4 gives White an initial chance to go wrong.
Tip 27
Principles do not need to be applied to either book opening or book endgame moves, or moves where your analysis tells you which move is clearly best. Principles are best applied in situations where you do not know what to do and analysis does not indicate a clearly best move.
A principle, unlike a rule, is a guideline, a heuristic. They are meant to help you if you don’t know what to do. But there are two cases where principles are not needed:
• If your analysis clearly reveals a best move, or one where you can prove that no other move could possibly be better.
• In ‘book’ positions in the opening and endgame.
Therefore, if you are in the opening and the engine, database, book, and/or master videos all say that in that exact same position you should move a piece twice, then you don’t care that the most important principle in the opening is ‘Move every piece once before you move any piece twice, unless there’s a tactic.’
Suppose you study the old main line in the Two Knights Defense,
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bc4 Nf6 4.Ng5
.
Black to play after 4.
N
g5
There is the trick question: Does 4.Ng5 violate the principle
that in the opening, move every piece once before you move any piece twice, unless there is a tactic?
Literally, it does seem to violate that principle, as no one could argue against the fact that the white knight has moved twice before some of the other pieces have moved once. Plus the fact that 4.Ng5 is a threat, but it is not a tactic; White threatens 5.Nxf7 or 5.Bxf7+, but that threat is easily parried by the main response 4...d5.
But here we reach a question of definition, opinion, and semantics: 4.Ng5 is a book move. It is acceptable (close to best) for both grandmasters and engines. If you studied that move and played it with the knowledge that it is recommended, then you don’t need to follow principles for that move; they simply would not apply. So in that strong sense 4.Ng5 is not violating the ‘don’t move pieces twice’ principle.
The next twist: suppose the player playing White does not know any book and plays 4.Ng5. Since he is not following book knowledge, we could conclude that he
is
violating the ‘don’t move any piece twice’ principle.
A final bit of semantics: Suppose that same book-lacking player mistakenly thought that 4.Ng5, for whatever reason, was a tactic. Then could we switch 180 degrees once more and say he is not violating the principle because it states that moving pieces twice for the purposes of executing – or avoiding – a tactic is often acceptable!
When I was teaching NM Dan Benjamin, I told Dan, “If your analysis clearly reveals the best move, then it doesn’t matter how many principles it violates; just do it!” Another way of saying this is: If you are willing to argue with a room full of grandmasters that none of them can prove a clearly superior move to the one you intend, then that is a sufficient ‘trigger’ to stop thinking and play it.
Tip 28
When multiple principles conflict, ask yourself which ones are the more important or best apply to the current state of the position.
There are many – to put it mildly – situations in chess where you are not in a ‘book’ position and your analysis may not yield a clearly best move. That is when you want to take your time and augment your analysis with as many principles as might possibly apply to that position. In those situations, if all the principles point to one move, there is a good chance that this move is a strong candidate, if not the clearly best move.
Suppose your analysis does not yield a clearly best move, multiple principles apply, and they conflict by pointing toward multiple moves. In those situations, you need to use your experience and judgement to augment your analysis and knowledge of the principles.
For example, if there are few pawns on the board and you are ahead on material, and one principle says ‘In deep endgames you need pawns to promote to win the game, so don’t trade too many pawns’ and another principle says ‘Trade off your opponent’s dangerous pawns that have promotion potential’, then you could ask “How dangerous is my opponent’s pawn?” If you get rid of your opponent’s pawn, will that leave you with enough material to win the game? If you do not get rid of your opponent’s pawn, will it threaten to promote and cause you to lose your material advantage? In those situations, one usually needs the extra pawn(s) to win, but it depends on the position.
The following position illustrates the conflict of the two above principles:
White to play after 1...c6
If White does not capture the pawn but instead plays 2.d6, then Black will have a passed c-pawn. If White allows Black the option of capturing his d-pawn, he will be left with only one pawn on the board.
From the above, which principle would be the stronger one here? If White retains the pawn with 2.d6, is either Black’s c-pawn sufficiently dangerous or can Black just win the d-pawn anyway with 2...Kc5?
Finding the correct answer requires both judgement and analysis. If 2.d6 Kc5 White can save the pawn with 3.Nf5 or possibly 3.Ne4+. If White trades the pawns, or does nothing and allows Black to trade, then his extra knight might be meaningless if Black can sacrifice his knight for White’s final pawn, since a king and two knights cannot force checkmate against a lone king (there are mating positions, but the knights cannot force the king into them unless it is already in an almost mate, awkward position). The threat of Black sacrificing his knight for the last remaining pawn would make the win difficult, to say the least.
Moreover, if 2.d6 the passed black c-pawn is not that dangerous, so the correct idea is to follow the principle that suggests that when you are ahead on material, unless you are a rook or more ahead, you want to retain enough pawns to threaten to promote. Therefore,
2.d6
is by far the best move to try to win.
Sometimes one guideline is more important than the other because it involves the likely outcome of the game, while the other principle only involves the relative values of the pieces. Or one principle may be more pertinent because it applies specifically to the current state of the game while the other is more general and is less effective in the current state. In most cases, when principles conflict, there is often a reason why one might be more helpful. In the occasional case where the principles conflict and appear to cancel out, use your experience and judgement, backed by some analysis, to determine which moves and/or plans might be the best course of action.
Tip 29
The stronger or more important a principle is, the more vital it is to know it, understand it, and try to follow it.
Strong principles are ones with few exceptions, almost rules.
Important principles are ones which occur frequently or, if violated, could have a major impact on the outcome of a game.
In his book
Grandmaster Secrets: Openings
, GM Andrew Soltis writes: “But there’s at least one bit of development advice that turns out to be true more often than any other...Line up a rook against the enemy queen.”
Most authors have never heard of my definitions of strong and important principles. But if GM Soltis had, it is clear that when he wrote “turns out to be true more often than any other” that would be synonymous with ‘stronger’. So Soltis is claiming that this principle is not just strong, but possibly the strongest opening principle!
In the endgame, the situation with king and pawn versus king where it is not a rook’s pawn, the offensive king is immediately in front of the pawn and the defending king is two squares in front of the offensive king is a common case of the opposition (whoever does
not
have the move has the opposition and gets the result they want with best play). This is a rule, but it does have one exception, when the offensive king is on the sixth rank and it is the offensive king’s move:
White to play
Even though it is White’s move and Black technically has the opposition, this position is a win for White no matter whose move it is; therefore it is the sole exception to the general rule for king and pawn vs king opposition. I call this exceptional situation part of my Tic-Tac-Toe rule (https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=kzfFjehUSJs), which has no exceptions. Other authors give this exception a different description, like ‘the offensive king has reached one of the critical squares’ (which I think is less specific than my Tic-Tac-Toe rule, but that is just my opinion).
Therefore, a rule is different than a principle such as Soltis’s rook opposite queen advice above, or any of thousands of others, such as those found in Alburt and Lawrence’s
Chess Rules of Thumb
or many in Soltis’s
The Wisest Things Ever Said About Chess
.
If important principles consist of two flavors, common and critical, we should give an example of each:
An important principle because of frequency would be ‘Develop knights before bishops’ (I should add this principle is interpreted as usually NBNB rather than NNBB, so that one can castle as quickly as possible). The principle is important because in every game one should develop all four minor pieces: two knights and two bishops. It is not a strong principle because there are many exceptions where bishops are developed before knights and it is not critical; you can often develop a bishop first without serious consequence.
An important principle that’s less frequent but critical would be ‘When ahead a piece (bishop or knight) in the endgame, make sure you can retain at least one pawn to promote or, in other words, (in those situations) avoid unnecessary trades of too many pawns’ (see the previous Tip #28). This is critical because if you trade your final pawn and are only ahead a piece, even if there are other pieces on the board, it is often impossible to win. For example, most pawnless endgames with rook and minor piece versus a rook are drawn.
Many principles need to be placed in context, such as the intended state of the game for which they are intended or how and why they make sense. I created the definitions of strong and important
to help students better understand principles and how they should be learned and applied.
Tip 30
I saw a recent study confirming an earlier one that the best way to ‘cram’ for a tournament/event is get lots of sleep leading to the event!
You need to be fresh to play. Students often ask: “I have a tournament next weekend. What is the best way to prepare for the event?”
Getting ready for a tournament in some ways resembles getting ready for a test, but in other ways does not. With a test, you are often studying, putting things into your memory (short term and long), that you will have to regurgitate during the test. But in chess events, unless your opponent is playing an opening you just learned and need to remember, it is unlikely getting information into your short-term memory for that time period is going to be very helpful. In that sense, you can’t ‘cram’ for a chess tournament.
Besides getting a lot of rest, there are some things you can do, in moderation, which should help:
• If you are not intimately familiar with how to play games at the time control of the event (*Tip Alert!*), play one or two games a week or so ahead of time at that time control to learn how to pace yourself using the ‘Goldilocks Principle’ of not playing too fast or too slow. You don’t want to wait until you are halfway through the event to realize you are playing too fast or too slow!
• Do some tactical puzzles to warm up. Emphasize ones you do not always catch – many players U1700 often miss ‘removal of the guard’ tactics, so that might be one motif to brush up.
• Review your most frequently met opening tabiyas (standard opening sequences in your repertoire). For example, if everyone is playing the Advanced French (3.e5) against your French Defense, go over the main lines or traps. If instead you are having trouble with Black when opponents play the London, use a book, engine, or video to review how you want to meet that opening.
• There may be some special problem you are having, e.g. maybe forgetting to check if your moves are safe, or playing too fast when your opponent is short on time. If so, you can make a short ‘mental checklist’ of things you want to keep in mind to work on, or improve, for this event. Unfortunately, you cannot consult a physical checklist during a formal over-the-board tournament; it’s against the rules!
• If the games are long enough, prepare an appropriate snack to eat during the game to keep nourished. You can google ‘chess diet’ or something comparable to get some ideas or use the link chess.jliptrap.us/grandmasterdiet.htm (anything that makes noise to eat or shoots up your blood sugar is likely a no-no).
• *Tip Alert!* Go in with a positive attitude! If you think you are going to lose, it often becomes a self-fulfilling prophecy. That is another reason it often makes sense not to see an opponent’s rating until after a game.
Finally, do not forget to get lots of rest before and during the event!
Tip 31
If you get tired during a long game, see if you can take a mini-break to splash water on your face, get something to drink (caffeine not suggested!), etc. It is best to start a game fresh, if possible, but when you get tired, a mini-break might be a game-saving move!
When I review slow games with students, some of the common reasons I hear for mistakes later in the game are “I got tired”, “I lost focus” or “I could not concentrate”. We saw in Tip #30 that the best way to prepare for a game is to be fully rested, but either that is not always possible, or even a fully-rested player can run into extremely long games, or multiple tiring games in one day. Therefore, learning to deal with fatigue, hunger, or other bodily issues is part of becoming a good player.
If you have sufficient time on your clock, this gives you the opportunity you might need to re-set your brain. For example, after you make a move, especially one where your opponent has time and will need to take it, get up and do whatever is best to refresh: a restroom break, splash water on your face, get a drink or snack (best to avoid sugar or any ‘quick energy fix’), take a walk, stretch, or maybe even meditate for a few minutes.
Everyone is different and even the same person might require a different type of break depending upon the circumstances. One of my students, who is about the same age as I am, plays in team events on the internet. He has found that during most endgames, he needs a mini-break to splash cold water on his face and, I suppose, take a few deep breaths. When he doesn’t take that break, his play often deteriorates and he makes silly mistakes. When he does take a mini-break, he feels he has a fighting chance.
GM Soltis has written that whenever he goes into an endgame, he gets up and gets a glass of water. He says that is to remind him that endgame principles (and goals) are different than those in the earlier phases. But I would bet that this also serves as a mini-break where he can stretch his legs and clear his mind.
Sometimes when you are competing and your adrenaline is running you are not aware that you are tired or hungry. So be aware that adrenaline may ‘mask’ your real needs. Finally, don’t wait until your clock is so low that it is too late to refresh. Be proactive about these mini-breaks – better safe than sorry!
Tip 32
Know the Rules!
Whether you are playing online or, especially, over-the-board, it is very helpful, almost necessary, to understand some of the common rules. At our local club, despite us announcing some of the more common rules to know periodically, we have incidents many weeks where the players not knowing – or knowing, but not following – the rules cause problems.
Before getting into any details, there is one meta-rule to know which supersedes all the others:
If anything unusual or out-of-the-ordinary is happening in your game, don’t discuss it or argue with your opponent, and don’t try to resolve it by yourself. Stop the game and get the tournament director (arbiter), describe the situation, and let him/her resolve it.
I cannot tell you the number of times players, especially those who should know better, failed to get the tournament director, tried to resolve an issue with their opponent, and adversely affected the outcome of the game. It happens all the time, unfortunately.
The important point is that although the rule book is rather large, you don’t need to know most of it; this one rule about getting the tournament director removes the onus from the players and puts the knowledge of the rules where it belongs.
Having said that, here are some rules that are helpful to know. Many of these rules apply more to over-the-board play (and thus fall under the jurisdiction of FIDE or country rules, like the US Chess Federation, the USCF), while some may also apply to online play (where each server makes their own rules):
• Only the two players can be involved with the game. Spectators cannot interfere if they see an illegal move or other issues; spectators who notice a problem can alert the tournament director. In some cases, the tournament director can interfere without being called, but most of the time the tournament director only becomes involved when alerted by one of the players.
• The correct way to offer a draw over-the-board is to make a move, take your hand off the piece, offer the draw with your clock still running, and then press the clock to let your opponent think about it. The opponent then has three choices: verbally decline or accept the draw while thinking on their next move, or make a move, which de facto declines the draw. An illegal draw offer made by a player while they are thinking about their own move must stand after that player makes a move.
• You can’t ‘beg’ for a draw. The USCF rule is that once you have offered a draw and it is declined, you can’t offer another draw unless either the opponent has offered a draw in-between, or the position has changed substantially. Unfortunately, some online servers have no implementation of this rule, and players can hit the draw offer button repeatedly even on the same move. This is rude and annoying, to say the least; I once had someone hit the draw offer button dozens of times in an online speed game where I was winning and, when I refused to play him again, he brashly typed at me “What did I do?”.
• An illegal move is only established once the clock is pressed (before that it is just subject to the ‘touch move’ rule), and must be claimed to the tournament director by the opponent. A normal penalty in a classic time control would be to add two minutes to the opponent’s clock (USCF), but a recent FIDE rules change makes that only one minute in a rapid game.
• Touch move has three aspects:
i. If you purposely touch a piece, you must move it (but accidentally knocking over a piece is not subject to the touch move rule).
ii. If you purposely touch an opponent’s piece, you must capture it. If you both touch a piece and an opponent’s piece that can be captured, but the piece you touch cannot capture that piece, whichever one occurred first must be followed (e.g. if you touch the opponent’s piece first you must capture it with a piece that can capture it).
iii. When you let your hand off a piece (‘determining your move’) then you cannot move it anywhere else, but instead should press the clock (‘completing your move’).
• Players are not allowed to bother their opponents. But that is subjective, so if your opponent is doing something that bothers you, stop your clock and get the tournament director. If the tournament director deems your objection to be valid, he will ask your opponent to stop the annoying behavior, with a punishment that is appropriate to the intent and severity of your opponent’s actions, usually just starting with a warning.
• In over-the-board play, players must press the clock with the same hand they used to move the piece. Otherwise, they could press the clock before they determine their move. Similarly, you cannot make your move until the opponent has pressed the clock; this caused a major controversy in a time scramble during a US Women’s Championship several years ago.
• In over-the-board play, if you want to adjust a piece, you must do it on your own time and, before adjusting, you must make your intentions known by saying “I adjust” or “J’adoube”.
You can only stop the clock if it is your move and you have a legitimate claim/question for the tournament director or the game is over. Shaking hands has no legitimate meaning; one player might think it is a draw offer, while the other thinks it is a resignation. Therefore, the only proper way to resign is to say “I resign” or to purposely tip over your king.
Tip 33
In correspondence (‘daily’) games where databases, books, and moving the pieces is allowed, take full advantage of the rules, to enhance both learning and results.
Many of my students play what formerly was called correspondence chess, but now, through the internet, is commonly called ‘daily chess’, where one has between one to three days to make a move.
Each daily chess site has its own rules, but the most common are that any help except engines or other humans can be used during play. These rules would therefore allow players to use books, videos, databases, plus setting up a board and moving the pieces. These are all devices that are not allowed in serious, over-the-board play. Moreover, the current International Correspondence Chess Federation (iccf.org) rules now allow even engines to be consulted for the world championship (which my friend Jon Edwards won in 2022)! This surprised me; I suppose the reason is it simplifies trying to enforce rules and detect cheating, since once engines are allowed there basically cannot be cheating, except possibly collusion to throw games!
Of my students who play daily chess many play it using personal over-the-board rules; they did not move the pieces or consult books or databases. Some avoiding these aids because they were not aware that they were allowed; others thought they were legal, but personally felt it was unfair in some way; still others wanted to visualize moves like they would in an over-the-board contest.
I suggest my students make full use of any legal aid for the following reasons:
• To not do so would put you at a competitive disadvantage. If your opponent is using a database and you are not, the chances of obtaining a normal opening evaluation is greatly diminished.
• If you carefully study an opening database each move in the opening to choose which line you wish to play, you will likely learn considerably more about that opening than even if you properly looked it up after the game to see what you could have done better.
• Due to limits of the amateur’s visualization and other analysis skills, moving the pieces gives them the opportunity to analyze further and wider, take more time, and learn more about analysis and evaluation skills than they would normally get in a couple of minutes of visualization in an over-the-board game where they cannot move the pieces.
The detriment is that moving the pieces does not allow one to practice as much visualization, but I feel this one drawback is strongly outweighed by the benefits.
Tip 34
Intermediate time controls can hinder improvement.
With the advent of online play, faster time controls among both professionals and amateurs have dramatically increased. But a main reason professionals got so proficient is that in order to learn how to think and qualify for international titles, they had to play hundreds of over-the-board slow events.
*Tip Alert!*
If you want to become a really strong player, playing hundreds, if not thousands of long time control games
(30 minutes with a five-second increment/time delay or preferably slower)
is almost mandatory.
Once you master slow play, your board vision, visualization, and tactical vision increase greatly, which makes it easier to play faster time limits well. Moreover, if you have a good thought process in slow play and are given a faster time control, you know how to best and most efficiently adjust your thought process to do the best you can with less time to think.
But players (mostly online) who think a fast game is three minutes with no increment (3 0) and a slow game is fifteen minutes with a ten-second increment (15 10) have difficulty ever developing the type of proficiency that would lead them to over-the-board titles. Let’s do a little math to figure out the average time per move in a 15 10 game:
Assume 40 moves in a game. That is 15 x 60 seconds plus 40 moves x 10 second increment = 900+400 = 1300 seconds, a little over 30 seconds per move. But if there are three or four reasonable moves, to analyze well you not only have to figure out which ones are safe, you must compare them to see which might give you the best position. For most players, doing that reasonably well is impossible in 30 seconds. But that means some of the most important chess skills are being skimped not learned, or, worse, allowing the formation of bad habits. Those bad habits will be difficult to overcome once a player decides to play the type of serious chess that could result in national and international titles.
Another way of saying this is: If you have never played slow chess and don’t know how to analyze and evaluate carefully, it is very difficult to develop those skills when you are never given adequate time to think.
This is not just idle speculation on my part. Having taught over 1,000 players one to one privately over the past 30 years, and lately having many who play almost exclusively rapid chess online, I see these problems manifested in many of my students.
Very often, primarily online players will come to me for lessons and when I ask them their goals, a common one is “I want to be an expert or master.” That is a very good and noble goal, but, at the moment, titles are given out for over-the-board long time controls (30 0 or slower), and not for online rapid games.
My students do not see this as a problem, but it turns out to be, on a consistent basis. When these players attempt to play over-the-board, they are usually surprised that they don’t know how to analyze well, they are poor at visualization because in faster time controls they need not or cannot look very far, their time management is very poor, and the same skills that made them confident in faster time controls seem to somehow elude them when both sides are given more time to think.
Some can adjust quickly, but for most those ‘bad habits’ form a drawback that must be overcome. Superficial thinking, sloppy play, not checking if their moves are safe (hope chess), and hand-waving are common problems in this group.
That is why I write that for most aspiring-to-improve players, intermediate time controls can hinder improvement. Notice I carefully chose the word ‘hinder’. It is not that intermediate time controls necessarily make a player worse. Take any beginner and have him play 1,000 games at 15 10 and at the end he will definitely be much better than he was when he started. But he likely will have trouble switching to longer time controls and his chances of becoming a titled player will not have improved as much as if he had practiced a different playing regime.
What about time controls that are faster than ‘intermediate’? Ironically, those who play consistent slower games (30 5 or preferably much slower) and are proficient enough to enjoy fast chess can often benefit from many fast/blitz games, where they can practice openings, quick tactical sight, and time management. Why is this not quite as detrimental as intermediate time controls?
My theory is that when you play a game with fast enough time controls, it seems ‘less serious’ and doesn’t create the impression that the type of superficial thinking necessary in those games should carry over to the slow games. By separating the time limits between fast and very slow, it is easier for individuals to separate those types of thinking.
Is my theory true for everyone? No, it never is. But it seems to work well enough that I am often able to help those online ‘intermediate’ players get on to a more serious thinking track.
Tip 35
*Top-10 Tip*
Time Management is a major chess skill, so work to develop it like you would other chess skills.
When I first started playing tournament chess, I learned how a chess clock worked, but it wasn’t a major factor since I did not know what to think about and I played much too quickly. But by my fourth tournament I learned better what to think about (what today I call ‘avoiding Hope Chess’; Tip #65) and I began to use almost all my time in games. Most of our games were played at a time limit something like 48 moves in two hours.
I noticed some players played much too slowly and got into unnecessary time trouble, while others played much too quickly and made inferior moves while still retaining enormous time remaining at the end of the game. It was just common sense to me that these errors should not only be avoided, but that they could have an enormous effect on the outcome of the games. Today I call the handling of clock for the entire game ‘macro time management’.
For those at about age 12+ I like to compare macro time management in chess to how you should best use your time when taking an essay test at school or college. If a teacher gives you two hours for an essay test, what is the best way to take the essay? Write two sentences as fast as you can and turn in your essay after two minutes? Write nothing on your paper until there is one minute left and then try to scribble everything you have been thinking about for the first hour and 59 minutes in that remaining minute? Never look at the clock and have no idea how much time you have left? None of these are close to optimal; you should pace yourself to write the best essay (make the best chess moves) you can in the allotted time. Only this way can you try your best (Tip #2).
But there is a second type of time management. The one that says you should allocate different amounts of time to different types of moves in different types of positions. I call this ‘micro time management’ and the aspect of that which is derived from the position (as opposed to the clock situation, like the time control, time remaining, or move number) is called ‘criticality assessment’.
When I started as an instructor, I would tell students “When I was an improving player and I started to analyze well, but I still did not know my openings that well, I would just ‘hang in there’ until the position got complicated. Then I would take my time, analyze carefully, and hopefully outplay my opponent to win despite their greater knowledge.” Therefore, I was recognizing the importance of micro time management and criticality assessment without using those terms.
By 1970 I was an expert and I purchased the book
Tal-Botvinnik 1960
, which reviewed their world championship match of that year. Every move in the book was time-stamped, and I was amazed how much I learned just from seeing on which moves the players spent their time, and how much. I realized how important this must be, and so I incorporated writing down how much time I had remaining after each move (in minutes – the seconds were not that important when I had plenty of time), whenever I played games slow enough to record.
In the intervening years, I have written extensively on most aspects of time management. On my YouTube channel I have videos that help players who play too quickly slow down and those that play too slowly speed up. I tell those that play too slowly that if they play more quickly (normally!), mistakes will happen, but, to paraphrase Rowson in
The Seven Deadly Chess Sins
, to play too slowly to try to avoid these mistakes is an even bigger mistake! Another way of saying this is ‘Don’t beat yourself!’ It is one thing to be outplayed; quite another to play so slowly that you either lose the game on time or make ridiculous moves and lose because you did not have proper time to think, thus beating yourself.
I have also written articles and made videos about criticality assessment. Obviously one type of critical position occurs when the position is complicated. But there are other, non-complicated types of critical positions, such as ones where you decide which side to castle, whether to trade queens, or whether to trade into a king and pawn endgame. In the latter I usually advise “Don’t voluntarily go into a king and pawn endgame unless you are pretty sure you are going to get
the result you want – a draw if you are worse or a win if you are better” (Tip #157).
When you play a game, your resources are mostly your brain, the pieces, and the clock. Everyone understands how the first two are involved, but many underestimate the aspect of time management and its possibly great effect on the outcome of the game.
Tip 36
In most situations, what is important is not how your clock time stands in relation to your opponent’s time, but how your time stands in relation to how much time is reasonable.
When a child says “I want to do it because Johnny did it,” we sometimes hear parents reply: “If Johnny jumped off a cliff would you want to do it, too?”
This is a semi-serious analogy of what happens when you compare clock times with your opponent. Suppose you want to have as much time or more than your opponent in all circumstances, but you are paired with a maniac who plays all his moves immediately. Are you going to do it too so you do not fall behind on time?
I once had a club game with an opponent whom I did not know nor have any information on. The time limit was about 70 minutes with a five-second time delay. My opponent started blitzing off opening moves so I thought, ‘Either this guy knows a lot of book and he’s really strong, or he doesn’t know when to slow down.’ It soon occurred to me it was the latter, so while he continued to play all his moves quickly, I settled down and played at my normal speed.
At one point later in the game I had seven minutes left and my opponent had 67! That would have been cause for alarm if he could have played his moves much more accurately in the three minutes he took and had a decent position. Unfortunately for my opponent, by that point he was dead lost, so it was trivial for me to finish the game without trouble. My point is that I was able to take my time ‘normally’ even while my opponent was getting a large time advantage.
The goal should be obvious if you think about it: you want to use your clock wisely, no matter what your opponent is doing. It is not your clock versus his; it is your time to use the best you can. The ‘macro’ time management goal is to finish a ‘normal’ game using almost all your time without getting into unnecessary time pressure. If you do that, then you have done your best. Sure, if someone blunders on move 12 and resigns on move 14 you will have a ton of time left on your clock. However, like a marathon runner, you need to pace self so that you can try your best whenever a chess game goes the distance.
Does your opponent’s time ever come into consideration? Sure. As will be discussed in Tip #84, in extreme time situations, like mutual time trouble or when one side is desperately short on time and the other is not, there are sometimes adjustments that can be made to optimize your chances (but, as mentioned in #Tip 84, not all these options are the ones usually chosen).
Play your game wisely and let your opponent worry about his clock. Do not jump over the cliff trying to ‘out-do’ someone who is not using their time wisely.
Tip 37
Sometimes time trouble is unavoidable, but do not beat yourself by consistently getting into unnecessary time trouble.
One of the biggest, and most difficult to correct, problems that some students have is playing too slowly. The result is that they get into what I call unnecessary time trouble and often, if not usually, throw the game away.
Sure, there are some games where the situation gets tense, additional time is required for moves and then, instead of resolving into a situation where a player can play more normally and stay on a normal time track, the position remains tense an inordinate number of moves. In this case, it is quite understandable if one or both players run abnormally short on time. We could call this occasional situation ‘normal’ or ‘understandable’ time trouble.
But players who play too slowly get into time trouble regularly even if the position never gets that complicated. They dither and dather for three to five minutes on every opening move, wondering if Bf1-e2 is critically different than Bf1-d3 on the fifth move (it almost never is). Then, when they finally do get a critical position on move 26, instead of having 40 minutes to finish the game, they have four and they make silly mistakes playing quickly and their game crumbles.
In his book
The Seven Deadly Chess Sins
, GM Rowson lists 18 reasons why players might play too slowly, and attempts to debunk all 18 reasons.
When I discuss time management with my slow-playing students, they often protest that if they play more quickly (I call that more reasonably!), they will lose more games earlier because they are more likely to make mistakes. My reply is: “That is true, but for every game that you lose earlier, there will be many more games where you have a fighting chance later in the game and, instead of losing almost all those games because you don’t have time to make reasonable moves, you will draw and win your share of them.”
But even armed with that knowledge, it is still psychologically difficult for many ‘slow’ students to speed up. Fear is a strong driving force, and many of them fear making a mistake and losing so much that it causes a loss later in the game. Interestingly, many of my slow-playing students don’t mind losing games later due to time trouble, which they brush away as a good excuse! “I played him even for 32 moves before it all fell apart!” is a common refrain. I think psychologists would call their reaction a type of defensive mechanism, which causes them to accept those losses more easily, which of course only reinforces the problem.
There are several actions a slow playing student can take to deal with this, and they roughly mimic the Alcoholics Anonymous steps:
• Admit you have a problem with playing too slowly.
• Resolve to do something about your problem.
• Learn things you can do to alleviate your problem.
• Make a proactive conscious effort to apply the things you learned in Step 3.
• Review your progress and continue to take proactive action until the problem is gone.
Let us now list some of the things you can do in Step 3 to speed up your game toward a normal pace:
• When playing over-the-board, record how many minutes you have left after each move; when playing online, look at your clock after each move.
• Ask yourself ‘Am I playing too slow or too fast?’ and adjust immediately to asymptotically get back on track (you often cannot do it in a move or two).
• Learn basic tactics better, recognizing most common safety issues more quickly and accurately (Tip #91).
• Learn the ideas in your openings and look up your openings after each game (See Tip #3).
• Learn Botvinnik’s Rule (Tip #153) and try to follow it rigorously every time there is a normal (not crazy) opening.
• Always play with an increment or time delay to avoid games with race conditions at the end.
• Don’t dilly with indecision between non-critical moves. Ask yourself, “Is the choice I have between these candidates likely to decide the game?” If the answer is “No”, then choose a move and play it, flipping a coin if you must. The best time to figure out if one non-critical move is better than another is in the post-game analysis, not during the game!
• If necessary, consult a ‘sports psychologist’ to get additional help in overcoming psychological issues that prevent you from playing at a normal speed. I hope it doesn’t come to that, but I had to list it.
These actions are not a panacea for slow play, but they form the basis for a possible cure. The real key is having the slow player resolve to take consistent action; we coaches can take the horse to water, but we can’t make them drink.
Tip 38
Losing on time is much worse than making bad moves.
Several years ago I played a series of 20 0 (20 minutes per game with no increment) games against an expert-level opponent. Being the better player, I often found myself in superior positions as time ran lower. For the sake of argument, let’s say we often reached a point where my winning chances were 60-70% and we both had two minutes remaining. But then something odd often happened.
My opponent, contrary to what most experts would do, refused to speed up to try to avoid losing on time. I, on the other hand, did speed up. What inevitably happened was that we would make several moves and I would still have over a minute left when my opponent lost on time. Therefore, I would duly claim the win, but then my opponent would make the unusual request: “Would you like to finish the game?”
Unusual because the game was over! During the past few moves my position would often deteriorate slightly as I speed up to avoid flagging while my opponent continued to play slowly. Let us assume my ‘final’ position, if the game were not over, would now be completely even. However, if we look at the ‘overall’ evaluation (properly taking time into consideration), as my position slightly deteriorated my chances of winning (and your willingness to bet on me!) would soar from 60-70% to 100% once my opponent had so little time that he could not checkmate me before his flag fell. While my expert opponent’s attitude is extremely unusual for players rated 2000+, it is much more common among weaker players, especially those who grew up playing without a clock.
As a long-time professional chess instructor my job is to help my students get better results. Over the years, several of them admitted that they would rather let their flag fall, even in equal or superior(!) positions, than hastily make ‘bad’ moves. While this is, in a weird way, somewhat admirable, it is also quite averse to their improvement goals, and often leads to stunting their chess growth.
Let us make the point further by examining the rule book. Basically, there are three main ways to lose a game: get checkmated, resign, or lose on time (when the opponent has mating material). All are scored equally: 0. It does not matter if you get Fool’s mated in the opening or last 100 moves, the loss is scored and rated the same. Similarly, if you lose on time, it is scored the same whether you were winning or about to be checkmated.
But there is actually a big difference in how important it is not to lose on time when you are in a good position as opposed to when your position is resignable anyway.
My wife does not really know how to play chess, but she can beat anyone who loses on time against her. What I am trying to say is that giving away a game is a lot different than trying your best and then having your opponent beat you.
If you are playing a good player and have a resignable position and lose on time in a vain attempt to find a move that might keep you in the game, you are not losing very much. From an expected value standpoint, you are going from a position where your expected number of points is close to zero to a situation (lost on time) with exactly zero.
But if you don’t have a losing position, then you are losing much more. For example, if you have a king and nine queens and your opponent has a king and a pawn (necessary mating material,) and you zone out and flag, you are basically losing an entire point (should have scored 1; got 0 = losing one point more than expected). This is twice as bad as stalemating someone with a king and queen vs king (expected score 1; actual score ½ = losing half a point).
Some players who suffer from losing on time claim “But I feel better if I have a good position and lose on time; if I make bad moves and lose I feel worse!” OK, let’s assume that their perception does hold for them. However, they are suffering under a misconception that somehow it is ‘better’ to have that good position and throw it 100% away in an instant by simply letting their clock fall than it is to suffer bad moves and possibly survive.
They are basically making an excuse, one that won’t just jeopardize their position (as bad moves do), but destroy their score. And on top of that, they learn much less when they let their clock fall; there’s nothing to be learned there. However, there are a ton of things you can learn if you consistently try to win games from good positions or draw them from equal ones, even (or especially) when short of time. You won’t always win, but you will try, learn, have some fun, and score at least some points.
The bottom line is that when you make moves you are human and will make mistakes. So will your opponent. If you play fast enough to not lose on time, sometimes you are going to throw away the win and get only a draw, and you may even lose. But if you lose on time, you will lose every time that happens, and even my wife could beat you (your opponent no longer makes mistakes when your flag falls). The only way to maximize your score – and thus rating – would be to try your best and make sure you never lose on time in non-losing positions (this should be possible if you have a time delay or increment). Make your opponent beat you. Don’t beat yourself.
Tip 39
Do not be afraid of online cheaters.
I run into several new students who don’t want to play over-the-board because they are still afraid of COVID, evil opponents, that players in the clubs and tournaments are too good, or whatever. But I also run into the opposite case of players who only want to play over-the-board because they are afraid of online cheaters (those that illegally use engines to find moves). I don’t find either viewpoint very convincing, but let’s address the latter one in this tip.
There are only a few cases, so let us consider each one:
• Suppose an opponent is using an engine to cheat on every move or even just enough moves to win every game. Then their rating should be about 3500 (give or take some, depending on the server, since ratings are relative and not absolute), and you would see their rating before the game and know they are not a suitable opponent.
• Suppose they are only cheating enough to make the game competitive, that could be viewed as beneficial. Why? Suppose a player who would normally play at an 1100 level cheats enough to get a rating of 1700. Then if you are rated 1700 and they use an engine to play like a 1700, you will win half the games against them when, without cheating, you would beat them almost all the time. But you would seek them out as a comparably rated opponent to yourself, so that would not make much difference from playing a real 1700.
• Finally, suppose they only cheat when the game is important or only against you and not everyone else (Why? Your guess is as good as mine!). This is the worst case, but it assumes that your game is important enough that they would throw in a rare cheat just for those important games. Obviously, this would not happen every game they play or we are back to the first bullet. In this case they may temporarily get away with some cheating in the short run, but online cheating detection at the main servers has improved dramatically in the past decade or so and, possibly with the help of your notification to the server, they won’t get away with it for long.
I believe I read somewhere that IM John Watson had written comparable arguments to mine as to why the average player should not worry about online cheaters.
If all this doesn’t convince you that you should not worry about online cheating, then you are missing a wonderful opportunity to play opponents of your choosing 24/7, and in many fine online events.
Chapter Two
Thought Process
This chapter deals primarily with the process – more than content – of chess thinking. Process involves which steps are being taken, their order, and priority. Thought process tips deal with areas like analysis, evaluation, and the important but commonly overlooked topic of time management. It also includes tips involving questions to ask during the thought process.#
Tip 40
There is not one single correct thought process that would cover all types of positions...
...unless you want it to include a ‘meta-thought process’ decision of what type of thought process would apply to the current position!
In his interesting book
Move First, Think Later: Sense and Nonsense in Improving Your Chess
, Willy Hendriks suggests that teaching someone to think by giving them instructions like “Do A first then do B” is not the way good players think, and by telling someone that you are likely doing more harm than good.
Hendriks’s opinion is comparable to what I am suggesting with this tip. For example, you certainly do not approach highly analytical and completely non-analytical positions the same. A good example of a non-analytical position might occur for Black’s move if White opens the game 1.a4.
Black to play after 1.a4 – What is his thought process?
In this and similar non-analytical (‘judgmental’) positions Black should not necessarily be thinking analytical ideas like ‘If I make move x, what will White do to me on the next move and how will I be able to meet that?’ Instead, he should be thinking in general terms like ‘What openings would make sense reversed with a pawn on a4 since I am effectively playing White?’ or ‘White is leaving the first thrust in the center up to me; how can I best take advantage of that?’
This latter type of thought process is ‘hand-waving’ (moving on principles and general observation, but not via detailed analysis), but in a very acceptable way. It is quite different to an analytical situation, for example, a complex position with many checks, captures, and threats for both sides. Then hand-waving is a serious mistake; you must be more analytical.
I agree with Hendriks that no strong player has a strict process they use every move and telling a student that they should have one can be counterproductive. But I will make a general observation from my experience:
The more advanced a player is, the more he can use intuition, judgment, and jump all around searching for what he wants to play; all the ingredients are there. The more a player is towards beginner, the more he needs at least some ingredients that get him off step one and systematically help him find moves that are at least safe.
For example, no one would tell an advanced reader to sound out ‘t’, ‘h’ and ‘e’ when reading the word ‘the’, but it is almost impossible to teach someone how to read English without first helping them recognize letters, their sounds, and how to combine those sounds into words before they can read for meaning, as all advanced readers do.
So, while it is true that teaching inexperienced players rigorous, structured thought processes can not only be unhelpful, but even counter-productive, the opposite extreme of telling them to just ‘go with the flow’ isn’t going to provide any basis for going forward if they don’t have the board vision and tactical vision to spot meaningful patterns to use in the game.
Instead, a practical middle-ground is possible where you can teach someone many of the basic precepts and principles of what needs, as a minimum, to be done at some point in their thought process. For example, you can teach someone to ask first about their opponent’s move ‘What are
all
the important things that move does?’ and ‘Is that move safe?’ and to ask about each of their own candidate moves, ‘Is it safe?’
It is true that if you are required to think about the process of what you should be thinking that can detract from what your thinking should be. But the other extreme (which I don’t think Hendriks is fully espousing) of giving an inexperienced player no guidance for fear of stifling their creativity or causing them to think too mechanically is probably also not optimum. As usual, the best solution probably lies somewhere in the middle.
Tip 41
There’s not always a clearly best move or idea, and assuming there is (outside of puzzles, which state a clear goal) might be counterproductive.
In the previous tip I suggested that
there’s not always one single correct thought process that covers all situations (positions; time control issues), and I will also deal with circumstances when a player states “I reach positions where I don’t know what to do” (Tip #46).
