But there is a third key ‘don’t know’ issue: in many positions there is no single correct move or plan, so those looking for a clear best move won’t find one.
In his wide-ranging book
Chess for Zebras
, GM Rowson has a chapter called ‘Psycho-Logics’ and, within that chapter there is a section ‘The Importance of Not Having a Clue’ where he makes a statement in bold which I would like to repeat: “The stronger a player is, the more likely he is to begin by saying ‘I don’t know’ when you ask him what is happening in a position!”
There are several reasons for this, but one is that many positions contain several issues and factors (imbalances, to use the IM Silman term) that could be dealt with by a variety of logical/reasonable moves and ideas. Sometimes the best idea requires a specific move order or plan (most likely in a combination or in the endgame), but other times the position is much more fluid and a variety of moves and plans might all be reasonable. The key is that to assume
a priori
that there is always only one right move and plan is a falsehood that will make your thought process and analysis, not to mention your ability to finally decide on a move, much more difficult.
In the middlegame, if the position is objectively equal, there may be a variety of moves that will keep it about equal. In an endgame which is drawn, there may be multiple paths (with possibly more than one initial move) to achieving that draw. If you give a position to a computer engine, and ask it to display the top several moves and these moves end up separated by about a tenth of a pawn or less, it is likely that any of them are reasonable and, in most senses, equally good. So, in these situations, if you don’t know what to do and end up picking any of these moves, that’s quite OK, despite what doing lots of ‘Solitaire Chess’ (where you are often asked to find the ‘right’ moves and are penalized if you don’t) may lead you to believe.
Here is an example. On several moves in the opening (shown after Black’s 7th move) my student, rated 1206 and White, had trouble figuring out what to do against their 1339-rated opponent:
White to play
At the time this game was played I gave this position to an engine and let it show the top six moves. At 22 ply there was about a 0.15 pawn difference between the top move and the sixth move, and it stayed that way for most of the opening.
In all the lines presented by the engine, White eventually got his kingside pieces out and, despite Black’s menacing pawn storm, often castled that side. But that is not the point. The point was that on most moves White had a choice of several ideas and continuations that were reasonable and led to a slight advantage. In lines where Black played ...Bxc3+; bxc3 and gave up the bishop-pair, then White eventually playing c4xd5 to undouble the pawns and open the position for the bishops was usually best and logical, but even that was not absolutely necessary. This is not at all surprising for a ‘normal’ opening position where there are not many threats and several pieces left to activate.
In these types of ‘non-critical’ positions, for me to suggest to a student that he did not find my (or ‘the right’) idea would be to send the wrong message. This student wanted me to teach him how to find that right move in this opening, but I had to admit that if he just kept developing his pieces and did it reasonably without wasting time, there were many ways to skin this cat.
Puzzles often psychologically prepare us to find only those ‘best’ moves and sometimes in games your
criticality assessment
tells you that you must do exactly that. However, it is important to understand that sometimes looking for the exact right move (or plan) will not only waste time, but could be a futile effort since ‘any among equals’ in many cases might be a more reasonable goal.
Tip 42
More than anything, your ability to analyze and evaluate positions determines your playing strength. Therefore, strengthening those does more than anything else to help you improve.
In Tip #24, we did a thought experiment about swallowing a pill to help you learn openings. That idea applies to this tip as well, in spades.
Why is it that when one attends tournaments, it is likely you can see an 11-year-old rated 2000 beating 40-year-olds rated 1600 who have a lot more experience and, presumably a lot more chess knowledge?
The answer is easy; if chess were all about accumulating knowledge, then as you get older and read more books or watch more videos, you would just get better and better, and 11-year-olds would rarely beat experienced 40-year-olds. But chess is not mostly knowledge. It is mostly skills like analysis, evaluation, time management, stamina, perseverance, ability to learn from losses, ability to take instruction, open-mindedness, concentration, and a few others.
As a refresher, a quick definition of:
Analysis – the part of the thought process that attempts to find the candidate moves for both players, the famous ‘If I go there and he goes there, what would I do?’ type of thinking.
Evaluation – determining in a position who stands better, how much better, and why. Evaluation is usually done at the end of analysis when the position becomes quiescent (no more pertinent checks, captures, or threats which could likely change the evaluation of the quiescent position).
In most positions outside the opening and deep endgame, a player is not dealing solely with memory. It is true that knowledge of how to play similar positions plays a big part in analysis, but it is just a part. A player’s ability to analyze an unfamiliar position, to generate candidate moves for both sides, and then determine likely sequences and then evaluate them to choose the best move possible is the core of anyone’s playing strength.
Therefore, in order to truly improve, a player not only has to acquire more chess knowledge, but also work on improving those skills.
Tip 43
*Top-10 Tip*
There is more than one way to play better moves.
One of my YouTube videos won the award for ‘Best Instruction’ by the Chess Journalists of America when I addressed this obvious, but little-discussed fact. In order to become a better player, you must make better moves than you did before, but there is more than one way you can do this! The main ways are:
• Analyze more accurately (skill).
• Evaluate resulting positions better (skill).
• Memorize more opening and endgame patterns/sequences (knowledge).
• Learn more tactical patterns/sequences (knowledge).
• Learn more principles and how to apply them better (knowledge).
• Become more consistent in thought process (skill).
• Manage your time better (skill).
I would call the first two bullets above ‘finding’ better moves, as opposed to knowing them. When I discussed in the video that playing better moves often means you cannot just memorize more openings and endgames, but rather that you must be able to ‘find’ better moves, I was criticized by a viewer or two that I was just playing semantics. I respect everyone’s opinion, but I think they missed the point.
In Tip #14 I told the story of a player who had been improving steadily up to about 1500 just with the paradigm of accumulating more chess knowledge. But then he started to realize that he would begin to get diminishing returns on his chess work if he did not start learning how to analyze and evaluate positions better. I complimented him on his insight, but I fear that many others are stuck forever in the ‘I have to memorize more stuff to become a titled player’ mindset.
On the day I wrote this page, GM Noel Studer tweeted: “The most important skill in chess improvement: consistency. Be consistent in your training and you will improve. Be consistent during the game and you will pick up many points...” I replied:
“You are correct about the importance of consistency. If you play like a 1600 for 39 moves and then like a 700 for one, your playing strength is a lot lower than the average (mean) of the moves: ((1600x39)+(1x700))/40.”
Tip 44
One of the most common causes of thought process errors among amateurs rated under 1800 is quiescent errors.
My bet is that you can find White’s best move in this diagram:
White to play
What do you think is the average rating of players that might make a quiescent error and think ‘I can’t play 1.Qe8+ because I will lose my queen to 1...Rxe8’? Possibly about 700 or below? Almost no one with any experience would miss this either in a game, or especially in a puzzle if we change this to ‘White to play and win’ or even easier, ‘White to play and mate in two’.
So, what is a quiescence error?
Quiescence error: A common thought process error made (mostly by lower-rated players) where a player stops analyzing and evaluates a position as ‘quiet’ when there are still further forcing moves (Tips #63 and #93) which would likely (or will) change that evaluation.
From teaching over 1,000 players private one-to-one and discussing the causes of their mistakes and hearing them think out loud, I can say for certain that quiescence errors are not only one of the main causes of amateur errors, but they also answer their common question “Why is it that I do so well in puzzles, but I miss those same continuations in my games?”
When someone holds up a sign (puzzle requirement) “White to play and win” then players don’t stop looking until they find it. In games, lower rated players often stop as soon as they see possible material loss, superficially thinking a move is not safe.
Let’s consider a more typical example using a problem from John Bain’s
Chess Tactics for Students
:
White to play and win
Solution:
1.Rxe7!
and if
1...Kxe7
(1...c6!? 2.Rxb7! cxb5 3.Rb8+ Ke7 4.Bb4+ wins the rook)
2.Bb4+
not only skewers the black king and rook, but the black king has no squares to guard the rook, so it is also a type of removal of the guard.
This puzzle is also an excellent example of the type that players under 1800 FIDE often miss in games since 1.Rxe7 is not superficially safe (it loses the exchange – a rook for a bishop or knight) and they reject this candidate move, making a quiescence error.
Strong players rarely make basic quiescence errors because they don’t simply think “I can’t play move X because it isn’t safe”, but rather “If I play move x and give up material are there further moves (almost always forcing moves: checks, captures, or threats) which would indicate that further analysis is suggested which possibly could change my initial evaluation?
In this case 1.Rxe7 Kxe7 is not quiet. The check 2.Bb4+ initiates a skewer indicating that White may regain his material or even more, and thus White should not stop, but instead continue to analyze to get a more accurate evaluation of 1.Rxe7.
In puzzles, players rated 1000-1800 usually get these solutions correctly because of the sign next to the position that says “White to play and win”. That tells them that something is there and they should not stop looking until they find it. So when doing a puzzle like this, they are much more likely to think ‘If I play 1.Rxe7 and Black captures 1...Kxe7 and wins the exchange, is there something there that might actually be the solution to the puzzle even though 1.Rxe7 is not a safe move?’
That type of thought process will be adequate for puzzles with a defined requirement or goal, but in a game they are much more likely to superficially reject moves like 1.Rxe7 because they lose material, despite doing puzzles of analogous positions which indicate these types of moves often require further investigation.
I have given the following puzzle to hundreds of my students. I start with the question: “Black is threatening 1...Bxh4. What are all the moves that might not just lose the knight?” The main three answer are 1.Nf3, 1.Nf5, and 1.g5. Then I ask, “List these three moves in order of safety,” so I ask the reader to do that also:
List 1.
N
f3, 1.
N
f5 and 1.g5 in order of safety
By far the most common answer is 1.Nf3 is the safest, 1.g5 is second,
and 1.Nf5 is third because it clearly loses a pawn.
But then I tell students that 1.Nf3 is the least safe(!), and ask them: why? With that new information, it is now more like a puzzle because I have given them a defined goal, and many finally realize 1.Nf3? loses a piece to 1...Rxf3! and if 2.Kxf3? Ne5+ wins the queen! When I quiz them on why they thought 1.Nf3 was safe, about 70% admit they simply stopped after 1...Rxf3 2.Kxf3 as losing material, making a quiescence error. But if you want to be a good player, you need to be able to recognize that a move like 1.Nf3? is not safe.
Final note: When I first saw this position in a student game, I said immediately that 1.Nf3 was not safe! How? Because the only possible capture of the moving knight, 1...Rxf3, was the first candidate I considered (no quiescence error!), and then immediately I saw the fork (tactical vision!) if the only recapture, 2.Kxf3, was attempted.
Tip 45
You are not trying to prove how good your move is; you are trying to prove it is better than other moves.
What is your goal each move? Think about this question and answer before you read the next paragraph.
Your goal each move is to play the best move you can in a reasonable amount of time.
There are exceptions: if you are winning easily, your goal is to play a move that preserves the easy win, again in a reasonable amount of time. You don’t need the best move you can because if your move wins easily, you can’t do much better.
If you are losing badly, you want moves that might give your opponent a chance to go wrong and let you back in the game. Those might not theoretically be the engine’s ‘highest scoring’ moves, but from a practical sense, they are the moves that give you the best chance.
However, in most competitive positions, you want to play the best move you can. But determining “best” requires comparison – you can’t determine if something is best unless you know what set you are comparing, and then doing the comparisons.
In the seminal work by de Groot on how players think,
Thought and Choice in Chess
, world champion Dr. Max Euwe finishes his analysis of de Groot position ‘A’ this way:
“1.Bxd5 exd5 looks bad for Black. Probably some more accidents will happen. Much is still up in the air. One plays, for instance, 2.Qf3. Defending the knight on f6 is not so easy; 2...Kg7 looks very unpleasant...Yes, I play 1.Bxd5”.
There is a much uncertainly in that paragraph: “looks bad”, “Probably”, “up in the air”, “not so easy” and “looks unpleasant”! If you were able to go back in time to just after the exercise and asked Dr. Euwe if he thought White was winning, he would probably say something like “I don’t know; it looks pretty good.” But then if you pressed him by asking why he did not analyze further (he certainly could have continued to analyze more deeply), he might have said “My clock was running and Dr. de Groot asked me to think like I would in a game. I think I have determined that 1.Bxd5 is the best move, so I finished thinking and selected that move.”
In other words, once a player has proven that a move is better than all the alternatives, it rarely matters exactly how good it is! Trying to determine exactly how much better is often just a waste of time. When I was an improving player I once lost a game primarily because I spend so much time trying to figure out how good my best move was that I got confused and did not play it, and instead got mated! I resolved never to do that again; once I have shown what the best move is, my only other action would be to confirm that conclusion and then play that move. Spending time doing things like trying to figure out exactly how good the best move is in that case was even worse than just a waste of time.
De Groot called one of the final aspects of the thought process ‘Striving for Proof”. What is a good player trying to prove? He/she is trying to prove that the move he is about to play is at least as good, if not better, than the alternatives.
I also wrote a book about players doing de Groot exercises,
The Improving Chess Thinker
. I can state that one of the many differences between lower-rated players and higher-rated ones is that the lower-rated jump around until they find a move they like and then try to convince themselves it is as good as they think it is. They don’t do many comparisons of positions that occur after specific sequences between the top candidate moves. But stronger players analyze specific sequences they think may occur and then compare the evaluations of the resultant positions.
This is another way of saying strong players are trying to prove which move they are analyzing is the best move by making comparisons, while weaker players much more rarely analyze specific sequences and, even when they do, are much less likely to compare evaluations between likely sequences.
It is often irrelevant to determine exactly how much better the best candidate move is than the alternatives, especially before the endgame (where evaluations are often win/draw/lose). Once you know a move is at least as good as any other possible candidates, that’s almost always sufficient reason for taking some time to confirm your decision and then making the move.
Tip 46
Not knowing what to do in a position can be good! It is not knowing how to figure out what might be done which is usually bad.
Students often say to me, “I reached this position and I did not know what to do!” and they are almost always surprised when I reply “That can be good!” Why would I say that?
Let us consider this from the opposite view: suppose you were playing a game where you always knew what to do on every move/decision. We have such games, such as Tic-Tac-Toe. I can give you a short Tic-Tac-Toe lesson and you will always know what to do, and you will never lose. Is that fun? Is that challenging? How many professional Tic-Tac-Toe coaches or authors do you know? What makes the game fun is that you sometimes, if not often, do not know what to do, and have the challenge of trying to figure it out!
Now for the bad news: there is a big difference between not knowing what to do (acceptable), and not being able to figure out what might be done, and then differentiating between the alternatives (usually unacceptable).
Having stated that, there are some types of positions, often ones that are resignable or at least clearly lost, where there is nothing to be done. Looking for a saving move or idea in a position where your opponent dominates the entire board and has you in a bind may be a futile effort.
Let us assume that you are in a position where there is something to be done, but you do not know what it is. Chess authors write entire books about strategy and planning and finding combinations, so I will not be able to summarize them in a tip! But the more you know about these things, by reading many annotated game books, doing puzzles of all types, and reviewing games with strong players, the more likely you will have some idea what to do.
Sometimes it is as easy as systematically searching both sides forcing moves: checks, captures, and threats. If some of these click, it may be possible to initiate a tactic or, more likely, prevent one.
Another basic idea, popularized – but certainly not invented! – by IM Jeremy Silman in his book
How To Reassess Your Chess
(4th edition) is to examine the imbalances in the position. These are strengths and weaknesses of the position that one side has that the other does not, hence the imbalance. Learning how to ‘read’ imbalances and try to take advantage of them is another strong indicator of ‘what to do’. Finally, Silman says
for a plan to be strongly considered, it must be both feasible (doable) and effective
.
Tip 47
The language you use when you think can greatly affect your decisions.
When I listen to students think out loud, it strikes me that sometimes they either do not know the nomenclature, misuse it or, even worse, use it in such a way that it negatively affects their decisions. For example, if a player thinks trading queens is bad because he/she doesn’t want to “lose their queen” then they will rarely trade queens correctly because they gave it a negative connotation by using the word ‘lose’.
Players know that backward pawns are a detriment, but most of my lower-rated students have little idea what a backward pawn is, often thinking the ‘back’ pawn in a pawn chain is the definition. Therefore, they often evaluate the rear pawn in a pawn chain as weak even when it is a very strong pawn!
Even if players use a term correctly, the connotation of the term may strongly influence them. Most beginners learn early in their development that doubled pawns are bad, but they have no idea in which positions doubled pawns are a significant factor, so I often hear analysis something like “If I do x and he captures, when I recapture that gives me doubled pawns so I can’t do that...” There’s the problem: ‘they can’t do that’ no matter how good it is for them because it gives them – horror! –
doubled pawns!
True story: I once had a student who had to make a recapture. He had two possibilities: one lost a pawn and the other gave him an isolated pawn (another no-no!). When analyzing the recapture, I automatically assumed he made the move which did not lose a pawn, when he corrected me and told me he recaptured the other way. When I protested that his way of capturing lost a pawn, he replied “Yes, but the other way gets an isolated pawn and I did not want to do that, so I recaptured that way.” In other words, he valued isolating a pawn as worse than losing a pawn! This is not exactly a problem with nomenclature, but he attributed to the term ‘isolated pawn’ a very large negative implication.
A semantic problem comparable to the ‘back’ vs ‘backward; confusion would be my reaction to ‘Think defense first’ when a player is way ahead on material, which is often misinterpreted as ‘Play defensively’. That is incorrect for at least two reasons:
• ‘Think defense first’ is about ordering your thinking; ‘play defensively’ is about the style your moves should reflect.
• ‘Think defense first’ suggests first considering moves that stop something the opponent might do (e.g. a threat), and still possibly maintaining an aggressive/offensive posture, while ‘play defensively’ is often synonymous with ‘play passively’ which is not at all what I would suggest for someone who correctly thinks defense first.
A player may think the word ‘attack’ only means ‘attack the king’ when it could mean any kind of aggressive maneuver, anywhere on the board. Or a player might think an ‘outpost’ could be occupied by a pawn, or perhaps be on their side of the board.
Another common beginner misconception is that an open file cannot have anything on it, rather than just the absence of pawns. Or they might think a ‘bad bishop’ is one that is blocked by other mobile pieces, such as a white bishop on g2 being blocked by a white knight on f3.
These type of phrase misconceptions often lead to misguided analysis and evaluation. Players who go to chess clubs and talk with other players (Tip #81) are more likely to get over these misconceptions more quickly than those that only play online and rarely converse with other players.
Tip 48
When your opponent is thinking, you can do your time management, general strategy, and maybe take a brief walk to stretch. If you think you have a very good idea of what he/she might play, then start analyzing what you would do on the next move.
I often get asked, “What’s the best way to use your opponent’s thinking time?” Good question; after all, if both sides have good time management, about half your chess playing time will be when your opponent is thinking.
So, while the issue of time management is still in our thoughts, that is one of the first things you can do when your opponent is thinking: check your time management. When I play in over-the-board events and make a move, that consists of at least four automatic things:
• Move the piece.
• Press the clock.
• Record (not notate!) the move.
• Record how many minutes I have remaining.
It follows that my next step is to compare my time to the required time control information (e.g. if I must make a specific number of moves before another time control) and ask myself, “Am I playing too fast or too slow?” Once I can guesstimate that information, my next step would be to understand that if I am playing too fast or too slow, I need to adjust my time management asymptotically to get back on track.
I once heard the suggestion ‘On your time you do the specific analysis of your moves; on your opponent’s time you can do your general strategic planning.’ I think this is good advice, although it is a little over-simplified. For example, take the case where your opponent has a virtually forced move, but for whatever reason is taking time to make it. In that case, it makes sense to double check to make sure that move is really forced for him/her and, if so, then assume that move and start analyzing on their time exactly what you would do. But, unless you are in time trouble, when priorities change greatly, (*Tip Alert!*)
don’t just make your move quickly even if you had been thinking about that move on their time
– that’s a common mistake that often leads to hasty errors.
I also get asked if you should start calculating your move even if the opponent’s move is not forced. While this is not impossible, it is inefficient in the sense that you are just guessing what they are going to do. If you have absolutely nothing else constructive you can do at that moment – like even take a mental break! (Tip #31) – then sure, you can guess and calculate. I don’t often do this, but I don’t think it would be correct to indicate that I almost never do.
Besides time management, strategic planning, and analyzing potential replies, what else can be done on the opponent’s time? Obviously, any logistic concerns can be covered such as getting up and stretching, getting some water, having a quiet snack, a restroom break, or anything you would not want to do when your clock is running.
I am not like everyone else, but I often found it helpful to not only get up and stretch whenever my opponent had a long think, but to get my mind completely off the game and see how the games of some of my nearby friends and colleagues were doing. I found it relaxing to not only walk, but see what I might do on some of the other boards – like following the plot of several streaming television shows, except the intrigue was on other boards, not on a screen, and I was starring in one of the shows!
In this situation, when someone is walking around when their opponent is thinking, it is their responsibility to become aware when the opponent has moved and they should return to their board. One of the most common forms of ‘inadvertent’ cheating is to help someone by telling them “Your opponent just moved; your clock is running.” I see this occasionally and, as a former senior tournament director, I try to kindly advise the informer that they really should not be helping the wanderer this way – getting them back to their board two minutes sooner might have a large effect on the outcome of the game.
Obviously, all of the above is greatly affected by the time control and how much time your opponent is taking on a move. If the time control is fairly short then, by the time you check your time management, your opponent may already be moving. In these shorter time controls it is much more dangerous to wander around and look at other games like I did with very slow time controls. It would not be unreasonable to sit for the entire hour for a 30 5 game, while for most players, it would be almost impossible – and unhealthy – to sit the entire four hours in a time control like 40 moves in two hours.
In summary, analyzing the position as if it were your move during your opponent’s thinking time is sometimes beneficial, but often inefficient and gets diminishing returns. But if there is nothing else helpful to be done (like take a mental break!), then obviously it sometimes can be helpful. The shorter the time control, the more you are going to be analyzing during your opponent’s move, just out of momentum and necessity.
Tip 49
There are three ways to keep track of material during a series of exchanges (trades); use one method carefully and then, if necessary, verify with another method, to help ensure you get the accurate answer.
The three methods to keep track (‘count’) material during a sequence of exchanges are:
i. What’s left on the board at the end of exchanges compared to the start?
ii. A ‘running count’ (after each capture).
iii. How much material was captured by each player during the sequence of captures?
I was playing in my fourth tournament (my first with a rating!), and I had a series of captures on the f2-square. I counted and determined I would win the exchange (a rook for a bishop or knight), but when I actually played the moves on the board I lost two pieces for a rook! That cost me the game. I was so upset with myself that I vowed that would never happen again in a slow game where I had enough time to verify my count. It never has, partially thanks to this tip.
Let us try all three methods to see if 1) White wins material when capturing on f7 and 2) all methods give the same result (they should!):
White to play
White calculates
1.Nxf7 Rxf7
(if Black does not recapture then White is probably at least ahead a pawn, so the critical line is to assume what happens if Black does capture)
2.Bxf7+
(this is not forced, since the rook is pinned so White could try to apply more pressure but, assuming he captures right away, what is the consequence?)
2...Qxf7 3.Qxf7+
(again, White could stop and not capture, but the trade of queens should not change the bottom line)
3...Kxf7
.
Method 1: At the start the material was even. At the end Black has two extra pieces (and the advantage of the bishop-pair), and White has an extra rook and pawn. By the Reinfeld valuation system (1-3-3-5-9; Tip #98) the final position is even, but by more sophisticated systems these trades are a big advantage to Black.
Method 2: Running count: 1.Nxf7 (White is ahead a pawn) 1...Rxf7 (Black is ahead a knight for a pawn) 2.Bxf7+ (White is ahead a rook and pawn for a knight) 2...Qxf7 (Black has a knight and bishop for a rook and pawn) 3.Qxf7+ (White has a queen, rook, and pawn for a bishop and knight) 3...Kxf7 (Black has a knight and bishop for a rook and pawn, which is the same answer as method #1).
Method 3: During the trades, White captured a queen, and rook and a pawn. Black captured a queen, knight and bishop (notice how in call cases the queen trade cancels out, making the remaining calculation easier). So White gave up a knight and bishop and got a rook and pawn (the same answer as methods #1 and #2)
When you are playing and there is a difficult ‘counting’ problem with multiple exchanges, it makes sense to not only count it multiple times to make sure they agree – if they do not, more trials are required until they do – but also to use more than one method as an additional double-check on your answer. The entire game is often riding on making sure you count correctly!
Tip 50
You can’t play what you don’t see.
This is one of my favorite Danisms – idioms/principles I have made up to describe situations often met while instructing aspiring players. It has several reasonable interpretations:
• When visualizing sequences during analysis, if you don’t see a move is possible/legal, it won’t be a candidate move. Moreover, if you are analyzing deeply and can’t visualize well, you can’t analyze well.
• When developing candidate moves, if you don’t consider a move to be reasonable or don’t even consider it at all, it can’t become a candidate move.
• If a move isn’t seen as a candidate, you can’t analyze it, much less play it.
Quite often an engine (or a grandmaster) will play a move that I would not have identified as a candidate. In the following student game, what moves would you consider for White?
White to play
In this position, at 37(!) ply,
Stockfish
’s top candidate is 19.Ra1(!). I must admit, this move would not register with me. Yes, I need to develop the king’s rook, but to the a-file? For a move to be a one of my candidates, it needs to ‘do’ something that I can understand. But what does 19.Ra1 do? The file is closed and pushing the a-pawn might vaguely make sense once the b-pawn is guarded, but is that something that my eye was drawn toward? Not at all.
Occasionally when a student shows me a game in a lesson, they will play a move I don’t consider a candidate. I often reply with the bewildered “I don’t understand that move; what does it do?” Later in the same game as in the previous example, it was White’s move:
White to play
Here White played the surprising
26.Ke1?!
.
I could readily see 26.Ke2 as a candidate, but what does 26.Ke1 do that the more active 26.Ke2 doesn’t? There are no tactical dangers with the king on e2. So I would not have listed 26.Ke1 among my candidates since 26.Ke2 ‘dominates’ 26.Ke1. I would have trouble seeing 26.Ke1.
Former World Correspondence Champion and insightful author Cecil Purdy said,
“
Look wide before you look deep
.”
In other words, when playing long time control games, you can often start the search part of your thought process with the question: ‘What are all (wide!) the things both sides are trying to do?’ That should lead to the follow-up, ‘What are all (wide!) the reasonable moves I therefore might consider?’
That is a good way to augment seeing more possibilities early in the thought process!
Tip 51
Invisible Moves are those that are harder for (weaker) players to see, because they filter them out due to initial safety factors (quiescence errors) or they involve pieces moving in less conventional ways
.
Here are examples of the types of moves that are often overlooked or filtered out:
• Sneaky pins, which utilize squares that pinned pieces are not actually guarding (Tip #105).
• Castling in the endgame when it is assumed castling is no longer legal.
• Moving a piece to a square where a less valuable pieces could capture it.
• Moving a piece in a less common way, such as en passant, ‘long’ moves, attacking with the king early in the game, knight retreats, retracting the previous move, or moving the king away from the action in the endgame.
The following problem by Kubbel is a mate in one!
White to play and mate in one
The author uses several of the above-mentioned ideas to both create false leads, and also to make the correct solution more difficult to spot. The solution is: 1.Qa3#.
The following interesting position was given to me by a student, and I have used it for many de Groot ‘think out loud’ exercises. What would you play?
White to play
One ‘invisible’ candidate move for White is 1.Qf7. Most weaker players filter out this move due to the queen being placed
en prise and never consider it. A couple of intermediates spotted it and thought it had to be good, just because it is tricky (a very dangerous assumption!). But is it even good, much less best? I will leave that as a fun exercise for the reader. For a full analysis of this position, check out IM Greg Shahade’s instructive protocol at the end of Chapter 8 in my book
The Improving Chess Thinker
.
Several years ago, I wrote an original column about why players often miss moves like this, which I titled
Filter and Masks
. Later I made a similar YouTube video on this subject.
Filter and Masks
won a Chess Journalists of America
honorable
mention
award for Best Instruction. I think ‘filters’ help explain why amateur players make the common thought process error called quiescence errors, where they stop their analysis too soon and miss potentially important sequences that can dramatically change the evaluation (See Tip #44).
Tip 52
In highly analytical positions, analyze carefully. If you instead decide to move on general considerations or principles, I call that a ‘hand-waving’ error.
I have tested over a thousand students thinking ‘out loud’ and many, if not most, of lower-rated players are guilty of ‘hand-waving’ in some analytical positions, making their decisions based on generalities and principles, not specific analysis. There are a variety of reasons for this problem. They either:
• Play chess mostly by pattern recognition and never learned how to analyze.
• Don’t visualize very well, so they avoid serious analysis where visualization is required.
• Get so infatuated by an idea that they move solely on that idea, without regard to the analysis of whether that idea actually works or not.
There might be a position where I am thinking specific lines like ‘If I go 1.Bg5 and he responds with 1...Rfd8 and I then try 2.Rd3, I am not sure how I can best meet the pawn-break ...c5. So possibly I want to control the c-file first with 1.Rac1 and only then try 2.Bg5. If he responds with 1...b6 then something like 2.Ba4 could cause him problems.’ In such positions, lower-rated students instead think ‘more general’ things such as:
‘I want to attack kingside so maybe I should get a rook over there. But I am worried he might bring his queen out, so maybe I should bring my queen out first. My knight would like to get to the outpost on d5, but then he can attack kingside. The f-file is open so I can’t put my king there. A move like Qf2 looks interesting, or maybe my queen should go to g3. Yes, I like Qg3.’
The above was a generic example, but the following position, from a student game, is an excellent example of hand-waving from actual play:
White to play
White only has two legal moves, so that reduces the potential amount of analysis needed. However, my student, as he explained to me after the game, didn’t analyze much at all. Instead he moved on the following reasoning (I paraphrase):
“I am already ahead a bishop and two pawns, and I am threatening his queen twice. If I capture his rook (1.Kxh2) I am exposing my king to a bunch of checks like 1...Rh8+ and 1...Qh4+. But if I just tuck my king into g1 (1.Kg1), then my king is not nearly as exposed and I am far enough ahead on material to win. Moreover, I only have 17 minutes left on my clock (with a 45-second increment) so I don’t want to spend a lot of time analyzing because I am not very good in time pressure.”
An excellent hand-wave example (note: the well-known master ‘Coach Andras’, Andras Toth, calls this “story-telling” – there is no commonly accepted term yet in the chess world for this type of thought process error), but unfortunately, a little concrete analysis does not come close to backing up the student’s conclusion. In the game after
1.Kg1?? Qh4
threatening 2...Rh1#, White realized 2.Qxf7+ Kh6 did not help and played
2.Qh3
, but after
2...Rxh3 3.gxh3 Qg3+ 4.Kh1 Rh8
he had to resign.
Instead, 1.Kxh2 wins as after 1...Rh8+ 2.Kg1 Qh4 (or 1...Qh4+ 2.Kg1 Rh8, transposing) Black is not even threatening very much. White can play 3.Qxf7+ Kh6 with no problems for White. If 1...Qxd6+ 2.Kg1 Qd4+ 3.Rf2 also poses no problems.
When I asked my student if he thought he could calculate most of this in a reasonable amount of time, he thought he could not; he thought it might take him 10 minutes. When I asked him if he could beat a comparable opponent ahead a rook and a bishop with, say, seven minutes left and a 45-second increment on each move, he still expressed his doubts! That’s unfortunate, but hopefully most readers can complete a win when ahead large amounts of material with the aid of a big increment to ensure adequate time to make safe moves.
In
The Improving Chess Thinker
I showed several examples of players’ thought processes at all classes from below 1000 to International Master. One conclusion was that in analytical positions (complications and/or forcing moves possible)
higher-rated players think in very specific move sequences while lower-rated players often ‘hand-wave’ much more general analysis
.
Part, but not all, of the reason for this hand-waving is that lower-rated players have rarely practiced extensive visualization, so this basic analytical skill is under-developed. It only makes sense that if you can’t easily visualize four ply (two moves) ahead, you would tend to shy away from trying to analyze two moves ahead. When I ask students to describe where all the pieces are two moves ahead, even though only a maximum of four pieces have moved (excluding captures and castling), lower-rated students often describe the experience as being “fuzzy” – they lose track of which pieces have moved, which have not, and where the moved pieces have arrived (Tip #12).
There is no great fast fix for lack of visualization. Many just give up and say they lack the skill to visualize better, but it is not a ‘can do perfectly’ versus ‘can’t do at all’ issue. Although with visualization practice may not make perfect, practice does make one better. But to practice deeper visualization in a game requires two things: first, a slow enough time limit that on each move a player has time to look at various alternative candidate moves and likely sequences, and secondly a willingness of the player to practice a skill that initially they are not very proficient, and may thus be a source of frustration.
It is not a coincidence that my students who started playing chess online during the pandemic, maybe spurred by the Netflix show
The Queen’s Gambit
, think that 10 or 15 minute games are ‘slow’ games and thus never have a good opportunity to practice visualization. This leads them to get into bad thought process habits, much less fewer opportunities to practice
When you see a good move, don’t play it; look for a better one.
For this reason I have written a column and made a YouTube video, each with the title
Intermediate Time Controls Can Hinder Improvement
(Tip #34).
During lessons I try to encourage my lower-rated students to think in specific move sequences whenever possible and necessary. It is quite acceptable to ‘hand-wave’ your thinking on move 1 if you are Black, your opponent has played 1.a4, and you have no ‘book’ reply to that rare move. In that case there are no dangerous forcing moves (checks, captures or threats) that your opponent could reply on move 2, so using primarily generalities and principles will not get you into trouble (Tip #40).
But if there are possible dangerous forcing moves in reply to your candidate moves, there is little substitute for ‘doing the work’ and asking if a specific sequence is safe. For example, in some analytical position if you have a candidate move 1.Bg5, you have to take time and ask if you should play that, how would you meet replies like 1...Qa5+ or 1...h6, hitting the bishop, or 1...d4, attempting to fork your knight and rook. If you don’t attempt to find these specific dangers and assume that after each one of those you have at least a safe reply on your next move, I call that ‘Hope Chess’ because if you make the move without checking for danger and then the opponent does make a forcing move you did not anticipate, you think ‘Uh-oh’ and hope you have a safe reply.
Doing that specific analysis and not hand-waving (and thus just waiting to see what will happen, even if it is an unstoppable threat) requires at least some visualization, some available clock time, and the willingness and ability to find both potentially dangerous replies for your opponent and a way to safely meet each reply next move. At the very least, this specific ‘checking to see if each candidate move is safe’ will put you on the road to better visualization, less hand-waving, and finding safe and superior moves.
Tip 53
*Top-10 Tip*
In a slow game, when you reach a branch in analysis with multiple reasonable moves, first list all the reasonable moves, then prioritize how you want to analyze the list, then finally start analyzing the first prioritized move.
One mistake I see all the time when I ask my lower-rated students to analyze ‘out loud’ is that they come to a position (with either player to move) and they don’t consider what all the possible candidate moves are; they just ‘assume’ one and start analyzing it. Not considering all the reasonable alternatives is in direct conflict with Cecil Purdy’s famous advice “Look wide before you look deep.”
Sometimes the thought process error of just assuming one move can result in an adequate assessment, but much more often it can lead to big mistakes and wrong evaluations. The evaluation ends up wrong because the player has assumed a move that turns out to be clearly not best, which lowers the evaluation for the side making the assumed non-optimal move.
Here is an example I have given hundreds of students, with the goal of helping them learn more efficient analysis skills:
White to move
The question I ask students to consider is “Is 1.Nxe5 safe?”
Because of the obvious possible pin on the e-file, most students properly first notice that in deciding whether 1.Nxe5 is safe, they have to consider the reply 1...Qe7. But it is at this point where they go wrong. Instead of asking ‘If I play 1.Nxe5, what are all the moves (possibly in addition to the already noticed 1...Qe7) that Black might play that could possibly make 1.Nxe5 unsafe?’, most lower-rated players (roughly below 1500 FIDE) just assume that 1...Qe7 is the only move to investigate (Tip #54), and they start analyzing 1...Qe7 for a period of time. Then if they find that 1...Qe7 can be safely met by White with a move like 2.Qe2 or 2.f4 or sometimes they notice 2.0-0 (but 2.Bf4 runs into 2...g5!), they conclude that 1.Nxe5 must be safe.
Imagine their surprise when I ask them to play it, and I throw in 1...Qa5+, winning the knight!
This error sparks a discussion on how to deal with nodes of analysis where branches occur (such as the options White needs to identify Black having on his first move to try to refute 1.Nxe5).
The first step should be to make a list of dangerous moves (almost always checks, captures, or threats) that might make 1.Nxe5 unsafe. If you want to start the list with the noticed 1...Qe7, great. But instead of analyzing it, first complete the list – which obviously should also include 1...Qa5+.
Secondly, prioritize the list to see which move you want to analyze first. The first candidate to analyze should be the one most likely to refute 1.Nxe5 since, if the first one works, you already know 1.Nxe5 is not safe and you don’t have to analyze the rest of the list. In this case, the check 1...Qa5+ is more forcing, so it probably should be analyzed before 1...Qe7.
The third step is to start analyzing the list, in order. In this example, because the first move 1...Qa5+ wins the knight, it saves you (White) all the time analyzing 1...Qe7 since you already knows that 1.Nxe5 is not safe. You are no longer interested in whether 1...Qe7 also wins the knight; once you find one move that eliminates 1.Nxe5, it should be of no interest (during a game) how many other moves might also refute that candidate.
By prioritizing the list and analyzing the most likely move to refute 1.Nxe5 first, you are optimizing your thought process to be as efficient as possible and thus allowing yourself to determine as quickly and accurately as possible whether 1.Nxe5 should remain a candidate.
Tip 54
Do not just ‘assume’ moves without realizing alternatives.
This is the companion tip to the previous one, about first listing alternatives at analytical nodes. In
The Improving Chess Thinker
I used my over 50 years (I hate writing that) of experience giving de Groot ‘think out loud’ exercises and showed example protocols of players of all levels. The extensive supporting material in the book was primarily about aspects of a good thought process and typical mistakes in a poor one.
One of the common errors I find in an amateur’s thought process is getting to a node (a move in a sequence) in the move tree where there are multiple possible reasonable candidates – especially those deeper than the first ply candidates – and they just ‘assume’ a move instead of listing the possible replies, prioritizing them, and then start analyzing the one with the highest priority (Tip #53). Instead, just ‘assuming’ a move often leads to possible further problems.
The moves that are skipped may never be considered, even if the assumed move does not achieve a desired purpose for the player whose move that would be, and/or the evaluation of the assumed move might erroneously be taken to be the ‘bottom line’ evaluation of the variation, when the untried moves might greatly change that evaluation. For an example, let’s take position ‘A’ from de Groot’s famous work
Thought and Choice in Chess:
White to play
Often students will think out loud during a de Groot exercise and say something like “If 1.Nxd5 Bxd5 2.Bxd5 Nxd5 and I win a piece with 3.Bxe7.”
Aside from the fact that they are making a retained image error by failing to see that Black’s capturing knight on d5 defends the bishop on e7, they also often assume such a line makes 1.Nxd5 a good move, when all it does is assume, and examine, one of many possible capturing sequences. I would prefer they start out instead: “If I play 1.Nxd5, Black has three possible recaptures: 1...Bxd5, 1...exd5, and 1...Nxd5. Let’s examine 1...Bxd5 first. Then my bishop on a2 hangs, so I probably have to play 2.Bxd5 or maybe 2.Bxf6. If 2.Bxd5...” This thought process identifies what the branches of analysis are and the order they will be examined.
In the next example from an amateur game, Black’s previous move had captured on f1 and, of course, White should just have recaptured. But instead, White felt he could be clever and threw in
12.
Q
a4+
, assuming Black would safely block with 12...c6, 12...Nd7 or 12...Qd7.
Black to play after 12.
Q
a4+
But if White had listed all of Black’s legal responses to 12.Qa4+, one of the safe ones is the AWL
12...b5!
, which was played in the game. By hitting the queen with the pawn, Black does not give White time to capture the bishop on f1, which is ironically guarding b5. After
13.Qb4 a5
(not necessary, but sufficient)
14.Qb2 Bc4
Black was ahead a solid piece and went on to win easily.
This example not only deals with a poor assumption, but also an unnecessarily tricky move. It is certainly understandable that looking and finding tricky moves is fun and occasionally fruitful, but often it is better not to try to show people how ‘clever’ you are, but just to play the simple, good move.
Tip 55
When you see a good move, do not just look for a better one; also make sure the move you think is good is as good as you thought before you make it.
The famous ‘Lasker principle’ is “When you see a good move, don’t play it, look for a better one” to which I often add: “You are trying to play the best move you can within a reasonable amount of time.” Without that additional caveat, you would lose as you keep searching for an ever better move until your clock falls!
But when I review games with amateurs and ask why they made a certain mistake, one common cause is they got excited when they saw what they thought was a good move and played it without further thought. Unfortunately, that ‘good’ move turned out to be disastrous as they missed something critical – which often was not that difficult to see if they had given the move any further thought.
This type of error led me to develop this tip. I ‘twisted’ the famous proverb to add the additional concern that for many players what initially looks like a good move is not really one at all. Making them aware of this danger is the first step toward getting them to be a little more careful in the future.
Taking the ‘see a good move’ idea to the extreme, suppose you see a move that you think wins the game. Should you get excited and play it right away to show everyone how smart you are? No, it is in fact unwise and illogical to do so. Why? Consider the two possibilities:
• The move is truly as good as you think it is, and the game is virtually over – you have a big enough advantage to beat anyone easily.
• The move is not nearly as good as you thought it was, which often means the move is not even that good, nor best, and should not be played.
In both cases finding out is critical. In the first case, if the game is going to be virtually over, your opponent might resign or the winning position might become much easier to play. In those cases, having spent more time on the move to make sure it was really winning was not very costly.
In the second case, where further investigation reveals the ‘winning’ move to in reality not only be not winning, but possibly a mistake, the extra time to figure that out was crucial and possibly saved the game.
Therefore, in any case, when you see a move that seems to win or even just a move you think is good, it could be critical or not nearly as good as you think, so do not play it right away. Quadruple check to make sure it is really as good as you think!
Tip 56
Don’t just ask ‘Why did he make that move?’; instead determine ‘What are
all
the (important) things that move does?’
I have written and made videos about this common error on several occasions. When a student makes a mistake in a game, I don’t just say “That’s a mistake; don’t do that!”. That would not be very constructive. Instead, I try to figure out why the student made that mistake and what steps they can take to lessen the chance of a similar mistake in the future.
One of the common thought process errors occurs when a student’s opponent makes a move and the student asks him or herself ‘Why did they make that move?’ or ‘What does that move do?’ Once they find a reason, they are satisfied and abandon that line of thinking, assuming the move doesn’t do anything else. Of course, when it does, the part that they missed can be deadly.
Instead, the two questions to ask first when an opponent makes a move are “Is that move safe?” and “What are
all
the (important) things that move does?” If you miss one, that can be decisive.
Here are a couple of examples where just asking why the opponent made the move caused errors. In the following position from a student game, White’s previous move attacked the f4-knight with
20.
Q
e3
and Black responded
20...
Q
f6
, obviously guarding:
White to play after 20...
Q
f6
White assumed he knew the (only) reason for
20...
Q
f6
and decided to put a rook on the f-file to set up a future pin. Luckily for White, he played
21.Rf2?
and only lost the exchange after the unexpected (to him)
21...Qa1+ 22.Kd2 Qxh1 23.Qxf4
.
If White had instead set up the pin with the inactive rook on h1, it would have led to 21.Rf1?? Qa1#. If White had instead asked ‘What are
all
the things 20...Qf6 does?’, he might have also seen the 21...Qa1# threat.
This error is especially common among lower-rated players when the opponent’s move that has multiple purposes was forced by a player’s previous move. I made up the following easy example where White has just played
1.d5
.
Black to play after 1.d5
Black responds
1...Nf4
and White thinks ‘I know why he moved the knight – I was attacking it with a pawn; if he didn’t move it, I would have won the knight’ stops thinking, and misses the threat of 2...Qxg2#.
Similar mistakes when amateurs just think ‘Why did he make that move?’ happen all the time...
Tip 57
When asking ‘What are
all
the important things that move does?’ do not concentrate too much on just the piece that made the move.
This is a follow-up to the previous tip about finding all the things a move does. Show an absolute beginner the position after
1.Nf3 b5 2.e4
...
Black to play: Though the e-pawn moved, the discovered threat is 3.
B
xb5
...and ask him/her “What does the move 2.e4 do?” Likely he/she will miss that this creates a discovered attack by the bishop on f1 upon the black pawn on b5. That is primarily because beginners have enough difficulty concentrating upon the piece that moved (e2-e4), much less its ‘side effects’ on other pieces.
However, in teaching about 1,000 students, I have noticed that intermediate players often overlook one or more of the ways a move affects the board. So, just for the fun of it, I thought I would try to make a list of
all the ways a move can affect (or, in some cases, fail to affect) the new position it creates on the board, along with some simple examples. When a piece moves, it affects:
• The new squares the piece that moved is now attacking (or guarding). For example, after 1.e4 the e-pawn now attacks both d5 and f5. After 1.e4 e5 2.Nc3 now guards e4 (among others).
• The squares the moved piece is no longer attacking (or guarding). For example, moving the pawn with e2-e4 no longer attacks or guards d3 and f3.
• If the moved piece is a pawn, then moving it affects the squares it can no longer guard. For example, after 1.e4 not only does the pawn no longer guard d3 and f3 (as in #2), but it no longer has the option to guard d4 or f4 either (this is how weak squares are eventually created; a weak square is a square that can no longer be guarded by a pawn, e.g. 1.e3 and 2.g3 weaken both f3 and h3).
• The effect on the square where the moved piece is now residing. If this is a capture, that is a major effect. But even if it is not a capture, the piece that moved to that square may or may not be safe, e.g. 1.e4 f5 and the pawn that moved to f5 is not safe on that square,
• Discovered attacks (or moves) for your pieces (you could count this as two different affects if you prefer). The discovered attack is the example we used at the start of the tip: 1.Nf3 b5 2.e4 discovers and attack on an opponent’s piece, the white bishop on f1 on the black pawn on b5. A simple example of a discovered move is that 1.e4 discovers lines for the white queen, king, and bishop, not that the king is likely to play Ke2 soon thereafter/
• Discovered attacks (or moves) for the opponent’s pieces. For example, Black to play 1...b5.
Black to move before 1...b5
1...b5
discovers an attack for the opponent’s rook upon his own bishop.
• Blocking an attack or guard (or just the moves) of your own pieces, e.g. after our earlier example of 1.Nf3 b5 2.e4 if Black ignores the attack on b5 with 2...e6, then if White plays 3.d3 that blocks (interferes with) his own bishop’s attack on b5.
An example of just blocking moves would be that 1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 at least temporarily blocks the white queen’s possibilities to go to f3, g4 and h5.
• Blocking an attack (or moves or guard) of an opponent’s piece. This is the common ‘interposition’. So in the King’s Gambit if 1.e4 e5 2.f4 Qh4+, then White can simply block with 3.g3. This not only stops the check on e1, but prevents the black queen’s ability to go to f2 as well.
• A move with the king or rook might affect the right to castle. For example after 1.Nf3 d5 2.Rg1 White can no longer castle kingside later in the game, even if he returns the rook to h1.
• Finally, there is the ‘effect’ of not addressing (and thus allowing) other issues on the board. For example:
White to play
Suppose Black answers the attack
1.f4
with
1...b5
. This affects the board in a strong way by
not
saving the queen. The decision to consciously (or not intentionally) not save the queen has a major effect.
As a further example of the final bulleted item, a move can leave other issue(s) unaddressed. For example, after 1.Nf3 b5 2.e4 e6 White can capture with 3.Bxb5, but suppose he instead plays 3.d4. Then Black can leave the issue of the hanging b5-pawn unaddressed with a move like 3...Be7, and White can further do the same with a move like 4.Be2, when either or both players can, purposely or not, leave unresolved issues like the safety of b5.
In most games between strong players, if one player misses an issue, the other is likely to resolve it in his favor immediately. However, watch any two beginners play and important safety issues often stay on the board move after move. Moreover, with absolute beginners, when they play over-the-board (where illegal moves are possible; online the server does not allow them), you might see a position where both kings are in check for several moves!
I may have omitted a few esoteric ways a move can affect the board, but hopefully you get the idea: when you contemplate your opponent’s move and each of your own candidate moves and ask the most important questions ‘What are
all
the important things that move does?’ and ‘Is that move safe?’, you can’t only concentrate on the piece that moved and the squares it attacks. It only takes missing one consequence of a move to allow the opponent an opportunity. And since it may only require one bad move to lose a game, missing that one key point of one move can be enough to tip the balance.
If you are not an experienced player, hopefully this tip will help you assess the effects of both sides’ moves more carefully. And even if you are an experienced player and all of this was already known to you, it does not hurt to have a fresh reminder.
Tip 58
The best (only?) way to prove a move is safe is to assume it is not safe and try to show why. Then, if all attempts to show it is not safe (via the opponent’s checks, captures, threats) fail, it must be safe.
It is sometimes easier to prove a positive (‘safe’) by assuming a negative and then, if the negative is not true, the positive is correct. In chess, that means when attempting to prove if a move is safe – yours or your opponent’s – you could start by assuming it is not safe, try to find a sequence/combination that wins material or mate and, if you can prove that the attempt fails, therefore assume the move is safe.
In the following position, Black to play, suppose our candidate move is 1...Qc7 to get out of the pin, but we want to prove it is safe. Then we can visualize 1...Qc7 and try to find a combination for White that would win material or mate.
Black to play
The moves we are most worried about are White’s checks, captures, and threats. After
1...Qc7
White could try:
• 2.N4xe5. Then 2...Qxe5 allows at least 3.Rxd7, so 2...Nxe5 and the further 3.Nxe5? Bxe2 4.Rd7 Qxe5 is safe.
• 2.Bxd7 Nxd7 (2...Bxf3 is also possible) is safe.
• 2.N3xe5?! Bxe2! (simply 2...Nxe5 guarding g4 should already be safe, but not as good) 3.Rxd7 R(either)d8! (3...Rad8! 3...Rfd8!), pinning the rook to the back-rank mate and saving the queen (but 3...Qb8 was sufficient).
None of these candidates win material for White so we can conclude 1...Qc7 is safe. If you don’t figure this out before you move, that’s Hope Chess and you are just lucky if it turns out White hasn’t a move that wins material or mates.
In our second example my first candidate move is 1.Nc3. But just because a move is put first on my list of candidates does not make it safe or even a good move. Is 1.Nc3 safe?
White to play
After
1.Nc3
we again have to consider Black’s possible dangerous checks, captures, and threats:
• All of Black’s checks are easily met.
• The capture 1...Nxc3 2.bxc3 doubles White’s c-pawns, but guards the discovered attack on d4 with the pawn capturing on c3.
• The capture 1...Rxh3 2.Kxh3 Rh8+ leads nowhere for Black.
• Black can make other discovered attacks on the d-pawn by moving the d5-knight other places than c3. Are any dangerous? Most can be met by 2.Be3 or 2.Ne2, but the double threat 1...Nb4 is the one that’s concerning. Then both c2 and d4 are threatened and there’s no way to stop 2...Nxc2 winning a pawn.
Therefore, 1.Nc3 must be rejected because of the reply 1...Nb4, winning a pawn. 1.Nc3 is not safe. I’ve given this position to hundreds of students and the break-even point is about 1600 USCF/FIDE; half the students rated at that level get the problem correct. But they are alerted because this is a lesson problem; if this position occurred in a game I am assuming even players around 1700 might easily consider 1.Nc3 safe.
Tip 59
In chess, all flashing lights are red! Whether you see danger that will take time to best meet or you see a move that seems to win, either is critical, so in both cases take your time.
We covered the ‘move that seems to win’ case in Tip #55. To build on the ‘flashing red’ idea, I like to tell the following story to students because it gives them some insight into time management and criticality assessment.
After he became world champion, Vladimir Kramnik played a match in 2002 against
Deep Fritz,
with a prize of about $1,000,000. (Note: This was during the relatively brief period when the best PC-based engines and the top grandmasters were relatively on the same level, so such a contest was interesting. Today it would be like a human trying to race against a car.)
Before Kramnik’s final move, the engine had played a move that allowed Kramnik to trade rooks into an apparently easily winning king and pawn endgame. If I remember correctly, when the engine made this move, Kramnik had 17 minutes on his clock. I was watching on the Internet Chess Club (ICC), which at the time was likely the most active chess server. There were probably a thousand or more also watching and commenting online on the relayed ICC game board.
When the engine made the move, everyone starting typing “Rd5+ wins!” and even in those days many had engines running to confirm that the trade was indeed winning. But when Kramnik did not move right away, the enthusiasm soon turned to scorn. At first they typed comments like “Why is he not moving?” or “What is wrong with Kramnik?”. But later the scorn turned to outright criticism with remarks like: “Kramnik can’t be that good – how could he not have played Rd5+ already?” Or “I must be better than Kramnik – I would have played that move a long time ago!”
I watched in amazement as many of the spectators continued to criticize Kramnik. Finally, after a 10-minute think and with seven minutes left on his clock, Kramnik made the move which traded rooks.
I thought the computer resigned a couple moves later, but the ChessGames website shows the engine resigned on that move. In any case, after the game, Kramnik went to the press briefing and one of the first questions was: “Why did you take so long to play 57.Rd5+?”
Kramnik looked a little startled as if it was a strange question, but immediately settled in to explain (I paraphrase):
“Well, of course I saw right away that trading rooks was probably a win. But I am playing for a million dollars, so I wanted to analyze it carefully to make sure. I spent the next few minutes analyzing the lines and came to the conclusion that indeed it did win. So, I looked at my clock and I still had 13 minutes left, so I figured I would look for some crazy computer moves that a human might not think of, in case there was something there that I missed. So, I searched for some, but I couldn’t find any. Then I looked at my clock and I still had 10 minutes left, so I decided I may as well check my original analysis to see if I might have missed anything. I could not find any problems and then I checked my clock again. At that point I was down to 7 minutes and I thought that’s about right, so I made the move. Then, of course, the engine soon resigned.”
At this point I ask my students: “Who was right, Kramnik or the critics?” Of course, they all agree with Kramnik. Many of his critics made the very poor assumption that ‘the faster you see and play something, the smarter you are’ when in fact playing a critical move quickly, even if it seems to win, is not just not smart, it is also not wise. Kramnik did not get to be world champion because he played critical moves hastily.
There is a lot to be learned about criticality assessment and time management from this story. In their book
Chess Rules of Thumb
, Alburt and Lawrence state that any position where you see a move that “seems to win” is a critical position, and a critical position is “one about which you should think long and hard.”
I agree and go a step further to tell my students, “In chess, all flashing lights are red!” meaning whether you see good stuff (apparent wins) or bad stuff (danger), those both require extra careful thought. It is wrong to play quickly and then slow down only after you get into trouble; instead, play slowly to try to avoid trouble. However, if trouble does occur, most players at least understand that getting out of it may be critical and they usually play slowly. But it’s just as important to be careful when you think you are winning and have a lot to lose by hastily trying to win.
Tip 60
If you are not sure a move is critical, you must assume it may be!
Suppose you are driving a car at night and you come to a sharp curve in the road. Do you: Assume all is clear and just zoom around the corner blindly, or assume there might be something there and slow down in case there is?
If your answer is #1, good luck! You may not be around to read too many more of my chess tips!
In driving we call this ‘defensive driving’ and in life we call this ‘assume the worst case’. Many chess situations call for very similar logic. I call the ability to determine if a move may possibly have a great effect on the outcome ‘criticality assessment’. But suppose your criticality assessment is not that good and you don’t know if a move is critical. In that case, if you have sufficient time on your clock, then you assume the worst case – that the position might be critical – take your time, and do the best analysis you can. This would be true no matter how good you are at analysis; you have nothing to lose, so why not try and do your best?
The following game has a position which illustrates that. On move 51, White has plenty of time and there is only a king, rook, and pawn versus king and rook. The game will quickly dissolve into a win for the player with the pawn or a draw. If you are inexperienced, you might not know which it would be, but you should be able to sense that you are nearing the end, so therefore you have plenty of time to see if you can figure it out. You have nothing to lose.
White to play after 50...
R
xd5
51.a7??
Played in about 17 seconds even though White had 27 minutes left! Can you do better? The game concluded:
51...Kb7 52.Rh8 Ra5 53.Rh7+
½-½
White very quickly played 51.a7. If he had looked for his checks, captures, and threats, he might, just might, have seen that 51.Rc8+, his only check, wins: if 51...Kb6 52.Kxd5, 51...Kd6 52.Rd8+ wins the rook, and 51...Kd7 (the best try) 52.a7 (but not 52.Kxd5 Kxc8 with an easy draw) 52...Ra5 (or 52...Kxc8 53.a8=Q+ and 54.Qxd5) 53.a8=Q wins.
Even if you are not a good analyst, it doesn’t take a lot of skill or experience to realize that you have nothing to lose by trying to figure this out. And it not only doesn’t hurt to try – it actually helps! You will slowly improve. That means it is doubly good to try – you might succeed and, at worst, you will enhance your ability to try in the future! To realize that you must take your time and at least try – especially in the endgame where moves are much more likely to be critical – does not require any great chess skill. It only requires a little ‘life’ wisdom, something any wise adult should possess.
I have dubbed this problem of not playing slowly when you have plenty of time, especially when you may not know if the move is critical (essentially not assuming the worst case, that it is), ‘The Wise Adult Syndrome’. Hopefully any wise adult would realize they have nothing to lose and should play slowly and try, no matter what their expectation and analysis skills. You do not have to be a great player to at least take some time and see what you can find.
Tip 61
Just because a move is tricky and clever doesn’t necessarily make it best or even good.
I have seen many players who consistently do one or more of the following:
• If one of their pieces is attacked by a piece of lesser value (AWL; Tip #94) and there is a zwischenzug (in-between move) they can play instead of just moving the piece to a safe square, they will play the zwischenzug,
• If there is an en passant possibility, they will always play it.
• If they see any tricky sequence, they will more likely play it than an equally better or even superior calm move.
• If they make a mistake and the only way of saving grace is, on the next move, to take back (reverse) their previous move, they will compound their mistake by making an inferior move rather than admit their mistake.
For example, I have seen the following mistake several times in similar positions:
Black to play
Instead of just saving the attacked knight by simply moving it, Black ‘counter-attacks’ with
1...Bb4+??
and after
2.c3
will lose a piece with two pieces attacked by pawns. I think there are several explanations for this:
• Players like to show how smart they are. Therefore, instead of making the simple move which anyone can make, they will show everyone that they can find a more clever response.
• Players want to show their opponent they can’t be bullied or pushed around. Therefore, instead of a “meek” (smart) retreating move, they will play an aggressive counter-attacking move.
• The games published in books, videos and other media are often the exceptional games where something imbalanced happens and one player eventually does make clever moves to win. The majority of games, where both sides play well and end in draws (or perhaps more mundane wins) are not the ones published. This is called a ‘biased sample’ and the amateur public that consistently sees these exceptional master games conclude that finding extraordinary moves is the right way to play chess.
• It is more fun to play tricky moves!
Occam’s Razor states that the simplest solution is often correct. But the lure of the reasons listed above often entices weaker players to play inferior moves. Here is another example; White has just captured a piece on d4:
Black to play – Is 1...
B
h3 good, bad, or neutral?
Black has a choice of two moves: he can recapture the piece with 1...exd4, or he can play the zwischenzug 1...Bh3 first, threatening mate and then, after White stops the mate, he can then play 2...exd4, regaining the piece.
Whenever one has a zwischenzug, it is like any other move: it can be good/beneficial, bad, or it might just have a neutral effect and be no better or worse than another good move.
In the diagram above, is 1...Bh3 good, bad, or neutral?
The game continued
1...Bh3?? 2.Bf3 exd4 3.Re1+??
, but when I reviewed the game with my student, I pointed out he could play a removal-of-the-guard tactic instead with 3.Qe1+!. The double attack on the black queen forces 3...Qxe1 4.Rxe1+, but now the g-pawn is no longer pinned and thus the bishop on h3 is no longer guarded. Further, the retreat 4...Be6 also loses the bishop.
Years later another student cleverly noticed that White has another way of winning with 1...Bh3?? 2.Bb5+ when, no matter how Black gets out of check, White can play 3.Qf3. There are multiple possibilities, but he was correct; this is more complicated but, again, White is winning material.
Therefore, when you see a move like 1...Bh3, don’t think ‘Aha, I will show him! Instead of recapturing I will threaten mate!’ but rather ‘Is 1...Bh3 better than simply 1...exd4? If I play 1...Bh3 how will White stop mate and, in doing so, is that better for me than if I just recapture now?’
Remember Tip #8, which stated that you usually learn more from unclear variations than from ‘dull’ equal ones. But that does not mean that tricky moves are always good (some are clearly terrible!). Next time you see a tricky move – and we hope that is often – just don’t be swayed into thinking that tricky must be good. Especially when you are in otherwise good positions, don’t spoil them by quickly playing clever but unchecked variations. Another way of saying this is: Don’t make chess harder than it is; sometimes Occam’s Razor is correct: the simplest solution is often the best one.
Tip 62
Avoid the common ‘ABC’ error of moving quickly without recalculating sequences that you analyzed on previous moves.
When a student makes a mistake, I don’t just say “That’s a bad move; don’t do it again”. Instead, I ask “What caused that error?” so that if we identify the cause, I have a chance to make a suggestion that will make that error less likely to occur in future similar situations. One of the common errors I find occurs when my student says “I made that move fast because I had calculated it on the previous move.”
For the purposes of this discussion, let us label a player’s current candidate move ‘A’, the opponent’s expected (or most dangerous) reply ‘B’ and the player’s expected reply to ‘B’ on his next move as ‘C’. We can shortcut our nomenclature by calling this expected sequence ‘ABC’ as calculated when A is the candidate move.
The ABC error occurs when A and B are played and then the player makes move C immediately (or at least very quickly), and it turns out C is either not optimum or maybe an outright blunder.
Start with an important fact: when analyzing A, the purpose of C is often to ensure that you can safely meet B – in chess we call the possibility ‘BC’ the ‘tactical justification’ of candidate A. But just because C justifies A does
not
mean that C is forced or even the best move if B is played. Sometimes C is the best move, and sometimes it is not, but you need to make sure there is a C when you play A or else you are playing Hope Chess (Tip #65), and if someone makes a forcing move B in response to your move A then without a C already in mind, you are hoping that you have a defense to B and, if not, that can cost you the game.
So even though the existence of a saving or follow-up C is necessary to play A, it may not be that good or you may have calculated incorrectly! Sometimes A is fine, but the error was thinking C should follow B, when that may be a blunder.
Therefore, the correct thing to do when you calculate ABC, play A, and your opponent responds with B is:
• Re-analyze C slowly and carefully to make sure it is just as good as you thought it was when you anticipated it on the previous move (note: the position has changed from when you calculated C last move, so at the least your visualization should be better!).
• If move C is as good as you previously thought
and
it wins the game easily, quadruple check it and play it. But more likely it doesn’t win the game so, in that case, put it in your pocket as your ‘King of the Hill’ (the best move found so far), and do your normal search for a better move.
• If move C is not as good as you thought it was, it is possible you are in trouble, especially if it is the best move. But you don’t know that yet, so since it’s not as good, immediately start searching for a better move.
Confused by too many letters? Here’s an example:
White to play
White calculated 1.Bh7+ (A) and if 1...Kh8 (B), then 2.Ne5 (C) hitting the pinned knight and threatening 3.Nxf7#. So White played
1.Bh7+
and after
1...Kh8
(1...Kf8 is better, but also bad for Black) White fairly quickly played
2.Ne5
. Black defended by sacrificing his queen with
2...Nxe5!
, a move White did not expect. After
3.Rxd8 Rcxd8
Black had a rook and a knight for his queen with plenty of play left on the board. Although White was much better, Black eventually won.
But instead of rattling off his intended C, 2.Ne5, so quickly, White had a much better move by reversing the move order and playing 2.Rxd7! first then after 2...Qxd7, 3.Ne5 would threaten both 4.Nxd7 and 4.Nxf7#. After 3...Bxh4 (what else?) 4.Nxd7 White would be ahead a queen for a rook, an entire piece better than he was after the inferior 2.Ne5.
Next time you calculate a sequence, make sure to ‘start afresh’ on the next move and (re-) do the work. Relying on what you calculated on the previous move can be dangerous to your chess health.
Tip 63
Suppose you analyze a promising line that does not seem to work because it superficially seems to loses material. At the point the material is lost, ask ‘Are there further checks, captures, or threats that might change the evaluation?’
