If there are further forcing moves which might change the evaluation, it is worthwhile to investigate that line further. If you don’t analyze further and the line does turn out to be beneficial, that’s called a quiescence error (Tip #44), because you stopped analyzing too soon when the position wasn’t ‘quiet’.
Avoiding quiescence errors is one of the big differences between lower class players (1000-1400) and upper class players (1600-1900). Lower-rated players complain, “I can get all the puzzles right in a book or an online puzzle set [when the requirement of the puzzle tells them to keep looking because something is there!], but then I miss the same thing in a game [when they must create their own stopping point by asking the question above].”
Here’s a common example I give to my students:
White to play
In this position, notice that ‘White to play’ is different than ‘White to play and win’ and ‘White to play and mate’. The more defined the requirement, the easier the problem becomes!
If this position occurred in a game, a lower-rated player might miss the idea completely. If the position is shown as a problem that simply asks for White’s best move, then it is also possible to miss the main idea, but less likely. However, if we go all the way to White to play and mate, that narrows down the possibilities so much that it is much easier to find the solution, since only a few moves even threaten mate.
For example, a move like 1.Qd3, threatening the sneaky pin 2.Qxg6 might be enough for ‘White to play and win’ if there were no defenses (there are).
In a game, if White rejects
1.Qxd4
(threatening 2.Qxg7#) because it ‘loses the queen’ to
1...Bxd4
, that would be a quiescence error. But even in a game – much less a puzzle – a stronger player would easily notice that
2.Bxd4
threatens 3.Rh8#, and so the sequence is worth investigating further. That is when most notice that the ‘obvious’ defense 2...f6 is illegal due to the pin of the bishop on c4, and now the tactic is ‘on’!
When I was first shown this position, I was under the impression this was a ‘White to play and win’ problem. I stopped analyzing after
1.Qxd4 Bxd4 2.Bxd4 Qxe2+ 3.Kxe2 Rfe8+ 4.Kf1 Kf8
when White is ahead a bishop. In fact, White can continue
5.Bf6
and it’s going to be mate anyway. Black can throw a few pieces in the way to make the longest line mate in 9, but it is clear White is winning with just the analysis above.
The concept in criticality assessment that if you see a potentially powerful line that at first does not seem to work, but you continue because the risk/reward benefit is so great, is called ‘trying to make it work’ where ‘it’ represents your potentially winning move or idea. So next time you see an idea and/or move that might win, but at first it seems the opponent has a defense, it is likely worth some extra time to see if you can make it work!
Tip 64
If your opponent makes a game winning threat and you have time on your clock to try and thwart it, you need to take whatever time is necessary to see if there is a solution.
If you do
not
take your time and just let him execute his threat, I call that ‘acquiescing’. Here is an example from a recent student game:
Black to move after 30.
Q
xg6
Black is in trouble as he only has two bishops for a rook and three pawns, plus his king is exposed. White’s last move double-attacked the rook on e8 and the bishop on g2. The time limit of the game was 45 minutes plus a 45-second increment on each move. Both sides had been playing too quickly and Black still had over 32 minutes on his clock. What should Black do?
There is only one move that saves both pieces and continues to make a fight of the game:
30...Bc6
, saving the bishop and guarding the rook. So Black needs to take whatever time is necessary to try to find this move. If he has a long think and still can’t find it, that happens; he is human and sometimes we fail to find even one-move solutions.
But instead of taking time to try to find the move, Black took 35 seconds – less than the increment and leaving him with still over 32 minutes remaining – and played the capitulating
30...Re7??
. After
31.Qxg2
he could have resigned, but he played a few more moves.
When I asked him after the game why he did not play 30...Bc6, he gave the common reply “I didn’t see it” (Tip #50). But it was not so much he did not see the saving move as he did not take the necessary time to fully try. This was an excellent example of acquiescing!
Here is another example:
Black to play
Unfortunately, I don’t have the time-stamping, but if I recall correctly, Black fairly quickly determined he did not have any safe moves for the knight, so he decided to use a tempo to sacrifice it with
1...Ne3??
, so that after
2.Qxe3 Qxe3+ 3.Kxe3
he could achieve something he wanted to do: trade queens.
This is definitely a sign of acquiescing to the apparent threat to the knight because Black had better alternatives. For example, simply the Counting (Tip #99) continuation 1...Qxf3+ 2.Qxf3 Rxf3+ 3.Rxf3 allows him save the knight with 3...Nf4 or maybe 3...Nh4 when Black is ahead two pieces for a rook. Unlike the game, where Black lost an entire piece, 1...Qxf3+ only loses the exchange (rook for knight), and in this position that makes all the difference.
But even better is the cute 1...Qxf3 2.Qxf3 Ne1+! when Black remains ahead a full piece. When you see a good move, look for a better one...
Tip 65
It is acceptable to hope for some things when playing chess, but do not play ‘Hope Chess’!
Let’s list some of the things you might hope for during a chess game:
• You make a threat and hope your opponent does not see it.
• You analyze a line and hope your opponent plays inferior moves which make the line look good.
• You make a mistake and hope your opponent does not see it and capitalize.
• You get a bad position and hope your opponent makes a mistake and lets you back in the game or even win.
These are many of the things you can hope for but, when I originally wrote about ‘Hope Chess’ in my
Chess Café
article ‘The Secrets of Real Chess’, I meant none of the above!
My definition of Hope Chess was: “When you have a candidate move and do
not
analyze ‘If I make this move and my opponent replies with a dangerous check, capture, or threat, can I safely meet it
next
move?’”
Therefore, if you do make such a move without analyzing for dangerous replies and the opponent
does
reply with a check, capture or threat, you think ‘Uh-oh! I did not anticipate that; what should I do now?’ That is Hope Chess because now you are just ‘hoping’ you can meet their check, capture, or threat safely. If you can, you are just lucky; if you can’t, your previous move may have been the critical mistake that loses the game.
In my experience I have found that most players under 1700 play some form of Hope Chess, although the ones in the 1400-1700 range at least usually use their tactical vision to see if there is any apparent danger before they play their candidate move.
When asked “What’s the worse example of Hope Chess you have ever seen?” (and indeed, arguably one of the worst moves, period), I offer the following amusing but painful example from a game where a student had White:
White to play
White should play 1.Qf3+ Kg1 (1...Ke1 2.Qe2#) 2.Qf2+ Kh1 3.Qxh4+ and now:
a) 3...Kg2 4.Qf2+ Kh3 (or 4...Kh1 5.Qf3+ and if the king moves, 6.Qxc6 with a tablebase win, or if 5...Qg2 6.Qxg2+ Kxg2 7.Ke4 will win the c-pawn and the game) 5.Qf3+ and again either the king moves and 6.Qxc6 or 5...Qg3 6.Qxg3+ Kxg3 7.Ke4 wins the c-pawn as before.
b) 3...Kg1 4.Qf2+ Kh1 5.Qf3+ transposing into the parenthetical 4...Kh1 5.Qf3+ line above, winning.
Instead, White used hand-waving to make ‘the most forcing’ move, also a monstrous Hope Chess example:
1.Qh1+??
This is not just Hope Chess, but forcing Black to win!
1...Qg1+
What did White expect? He could not have analyzed this move, which is Black’s only legal reply, because the double threat of check and taking the white queen wins easily and instantly for his opponent. If he had analyzed 1.Qh1+??, he could not have missed that he could not safely play it!
2.Qxg1+
Forced, or else White loses his queen.
2...Kxg1 3.Kf3 h3
And White resigned! It is difficult to find game positions where you can not only blunder, but practically force your opponent to see it and win, but this was a doozy!
Tip 66
For positional and tactical considerations, short-term ‘sure’ aspects usually outweigh long-term possible consequences.
When I analyze students’ games with them and ask why they made one move and not another, I notice they sometimes make the curious error of weighing vague long-term possibilities which have a small chance to occur over short-term considerations that surely will occur. While that occasionally might be the correct weighting, much more often the issues that will occur should have greater weight.
White to play after 1...
N
xf3+
The above position was created from one that happened in a recent student game. I have added a white pawn on c4 to compensate for the one my student had lost, so we can consider a position of equal material:
In similar positions almost all students rated under 1800 play 2.Bxf3,
since 2.Rxf3? loses the exchange and 2.gxf3 “opens up the white king for attack”. But when I gave this position to the engine, it preferred 2.gxf3 by about a third of a pawn.
Here are the benefits of 2.gxf3:
• It brings more pawns to the middle (Tip #129).
• It overprotects e4 so the rest of the white pieces don’t have to.
• It attacks the bishop with a pawn (AWL tip #94) so forces Black to use the tempo to move the bishop.
All these ‘immediate’ benefits outweigh the longer-term concern of ‘opening up the king’ to a nebulous attack where currently the only black piece in the vicinity is the bishop and it is difficult for Black to transfer his queen to the kingside.
Tip 67
The main thing to do when way ahead is ‘Think defense first’, which is not at all the same as ‘Play defensively’.
I have a list of six things to keep in mind when you are way ahead (roughly the exchange or more, or 1.75+ on an engine evaluation):
• Think Defense First.
• Keep It Simple (the KISS principle).
• Make fair trades of pieces, not necessarily pawns.
• Don’t worry about the little things.
• Use all your pieces all the time.
• Don’t get into unnecessary time pressure.
Of these six the most misunderstood/misquoted is #1. I have seen many players quote me as saying something like “
Dan says when you are way ahead you should play defensively.
” I do not believe this and I have never said or written this anywhere.
It is true the words ‘defense’ and ‘defensively’ are similar; but “think defense first” is
an ordering
of how you should think, while “play defensively” is a style and mindset. They are, in practice, completely different.
I give the example: Suppose there are 100 issues you could address on a move when you are winning easily. Three of them involve ideas of how your opponent could get back in the game and draw or win (e.g., trapping your queen or back-rank mate), and the other 97 are offensive/attacking ideas. Then all I am suggesting is to first check those three issues where you could get into trouble; if they are all covered, go offense! Go attack! In that case your opponent has nothing that can harm you so play an offense move – don’t move defensively after you have thought defense first and everything checked out 100% safely.
As a further example, we will compare two positions:
White to play
White is ahead a piece for a pawn, normally enough to win easily with some care so, if White thinks defense first, he should recognize at least two ways for Black to get back in the game:
• His back rank is weak so ...Re1# must be stopped.
• The black passed pawn on the b-file at some point could become dangerous.
So, in this case offense must be delayed a move and a luft move like 1.g3 is required. Compare this to the following similar position:
White to play
Thinking defense first, Black still has a passed pawn, but the pawn is blockaded harmlessly by a knight on a square of the opposite color of the black bishop. Secondly, a back-rank mate is possible, but only if Black can get a rook to an open file to access the first rank, and also place the dark-squared bishop on the h2-b8 diagonal to cover h2.
Therefore, it would not be unreasonable to stop the back rank mates permanently with a move like 1.g3. But most strong (and weak!) players would not do this since the threat is not imminent and offensive moves to threaten f7 like 1.Bd5
or 1.Nd6
or 1.Ne5 are available.
The engine – who doesn’t need to think defense first since it can never accidentally forget to stop back rank mate or get its queen trapped – gives as its top three moves: 1.h4, 1.Bd5,
and 1.g3 (nonetheless!).
So in the second example we thought defense first, but due to the lack of an imminent threat, we could play offensively with a move like 1.Bd5. It is still nice for White to keep in mind (as Black surely will!) the possibility of ...Bf4 followed by ...Re8, threatening ...Re1# and make sure to stop it before it ever becomes threatening. As a general principle (*Tip Alert!*),
you want to prevent dangerous situations before they ever get dangerous rather than waiting until the threat is imminent and thinking ‘Uh-oh! What do I do now?’ when the answer might be ‘Too late! The threat is unstoppable!’
Tip 68
Weaker players are often advised by their coaches to do a ‘sanity check’ just before they make a move to make sure the intended move is not crazy. But stronger players do not usually do sanity checks – they are not efficient.
In the following position, the previous moves were
1...
R
g8+ 2.
K
g4-f5
R
e6-c6
:
White to play after 2...
R
c6
White thought for a while and then wrote 3.Rc1 on his scoresheet (note that writing moves on a scoresheet before making them is against modern FIDE rules; US Chess Federation rules do allow this as an option). After a while he did not play 3.Rc1, but instead played 3.f4. What did his actions, and their timing, tell us about his thought process?
First, the fact that he wrote the move down and thought for a while longer tells us White was doing a sanity check. It also tells us that while he was initially more concerned that Black’s previous move was threatening his c-pawn, he did not check to see if candidate moves that might save the c-pawn were safe. Because if he had asked, ‘If I play 3.Rc1 what are all of my opponent’s dangerous checks, captures, and threats?’ then he would have immediately found that the only reply of check, 3...Rg5#, was checkmate!
But he could not have done that because if he had, he would have rejected any defense of the c-pawn and would not have gotten so far as to write 3.Rc1 on his scoresheet. When you don’t check to see if a move is safe this way, I call that ‘Hope Chess’ (Tip #65), because if the opponent does make a check, capture, or threat in response to an unchecked move, you hope you have a defense. Many players who play Hope Chess are justified in doing a sanity check to prevent disasters.
However, since strong players do not play Hope Chess, they efficiently filter out unsafe moves long before it is time to make the move. To perform a sanity check and when the move is found to be not safe, to be forced to go from the apparent end of the thought process all the way back to ‘square one’ is inefficient, to say the least. Therefore, while strong players may surely make ‘last second’ double-checks to make sure the move they are about to play is good for the reasons they thought, they rarely perform sanity checks.
Tip 69
One of the main chess skills is evaluation: determining which side stands better, how much better, and why. Honing your evaluation skill(s) is very important.
While the central thought process skill is analysis, once analysis is finished for a particular sequence of moves, the position should be evaluated. It is entirely possible to analyze a sequence correctly, but then incorrectly evaluate the resultant position. In that case an inferior move might be chosen, or a superior move discarded.
The following position occurred when I was playing in the Pennsylvania State High School Championship during my second year of serious play. This game was the fourth one of the day and I was very tired. Earlier in the game I had mis-analyzed a position and lost the exchange for a pawn:
Wahrenberger–Heisman, 1968
Black to play
In this position my opponent offered a draw and knowing then only the what I call the ‘Reinfeld’ values for the pieces (§=1, B=N=3, R=5, Q=9; Tip #98), I erroneously concluded that I probably stood slightly worse and accepted the draw.
When I got back to the motel room with my friend Lester, he asked me why I gave my opponent a draw. I told him I was tired and had lost the exchange. He protested that I was much better and challenged me to play a few moves. I did and quickly realized that he was correct; I had badly misevaluated the position. This turned out to be the key game and I finished in second place behind my opponent.
Not that much later in my development I learned that the ‘integer-only’ Reinfeld values can be fairly inaccurate in many positions. GM Larry Kaufman has written extensively about determining the average values of the pieces and GM Soltis wrote a book
Rethinking the Chess Pieces
. Several years after this game was played, I wrote my first chess book
Elements of Positional Evaluation: How the Pieces Get Their Power
which was, ironically, not about evaluating positions, but more about evaluating the power of each piece in a position. There is, however, a new book devoted entirely to evaluating positions:
Evaluate Like a Grandmaster
by Perelshteyn and Solon.
As a short summary, both the Perelshteyn/Solon book and my works use the identical set of the four main evaluation criteria for positions:
•
Material Balance
– using ‘average’ piece values.
•
King Safety
– speaks for itself.
•
Piece Activity
– active pieces are more valuable than less active ones, in the same way that a queen is more valuable than a rook because it has more mobility, and thus activity.
•
Pawn Structure
– possibly the least important of the four, but still important enough to make the difference in a game.
Ideally, when analyzing, you want to reach ‘quiescence’ before you evaluate. Quiescence means there are no further forcing moves (checks, captures, and threats) that might strongly alter the evaluation of a position (Tip #44). While it is not always possible to reach quiescence in every evaluation (unclear sacrifices being an example), most evaluation is done once ‘the smoke has cleared’.
Besides purchasing a new book or just playing many slow games and analyzing them with opponents and strong players, another way to hone your evaluation skills is to take interesting, especially materially unbalanced, positions from your games and others – grandmasters will do – and make an ‘engine-like’ numerical evaluation where, for example, ‘-1.0’ means Black is better by about a pawn, ‘0.0’ is dead even, and ‘+1.0’ means White is better by about a pawn. Then turn on a strong engine like Stockfish and compare your answer with its answer. If they are close, great! If not so close, use Tip #7, learn by playing ‘What if” games’ with a strong engine, and try to figure out why your guess was wrong – we will assume a 3500 level engine’s evaluation is probably the right one!
Chapter Three
Psychology
This chapter provides tips which deal with a player’s mental approach to playing, studying, getting feedback, and the proper attitude to take when encountering various common chess situations. It also includes the important areas of how to adjust your psychology and approach depending on the state of the game, e.g. winning, losing, one or both players in time pressure, type of opponent, etc.
Tip 70
*Top-10 Tip*
Do not be afraid of losing; be concerned about playing a game and not learning anything (or learning something and failing to apply it in the future).
Almost all agree it is more ‘fun’ to lose 100 tough games against someone a little better than they are, than to scholar’s mate a beginner 100 games in a row. GM Yasser Seirawan once asked 1000+ scholastic players at the Nationals: “Who here has lost 10,000 games?” One kid near the back raised his hand, Yasser pointed at him and said, “You’re on the right track!”
Occasionally a new student, when asked about his/her weaknesses, will say “I am afraid of playing because I don’t like to lose.” That is understandable; the goal of the game is to win and, due to the ‘non-luck’ aspect to chess, losing is often taken more personally than it would on games where other excuses are more readily acceptable.
Then I ask students to consider a scale of “fear of losing” from 0-100 where:
• 0 represents absolutely no concern with losing. The result doesn’t matter at all; it’s just a game.
• 100 represents mortal fear – so much fear that losing a game puts you in a state where you can’t play, talk to anyone, etc.
Where do you think most grandmasters fall in such a scale? My answer is that most grandmasters fall between 60 and 85. Anything below that level and they won’t have sufficient drive to improve; you need to want to win enough that you are willing to do years of work to get to the highest level.
On the other hand, a score above 80-85 would indicate that losing is so painful that doing all the losing that GM Seirawan indicated would not be possible. A strong player’s score on this scale likely has to fall in the ‘sweet spot’ where losing is to be avoided as best possible by doing all that work, but not so much that progress is inhibited.
No one likes to lose, but in the long run most players are going to score only about 50% of their points, so learning to deal with losing is part of becoming a good player. Learn to ask ‘what is the main reason I lost?’ and takes proactive steps toward reducing the chances of the same mistake(s) in the future.
‘And not learning anything’ is the other aspect to this tip. When I first started out, I religiously consulted
MCO-10
after each game, fast or slow, to ask: ‘If I had to play this game again, on which opening move would I improve?’ Then I would do my best to remember and apply. It seemed obvious and paramount to me that if I had material that told me how I could do better in the future, I had to use it or I could not live with myself to make the same mistakes repeatedly.
I have taught over 1,000 students one-to-one privately and, when the subject comes up about looking up their openings after a game with a book, engine, database, and/or video, fewer than 10% were already doing that (some just occasionally). Moreover, even after making that suggestion – strongly – a surprisingly low percentage of my students began doing it consistently, even though it only involved a few minutes of time after each game.
I think this reluctance to take those few minutes consistently, even after being made aware of its benefits, says something about not only the perceived ‘difficulty’ of looking up an opening (everything is easy when you know how), but also to the problems lower-rated players have of remembering what they are shown. The better one’s board vision, the better they can remember a pattern to help reduce repetition of past mistakes.
Of course, there is much more to be learned from going over a game (win, lose, or draw) than the way you could have improved in the opening. See Tip #3 about analyzing with strong players and Tip #7 about learning by playing ‘What if?’ with an engine. Finally, there is only one person who knows the ‘whys’ of your opponent’s moves and ideas, and that is your opponent. So, if possible,
offer to review each slow game afterwards with your opponent
in a ‘post-mortem’ and learn as much as you can (Tip #18).
Tip 71
*Top-10 Tip*
Do not worry about your rating; work on your playing strength.
Ratings are a wonderful invention. The Elo system and its derivatives have expanded well beyond the chess world and are used not only in other games of skill, but even to predict and gamble on high stakes sporting events. The accuracy of the ratings makes them simultaneously attractive and yet dangerously seductive. I think we have all heard stories of players who have become more fascinated with their rating than they sometimes are in the actual playing of their chess games. The rating ‘game’ becomes a game unto itself.
Your rating is only a shadow of your playing strength
. Players who make draw offer decisions based on rating rather than ‘how much can I learn?’ factors are almost always misguided. My students who instead would play anyone anywhere and did not offer or accept ‘preserve rating’ draws learned fastest.
Consider the following ‘clone’ thought experiment: Suppose there is a scholastic player rated 1800 who does not like playing lower-rated players because he might lose rating points. Suppose we clone him in January, and further suppose he continues to avoid lower rated scholastic players while his clone plays in several scholastic tournaments versus a bunch of up-and-coming 1300 and 1400 players, who beat him more than their ratings would indicate, dropping his rating to 1700. Meanwhile the original non-clone sits at home and studies chess, but plays no instructive games.
If they both enter the same section of the World Open in July, who would you think would perform better? My money is on the 1700 clone rather than the higher-rated 1800 original; the clone is the better player, despite his lower rating. His experience includes learning from his losses, which the original lacks.
Sure, if you are 1800 and offered the choice of playing in one of two events on the same weekend, choosing the one with more challenging opponents – within reason – is the better choice. But to not play at all to protect your rating when there is only one event is likely the wrong choice. If your playing strength does not deteriorate and you have a terrible event and lose rating points and become underrated, it is not a disaster; just play some more and your rating will return to the level of your playing strength!
When entering events or especially when considering offering or accepting draw offers, it is usually best if you make the decisions as if ratings did not exist. I cannot tell you how many times a student has accepted a draw in a superior endgame where they could only lose if they started putting pieces en prise, with the explanation something like “They were higher rated so I was happy to get the draw.” I will bet they would not have said that if ratings did not exist and they just considered the position on the board!
If you are one of the top players of your category and you need a certain rating by a certain time to qualify for an invitational event or a FIDE title, it might make sense to ‘time’ your participation to ensure your rating qualification. But for the average player, such qualification opportunities are rare and quite the exception. One possibility is players who improved greatly during the pandemic by playing online and then are underrated for over-the-board play and want to take advantage of big money events like the World Open, which in turn had to consider some caveats to address those exceptional issues. Nevertheless, manipulating your rating – rather than just improving – is rarely required.
Do things that will improve your playing strength and your rating will surely follow.
Tip 72
Play as if the rating system had never been invented.
This tip applies to a variety of situations, including:
• Deciding which events to play.
• Deciding whether to accept or decline a draw.
• Choosing between moves, or the type of game you want to play.
• How to treat your opponents.
I once had a fairly strong student who was playing a 1900 player (at the time higher than his rating, but not by so much), and my student was ahead a rook. There was still a lot of play left in the game, but objectively it was a trivial win. His opponent could have resigned, except for the number of pieces on the board. Yet my student timidly offered a draw, which was immediately and happily accepted. When asked why he offered the draw, my student mumbled something about his opponent having vague threats, but he was just happy to get his five rating points (which would have been more like 20+ if he had won). If there was no rating system, there is no way my student would have offered a draw in such a clearly winning situation.
Another young, promising student did something similar versus a wise, older and higher-ranked player: he offered a draw in a position where he was winning so easily that it was more a question of whether the opponent should resign than whether a draw should be offered or accepted. Amazingly, his opponent did something I have never seen before or since – he declined the draw and resigned! He then told my student something like: “I could not in good conscience accept a draw in such a losing position and you should not have offered it. If you offer draws in winning positions that is not the way to become a strong player.”
These are just a couple of many, many examples of how players let ratings negatively affect their actions. Yes, there are the rare cases where a certain result or rating level is needed to achieve a norm or an invitation to a future event, but these are few and far between compared to the everyday actions players take which detract from both their results and their possibilities for learning and improving.
I suggest instead that in most cases the proper action can be made by simply eliminating the rating consideration from the decisions. Play in the events or sections that are the most fun or offer the most opportunity for learning. Offer draws if the position indicates that a draw is the likely result and you are wasting time and learning nothing by continuing. This results in another one of my tips (#19):
Think of a draw offer as an offer to remain ignorant of what you would have learned in the rest of the game.
This last suggestion must be good, as I have seen at least one other author quote it!
Tip 73
In order to become a rating, you must lose your fear of players of that rating.
From teaching over 1,000 students, I can say that some of the most common problems new players have are due to the rating system and one of those is fear of players with ratings a little above theirs. If the rating system had never existed, I don’t see how any of those fears would have occurred.
When I find a player extraordinarily afraid of players just above him/her, I ask “According to the rating system how many points should you score in a 100-game match? Usually they guess a number much lower than the expected value of 36 (a win equals one point, a draw half), due to their fear and actual results. But what happens when this fear exists is a ‘self-fulfilling prophecy’ that they do not do as well.
I suggest to such players that, whenever possible, they do not try to discover their opponent’s rating before each game. That way they can treat everyone the same and play with less fear against those that are higher. I have a couple of anecdotal stories, not necessary to relate here, which indicate that not knowing the opponent’s rating, when possible, usually works very well.
So long as the fear exists, even if the player gets stronger their self-fulfilling prophecy makes rating gains more difficult. There is also the rating factor. For a USCF 1400 player, a draw versus a player 1500 gains about four points, but a win gets about 20, a 5-1 ratio (compared to the 2-1 ratio on the scoreboard of a full point versus a half).
If you are afraid of players rated higher than yourself and get good positions, but offer draws out of fear that you might lose the game, it makes it much more difficult to gain rating points. It especially makes it difficult to take advantage of the USCF ‘bonus’ points, when established players have a much better than expected result, because scoring well above your expected score usually requires at least one, if not more, wins versus higher-rated opponents in an event.
Obviously once the fear goes away, it is much easier psychologically to play for wins, and to achieve them. That why I tell players that have certain rating goals that if they continue to fear players at their goal level, it makes that goal much harder to achieve, even when their playing strength improves.
Tip 74
Some positive chess attributes do not require you to be a strong or experienced player; it just requires players of all ages to act like a ‘wise adult’.
This tip is the psychological follow-up to the ‘try your best’ Tip #2. Imagine you are a track coach and some of your runners are practicing not by running around the track, as they are supposed to, but instead leisurely walking. It would not be difficult for you to figure out that you need to tell them to run as directed or else they will not be long for the track team.
But it is interesting that many lower-rated players who come to me for lessons make many of their mistakes simply by not trying their best, for a variety of reasons, many of them psychological. Higher-rated players rarely have this problem since it would be very difficult for them to have become higher rated if they did not try hard on every move. After all, it only takes one bad move to lose a game, so it only makes sense to be vigilant on every move.
Here is a good example from a recent game by an adult student. He is playing a slow game, has plenty of time on his clock (I think about 19 minutes), and he is ahead the exchange for a pawn in the endgame. Can you find his best move?
Black to play
Black can obtain a winning position only by 1...Ra4!. Then if White tries to guard the pawn, say by 2.Bd5, the attack on the pinned pawn 2...b5 gives Black a winning advantage. This is not trivially easy, but it is not all that difficult, either. We could excuse a lower-rated player for missing this, but only under the condition they reasonably tried to find their best move.
But my student said he took seven seconds(!) to play 1...Rc3 when after 2.Bd5 he is not winning. Not only did my student not win, he actually lost this game and, thanks to the increment, still had about 19 minutes left on his clock when he resigned.
Part of my student’s problem was that he started to play much faster when he got less than 20 minutes on his clock, even though he had plenty of time, a nice increment, and the game was nearing the end. It was a form of psychological panic partly due to inexperience, but also due to lack of reasonable judgment of his time situation, which was by no means objectively difficult. He did not need to be a strong player to take more than seven seconds on a critical move when he had almost twenty minutes on his clock plus an increment; he just had to act like a ‘wise adult’, take a deep breath, and realize he had more time to think.
To reiterate a point from Tip #2, I tell my students that the first step in improvement is to try your best, not playing too fast or too slow, on every move. If they do not, that ‘masks’ other things they might do to improve. It is somewhat like learning to be good at reading is a strong prerequisite for other disciplines, so you can study them more efficiently. In chess,
trying your best on each move is the prerequisite.
An important point is that you don’t have to be a strong player or have excellent chess skills to both understand issues like ‘trying your best on every move’ or ‘when something critical is happening, be careful’ and trying to implement them each and every game. It does take willpower, consistency, and perseverance. I call issues that do not require high chess skills or knowledge the ‘wise adult syndrome’ because those who don’t do them suffer consequences that could be alleviated with just some wise decisions most adults could implement with that understanding and willpower. This leads us to Tip #75...
Tip 75
Try Your Best or Resign – do not have other ‘gears’.
When I first started improving as a player, it eventually dawned on me that there were only two gears to play chess: try your best or resign
.
This is a follow-up tip relating to the previous tip, about not needing to be a strong player to play wisely.
I would like to relate a story of how dramatic that difference can be between a 2200+ player and a class player, in this case two 1700s. About 25 years ago I was watching a game in the Under-1800 Section of the World Open, which was then held at the Adam’s Mark Hotel, about three miles down the road from my home.
I did not know either of the players I was watching. They were both about college age and rated about 1700. The round had been going on for more than four hours, so they were in the second time control (40 moves in two hours followed by sudden death in one hour), around move 44. Both had about 50 minutes left to complete the game. They had reached a king and pawn endgame where one side was up a pawn; I think it was Black. Let us assume Black had five pawns and White had four. If I remember correctly, Black had the more active king, but White had some compensating advantages in the pawn structure.
It was a pretty difficult position; it looked at first glance that Black should win (as is usual in king and pawn endgames ahead a pawn and with many pawns on the board), but it wasn’t trivially easy.
First, I looked at the plan where Black would bring his king to the queenside and attack the b-pawn. Passive defense by White was clearly hopeless, so he would have to make it a race by bringing his king to the kingside. I counted the race and apparently both sides would queen, but Black would remain a pawn ahead in a queen and pawn endgame.
Then I calculated what would happen if the black king stayed on the kingside. Again, that was murky as it would not be easy to create a winning passed pawn, due to the pawn structure. It was not easy to calculate exactly what would happen. Even if I could calculate what the eventual stability of the kingside would be in that line, Black’s work would not be done, as he would have to calculate whether staying kingside first and then later trying to go queenside was better than just going to queenside right away. There were several possible alternatives; too much for me to calculate in a few minutes. I still felt that with careful play Black was probably winning.
While I was calculating Black’s 44th move, an amazing thing happened: the players both played many moves extremely quickly and White resigned! Black had immediately moved his king to the queenside and White’s monarch had hopelessly followed, resulting in inevitable defeat.
After the game I asked the players if they would like to review the game with me in the Skittles room. They accepted and when we got there, I introduced myself as a master and asked them to set up the position on the 44th move, which they gladly did, happy to get some masterly help. I then proceeded to show them my analysis: why immediately bringing Black’s king to the queenside, as in the game, was problematic and how White could also queen in that race. But I also showed them that the other lines were not immediately decisive, either, and that it was going to take some effort for Black to force a win.
Obviously both players were relatively careful and slow: both had taken about 130 minutes for their first 43 moves. Therefore, I submitted the question I had been waiting to ask: “You guys are apparently careful because you both used over two hours, but how could you play out such a critical king and pawn endgame so fast? After all, the decision of what to do on move 44 was not at all easy and I was unable to come to a clear decision just about that one move – while you were playing out the entire remainder of the game!”
Black’s answer: “I thought I was winning and that it was trivial so I just played it quickly, expecting to win. White’s answer: “At that point I thought I was dead lost, but rather than resign, I thought I would play a few moves quickly just to see what would happen.”
Very reasonable and understandable answers, but not ones you would get from many extremely strong players. The 1700 players were using a gear I just do not have: ‘play fast and see what happens’. Another common bad gear that lower-rated players have is ‘I am winning easily so I will play really fast and show everyone how smart I am.’
My only two gears are (i.) Take your time and try your best on every move or (ii.) Resign. If these players had taken their time, they might have realized the position was not so clear or easy as they thought it was; for example, maybe with some care White could have drawn against the line Black quickly chose.
Sure, if I am ahead a queen and my opponent is not resigning, I am not going to take five minutes on every move; in some easily winning positions any careful, reasonable move will do. But, in those cases, I am still trying my best because I am being careful and making sure my move wins 100% with no possibility of stalemate, etc.
Trying your best does not mean always playing very slowly. In a speed game, playing slowly is not your best speed.
Playing your best suggests taking a reasonable amount of time given both the criticality of the position and the current time/clock situation.
If your move is forced, then taking extra time to see what might happen next can be wasteful, and not the best strategy.
Bottom line: If you want to know one difference between many 1700 players and most 2200 players, this story is worth remembering.
Tip 76
Always play with confidence, aggression, and respect for your opponent’s moves and ideas.
This is one of my favorite (and personally created) suggestions. It primarily applies toward the start of the game; if you are dead lost, then it makes less sense. As will be discussed in Tip #80, chess is not the type of game where playing conservatively (as opposed to safely) is the best way to win and improve. Chess is a mental sport, so your mental state has a very large effect on your playing strength.
If you think you are going to lose, that usually becomes a self-fulfilling prophecy
(Tip #73).
I like to tell the story of the first time I was paired with a master in a slow-rated event. I had been playing tournaments for 18 months and had a USCF rating of 1676. The tournament was a one-section open event and of the 102 players I was rated 52nd (in the first round player rated #1 plays #52; #2 plays #53, #3 plays #54, etc). Rating were lower in those days, so there was only one active master in the Philadelphia area, NM Sergei Goregliad, and I was paired against him, rated 1676 to Sergei’s 2242.
When my friend Lester Shelton and I saw the first round pairings, Les exclaimed “What a shame! If you had been rated a few points higher, you would have played an unrated, but now you have the play the master and he is going to kill you!” I calmly replied, “Les, I can’t worry about that. I am just going to do what I always do: look at the board, take my time, and make the best move I can. If I lose – and I probably will because he is a master – I will still look myself in the mirror and think ‘I did the best I could’.”
Notice although I estimated I probably would lose (if I had to bet!), I was not pessimistic at all; I was not afraid of the master nor afraid of losing. When I played, I felt fine, pretty much exactly how I felt in many other games I played against strong opposition. How did the game turn out?
Dan Heisman – Sergei Goregliad
Liberty Bell Open 1968
Dutch Defence
1.d4 f5 2.g3 g6 3.Bg2 Nf6 4.c4 Bg7 5.Nc3 0-0 6.Nh3 d6 7.0-0 c6 8.Ng5 e5 9.dxe5 dxe5 10.e4 h6 11.Nf3 Qc7 12.Nh4 Kh7 13.exf5 gxf5 14.Qc2 e4 15.Bf4 Qf7 16.Rad1 Na6 17.Ne2 Nb4 18.Qb3 Nd3 19.f3 Be6 20.fxe4 Bxc4 21.Qc2 Nb4 22.Qd2 Rad8 23.Qxb4 Bxe2 24.Rxd8 Bxf1 25.Rxf8 Bxf8
White to play after 25...
B
xf8
26.Qa5 Bb5 27.Nxf5 Qc4 28.Qc7+ Kg8 29.h3 Qf7 30.Nxh6+ Bxh6 31.Qxf7+ Kxf7 32.Bxh6 Bd3 33.e5 Nd7 34.Bf4 Ke6 35.Bf1 Bc2 36.Bc4+ Kf5 37.e6 Nf6 38.e7 Kg6 39.Be5 Ne8 40.Kf2 b5 41.Bg8 c5 42.h4 Bd1 43.Ke3 Bh5 44.Kf4 Nf6 45.Be6 Kh6 46.Bxf6 Bg6 47.Bd7 Bf7 48.Bxb5 Kg6 49.Be5 Kh7 50.e8=Q 1-0
I won! And six months later I was paired with Sergei again and he was out for revenge, but I won again and proved my first win was no fluke. After the second event where I beat Sergei, my rating soared to over 1900 after two years of play.
We have covered confidence and aggression, but what about ‘and respect for your opponent’s moves and ideas’? This primarily applies when playing inferior opponents. I often hear something like “I played fast because he was playing fast” or “I did not think he would see my threat” or “I was not paying attention so I missed what he was trying to do.”
Many players get overconfident when they get paired with clearly weaker opposition. But you don’t want to play down to the level of the opposition. In chess strong players can make bad mistakes (admittedly less frequently, and weak players can make strong moves (some intentional, some not!). Therefore, do not be surprised if you ‘put your head in the mouth’ of a weak player and he bites it off!
Take all opponent’s moves seriously and the first two questions to ask are:
• ‘Is that move safe?’
• ‘What are
all
the important/dangerous things that move does?’ (Tip #56)
Tip 77
Make the work fun. If you cannot, it will not help you nearly as much.
Over the years, I have had several students tell me something analogous to “I don’t find the homework you have suggested fun, but I am doing it anyway since I believe you that you say doing it will help me improve.”
My answer is always something like: “Never do homework that is not fun. Stop as soon as it ceases to be fun and tell me, and we will try to find out why it’s not fun and maybe find some ways to make it more fun. But in my experience, students who do homework that is not fun for them at best find it gives them diminishing returns and, over time, is not sustainable. Chess is
not like work or even schoolwork; it is a hobby and if the work is not fun, in the long run you won’t get much benefit from it.”
As an example of making the work more fun, assume the student does not find reading instructive anthology game books (game books written for instruction as opposed to other purposes, like historical and/or best game collections). Then I might ask “What about the reading the games don’t you find fun?”
One of the answers I get is: “I can’t stand reading all those side variations” or “I can do the main game, but I don’t have a second board to do the side variations and if I do them on the main board I have difficulty getting back to the main game position when I finish with the side variation.” Fair enough. But then I ask...
“When I started out and I read about 2,000 annotated games in my first three years of competitive play, which took me from unrated to expert (2000 USCF), what percentage of the side variations do you think I played out?”
Students’ guesses on this vary, but my estimate is I only read about 15% of the side variations. Then I ask, “Which side variations do you think were the ones I read?” The answer is “the ones where I was very curious as to what was to be learned from playing out the side variation or ones that looked like the most fun.” For example, if Alekhine wrote “This blunder lost immediately, but if my opponent had played correctly I would have won anyway, as follows...” and then he gave a two-page analysis, I would often skip this, thinking ‘I believe you, Alexander!’
Then I am often hit with the crucial question, “But if you skip those variations, won’t you learn less than if you had read them?” When someone asks this question, my first thought is the conversation between Professor Barnhart (the Einstein type) to alien Klaatu in the original
The Day the Earth Stood Still
movie:
Barnhardt:
“Have you tested this theory?”
Klaatu: “I find it works well enough to get me from one planet to another.”
So, my temptation is to say, “I found it worked well enough to get me from beginner to expert in three years and among the Top 25 Juniors in the United States even though I did not start playing seriously until I was 16 years old!”
But instead, I usually make the key point: “We are not trying to maximize the amount of unit learning you get from one game or one annotation; we are trying to maximize the amount of learning you get per unit time!” (This discussion was also covered in detail in Tip #17, which was not about fun, but just about learning per unit time.) In other words, if I get less out of each game, but could read that many more games and different authors, by maximizing my ‘fun’, I would read longer and learn more. It worked!
Let’s give another example about adding fun. Suppose you are not great at basic tactics and in doing them repetitively so you can recognize them faster and more accurately (a goal; Tip #91), you find the repetition tedious and not fun.
One sure solution would be to stop and give up! But maybe we can find a work-around that would make it more fun. If you are goal-oriented or like personal improvement statistics, you could either time yourself for problem sets and/or keep careful statistics about how well you did on each pass through the problems and compare your statistics to some improvement goals that you set. If instead you stop, this leads to another *Tip Alert*:
Perseverance is an important chess skill.
The ‘Tortoise and the Hare’ fable applies here. Players who can make things fun not only last longer, but eventually, on average, do much better. Making your study into a game mirrors a modern work-ethic model that says most people learn/perform better when the learning/performing is in a game-like situation.
Still another ‘make it fun’ solution by some students is to take a fixed problem set (like Bain’s
Chess Tactics for Students
or Giannatos’s
Everyone’s First Chess Workbook
) and make them into flashcards. This makes it more fun for some because they are not doing the problems in the same order and by removing them from the book form hides the tactical motif (pin, double attack, removal of the guard, etc), and makes it more challenging.
Sometimes the challenge of making helpful work more fun is meta-fun in itself!
Tip 78
Everyone has real barriers to improvement, but work to break down the artificial barriers.
For almost everyone (including almost all the readers of this book), chess is a hobby, and hobbies usually must take a lower priority to life’s more pressing needs: family, work, school, food, shelter, exercise, etc. Those often pose real barriers to something you might want to do in the hobby, like attend a tournament, go to a club regularly, play more slow time control games, or whatever.
However, when discussing issues with students what would help them improve their game, they are often resistant for reasons that I would categorize as artificial barriers:
• “I can’t play yet – I’m not good enough.”
• “I can’t play online – opponents cheat.”
• “I can’t play over-the-board – some opponents are rude.”
• “I can’t go to the club – they mostly play fast games.”
• “I can’t read
Pawn Power in Chess
because Kmoch uses archaic terminology.”
• “I can’t read that chess book because it’s in descriptive notation.”
• “I can’t play slow games online because no one will play with me.”
The list goes on and on! In each of these cases the student may have a legitimate point, but in none of the cases are these barriers impossible – or even difficult – to overcome, sometimes with just a little willpower or ingenuity.
In my Novice Nook column for
Chess Café
, ‘Breaking Down Barriers’, I wrote that we can segment the world’s population (and thus chess players too) into two types: Those that go out of their way to destroy artificial barriers that bar their success or happiness, and those that, due to some fear or resistance, take time to erect such barriers in many aspects of their life.
After that column was published one of my former students, a retired lawyer in Florida, dropped me an email that said (I paraphrase): “Dan, that was the best column you ever wrote. It applies not just to chess, but to many things in life. Long after you and I are both gone they will be pointing to that column and saying what great advice it is.”
I am not going to repeat the entire column here, but you already have a gist of what it says. Sometimes to get ahead in life, or get what you want, you must overcome these obstacles that seemingly block your way. You often hear it on television when they interview a famous person or someone wins an award and they say something like: “They told me I couldn’t do it; they said I was too x and too y, but I did it anyway. Don’t let anyone get in the way of your dreams. If there is something you want to do, go out there and find a way to do it!”
These people succeeded despite the adversities and naysayers, and not everyone who overcomes adversities and naysayers gets rich or famous. But if you let these barriers stop you, then you most likely will not achieve your goals.
Let’s take the objection about Kmoch’s archaic terminology, like ‘leucopenia’ for weak light squares and
‘
melanopenia’ for weak dark squares. He also has terms that have been superseded like ‘dispersion’, which we now call too many pawn islands or ‘liberating levers’, now known as pawn breaks or break moves.
When I read
Pawn Power in Chess
, none of this bothered me. I was not interested in what he called weak squares; I was interested in what weak squares were, how to recognize them, avoid them, and take advantage of them. I knew there was not going to be a quiz at the end of the book; I also had started to realize that some of these terms had not caught on among the chess world, so when I attended my club or tournaments no one was going to use them or expect me to know them. So his terminology was just not an issue; I ignored most of the terms while absorbing Kmoch’s wonderfully instructive discussion of them.
Imagine my shock many years later when I became a full-time instructor and many of my intermediate students told me they had to put down the book (
Pawn Power in Chess
is not yet appropriate for low-rated players who don’t consistently make safe moves or recognize when opponent’s safe moves allow easy tactics). When I asked why they could not read it, those who objected never cited any lack of good instruction; it was almost always because they could not get past the same archaic nomenclature that I completely ignored.
For me it was a barrier that I could ignore, and learn; for them, it was a barrier to prevent learning. When I questioned these students on why they could not just ignore the nomenclature and learn from the rest of the material, they just felt like they couldn’t read it. Very few of those, after discussing it with me, were able to overcome their objection; they let the barrier remain instead of finding ways of knocking it down.
This is just one small illustration of a barrier, but I think most of readers will be able to think of examples of their own. How we deal with these barriers will go a long way toward determining how much progress you can make, and how quickly and easily that will be.
Tip 79
Sometimes learning to be more consistent is more helpful than learning more about your opening repertoire.
One advantage I have teaching over 1,000 students one-on-one privately is seeing which issues and problems are the most prevalent, which are the most difficult to overcome, etc. Just as learning to find better moves by analysis and evaluation is paramount, and knowledge of openings, endgames, tactical patterns and principles is essential, being able to apply what you know consistently can be a major factor in a player’s ability to play well.
In a related example, I once asked players on Twitter “If you made a knowledge mistake, was it more likely something you did not know that caused the mistake, or something you did know that you failed to apply, or applied incorrectly?” The overwhelming majority voted for the latter. In other words, they often knew what to do, but in crucial, critical situations they often did not consistently apply that knowledge, whether it was to ‘have a better idea’ or just forgetting.
There are many reasons a player might be inconsistent (and just as many different ‘fixes’ that might be helpful):
• Bad time management – playing too fast or too slow for the entire time control, or playing too fast or slow according to a position’s criticality can cause major inconsistencies in the qualities of a player’s moves. Play too fast and it adds a large amount of randomness and error possibilities. Play too slow and later in the game you will either lose on time or be forced to play too fast for the situation, and ‘magically’ your play will deteriorate badly as the game reaches the latter stages.
• If you have a poor thought process, that creates a ‘hit and miss’ quality to your play. On some moves you might ‘hit’ on a good idea, but then two moves later miss a simple, necessary continuation. Another example is when you settle for the first acceptable move you see; sometimes that may be best or close to best, but other times that will cause you to miss obvious (in retrospect) clearly better moves.
• Stamina and concentration go hand-in-hand. There are many factors that can cause a player to get tired and confused. The player might not have had sufficient rest before the game, may not have nourished properly during the game, might be in overall poor physical or mental health, or maybe played the game at an awkward time when they could not be at their physical and/or mental best.
• Another issue that affects consistency is the ability to deal with pressure, excitement, unexpected opponent’s moves (or interactions), and other factors that occur during a game. Occasionally a student will explain a hasty move by saying “I got excited because I thought I was winning/losing and therefore forgot to check if my move was safe” (or fill in the blank with another bad result like moved too fast or didn’t see his other threat).
There is no one ‘cure’ for these inconsistencies. The factors that cause them can be a single, identifiable problem (which may or may not be easy to fix), but more likely can be a combination of factors which need to be identified, possibly prioritized, and dealt with in a reasonable fashion (it can take time and effort!).
They always say it only takes one bad move to lose a game. In the same vein we have discussed how important it is to try your best on every move (Tip #2). One way to help achieve this and avoid the bad moves is to do what you have learned, and apply what you know consistently throughout the game.
No one said that being a good chess instructor is always easy! I guess if all we need do is teach someone a new line in the Caro-Kann that would be much simpler, but most players’ path to becoming a better player is usually more complex than just feeding them information.
Tip 80
The best way to get a draw against a higher-rated player is to play for a win and if you fail, you might get your draw.
This was one of the earliest pieces of advice I was given as a beginner. I thought it made sense then, and I still do.
Many players, when facing a higher-rated opponent, try to obtain a draw from the first move by playing passively and trading off the pieces. They figure if there are fewer pieces on the board, then they must be closer to an equal position with fewer winning chances for the better player. They also believe that playing passively avoids complications and gives the stronger player fewer opportunities to outplay them when things are more difficult to calculate. But this strategy of passive play and wanton trading is often misguided for the following reasons:
• Fewer pieces on the board does not always translate into fewer chances to lose. I have often seen lower-rated players get into somewhat equal endgames with one piece and several pawns (especially rook and pawns), and then force the trade of that piece, thinking that it gets them closer to a draw. Instead, it often lands them in a tricky king and pawn endgame where careful calculation is required and the stronger player out-calculates them and wins an otherwise possibly drawn endgame.
• Chess is not game where passive play translates into a better chance to draw. I like to tell the story of a time I played some skittles games against someone who did not know who I was. He played aggressively and fairly well, but I was too strong for him and won three hard-fought games in a row. Then a mutual friend advised him that he was playing the Philadelphia area champion and he challenged me to three more games. In these games he played much more ‘respectfully’ (passively) and I won all three much easier than I had when he had been aggressive and had been trying to win.
• When someone tries to trade just to trade, a good player can make sure that does not happen unless the trade is at least equal or better for him. As one grandmaster once wrote: “There’s no such thing as a fair trade”, meaning in almost all trades of equal valued pieces, it is at least slightly favorable for one side or the other. The stronger player can take advantage of the weaker one who is too eager to trade and, in doing so, obtain some advantage.
All of this backs up the advice: Happy with a drawn result? Then play to win!
Tip 81
Almost everyone is a stranger the first time they enter a chess club.
The ideas behind this tip include:
• Don’t be afraid of going to a club where you don’t know anyone; that’s the way most of us start, and you have to start somewhere.
• Chess clubs are great places to meet new people.
• Getting better at something requires a modicum of proactive involvement and overcoming inertia.
• When you do go to a club for the first time, be prepared to deal with strangers and new circumstances, but be optimistic.
Of the above, the two that stand out to me, from my experience and talking with others, are overcoming inertia and being proactive.
I selected overcoming inertia because when I interview a new student and ask if they are a member of a local chess club, I sometimes get answers like “I don’t know; I mostly play online”, or “Yes, but I have never/rarely attended”, or “There is a club, but I’m not good enough to go there yet.” Humans are creatures of habit and if your habit is to not attend the local club, it often takes a little encouragement from a friend or coach to get a player to even attempt to attend.
Being proactive is important. Some clubs have active officers, but often a club is more informal. More than once a student has told me “I can’t play slow games there – they only want to play blitz.” If your club only plays blitz and you are looking for some slow games, you can walk out forever, but that won’t help. Less dramatic but also non-optimal is to ask the club officers to find you a slow game. I think the best approach is to ask the officers if they would not mind if you organized or found your own slow games among the attendees. Perhaps they have a website where they can put a note that a player is looking for some slow chess opponents. Almost all clubs will let you make an announcement that you are looking for slow games; you can ask if anyone is interested. Again, this type of proactive approach is likely the most productive
When I ask a student if he/she inquired if they could make an announcement that they were looking for opponents who would play slow games, the answer is almost always they did not. Then I suggest that the answer they receive might surprise them; someone might reply that they had greatly hoped to find someone else to play a more serious game. It doesn’t hurt to ask!
Until the advent of the internet, clubs, more than tournaments, were the lifeblood of chess play. In order to find others who enjoyed your hobby, it made sense to see if there was a local club where you could find a game. However, some of my students have been hesitant to go to a local club, and not just because of the pandemic. I hear concerns like “They are mostly unfriendly”, or “I want to play slow chess and all they do is play blitz”, or “I’m not good enough; I want to study more so when I go I won’t lose.”
All these concerns are understandable, but these barriers most likely can be broken. Some clubs are proactive about welcoming new players, but others have a more laissez-faire attitude. I suggest newcomers be a little proactive if they need to be; it is intimidating being the only stranger, but sometimes a little friendly conversation will help break the ice.
Eventually, if you want the club to do activities that suit you, you can run for a club office, such as Activities Director or even President. They are usually glad to have your help!
Tip 82
Learn to deal with difficult opponents; if necessary, get the aid of the tournament director/arbiter.
I have run into several students who say they do not want to play over-the-board (OTB), or stopped doing so, because they ran into opponents who were not nice (to put it politely). The problem is that these are some of the very same students who say their goal is to one day become an expert (USCF 2000-2199) or master.
There is clearly a disconnect because at the current time (it could change!), these titles are only granted for serious, slow OTB play. In other words, these players’ goals are not possible if they refuse to play OTB. Tilt! Just as a golfer cannot become a good player just by going to the driving range, but must learn things like course management from extensive playing experience, an aspiring chess player must learn to deal with a variety of off-the-board issues, not the least of which is dealing with unpleasant opponents.
Unpleasant opponents come in a variety of sizes and types. Many of my adult students go into ‘tilt’ mode any time they are paired with someone below age 13, no matter how pleasant or well-behaved these youngsters are. I tell them, “Don’t worry about what your opponent looks like, their age, height, or whatever; look at the moves they play on the board and respond accordingly no matter who or what made the move.” To be fair, some youngsters can’t sit still or talk inappropriately, but, with some exceptions, you have to deal with them much the same way you would deal with an adult doing those same things.
If you have an opponent who is violating the rule ‘you are not allowed to purposely disturb your opponent’, if what they are doing is not malicious or intentional, you can begin by pleasantly asking them to stop. But in the long run at some point you may need to stop the clock (assuming it is your turn), get the tournament director (TD), explain the situation, let them investigate and hear the other side of the story, and take appropriate action.
If the TD feels that your complaint is legitimate, he will (or should) take increasingly strong steps to ensure the opponent stops bothering you. Once in a great while, you may even have to go over the TD’s head to get a senior or regional director to take action. However, if you don’t complain and try to take matters into your own hands, unpredictable things might happen which can strongly affect the outcome of your game and maybe even your willingness to continue playing.
Every TD has a myriad of stories along these lines; as a player and senior TD for over 30 years, I have my share. At the World Open one year there was a college-aged player who was clearly trying to disturb and intimidate his opponents. He was paired with one of my younger students, who was only about 12 at the time. My student properly complained to the TD, who misunderstood and thought my student was being overly sensitive. The TD improperly told my student to not worry about it and try to ignore his opponent, not realize the opponent’s purposeful and malicious intentions. If I recall correctly, my son Delen, then a teenager, realized what was happening, grabbed a chair and sat next to my student to try to support him, witness further infractions, and send a message to the opponent that someone was watching his unethical tricks.
The next round the college-aged provocateur was paired with an experienced Russian-speaking opponent and tried the exact same antics. The Russian was not taking any of that and went to the TD and properly demanded the TD take appropriate action to stop his opponent’s misdeeds. By now the TD realized his previous mistake and the malicious intent of the Russian’s opponent and assigned not one, but two assistant TDs to sit and watch the game.
When the players got into time trouble the college kid purposely placed his piece in-between two squares and hit his clock. His Russian opponent did not move, but properly said “please place your piece on a square” and repressed the clock. The college kid did not adjust his piece, and instead again hit the clock saying “your move!” The Russian calmly hit the clock once again, indicating he was not going to move until the opponent made a clearly legal move. But again his opponent did not adjust his piece, but hit the clock right back a second time. At that point one of the TDs got up and stopped the clock, pointed to the Russian, and understandably ruled “you win!”
Normally the punishment must fit the crime and awarding a forfeit of the game is a big punishment. But here the forfeit was fully justified as this latest incident was just the culmination of what the college kid had been pulling all day. I would not have been surprised if this type of behavior had also resulted in a temporary or even permanent ban of this player from all future events held by that organizer.
It is worth awarding kudos to his opponents for not putting up with the poor behavior, not trying to start a fight or become mentally disturbed, but rather calmly turning the situation over to the TD. The TD doesn’t get as high marks for his failure to initially comprehend the seriousness of the allegations. In the end justice is not always meted perfectly, but the rules and the system are set up to make the event fun and fair and enjoyable for all. We do want you to come back!
Finally, it is also worth noting that online behavior is often worse than OTB behavior because online players can hide behind the online anonymity and the lack of physical retribution. Therefore, it is a little ironic that some players who play only online would be afraid to play OTB due to possible bad behavior!
Tip 83
Like poker, chess opponents often have ‘tells’ that may give you information about what you should do.
Chess is considered a game of ‘complete information’ and poker is not, so it is well-known that ‘tells’ in poker help give players more information. But in chess it is also possible to gather additional information, usually from the time management of a player or from some aspects of their move.
Let’s consider how this type of information might be obtained and interpreted:
A player blitzes out a bunch of opening moves and then slows down – this slowing usually indicates that they are usually ‘out of their book’ and no longer know exactly what to do. This could be a ‘fake’ and they just want you to think that, or they know what to do but they are no longer 100% sure. But, in most cases, it tells you how much they probably know about the opening.
A non-beginning player puts a piece en prise
or seemingly makes a terrible move – they are likely telling you that capturing that piece is probably poison, but, on the other hand, the move just might be a blunder. Either way it tells you your decision about whether to take or not is critical, so be careful!
A player takes unreasonably long on several non-critical moves early in the game – they are likely an opponent who plays too slowly and gets into unnecessary time pressure; therefore, a game that lasts many moves might be to your advantage (often more so than creating complications; Tip #84).
A player takes much longer on a move than you think they should – they might be contemplating some type of complicated maneuver or maybe the issues your previous made are more difficult to meet than you thought they were.
A player moves consistently too fast for the situation – they are probably playing Hope Chess and not really calculating whether their moves are safe, so be aware of possible opportunities. On the other hand, do not get overconfident and assume their moves cannot be any good; many of my students have lost games underestimating the quality of their opponent’s ‘too fast’ moves!
Of course, some ‘tells’ are more blatant than others. Garry Kasparov used to take off his wristwatch at the start of games and, if he achieved a winning position, he would ostentatiously put it back on, sending a ‘subtle’ message to his opponent!
Tip 84
Two mistakes commonly made when the opponent is short on time and you are not are: (i.) playing fast also, so they do not have time to think, and (ii.) unnecessarily complicating. Both seem ‘good’ for you, but
not
doing so is often even
more
beneficial for you!
Not all chess decisions are ‘zero-sum’. For example, if you have three candidate moves A, B, and C and you analyze A and B and don’t like them, you can’t automatically play C because it must be better – it could be much worse! This reasoning is analogous to the idea that complicating and playing fast when your opponent is short on time and you are not isn’t the best strategy. The argument in favor of doing so seems reasonable:
• If you complicate, you have extra time to figure out the complications (this may even be a great strategy if you are losing, but if you are not...).
• If you play fast even though you have a lot of time, your opponent who is short on time cannot even use your time to think, and is forced to play quick, less optimal moves.
I see players, even some experienced, strong players, use these two strategies all the time when their opponents are short on time. But since chess is not a zero-sum game, just because either of the arguments in favor of doing these strategies seems good, does not make them optimal is most situations. Let’s examine each strategy in turn:
Complicating when the opponent is short on time
If you have lots of time and your opponent has very little and you unnecessarily complicate just because of your opponent’s time situation, you are creating positions where either side can go heavily wrong much more easily. But you were
already
in a position where your opponent could go wrong much more easily due to his shortness of time. This has the bad effects of also increasing your mistakes (although with your extra time there should be fewer), and also shortening the game (since the position has a higher error rate).
But consider the following: suppose you are playing with a 10-second increment, it is move 25, your opponent has three minutes remaining and you have 30 minutes. You have two choices: complicate and get an explosive game that likely will be decided by the 35th move or just play solid chess where the game is could last 60 or 70 moves. Both are good for you because you have more time to navigate, but which is better? If you’ve played many endgames, you know they are full of critical moves which require careful calculation; by that time your opponent will likely be playing on the increment. Your advantage of not complicating (assuming those complications are not just tremendously in your favor, on the board) is much greater than your advantage of ‘fire on board’.
Playing fast when you have time but the opponent is short on time
Similarly, playing fast in your opponent’s time trouble gives both players less time to think. But unless you are losing, that throws away most of your advantage of having much more time. When your opponent analyzes on your time, it is not very efficient; unless your move is forced, he doesn’t know what you are going to play. Therefore, if you take your time, you are much more likely to analyze lines that could be played since you have a much better idea of what your move will be. If your move is forced, then play it quickly, of course. Otherwise use that time advantage!
There are circumstances when playing quickly if your opponent is low on time make sense, but many more where it does not. And in modern time controls, with big increments, it makes even less sense because your opponent will at least have that time on every move anyway. Just remember the logic that
just because something is beneficial for you still allows for the possibility the alternative could be even better, and if something is bad for you, it is possible the alternative could be even worse!
Tip 85
When you are way ahead it is a different game. In all sports, including chess, your strategy is much different when you are winning easily or losing badly than it would be if the game was close.
Tip #67 listed the six things I suggest a player do when winning easily. You can turn some of them around for what to do when losing badly. For example, if it is better for the player way ahead to keep the position clear and simple, then the player losing badly wants to make the position complicated (‘fire on board!’), because he/she has nothing to lose. In complications if the player who is losing makes a bad mistake they were lost anyway; but the player who is winning has everything to lose, so doesn’t want complicated decisions where they might lose material or get mated if they don’t calculate accurately.
Here is a recent position from a student’s game. He was White and ahead a piece. What should his strategy be?
White to play
White should castle kingside or even play 1.Kf1 and tuck the king away if he wants to keep his king’s rook on the semi-open h-file. Middlegames are usually much simpler when the kings are on the same side of the board. Instead, he felt like he had to keep the rook on the semi-open file and attack the black king; this is unnecessary since he is already winning! He could just play simply to trade the pieces.
Therefore, White played
1.0-0-0?
. This is just the opposite of what he should be trying to do. Now Black can attack on the queenside and make the game complicated. Luckily for White he won anyway, but I can tell you that many similar student games did not end so happily.
Since when way ahead you want to ‘keep the game simple’ (KISS principle), a follow-up to my comment above about king placement is (*Tip Alert!*):
If you win material before you castle, if possible and feasible, castle on the same side as your opponent
.
The reason is that castling opposite sides usually leads to unbalanced games where extra material is not as important, while same-side castling games, on average, tend to be more balanced and thus favor the player who has the extra material. If neither side has castled, castling first may be required for safety reasons, but it does give him/her the opportunity to then castle on the opposite side and get the unbalanced game they desire.
In the next example Black is ahead a piece and has unfortunately gone pawn hunting, allowing pressure on the d-file, but he can still win easily with some care:
Black to play
Black should just give White the e-pawn, e.g. 1...Rc8 (to get out of the pin) 2.Bxe5 Nxe5 3.Rxe5+ Kf8 and the extra material should win easily.
Instead, he guarded the e-pawn with
1...f6?
whereupon
2.Bc7
removed the guard on the knight and caused complications, which Black further misplayed and lost:
2...Rxf3 3.Bxd8 Kxd8??
(3...Nc5!)
4.Rxd7+
and already White is winning.
In our next position, both kings are exposed but it is Black’s turn and he is also ahead a knight. In such positions the most important issue is often keeping the initiative, while obviously not allowing the opponent to mate your king first.
Black to play
Black is ahead a knight and on the attack. He has mate in two with 1...b5+ 2.Kb3 Qb2#.
Instead he moved
