1...Nb2+
, played quickly, if I remember correctly. After
2.Kb3
there are no big checks now so Black needs to be a little careful, and wasn’t with
2...Qd4??
.
White to play after 2...
Q
d4??
Instead of 2...Qd4, Black had to ‘think defense first’ and stop the checks with a move like 2...Qh2. Even 2...Qe2 would work. The problem with 2...Qd4 is that Black is not threatening anything, which gives White time for 3.Rh3+ Kg7 4.Qd8! winning. But White also missed his opportunity and first played
3.Qf8??
.
Black to play after 3.
Q
f8??
Black can now offer to sacrifice the knight and mate in 9(!) with
3...Qxd5+! 4.Kc2 (or 4.Kxb2 Qe5+ 5.d4 Qe2+ 6.Kb3 Rb1+ 7.Kc3 Rc1+ 8.Kb3 Qc2#) 4...Rf2+ 5.Kb1 Qh1+ 6.Rg1 Qxg1+ 7.Ka2 Na4+ 8.Kb3 Qd1+ 9.Kc4 Rf4+ 10.Kd5 Qxd3 11.Ke5 Qd4#. All this is beyond most players’ pay grade, but in any case Black must do something to at least stop 4.Rh3+ with a move like 3...Qh4, but he clearly played Hope Chess with
3...Qf2??
and resigned after the obvious
4.Rh3+
with mate to follow.
When ahead, my main advice is to ‘think defense first’, which has nothing to do with playing defensively or passively, but simply to make sure the things the opponent can do to get back in the game are first prevented before taking offensive action. If you have a million ways to win and only two ways to lose, it makes sense to first make sure those two cannot happen – preferably permanently – and then you will be pretty sure to stumble upon one of the million ways you can win.
Tip 86
When you are winning, do not worry about the little things
Consider the following two positions, comparing the two trade opportunities:
Black to play
In the position above Black is ahead a piece, so forcing the queen trade with 1...Qh6 is a great idea to remove the opponent’s future tactical counterplay, even if it gives Black doubled isolated h-pawns. This is also in accordance with one of my six principles on how to play when way ahead: Don’t worry about the little things.
Black to play
But in the second position the material is even, so 1...Qh6? to force the trade unnecessarily weakens Black’s position after 2.Qxh6 gxh6. The engine rates the position as much better for Black (about -0.8) after 1...Nc5
or 1...c6.
Instead, after 1...Qh6 2.Qxh6 gxh6, White has the double attack 3.Nb5 which, on top of the superior pawn structure, is completely winning.
In the following game from my formulative years, I was White and Black has just played
1...
N
d7
, threatening to trade the awkward knight for the strong bishop and also double my c-pawns. However, all these relatively small concerns pale in comparison to White winning the e-pawn:
White to play after 1...
N
d7
Even though I was the lower-rated player (but not for long!), I correctly understood the relative value of the respective threats, ignored Black’s ‘threat’ and played
2.Rxe6
. If I stop and save the bishop Black easily saves the e-pawn. However, after
2...Nxc5 3.dxc5 Rf4 4.R1e2
I dutifully and eventually collected the whole point.
Many readers rated over 1600 FIDE might think “This example is too easy!”, yet I teach players almost every day who often make this kind of mistake because there is a value of ‘one’ to win a pawn, but no value for them to compare if their opponent doubles their pawns and trades their struggling knight for a strong bishop.
Tip 58’s final diagram contained an example for this tip, too. White was ahead a pawn. After 1.Nc3? Nxc3? 2.bxc3 gives White doubled pawns, but since White was ahead and would be trading an inactive knight for a strong centralized one, White should not worry about the doubled pawns that much. Of course, it was the reply 1...Nb4, not 1...Nxc3 that made 1.Nc3 unsafe.
Tip 87
When you fail to see a combination/tactic (either for you or for your opponent), try to understand why you missed it, so you can improve in that area and be less likely to miss something similar in the future
.
One of the reasons I hear quite often on why someone lost a game is they “didn’t see” something: the bishop that took their queen or the combination their opponent played on them, or the combination they missed that would have won. But there are many reasons why you did not see something and, if you want to improve, simply writing it off as ‘I didn’t see it’ will not help minimizing your chances to have the same problem recur again.
Let’s list some of the most common reasons you might not see something and then briefly discuss what you might do to minimize recurrence:
1. Failed to look for
all
the opponent’s threats from his/her previous move.
2. Failed to look for the opponent’s forcing moves (checks, captures, and threats) that he/she could make
in reply
to your move (common!).
3. Failed to look for your own checks, captures, and threats.
4. Literally did not see the piece that moved before it made the move (board vision).
5. Looked for his/her checks, captures and threats, but didn’t calculate that one correctly (analysis error).
6. Played too fast for the situation (another common one).
7. Tried to recognize/solve a tactical pattern, but was unfamiliar/unsuccessful (lack of pattern recognition/tactical problem study).
8. Got tired or lost concentration and failed to do what you normally do.
Next, let’s briefly discuss/link what you can do to minimize each from happening:
#1 often occurs when you erroneously ask ‘Why did he/she make that move?’ instead of ‘What are
all
the things my opponent’s move does?’ (Tip #56). It only takes one reason you miss to lose the game, so finding just one reason for a move that has multiple purposes may be fatal.
#2 is the infamous Hope Chess – Tip #65.
#3 is partly covered in several other tips and many books on how to spot tactics like Hertan’s
Power Chess for Kids
or my
Back to Basics: Tactics
.
#4 – You can improve board vision in many ways, such as playing many long time control games slowly on each move, playing over 100s if not 1000s of annotated games, and/or doing ‘Board Vision Puzzles’ (Tips #104 and #109).
#5 is a big subject; there are entire books on proper analysis as well as several tips in this book.
#6 – For time management suggestions, see Tips #34 and #35.
#7 – The purpose of repetitious easy tactics is not just for solving, but for quick and accurate recognition (Tip #91).
#8 – Stamina is a big part of playing consistently in long games. Obviously physical activities that keep you in good shape help tremendously, but so does adequate rest (Tip #30).
To summarize, if you can break down ‘didn’t see’ to the root cause, that often helps you decide how you can work to minimize that problem in the future. Good luck!
Tip 88
Transition to psychological ‘Game Mode while filling in the top of your scoresheet.
Occasionally I get asked, “How can I start the game with better concentration? I sometimes start the game and make a mistake before I can get into the flow of the game.”
There is no sure answer for this issue and, obviously, some players are better than others at focusing when they need to. Even playing ‘book moves’ in the opening requires concentration to make sure you are really remembering the line you think you are, and don’t accidentally reach out and grab the wrong piece.
But I do have a suggestion that often helps: When playing over-the-board, transition from ‘life’ mode to ‘game’ mode by carefully filling out the top of your scoresheet(!).
Sometimes when members of our local club show me a scoresheet, the only information I see on the top of their sheet is White: “Me” versus Black: “Joe”. That is not very helpful to a third party trying to figure out what happened in the game, or to the player re-examining his scoresheet many years later.
Instead, I would carefully fill in almost all the fields, including the name of the event, round, time control, date, and full opponent’s name. While doing that, I would breathe slowly and think “Time to get ready to play!” and start to lose track of the outside world and focus on the job at hand. If it takes about two minutes to do all that, no problem.
“Almost all the fields”? The one field I would probably omit is the opponent’s rating (Tip #71)! You probably would play better and make better decisions about playing for wins or draws if you did not take your opponent’s rating into account. Sometimes it is difficult not to know the opponent’s rating, but if you can wait until after the game, that’s usually helpful.
Tip 89
Chess ruts can often be cured by ‘filling in gaps’, a new mental approach, finding and fixing misconceptions, reworking your thought process, taking a break, or sometimes thinking ‘outside the box’.
There are two important issues within this tip:
• Ruts are different than their cousin, slumps. A slump is where you are not performing as well as normal and your short-term results have been poorer than expected. A rut is when you are stuck at a level below where you eventually hope/expect to be, and you do not know how to improve.
• Chess is not like math or other endeavours that are formally taught in a rigorous and logically building manner. Most improving chess players learn in a much more hodgepodge manner, often following well-intentioned but less helpful advice and/or absorbing material that is either too advanced for what is optimally needed or inappropriate in some manner.
When complex material is learned in a non-optimum (to say the least) way, what results is a player who often improves to a point where there are many ‘holes’ in their chess education; material they should have learned better earlier in their study, but now is either overlooked or discarded as ‘too basic’. In math terms, it might be like someone who understands how to do “2 x 2 + 7 = 11”, but can’t count past 30 and has to guess when someone asks what ‘1,000’ means.
Having taught over 1,000 students, I consistently run into this issue. Not only do students have a variety of ‘missing’ capabilities and knowledge to advance, but they are reading chess books of all different levels because they just don’t know better. A ‘school’ analogy would be that they are in third grade, but they are reading books meant for second grade, fifth grade, freshman college, and grad school!
I once saw a local young player, about 9, not one of the top 9-year-olds in the US, carrying around a copy of
My 60 Memorable Games
(!) by Evans and Fischer. This is a wonderful book, but it is hardly an appropriate game book for improving youngsters. I felt very sorry for this young boy, but it was not my job to seek out his coach and ask why his student had such inappropriate material.
This type of ‘hodge-podge’ chess learning is, unfortunately, more the rule than the exception. One of the results of studying the wrong material is that it leads to the type of gaps in abilities and knowledge that lead to ruts.
Got to any chess forum and you will see all kinds of advice on which openings to play or which advanced books are the best. But then watch amateurs play and you see the same mistakes repeatedly that cost games, such as:
• They make moves without checking to see if the moves are safe, then are surprised when the opponent makes a check, capture, or threat they can’t meet.
• They play too many fast/intermediate time control games and wonder why they never learn how to visualize well, or think carefully and critically.
• They play too fast or too slow and wonder why their results are so consistently poor.
• They make well-known inaccuracies early in the opening because they never properly reference all that opening knowledge they purchased.
• They miss basic tactics that cost them games against higher-rated players because repetitious study of basic tactics is ‘too easy’ for them.
• They never play over many (many!) games from instructive anthologies like my
The World’s Most Instructive Amateur Game Book
or McDonald’s
Chess: The Art of Logical Thinking
. Remember, I read about 2,000 games the first three years I played seriously.
• They never learn a good thought process or evaluation because those important skills are not widely taught.
• They do not activate
all
their pieces in the opening quickly, safely, and efficiently when they are out of book. They have a ‘better idea’.
When the fundamentals are not drilled in properly, it eventually leads to mysterious ‘ruts’ when doing more material appropriate to their level doesn’t seem to get the proper results. For example, if one cannot recognize (not just solve!) most of, if not almost all, the common basic tactics that frequently occur both quickly and accurately, then one will get diminishing returns from exclusively studying intermediate tactics. IM Mark Dvoretsky, one of the most legendary instructors, estimated that there were about 2,000 of these basic tactical patterns that frequently occur.
Getting mid-level players who are in a rut to go back and study what is to them ‘more basic’ material is difficult. Psychologically, they not only feel this is a step back, they also feel that the material is a waste of time because they ‘know’ most of it already or they can ‘solve’ things easily.
However, try the following experiment. Take a ‘stuck’ mid-level player, let us say around 1500 FIDE/USCF, and watch him play a bunch of quick games against someone around 1800-1900. Then categorize why they lost each game. What you will find is not that they made subtle positional mistakes and got outplayed (that does happen occasionally); more likely they overlooked simple safety issues, basic piece activation, made infamous opening inaccuracies, and/or played too fast or too slow. They need to build up their infrastructure!
So far, we have addressed ‘filling in the gaps;. but let’s consider other ways of breaking out of a rut:
• Finding and fixing misconceptions – sometimes ‘subtracting a negative’ is as powerful or more as ‘adding a positive’. I notice in my new students that many have long-standing misconceptions on a variety of issues, such as adding knowledge instead of increasing skills, overvaluing how ‘bad’ weak pawns are in many positions, the average values of the pieces, definitions of critical items like backward pawns or the bishop-pair, or dismissing entire openings just because they are not currently popular with grandmasters. Correcting a misconception can help bootstrap improvement.
• Re-working your thought process – players are never ‘taught’ a thought process; they develop it on their own. But this often leads to not only a poor process, but an ingrained one that may be inefficient, slow, fast, or otherwise deficient. Listening to (or reading how) strong players think or taking lessons from a coach who will dissect and improve your process can go a long way to getting one out of a rut.
• Taking a break – sometimes players try too hard and burn out. In doing so, they make it difficult to achieve the very thing they are trying to do. Occasionally I hear someone tell me they took a break to regain their joy in playing or studying, to assimilate many of the things they had been trying to learn, or just to let their brain ‘reset’ and get a rest. This doesn’t always work, but it should not be overlooked as a possibility.
• Thinking outside the box – sometimes a new paradigm, like the notes above about improving skills instead of adding knowledge, or subtracting some negatives instead of just adding positives, can be just what the chess doctor ordered. Taking a step back and looking at the big picture can be helpful. A new coach can sometimes provide that, but it could just be asking the currently coach to try a different approach, if he/she is open to that possibility.
One final bit of extra good news – many of the same suggestions which can get you out of a rut can also help cure a slump!
Tip 90
Retreating or moving a piece back to its original square is not a sign of weakness.
I have been quoted by another author for my saying “That move is a retreat in direction only!”, meaning that sometimes moves that move pieces backward can be strong attacking moves as well.
I have noticed that weaker players will often not retreat a piece, nor move it back to its original square, no matter how much better it is to do so. When I ask them why they did not, they answer is usually “because that’s where I came from.” This illogic often is applied to retreating a bishop along the same path it came from. For example, if a beginner plays an early Bf1-c4 and that bishop gets attacked, they will almost always move Bc4-b3 even if Bc4-e2 or Bc4-f1 is clearly superior. They just feel that Bc4-b3 ‘makes progress’ along the new diagonal, whereas retreating on the a6-f1 diagonal is either cowardly or makes no progress.
But good players do not think that way; they don’t care what any earlier position looked like. They just want to find the best move, and the history of the position is rarely much of a factor. As an amusing example, consider the Ulvestad Line in the Two Knights Defense
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bc4 Nf6 4.Ng5 d5 5.exd5 b5!?
.
White to play after 5...b5!?
I went to the Lichess databases, of which there are two: the master database and the ‘everyone’ database. In the master database the strong retreat 6.Bf1 was played in 80% of the games, with White winning more than he/she lost. However, in the ‘everyone’ database, the most popular move played over 56% of the opportunities was 6.Bxb5?, which lost most of the games. The correct 6.Bf1 was only played in a little over 15% of the games. Therefore, the ‘retreat’ 6.Bf1 was played 80% by strong players, but only about 15% by everyone!
Consider too Duda-Caruana, Bucharest 2023:
White to play after 17...
N
d5
From my experience, many amateurs would think “My rook is attacked, but retreating it to e2 is more active than putting it back on e1, where it came from; also, it might allow me to double rooks or play Qe1 and get a battery on the e-file.” They would thus play the inferior 18.Re2.
But the full retreat 18.Re1 is much better – it does not block the queen on the d1-h5 diagonal and it avoids further attack with moves like ...Nd5-f4.
Bottom line: if you want to find the best moves, hand-waving ideas like ‘I just came from there’ or ‘I don’t want to go backwards’ are usually not the best ways to determine a move. Forget what happened earlier or where the pieces started; look at all the reasonable candidate moves with fresh eyes and no prior prejudice.
Chapter Four
Tactics/Safety
This chapter provides tips that address what I consider the most important ‘on-the-board’ issues – determining if candidate moves (including, of course, the one you play) are safe, and whether opponent’s moves are safe. Of course, tactical tips include those addressing win of material and checkmate – how to study/recognize/analyze the patterns, and also some general tips on attacking.
Tip 91
*Top-10 Tip*
The purpose of doing basic tactics is not to just to win material/mate, but also to reject candidates which lose material/allow mate. Moreover, a principal goal of studying basic tactics is to
recognize
common tactical patterns, not just be able to solve them.
Playing safe moves requires the ability to recognize and analyze dangerous moves that the opponent can do in reply, and to make sure all of the opponent’s dangerous moves can be safely met next turn. The foundation for the ability to play safe moves is the study and recognition of basic safety patterns. Many new students think that repetitious study of basic problems is not helpful because they can solve them so easily. But the goal should be quick and accurate recognition, not just solving. Take the analogy of learning 6x7. You can add six 7s by going 7+7 =14 + 7 =21 +7 =28 +7 =35 + 7 =42. You can probably do that fairly quickly with a good deal of accuracy. But you can’t compete with someone who recognizes 6x7 is 42 instantly.
Moreover, if you are playing a competent opponent, your opportunities to win material will be limited, but if careless you could
lose
material on almost every move after the first few. Therefore, a majority of your use of basic tactics is to recognize/analyze when your
own
moves are not safe!
Let’s take a simple example. In the following game my student was Black and he had already misplayed the opening a little when he had to answer White’s
4.cxd5
:
Black to play
After 4...Qxd5, 5.Nc3 gives White a big advantage, but Black has nothing better.
However, my student quickly played
4...Nb4??
. When I asked him after the game why he played that move, he not unexpectedly replied that he was only thinking of the threat of 5...Nc2+.
But 4...Nb4?? is a combination of Hope Chess (not analyzing your opponent’s possible checks, captures, or threats in reply to your move to see if you can safely meet them), hand-waving (moving on principle or idea, and not based on analysis), and a silly threat (Tip #97). Of course, White can safely meet the threat with 5.Na3?, but he correctly played his only check
5.Qa4+
, won the knight and the game. The pattern of 5.Qa4+ double-attacking the knight and the king is common and basic.
I am not going to claim that knowing basic tactic patterns better would have prevented Black from playing Hope Chess and losing the knight, but it would have given him a better chance. You can’t just rely on your tactical vision to determine if a move is safe; you have to analyze your opponent’s possible replies of dangerous forcing moves as well. Knowing more tactical patterns is an aid to your analysis to determine whether your move is safe, not the sole determinator.
The following well-known position occurs in the Alapin Sicilian after
1.e4 c5 2.c3 d5 3.exd5 Qxd5 4.d4 cxd4 5.cxd4 Nc6 6.Nf3 Bg4 7.Be2
. If Black is not an experienced player, he might notice that the d-pawn has two guards and two attackers, but one of the attackers, the knight on f3, is ‘removable’ so a removal of the guard tactic starting with 7...Bxf3 is worth investigating.
Black to play after 7.
B
e2
If Black now makes a quiescence error, he could conclude he would win a pawn after
7...Bxf3? 8.Bxf3 Qxd4??
. But if so, White’s only check,
9.Bxc6+
,
would win the queen with a much more powerful removal of the guard after
9...bxc6 10.Qxd4.
When my son Delen was a beginning player, he fell for this tactic – twice! He did not calculate it correctly the first time, and he also did not remember it the second.
Strong players would not make this mistake not because they don’t usually make easy quiescence errors – they don’t – but because this is a well-known tactic and they would just recognize the position and know about the trap. If you are not a strong player, it is much easier to misplay this position if you need to figure it out than it would be if you just recognize the pattern. This is the kind of benefit you get when you study basic tactics repetitiously.
Which type of tactics are the best to study? The kind that occur frequently in games, and ones that win some material – a pawn, the exchange, a piece, a rook. That is why I recommend books such as Giannatos’s
Everyone’s First Chess Workbook
or Bain’s
Chess Tactics for Students
for repetitious study.
Interestingly, at the other end of the difficulty spectrum of tactical puzzles are endgame studies and composed mating problems, such as clever mate in two positions. In the Introduction to
Tactical Training in the Endgame
, author IM Lakdawala strongly recommends that intermediate players, even as low as 1200 FIDE, can greatly benefit from the imagination generated by even attempting to solve these difficult problems. He suggests these players incorporate these puzzles as part of their study plan.
In summary, I suggest that repetitious study of basic tactics, such as those in Giannatos and Bain, should be part of everyone’s study program until they are at least rated about 1900 FIDE. That does not mean players under 1900 can’t study more advanced tactics or other types of puzzles – as they get stronger, they should augment their basic tactics study with puzzles of a variety of types and levels, like the composed ones Lakdawala recommends or just intermediate tactics in a set like Coakley’s excellent
Winning Chess Exercises for Kids
– but it does mean that even when some puzzle sets seem ‘too easy’ those easy puzzles can still provide a big benefit.
Tip 92
Tactics and safety dominate positional and strategic aspects.
Another way of saying this is strategy is the tiebreak of equally safe moves. Of course, you can purposely play an unsafe move – we call these sacrifices! But sacrifices are more the exception than the rule. While positional and strategic aspects may often point toward certain candidate moves, if those candidates are not safe, they usually (but not always!) must be discarded, no matter their benefit.
I once had a student who was Black in a position that was roughly comparable to:
Black to play
When we got to this point while reviewing the game, I said: “OK, you are ahead a piece and should have an easy win. But your king is a little unsafe in the middle of the board so you should try to castle queenside. However, if you play a move like 1...Qe7? immediately, then White has 2.Qb5+ picking up the bishop,
so I would first play a move like 1...Bb6 and then move the queen and castle and you should win easily.”
My student replied: “Dan, I went through the entire same logic that you did, but I skipped the step where you checked to see if the queen move was safe so, for the same reason, I played 1...Qe7?? and after 2.Qb5+ I lost my bishop and ended up losing the game!”
Strategically it was clear what Black should do, but he had to first ensure his intended move was safe. He skipped the most important step of the process!
Tip 93
The forcing moves are checks, captures, and threats. So almost all tactics (except possibly zugzwang) involve checks, captures, and/or threats.
I have used the phrase ‘checks, captures, and threats’ (CCT) for so long and so repeatedly that it may only be second to ‘Hope Chess’ among chess ideas I have named that have caught on with the general public. CCT may have existed before I started using it, but I do not remember getting it from another source.
Amusingly, I used CCT so much in the lectures at our chess club that one of our members went to Café Press and designed hats with ‘checks, captures, and threats’ on it! Of course, if you wear the hat, it may help your opponent more than it helps you!
The functional use of CCT is very clear: either side cannot initiate a tactic unless the move is one of those three types. One problem I have found over my years of teaching this is that everyone knows what a check or capture is, but they can only vaguely describe a threat, so here goes:
Threat: A move which can win material, checkmate, or do something positive
next move
if the opponent does not stop it. Attacks on under-defended pieces are examples of a threat.
Note that a check or capture can also be a threat. Let’s return (from Tip #44) to the following ‘White to Play and Win’ position from Bain’s
Chess Tactics for Students
. The winning move was a capture, but can you find all the white moves that are
not
checks or captures, but can be categorized as threats?
Find all the White moves that are threats
The key to getting this correct is to understand that just as not all checks and captures are good moves, not all threats are good moves either. Here are all the threats:
• 1.Bb4 – this makes the strong threats of both 2.Rxe7 and 2.Bxe7+. Is it a good move? Of course not, since Black can counter the threat with 1...Bxb4.
• 1.Ba6 – this unsafe move has the clear threat of 2.Bxb7 winning a pawn.
• 1.Bc6 – also threatens 2.Bxb7.
• 1.Ba5 threatens the ‘overworked piece’ tactic 2.Bxc7+, winning a pawn.
• 1.Be5 also threatens 2.Bxc7+, winning a pawn.
Other moves that are not checks or captures are not immediate threats to do something positive
next move
. For example, 1.a5 is not a threat, but it does threaten to make the threat 2.a6 on the next move.
Similarly, 1.g4 doesn’t threaten anything; if Black does nothing, he’s safe, but next move it would allow White to make the Attack with something Worth Less (‘AWL’; see the next Tip, #94) threat of 2.g5, so it ‘threatens to threaten’. Can these long-term (‘threaten to threaten’ or even ‘threaten to threaten to threaten’ in three moves) ‘threats’ be strong moves which require the opponent to take notice or even take preventative action?
Of course, but we have to draw the definition line somewhere reasonable or else moves like the first move of the game already become threats as after 1.e4 we would could say White is ‘threatening’ 1...-- 2.Qf3 and 2...-- 3.Bc4 and 3...-- 4.Qxf7#!
The exceptions to the CCT moves initiating most tactics would primarily be endgame moves that either create immediate zugzwang opportunities or long-term promotion threats. When someone plays a strong tactical move that ‘threatens to threaten’, but does not immediately perform a CCT, we usually call that a ‘quiet move’. Quiet moves are more difficult to find for the very reason they are not the ‘loud’ CCTs and don’t catch our eye as easily, and are often award an ‘!’ by the annotator.
Tip 94
AWL (Attacking With a Lesser valued piece) is an important chess concept, and often a constructive threat.
There are often many ways to deal with a threatened piece: move it, guard it, block the attack, capture the attacking piece, counterattack. But when the threat is an Attack with Something Worth Less (AWL), concepts like ‘guarding it’ are removed or at least strongly diminished, making the AWL threat ‘more forcing’.
When AWL is used in the opening to develop a piece plus the piece being attacked cannot go to a clearly better square, this is the well-known concept of ‘winning a tempo’.
For example, after
1.e4 d5 2.exd5 Qxd5
we consider the AWL
3.Nc3
to be winning a tempo because the knight is developed from b1 to the good square c3, while the black queen on d5 has to move to another reasonable square (usually a5, d6, or maybe d8). To contrast, let’s say Black plays the inferior move
3...Qc5?
.
White to play after 3...
Q
c5?
Now 4.Na4? is clearly another AWL, but it’s
not
winning a tempo! In fact, we could consider 4.Na4 to be
losing
a tempo since the knight will be going to an inferior square while the black queen will be going to an acceptable square, say 4...Qa5.
But White did have a different AWL that
would
win a tempo. Do you see it? Of course, it is
4.d4
, which is a good developing move, and now the black queen will have to move again, and White is gaining more time.
To further illustrate this point, let’s consider two similar-looking move sequences: 1.e4 e5 2.d4 exd4 3.Qxd4 and 1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.d4 exd4 4.Nxd4 Nxd4 5.Qxd4.
The first line is the rare Center Game. Why is it rare for White to play this?
Black to play after 3.
Q
xd4
Answer: White does not wish to expose his queen to the AWL 3...Nc6.
But if so, why is the second sequence 1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.d4 exd4 4.Nxd4 Nxd4 5.Qxd4 also rare, but in this case avoided by Black? Because 4...Nxd4(?) 5.Qxd4 also attracts the queen to the center, but now there is no good AWL to win a tempo:
Black to play after 5.
Q
xd4
The missing knights make all the difference. In the previous diagram Black has 3...Nc6, but in the current diagram the only AWL to attack the queen is 5...c5, a move I see beginners make sometimes, but it doesn’t ‘win a tempo’ because moving the pawn to c5 is not only not a developing move, it actually has some negative consequences as it both blocks Black’s dark-squared bishop, makes the d-pawn somewhat backward, and weakens the squares d5 and d6.
Therefore, in the previous diagram where White played 1.e4 e5 2.d4 exd4 3.Qxd4 Nc6 Black is doing well, whereas in the last diagram after 4...Nxd4(?) 5.Qxd4 White’s queen is well-placed in the center (not developing it ‘too soon’!), and thus it is White that is doing better than normal.
Because AWL moves are more forcing than most other types of threats, it makes sense to prioritize them when looking for dangerous moves (e.g. checks, captures, and threats of consequence) for both attacking ideas on offense and safety issues, primarily when determining if your own moves are safe...
Tip 95
When looking for forcing moves (checks, captures, and threats) to either initiate tactics or keep the initiative, consider AWL moves to not only displace pieces, but as a key concept in trapping pieces.
Since checks, captures, and threats are the most forcing candidate moves and AWL is almost always a threat, AWLs are often strong candidate moves. If the tempo spent making the AWL (or any threat) is at least as worthwhile as the opponent’s use of tempo to meet the threat, then the threat is viable, but not always best.
To illustrate, let’s compare the early e4-e5 move to attack a knight in the Alekhine’s Defense (
1.e4 Nf6 2.e5
)
with the trap in the Vienna Game (
1.e4 e5 2.Nc3 Nf6 3.f4 exf4? 4.e5
). In Alekhine’s Defense...
Black to play after the AWL 2.e5
...Black’s knight is not forced to retreat, but can move to the central square, 2...Nd5. Note the tempo Black spends moving the knight twice is nullified by the tempo White spent moving the e-pawn twice, so neither side is gaining in development. But both 2.e5 and 2...Nd5 have pros and cons. For White he gains a little space and the black knight goes to another square (d5) where it can be hit with a further AWL (c2-c4). From Black’s point of view, the knight is perfectly safe and the advanced white e-pawn can be used as a target after a further ...d7-d6 break move by Black.
While Alekhine’s Defense is the not the first choice of most elite grandmasters, it has a respectable enough reputation that Fischer chose to use it in the 13th game of his famous 1972 world championship match versus Spassky.
On the other hand, let’s look at a similar AWL in the Vienna after
1.e4 e5 2.Nc3 Nf6 3.f4 exf4?
.
This is a common mistake at the amateur level. If you are supposed to capture in the King’s Gambit after f2-f4, why not here? Instead, 3...d5 is correct. White can now AWL with
4.e5
:
Black to play after the AWL 4.e5
There is one major difference between this position and the one previously in Alekhine’s Defense: the presence of the knight at c3 makes the centralization of the black knight with 4...Nd5 or 4...Ne4 unsafe, so the only safe knight move is 4...Ng8. In practice, amateur players who have fallen for this trap often temporize by first desperately pinning the knight with
4...Qe7(?)
, which only makes things worse if White counters with
5.Qe2
. Then the retreat
5...Ng8
is forced anyway, when White can get a big center with
6.d4
. Best play after that could be
6...Qh4+ 7.Kd1!
when Black is already in big trouble as moves like the AWL Ng1-f3 and a later Bc1xf4 are on the horizon; Black has extreme difficulty both making the queen safe and completing his development.
Compare this to White’s sixth move
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bc4 Bc5 4.c3 d6? 5.d4 Bb6 6.d5?
.
Black to play after 6.d5?
This AWL (attractive to beginners who want to ‘hit the knight and gain space’) not only doesn’t win a tempo – d4-d5 is hardly a developing move – but the space gain is also illusory; instead, White is just blocking his nice bishop on c4, removing the queen’s pressure on the d-file, and taking the pressure off the e5-square. Therefore, while 6.d5 is a candidate move and it forces the knight to move off a fairly good square, the net result is not that good for White and moves like 6.d5 should be considered carefully before playing.
Tip 96
The first important thing you need to know about a move is ‘Is it safe?’
This seems like such an obvious, simple suggestion/tip, but as a long-time instructor, I have found that most lower-rated players are quite eager to find out when their opponent’s moves are not safe, but not nearly so keen on checking on their own moves before they make them. There are three main reasons for this:
• 99% of the puzzles in tactics books or on tactics servers present issues from the ‘offensive’ view: “White to play and win”, “White to play and mate”, “White to play and mate in n”, but not so much “White to play and make sure Black can’t win”. I did produce some of that rare material; I wrote an entire book called
Is Your Move Safe?
• Offense is both more fun and natural. When you start playing it is a lot more fun to see if you can win your opponent’s queen or sacrifice yours for a checkmate than it is to make sure your opponent cannot do it to you on his/her next move.
• Offense is initially easier. It takes one less ply to see what you can initiate this move than it requires to first assume your move then see what your opponent can do with his/hers.
You want to know both if your moves are safe and if your opponent’s moves are safe. This is so important that several other tips are involved, which we will reference. For example, when your opponent moves, the first two questions you want to ask yourself are ‘Is his/her move safe?’ and ‘What are
all
the important things that move does?’ (Tip #56).
As for your own candidate moves, one of the first questions you need to ask is, ‘Is it safe?’ waiting until later to check the safety of your move with a Sanity Check – or not at all – is both inefficient and potentially dangerous (Tip #68).
Many lower and especially intermediate players do a superficial check to see if their move is safe by simply using their tactical vision to see if there is any danger. Unfortunately, that is only helpful, not sufficient. Instead, you need to visualize if the candidate move is made, what dangerous checks, captures, and threats does the opponent have in reply, and can you safely meet them next move. If you don’t do this, I call that Hope Chess (Tip #65).
When your opponent has a clear threat, the number of safe moves may be limited. In that case, one of the first questions you should ask is ‘What are
all
my safe moves?’ If you don’t see all of them, how would you know which is the best? Try that in this position; after
1.f4
, White is clearly threatening 2.fxe5. How many safe moves does Black have? List them.
Black to play after 1.f4
There are
four
safe moves: 1...Qa1,
1...Qb2,
1...Qd4 and 1...Qc3 (you can’t play what you don’t see – Tip #50).
When I test students many miss 1...Qc3 for obvious reasons: it doesn’t
look
safe, but, of course, if 2.Rxc3 then 2...gxh6 leaves Black ahead a bishop. 1...Qc3 is one of those invisible moves, like sneaky pins.
But in the game Black, even though he was ahead a piece and should keep things simple (Tip #85), tried to be clever (Tip #61) and played a move he thought was safe with
1...Kf8??
. Do you see why this is a blunder?
White can play 2.Qh8+ Ke7 3.Qxe8+! Kxe8 4.fxe5, winning a rook! Instead, White hallucinated thinking he also had a clever move and played
2.Rxg7??
, but after
2...Qxg7
White woke up and resigned due to this Counting error (Tip #99).
Tip 97
Do not make ‘silly’ threats that are easily met, hoping the opponent will not meet them correctly.
I define a ‘silly’ threat as one which is (i.) easily met, and (ii.) the move that meets the threat helps the opponent more than the move which made the threat (or hurts the opponent less than making the threat hurts the threatener). For example,
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bc4 Bc5 4.0-0 Nf6 5.Ng5
...
Black to move after 5.
N
g5(?)
...is a silly threat to capture on f7 because the simple reply
5...0-0
helps Black while 5.Ng5
detracts from White’s position. Here is another example from a recent student game:
Black to play
Black played
1...Nc4
, threatening the queen. But this overworks his own queen, which must both guard the knight on c4 and the pawn on d6. White can move his queen to a more central square, but the best way to save his queen is by playing
2.Bxc4 Qxc4 3.Qxd6
and White wins a pawn.
In the first diagram in Tip #94 after 1.e4 d5 2.exd5 Qxd5 3.Nc3 Qc5? the move 4.Na4? was a silly threat since this threat is easily seen and met, plus the black queen gains more (or in this case loses less) from moving from c5 to another square than the knight does by going from c3 to a4.
For a threat not to be silly, the move that meets the threat cannot be more helpful than the move that made the threat. If we turn that around, we get a powerful principle (*Tip Alert!*):
For a threat to be strongly considered, the move that makes the threat must do as much, or preferably more, for the player making the threat than the (best) move meeting the threat does for the opponent
.
In other words, making the threat must help you (or hurt you less) than meeting it does for the opponent.
When I watch games of low-rated players, they often make silly threats. They do not ask themselves what opponent replies would meet the threat or how helpful those replies are for the opponent. In many cases they just hope the threat will not be met (which I do not call Hope Chess – see Tip #65) so they can gain some advantage, even if the threat can easily be met.
Why do low-rated players make silly threats? Because when they are beginners and play other beginners, their opponents often pay little (or no) attention to what they are doing, so many silly threats are completely overlooked and the ‘easily met’ threat is instead allowed to be executed. For example, a piece attacks a queen, and the opponent will not see it and allow the queen to be captured next move. Allowing these silly threats to ‘work’ forms a psychological reinforcement that doing them was constructive. The problem is that even intermediate players can identify and meet silly threats, so, at the next level, that same strategy of making silly threats not only does not work, it usually backfires.
Is it ever correct to make a threat that’s even remotely close to silly? Sure (*Tip Alert!*):
If you are losing
it is often correct to make moves
that
give the opponent a chance to go wrong
. In those situations, you have nothing to lose by making moves that, if met correctly, could deteriorate the situation even more, moves that may not be best, or even good but if met incorrectly, can let you back in the game or even win on the spot. But if you not losing – or worse, winning – then making silly threats can easily deteriorate your position if properly met.
Tip 98
The average piece values are not exactly 1-3-3-5-9.
Beginners are properly taught average piece values so they can judge which piece trades are advantageous, fair, or disadvantageous. The common set of beginner values taught is:
• Pawn = 1.
• Knight and Bishop = 3.
• Rook = 5.
• Queen = 9.
I call these integer values the ‘Reinfeld’ values after prolific 20th century author Fred Reinfeld, a strong US Master who wrote many basic books aimed at the general public. My first chess book was written by Reinfeld.
At a fairly young age, I deduced the Reinfeld values could not be the exact correct averages (not that I knew better ones then...). Why? Because they are all integers, and the chances that the average value of each piece could so neatly fit into an integer multiple of a pawn’s value was too much of a coincidence. It would be like saying that everyone in your family is exactly 5, 6, or 7 feet tall and no one could be in-between, let’s say, 5’ 9½”.
For most positions and possible trades, the Reinfeld values are adequate, especially for beginners. But some students take these numbers as some sort of gospel, and resist when I try to explain that the actual averages are better approximated with some fractions.
When I ask lower-rated players why I knew the Reinfeld numbers could not be very accurate, they often mis-guess by saying that it is because a piece’s value depends on the position. While that is true, it doesn’t affect why the ‘average’ piece values can’t be too accurate if always represented by integers.
The fact that piece values vary with position helps underlie why we have terms like ‘good bishop’ and ‘bad bishop’ because the former is worth more than the latter in their respective positions.
However, the average values (over all positions) do not change; they simply get measured more accurately as we develop superior tools to measure them. In 1999 GM Larry Kaufman published an award-winning study ‘The Evaluation of Material Imbalances’, where he evaluated about 300,000 games by computer to estimate average piece value. Since then, GM Kaufman has updated his results in works like
The Kaufman Repertoire in Black and White.
GM Soltis has also written about average piece value schemes in the first chapter of his book
Rethinking the Chess Pieces.
As a rough summary of Kaufman’s results, here are some values:
• Pawn = 1.
• Knight and Bishop = 3.5 pawns.
• Rook = 5.25 pawns.
• Queen = 10 pawns.
• King is worth about 4.25 pawns fighting value.
• Having ‘the bishop-pair’ (one side has both bishops and the other side does not), is worth a bonus of about 0.5 pawns.
Notice I use pawns as the unit of measurement, not the popular ‘points’. Since under all ‘point’ systems 1 pawn = 1 point, it makes sense to me (and others) to use the tangible part of that, the pawn, as a basis, without introducing another intangible factor like points. Sure, a pawn can often be worth somewhat more or less than its average of one pawn, but in my opinion that’s not enough reason to introduce the abstract points as the base measure.
The piece values are used to determine the relative material balance on any board position, plus they are the basis for determining if one side or the other is losing (or winning) material on any sequence of forcing moves (Tip #49).
A very specific example of where Reinfeld values can be misleading will be discussed in Tip #123: Except in some deep endgame positions, two minor pieces are almost always worth more than a rook and a pawn.
Tip 99
When people see my tactic name ‘Counting’, many erroneously assume it is simply knowing Reinfeld average piece values (1-3-3-5-9). Instead, it is the potentially complex issue of deciding whether capturing sequences (on any squares) win and/or lose material.
Counting potential trades on one square is common and usually not very difficult, although weak players occasional ‘miscount’ and end up losing material when they think they should end up equal or ahead. But the more difficult ‘Counting tactics’ usually involve captures on multiple squares, such as when both sides have desperado pieces. Let’s consider a couple of example Counting positions, the first one being the less difficult:
Black to play
Black is in check, so he either must move the king or take the knight. If he moves the king, then 2.Qxf4 and the best Black can do is take the knight on the second move to get even, so it makes sense to probably take the knight first. If the ‘obvious’ 1...gxf5 then 2.Qxf4 and White remains up a pawn.
But the right move is the pseudo-sacrifice
1...Qxf5
when Black wins a piece; if
2.gxf5 Bxd2
or if the white queen moves, then Black saves his queen too.
In the next position, is 1.Nxd5 safe?
White to play
If you just count the number of attackers and defenders on d5, that won’t be sufficient because those captures are not independent of the safety of other squares. Moreover, just counting the number of attackers and defenders doesn’t work when the capturing pieces have different values. For example, it is less helpful for White to have his queen on d3 capturing first before the d1-rook.
More complications occur when Black at some point inserts the zwischenzug ...Nc6xe5, capturing a piece and attacking the queen and potentially overworking the c1-rook. Sometimes it seems only an engine can figure all the permutations. In this case
1.Nxd5?
is not safe, e.g.
1...Nxd5 2.Bxd5 Nxe5 3.Bxe5 Rxc1 4.Rxc1 Qxd5
wins a piece or 2.Nxc6 bxc6 guards d5 adequately and Black remains a piece ahead.
Just like other tactics, such as pins and double attacks, Counting is a concept which is not a tactic when there is no win of material. However, especially in games of lower-rated players, there are often Counting sequences that do win (or lose) material and these thus become tactics. Considering these circumstances, Counting is a fairly common tactic, but one that had never been given a name until I addressed it in a
Chess Café
column and wrote about it in my book
Back to Basics: Tactics
. I would like to thank GM John Nunn for ‘blessing’ me to use that name; I had contacted Dr. Nunn when I first decided to write about and name Counting and he said “it was as good a name as any.”
Tip 100
Use the Seeds of Tactical Destruction to determine whether it is worthwhile to analyze possible tactical sequences.
Long before Emmanuel Neiman wrote his book
Tune Your Chess Tactics Antenna
, I had been writing about the ‘weakened’ aspects of a position that would cause the opponent to think there might be a tactic. In my writings and my book
Back to Basics: Tactics
I called these the ‘Seeds of Tactical Destruction’, but Neiman chose to call them ‘signals’. Same exact thing.
While time will tell if either name catches on with the general chess public, the concept is powerful and important:
you cannot have a tactic unless something is wrong with your opponent’s position
. Just to make things more interesting, while it is difficult if not impossible to have a tactic without a Seed, it is very possible that the opponent has one or more Seeds, but there is no tactic!
As a very simple example, it is possible to have a very weak back-rank, but if the opponent has no major pieces (queen or rooks), a back-rank mate is not possible!
In his writings, GM Dr. Nunn has said that the number one Seed is loose (unguarded and vulnerable) pieces, so Nunn’s Dictum is:
Loose Pieces Drop Off
(LPDO).
A great example of LPDO at the highest level is the infamous game Christiansen-Karpov, Wijk aan Zee 1993:
Black to move
Black already has one hanging (loose) piece on h5, but he created another with
11...Bd6??
. Christiansen figured out the geometry – and probably could not believe his eyes or his luck – ‘retreated’ with
12.Qd1
(Tip #90), and Karpov had to resign.
The following is a common tactic allowed by lower-rated players, who create a discernible ‘Seed’:
Black to play after 1.
N
d5
Black feels the pressure of the pin and wants to alleviate it, so he ‘puts the question’ to the bishop with
1...h6?
What is the Seed of Tactical Destruction? It is the overworked (some call it overburdened) bishop on g7 who is trying to guard both f6 and h6. Therefore, it makes sense for White to see if he can capture either f6 or h6 in some sequence and end up ahead.
Clearly 2.Bxh6? Bxh6 doesn’t work because the queen is still guarding the knight on f6. Similarly 2.Bxf6 Bxf6 doesn’t work because it’s the white bishop on g5 that was attacking the pawn on h6. So the correct sequence is
2.Nxf6+
. Now the queen cannot recapture, so Black is forced to play
2...Bxf6
, removing the guard on h6, and then
3.Bxh6
wins a pawn. I see similar ‘overworked’ patterns all the time in amateur games – sometimes the white player sees he can win the pawn and sometimes when he doesn’t it becomes a double blunder (Black could lose a pawn, but White doesn’t see it).
What are other Seeds besides loose and overworked pieces? Pieces geometrically lined up for pins, skewers, discovered attacks, and/or double attacks/threats; weak back rank; pieces that have low mobility and might be trapped; an exposed king; letting an opponent’s pawn have promotion possibilities; etc.
Tip 101
A piece that is only guarded as many times as it is attacked (‘barely adequately defended’ or ‘inadequately defended/guarded’) can often be subject to tactics much like those that can be applied to an undefended piece.
Another way of saying this is that any piece attacked as many times as guarded, even if those numbers are both zero (not attacked nor defended), can easily fall victim to a double attack or other tactics. Let’s show two examples:
Black to play and win a piece
I have given this puzzle to many students and they see the bishop is attacked once and guarded once so many say the answer is 1...Qa5+(?), which could win the piece if White mundanely answers something like 2.c3? Nxg5. However, White has a defensive tactic that would minimize the damage. Can you find it?
The reason 1...Qa5+(?) is not correct is that White can use the tactic ‘attraction’ to play 2.b4!. Then obviously 2...Nxg5?? fails to 3.bxa5, but if the queen moves along the fifth rank, White will simply save the bishop. And after 2...Qxb4+ 3.Nbd2 White is only losing a pawn, since the black queen has been attracted to the fourth rank.
So correct is first to play
1...Nxg5 2.Nxg5
. Then instead of the piece on g5 being defended once and attacked once, it is attacked and defended zero times, but is subject to a double-attack with
2...Qa5+
, picking up the piece.
A tactic akin to this occurs in many queen’s pawn games when Black tries to get rid of his ‘bad’ light-squared bishop by pinning its counterpart before Black has castled, e.g.
1.d4 d5 2.Bf4 Nf6 3.Nf3 e6 4.e3 c5 5.c3 b6 6.Bd3 Be7 7.0-0 Ba6??
.
White to play after 7...
B
a6??
In this second example, we again see a barely adequately guarded piece, this time the bishop on a6 (attacked once, defended once) subject to danger. And once again the immediate check 8.Qa4+? is not the solution because Black has 8...Qd7 (8...b5 saves the piece, but drops a pawn), and White is not winning anything. If these examples teach us anything, it is that when the position is critical, we always must be careful! Therefore, the correct sequence is
8.Bxa6 Nxa6
and only now
9.Qa4+
, picking up the knight.
If Black wanted to trade off his ‘bad’ bishop, he should have played 7...0-0 first and if White continued mundanely with 8.Nbd2, then 8...Ba6 would be fine.
It was the famous (and eccentric) GM Aron Nimzowitsch, who suggested the concept of ‘overprotection’, where an attacked piece is protected more than it is attacked. One of the main ideas of overprotection is that it frees up all the defending pieces as any single one of them may not be necessary for the safety of the attacked but over-defended piece. However, a second benefit of overprotection is that it removes the possibility of the kind of tactics in our examples when a piece is only guarded as many times as it is attacked.
Tip 102
Studying ‘Removal of the Guard’ tactics generates exceptional returns on your time.
Having reviewed tens of thousands of students’ games, what I have noticed over the years is that the basic tactic most likely to be missed
(both on offense and defense)
is removal of the guard, with its sub-categories, deflection and overworked piece. Some call this motif ‘removing the defender’.
Why is studying this tactic so effective? I believe the reason that removal of the guard is a most commonly-missed tactical idea is because the concept is perhaps one step more abstract than the more mundane motifs, such as double attacks, discovered attacks, pins, etc. In other words, even though it is extremely common, it does not generate the kind of ‘Seeds of Tactical Destruction’ (Tip #100) alarm in lower-rated players’ tactical vision that causes them to spot this motif quite as easily.
When a student misses an easy tactic (either on offense or defense), I always try to get them to identify the name of the missed tactic, to not only generate awareness, but also help them identify areas of study. Removal of the guard came up quite often, and I made note of this.
About 20 years ago, armed with this information, I wrote a Novice Nook column for
Chess Café
called ‘The Underrated Removal of the Guard’, which also noted how often this tactic is missed and therefore the potential benefit from emphasizing it in study. About 4-6 months after I wrote the column, I received an email, which I will paraphrase:
“Hi Dan! You don’t know me, but I am an avid reader of your Novice Nook column and, when I read ‘The Underrated Removal of the Guard’ I realized it applied to me. So, I decided to stop playing in events for a couple of months and I got every book I could find that had a chapter on Removal of the Guard and I searched the web for Removal of the Guard problems. That is pretty much all the chess I did for 2 months. Then I went back to playing in tournaments and in my first few tournaments
my rating rose from 1500 to 1700!
I just wanted to let you know how much you had helped me.”
Besides being grateful for receiving such an email, my first reaction was ‘Should I believe him, sight and evidence unseen?’ If he had said his rating had risen from 700 to 900, then any serious chess study might have done that, so studying removal of the guard would not have done anything special. If he had said his rating had risen from 2100 to 2300, that would not have been possible if, for no other reason, 2100s are already sufficiently good at Removal of the Guard.
Finally, if he had written that he had studied the much rarer tactic ‘interference’, I could not see him going from 1500 to 1700 because that motif just doesn’t occur frequently enough to make that kind of difference. So, the bottom line was yes, going from 1500 to 1700 by mastering a common but often missed tactic was believable.
Here’s a typical removal of the guard problem from my book
Back to Basics: Tactics
.
Black to play and win
This is a typical pattern. White is threatening 2.Qxh7+, but Black can go on the offensive with
1...Qe1+ 2.Qf1 Bh2+
(removing the king’s guard of the queen)
3.Kxh2 Qxf1
. This is a deflection type, since the bishop check deflects the white king from his duty to guard the queen. Various removal of the guard patterns occur frequently in lower-rated players’ games, so not only does studying them give you some offensive chances, they also decrease the likelihood you will fall victim to one – assuming you are not playing Hope Chess.
Tip 103
A king is only as safe as the opponent’s ability to attack it.
A common misconception is that a king’s safety mainly depends upon the structural soundness of the pawns that protect it. While this factor is often an important one in determining king safety, it can also be meaningless in many situations.
The only thing that really makes a king unsafe is the opponent’s ability to attack it, assuming correct defense
.
Sometimes the safety issue may only be short term, or sometimes it is long term, but, in any case, it depends on several factors that affects the opponent’s ability to attack the king, such as, yes, the pawn structure, but also the relative power of the respective armies around the king, whether enough attacking force remains on the board, and specific ‘focal points’ (squares that the attacker can concentrate upon), which might be overwhelmed and vulnerable to attacking forces.
I like to use the following two ‘ad absurdum’ positions to illustrate this point. In each position it is Black to play and the question is: ‘Which king is safer?’
Black to play (Diagram 1)
The second position the same as the first except I switched the black pieces on g8 and h8:
Black to play (Diagram 2)
In both positions, Black has a pristine pawn cover around his king while White’s king is exposed in the middle of the board surrounded by Black pawns. Material is roughly equal; Black has two rooks and eight pawns for White’s extra two queens. If we use only the ‘conventional wisdom’ about pawn cover as a primary determination of king safety, then it is clear that Black’s king should be much safer.
However in the first diagram, despite it being Black’s move, White has at most a mate in four no matter what Black does, e.g.
1...Kc8 2.Qxb7+! Kxb7 3.Qc6+ Kb8 4.Qaa6 e4 5.Qab7#
.
In the second diagram, with just the one reversal of pieces, the situation could not have changed more dramatically, as Black has
1...f5#
. The overall structure did not change, but the single dynamic change to Black’s ability to immediately attack the white king made a 180 degree difference,
As an aside, when I give Diagram 2 to lower-rated students, the overwhelming majority start (or worse, only see) the vastly inferior 1...f6+? when, after 2.Ke4, Black is winning, but there’s still much work to do. This example alone says quite a bit about why these players are lower rated: they do not start by asking ‘What are all the reasonable candidate moves?’ (in this case, there’s only two of the necessary checks to start the attack), fail to heed Purdy’s famous tip “Look wide before you look deep!”, and even fail the well-known “If you see a good move, don’t play it; look for a better one...”; I usually add to this: “...you are trying to play the best move you can in a reasonable amount of time.”
Tip 104
There is no such thing as a ‘capture with the lowest valued piece’ rule!
Example 1: The first examples of this tip may seem too easy, but they are presented to make a point, so stick with me and see what you might learn:
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.d4 d6
. Which white piece should capture first on e5?
Which White piece should capture first on e5?
Of course, if White is to capture,
4.dxe5
is necessary as the first capture, since 4.Nxe5 loses a piece to 4...dxe5.
Example 2:
1.d4 d5 2.Nf3 Nf6 3.Nc3 b6 4.e3 c5 5.Be2 Qc7 6.dxc5 bxc5
. What should White’s 7th move be?
White to play after 6...bxc5
I suspect you got the correct answer is to win a pawn by capturing:
7.Nxd5
.
Example 3: In the following position, what should White play, and why?
White to play
Again, it is easy.
1.Rxd5
wins back the pawn,
while 1.Qxd5?? loses material.
From the preceding examples, what rule/principle can you generalize that would help a player find the right move?
The answer which superficially seems reasonable is: ‘When multiple pieces can capture on a guarded square, start by capturing with the piece worth the least.’
The problem is that
there is no such principle
, or at least you should understand that this ‘principle’ could only be applied in certain types of situations, primarily if the defending piece(s) are worth less than some of the pieces that might capture, but equal or greater to others. In each of the examples above, the first capturing piece was worth exactly equal to the lowest valued (or only) defender. But, in those cases the calculation is trivial (as it should have been). However, when the decision on which piece to first capture is not trivial, using this ‘principle’ will often lead you astray.
In Chapter Seven of his interesting book
Move First and Think Later
, Willy Hendriks discusses how one can develop/show false principles by using misleading examples: “If White advances with g4, block his aggression with ...g5.” He shows how one can provide seemingly typical examples to back up your ‘principle’ and mislead the reader toward false conclusions, comparable to what I just did.
The following diagram, Example 4, is similar to Example 2:
1.d4 d5 2.Nf3 b6 3.Nc3 c5 4.dxc5 bxc5
. What should White’s 5th move be?
White to play after 4...bxc5
When I give this and similar positions to students, most suggest that again 5.Nxd5 is correct. When I ask why, the two most common answers are: “Take with the least valuable piece first”, and “Don’t develop the queen too early.”
The problem with the first answer is, as we shall see, the principle does not exist; the problem with the second answer is that it does not mean much, since any principle with ‘too’ is almost self-defining and thus meaningless. For example, ‘don’t grow your business too fast’ or ‘don’t take too many risks in your investments’ don’t tell you how much you can do, since doing so below this unknown threshold may clearly be beneficial. In chess, if bringing out the queen early in a certain situation is good, then clearly it is not ‘too early’.
But neither of these objections mean that 5.Nxd5 is incorrect. They just mean that we still do not have meaningful criteria to decide. However, in this position
5.Qxd5 is much better because:
• The knight is already well-placed, and the queen is undeveloped, so get another piece into the game, everything else being equal.
• You do not want to pin your own pieces. After 5.Nxd5 the knight is not technically pinned to the queen, but it’s an awkward set-up where if the knight moves, trading queens might lose castling rights.
• Most importantly,
when ahead, trade pieces not necessarily pawns
.
The most important piece to trade is the queen. Here White is very wisely offering to Black the very annoying, and helpful for White, ‘Trade or retreat!’ option that is an important part of good technique. Black can’t very well decline since avoiding the trade of queens means that Black has to deal with 6.Qxa8. After 5...Qxd5 6.Nxd5 White is ahead a pawn in a queenless middlegame, is ahead in development, and has a better pawn structure to boot.
It’s not that 5.Nxd5 is bad (the engine evaluates 5.Nxd5 at about +2.5, but 5.Qxd5 at almost +6.5!),it is just that, unless you are a very strong player, you should learn to desire trading queens when you are winning (unless you have only a slight material lead and the opponent’s king is exposed, often a clear exception). Trading queens is an important step toward winning by attrition when ahead.
Black to play and win.
Hint: Black should not capture with the lowest valued piece!
The bottom line:
if capturing with something worth more than the least valuable piece is safe, then there are no guidelines; only analysis can decide which capturing order is optimum
. There are many, many positions when taking with the least valuable piece is clearly incorrect, so many that any ‘principle’ suggesting that you must would be misguided. Final Example 5: A student showed me this ‘Black to play and win’ position from one of his tactics sets (
see previous diagram)
:
Solution:
1...Rxe4 2.Rxe4 f5
, and if
3.Rg4 fxg4
(3...Bxf3?? 4.Rxg5). So, next time someone quotes “Take with the lowest valued piece first”, please realize this is quite a restricted (I prefer non-existent) principle. Following this advice in positions when taking with more valuable pieces is safe can often lead you to incorrect strategic – and possibly tactically questionable – decisions.
Tip 105
Kudos to Charles Hertan for creating the name ‘sneaky pin’ in his clever book
Power Chess for Kids
. Knowing the idea of sneaky pins can help your board vision; giving it a catchy name helps someone learn (and teach!) it.
Here is a position I give to my students to illustrate a sneaky pin:
White to play
Some students attempt to stop 1...Qg2# with 1.Ne3??,
but most get the point and eventually find
1.Qxg6#
, which is a ‘sneaky pin’ because it is not attacking the pinned f-pawn, but rather capturing on a square that the pinned piece is not really guarding. Notice how putting the white light-squared bishop and queen as far away as possible from their kingside targets makes the ‘board vision’ aspect tougher.
A good definition of a sneaky pin is: a move that does not attack a pinned piece, but takes advantage of its inability (or lesser ability) to guard a square. In the position above, it is the f-pawn that is pinned by the bishop on a2, so it is unable to capture the queen on g6.
These types of moves are often ‘invisible’ (difficult to see) for lower-rated players; sneaky pins are probably the most common type of these moves, but they are prevalent enough there is an entire book devoted to them:
Invisible Chess Moves
by Neiman and Afek.
Here is another sneaky pin, modeled on one I saw yesterday in an online simultaneous exhibition:
White to play
White played
1.Nxe5
as the pinned d-pawn can (unlike the previous example) legally take the knight, but if Black does so, he will lose a queen and pawn for a rook and knight. Instead, Black chose to settle for just losing the e-pawn.
I taught this type of pattern for years, but, to my dismay, never thought to give it a name! Once I saw NM Hertan’s clever naming, my first thought was ‘Why didn’t I think of that?’ So now I tip my hat to Charles and, when I teach this pattern, I never fail to call it a sneaky pin. Moreover, I can confidently state that my students remember – and recognize – it better now that it has a memorable name.
Tip 106
Don’t always immediately capture pinned pieces.
Let’s consider why immediately taking pinned pieces, even if it wins material, is often incorrect:
• The pinned piece usually cannot move and the pinning piece can, so you are trading a more active piece for a less active one.
• To get out of the pin will cost the defender a tempo, so wait until they spend that tempo and then possibly capture the piece.
• Instead of capturing, putting more pressure on the pinned piece may end up winning (additional) material.
What are some of the exceptions where you might capture a pinned piece immediately?
• If the defender has a defensive tactic that will cause the pin to be destroyed or useless if you wait a move.
• If there is an immediate tactic that can only be initiated by making the capture on this move.
• If there is some sort of positional advantage that may disappear if the capture is not made this move.
Here are some examples:
White to play
In this type of position, low-rated players think ‘I can win a queen for a bishop with 1.Bxg7+ and that is excellent for me so I should do it!’. But the queen is not going anywhere so taking it right away is not always so advantageous. In this case if White plays 1.Bxg7+? Kxg7 2.Ng3 Black can defend his h-pawn with 2...Kg6 and the resulting endgame up a pawn can be challenging to win.
But if instead White plays
1.Ng3
then Black must spend a tempo to get out of the pin with either
1...Kh7
or 1...Kg8 or even 1...Qxc3. But if Black moves the king, White will then play
2.Bxg7 Kxg7 3.Nxh5+
and be ahead two pawns with a much easier win. Taking the queen right away cost an important tempo.
In the following example, what would you play?
White to play
White can certainly win the exchange (i.e. winning a rook for a bishop or a knight) by playing 1.Bxf7+. But what’s the rush? When you see a good move, look for a better one.
What are other candidate moves? White cannot immediately attack the rook additional times because e7 is guarded and for the knight to get to e5, it would have to go through f3, where ...Bxf3 might be possible. But since White has a pin, maybe he should look for another one! How about
1.Bd5
, threatening 2.Bxc6? It is much better to win a piece than the exchange. The knight can’t move because if it did, the rook on a8 would be unguarded and 2.Bxa8 would win an entire rook. But no other piece can guard the knight, so
1.
B
d5
is far superior to 1.Bxf7+.
Let’s change things a bit; suppose we take away White’s tactic by starting the black knight at a7:
White to play
Even with this change, 1.Bxf7+ is still incorrect;
instead, White should put more pressure on the rook with
1.Re7!
.
This result is consistent with our tip; the rook will not run away, so the bishop is not only the more active piece, but the delay in capturing ties up the black army defending the pinned rook and (in many similar circumstances) might win more material.
Tip 107
Do not always immediately capture forked/double attacked pieces.
This principle is very similar to the previous one about not always capturing pinned pieces right away, and some of the same ideas and logic applies. So long as neither of the two pieces move, it may be more beneficial to wait until the opponent wastes a tempo trying to save one of them (assuming they are both of equal value) to capture the other. This is easily demonstrated when a knight forks two rooks:
White to play
Suppose we have a fork such as the above with many more pieces on the board for each side (material even before the fork). Then if White immediately plays 1.Nxa8
or 1.Nxe8, the other rook will recapture. But if White can safely delay, instead making a constructive move elsewhere and enticing Black to make a tempo move like either rook to c8, then White can capture the rook that did not move and win a tempo.
Such a position often becomes a game of ‘chicken’ where Black refuses to waste a tempo moving the rook and White responds by not capturing. However, White must be very careful when not capturing to make sure Black does not develop a defensive tactic (such as one of the rooks getting a check or a counter-threat), and then White does not win anything. When in doubt, just make the initial capture!
Another note is that if White does have the choice of which rook to capture when the other rook will recapture, it is almost always correct to take the
less
powerful rook! This seeming illogic is easily explained: if White captures the less powerful rook, the more powerful rook will have to recapture, thus moving the more powerful rook to the less powerful square.
Tip 108
If you are attempting a mating attack and looking for candidate moves, obviously you want to consider safe checks, but also sacrificial checks, moves that put your queen closer to the opposing king, and moves that bring an additional piece into the attack, even without check.
In the following position White has sacrificed a bishop for Black’s g- and h-pawns, exposing Black’s king. How can he best continue the attack?
White to play
Best play for both sides could be:
1.Ng3
Getting the knight into the attack and threatening Ng3-h5 in many lines.
1...Qe7
Getting ready to play ...Qe6 to defend the kingside.
2.a4!
The furthest away piece threatens to make an entrance with Ra1-a3.
2...Qe6
Queen trade anyone?
3.Qd2
Not in this type of position with the exposed black king and Black ahead on material!
3...f5
Weakening squares around the king, but giving necessary access to them for defense.
4.a5 Nd7 5.Bc4
Another piece activated.
5...Rf7 6.Ra3
Here it comes!
6...Rh7 7.Qg5+ Kh8 8.Nxf5
A third pawn for the piece.
8...Nf8 9.Nd6 Qe7
Black finally neutralizes the attack, but at the cost of a crucial fourth pawn.
10.Qxe7 Nxe7 11.Nxc8 Rxc8 12.Rxe4
The engine rates this position as +3.7.
Let’s start with a similar position, but change around just a few things and see how much that affects ‘best play’. The same principles apply, but lead to completely different moves:
White to play
1.Bd3
The bishop enters the attack with the threat of mate on h7.
1...f5
Forced to block the line.
2.exf6
Opening up lines for the attack.
2...Rxf6 3.Qh7+
Getting the queen closer restricts Black’s options.
3...Kf8 4.Ne4 Ng8
Black must give up the exchange (rook for minor piece). Instead, 4...Rf7 5.Qh8+ Ng8 6.Nd6 is even worse.
5.Nxf6 Ngxf6 6.Qh6+ Ke7 7.Re5
Not only clearing the way for the other rook to participate, but also allowing this rook to swing to the kingside in many lines.
7...Qf8 8.Qh4
Trading queens was also good, but in general you don’t want to trade queens when the king is exposed until the material situation is clearly in your favor.
8...Bd7 9.Rh5 Kd6 10.Qg3+ Ke7 11.Rg5 Qh6 12.Re1
The final piece has entered the play; although there is no longer serious danger to the black king, the damage has been done.
12...Rf8
Again the engine’s ‘perfect’ defense has averted the worst, but White is evaluated as close to winning with a +2.3 score.
I could have started each example with slight alterations that would have given White a quick win. For example, instead of the knight on d2 in the diagram, if it had been on c3, the engine gives the rook lift 1.Nxd5 (removing the guard of the e3-square) 1...exd5 (1...Nxd5 2.Qg5+ is mate in five after 2...Kh7 3.Re4) 2.Re3 as an immediate win. However, for the purposes of illustrating this tip, I wanted not just ‘play and mate’ positions, but rather more competitive ones where Black could defend, while White could keep up the attack by following many of the suggested attacking ideas.
Tip 109
Another good tip in mating attacks is to consider first making moves that block the opposing king’s escape squares rather than checking that king into those squares and then continue checking. If the lines with continuous checking do not work, sometimes the ‘don’t check but instead block the escape first’ plan does quite well.
Lower-rated players love to quickly follow the somewhat tongue-in-cheek principle ‘Always check, it might be mate’. But although checking is the most forceful type of move and many mating sequences consist entirely of checks, persistent checking is not always the best continuation.
Here is a clear example illustrating a not-so-rare situation when continuing to check does not work as well as first blocking the escape square:
White to play
White can continue with a series of checks: for example, 1.Qh8+? Ke7 2.Re1+ Kd7 3.Rd1+ Kc6, but that is just forcing the black king into the safety of the queenside and then Black’s extra rook will easily be decisive.
Therefore White should instead first cut off the Black king’s access to the e-file with
1.Re1!
, which threatens 2.Qh8# and leads to mate in five at the most, and that’s only if Black starts throwing away pieces:
1...Qe5 2.Rxe5 Rd1+ 3.Kh2 Rh1+ 4.Kxh1
any 5.Qh8#.
Tip 110
When doing an advanced puzzle or composition, keep in mind the ‘non-normal’ moves like castling, en passant, and under-promotion. These often form part of the answer, as they can escape the normal player’s board vision and make the solution more difficult or seem impossible.
The diagram on the next page is a problem composed by Karl Faber in 1950:
At first this problem seems easy: the two rooks should be able to do a corridor mate on the kingside. But you cannot place the black king where it is already in check because then it could not be White’s move.
If you place the black king on the f-file with the idea of 1.Rf1#, where would it go? If it’s too close, like f3, then 1.Rf1+ is met by 1...Kxg2. But place the black king any further away on the f-file and 1.Rf1+ is met by a bishop interposition.
White to play: Place the black king on the board and mate in 1
Therefore, without today’s tip the problem might seem impossible. But notice the position of the white king on e1 and the rook on h1. This placement brings up the FIDE Composition Rule: “If in a puzzle if it looks as though one side can castle, then castling is legal unless retrograde analysis proves that it is not legal.”
So that means 1.0-0 is legal. Does it help? It certainly does, since now the white king is guarding g2, so place the black king on f3 and then 1.0-0 is mate!
Here is a ‘retrograde’ problem by Dr. Niels Hoeg from 1916:
What was the previous move?
Black is in check, so White must have made the last move. But the checking bishop only has one diagonal, so it could not have come from another diagonal. If the rows and columns were not labeled, it might be possible that White is on top and the bishop is not on a1 but h8, when the answer could be h7-h8=B+! (under-promotion!). But since the columns
are
labeled, this cannot be the solution.
Maybe something has moved to discover a check on the black king. That can’t be the white king, so it would have to be the e6-pawn. But if it moved normally on White’s last move, it came from e5, where it is already checking the king, so the last move could not have been e4-e5+. Does that mean there can be no answer? No, my other tip was en passant.
Suppose the bishop had got to a1 earlier:
White to play
Only now did White play
1.d4-d5+ (1.d5+)
. Black responded
1...e7-e5
(1...e5)
then White played
2.dxe6
en passant, creating this position legally! So that is the answer.
For our final example, here is a mate in two; composition by Dowd (2011):
White to play and mate in 2
Getting a second queen on the first or second move sure looks promising, but another tip is:
In advanced, composed mate-in-2 problems, the first move is almost never a check
. If that advice holds for this composition, then 1.d8=Q+, the move most of us would play in a game with our clock running, is extremely unlikely to be the first move of the solution. 1.d8=Q+ Kc5 and there’s no immediate mate.
So, what’s next? Notice that if White does nothing (null move hypothesis), then if 1...Ke4 2.Qf3#. That’s probably part of the solution, but how does White move on the first move to mate after a defense like 1...Kc5?
