Then Qc6 would be the only mating square if it were guarded, but the only way to guard it is with
1.d8=
N. But does that work against other defenses than 1...Kf3 or 1...Kc5? Let’s check on that:
1.d8=N e4 2.Qd4#
or
1.d8=
N,
1...c6 (or 1...c5) 2.Qxc6#. Black only has five legal moves after
1.d8=
N and we’ve got them all: solution!
This kind of problem often proves very difficult for lower-rated players. When questioned they usually say things like “I never think about under-promotion” – but now you should!
Chapter Five
Positional Concepts and Strategy
The tips in this chapter address a variety of non-tactical issues involving assessing positions, finding good strategy, and implementing appropriate plans. Some of the tips revolve around the central issue of how to make your pieces as active/mobile/powerful as possible, both individually and in coordination with the other pieces. The tips also include other issues such as square strength/weakness and pawn structure.
Tip 111
Use all your pieces all the time!
It is strange that this principle (which I derive, if not steal, from Steinitz) is not more well-known. Amazingly, its corollaries are better known, such as:
• Find your worse piece and make it better (Tip #112).
• In the opening, move every piece once before you move any piece twice, unless there is a tactic (Tip #144).
• In the endgame, the king is a strong piece: use it (Tips #155 and #156).
Lower-rated players have trouble with these principles. They are lured by ‘having a better idea’, moving the same pieces repeatedly (grandmasters and engines do occasionally too, with a more discerning eye). But I have found that it takes a rating of about 1700 FIDE before players purposely ignoring basic principles can do so with at least a 50% chance of being right. Moral of the story: If you are a low-rated player, better to follow general principles than have a better idea!
In other sports everyone knows you always use the maximum force (number of players) allowed. In basketball, no one purposely plays with fewer than five players on the court. But in chess, lower-rated players are enticed by ‘early attacks’ and often do not want to ‘waste time’ getting all their pieces (often their second most powerful pieces, rooks) into the game.
In the following game a student, playing Black, managed to find attractive moves without ever moving one of his three most powerful pieces (queen and rooks). Hint: castling is considered a king move:
1.d4 Nf6 2.Nf3 e6 3.g3 d5 4.Bg2 b6
Probably better to get ready to castle by moving the king bishop first.
5.0-0 Bb7 6.Bg5 Nbd7 7.Ne5 c5 8.c3 Be7 9.Nd2 h6 10.Be3 0-0 11.Ndf3 Ne4
The engine is already suggesting that the natural 11...Rc8 is good, backing up the previous ...c5 break and a potentially semi-open file.
12.h3 a5 13.Nh2 b5 14.f3?? Nxg3
It is OK not to develop when you have a tactic, even an easy one like capturing an en prise pawn.
15.Rf2 b4 16.Nhg4 Nf5 17.Bxh6?
Now Black is too busy to finish development.
17...gxh6 18.Qd2 Bg5 19.f4 Bf6 20.Raf1 Nxe5 21.dxe5 Bg7
White to play after 21...
B
g7
Black is ahead a piece and won easily (I cannot remember when White resigned). To be fair, almost all of Black’s moves in this game were reasonable, but many of my students’ games are less so.
Of the first 700 or so students who had to be taught “move every piece once before you move any piece twice, unless there is a tactic” only one(!) was able to start doing it consistently from the time he was taught. Others, of course, were able to do it eventually, but only after they spent time ‘having a better idea’.
One problem is that when two weaker players play each other, and neither develops all his pieces, the game seems perfectly competitive to both parties, so they continue game after game in that manner. I learned a little the hard way too: once I got to about 1700 and was paired with 1900s, I found that if I wasted any moves in the opening, the 1900+ opponents started making threats and I was often unable to ever get all my pieces out. I was a little like the Little Dutch Boy futilely trying to plug the growing holes in the dyke. There is something to be said about playing strong enough opposition to punish your mistakes!
Another strong related tip is:
Make sure all your pieces have a future!
When deciding on various plans and pawn structures, make sure all your pieces can participate. Do not put knights where they have no path to become active, do not block in your (bad) bishops with fixed pawns, make sure rooks have access to open lines, and be willing and able to activate the king as soon as it is safe to do so. You can’t use all your pieces well if there’s no way to activate them.
Tip 112
Find your worst piece and make it better.
We first encountered this tip as a corollary of the previous tip #111,
Use all your pieces all the time.
If you are not initiating or preventing a tactic, you often want to improve the positions of your pieces, and you often get the most bang for your buck (tempo!) by improving your worst positioned piece.
I tell my students to think of ‘Find your worst piece and make it better’ as sort of a chessic game of leapfrog. In that game the player at the back of the line ‘leaps’ over all the (leaning) players in front of him to become the leader of the line. Similarly, if you can make your worst piece much better, it leaves another piece to be the worst piece next time, so your pieces take turns coordinating to continually better squares. Yes, this is an over-simplification of what often happens, but conceptually it is a good way to think about what you are commonly trying to do, sans tactic.
I know this tip is a big help to many students because, just as I am often reminding them “Don’t have a better idea; just move every piece once before you move every piece twice, unless there’s a tactic,” when they ask which piece should be developed next, I commonly reply: “Find your worst piece and make it better.”
For many amateurs, the worst piece early in the game is often a rook that is either left in the corner or castled, but not further activated (castling is considered a king move, not a rook move, too). But the worst piece in an opening could just as well be a dormant queen, a bad bishop, or a wayward knight.
Here is a recent student game where pieces vie for the ‘worst piece’ title throughout the early stages; my student was White:
1.e4 e6 2.d4 d5 3.e5 Nc6 4.Nf3 Nge7 5.c3 Ng6 6.h4 h5 7.Bd3 Nce7 8.Bg5 Nf4??
Deciding the game, but not ruining it for our purposes, as it contains good examples of this tip.
9.Bxf4 g6 10.b4 b6 11.0-0 Bb7 12.Na3
The knight becomes wayward.
12...Bg7 13.Qd2 Qd7 14.Bb5
This is not a tactic and the bishop was fine on d3; instead the engine prefers to make the worst pieces better with 14.Nc2 or 14.Rfb1.
14...c6 15.Bd3 0-0-0
What should White’s plan be here and what piece should he move next?
White to play after 15...0-0-0
This would now be a typical ‘castled opposite sides with queens on the board’ position (Tip #116), except White is already ahead a piece, so most reasonable plans win! Nevertheless, White should figure out a way to generate action on the queenside. The engine’s top move for starting this (and activating the worst piece) is 16.Nc2, intending if 16...Nf5 then the thematic 17.a4. Instead, White played:
16.Bg5
Another bishop sortie comparable to the one on the 14th move. 16.Bg5 is not a terrible move; Black is weak on the dark squares on the kingside. However, it is on the wrong side of the board. We could consider the knight on a3 to be the worst piece and the rook on f1 to be second worst. The other rook on a1 is at least on the right side of the board and if the errant knight moves then a2-a4 will nicely activate that rook.
16...Rde8 17.Qe2
Still not activating the worst pieces. The top three moves are 17.c4, 17.Nc2, and 17.Rfb1, all queenside moves and two of them activate the worst pieces.
17...b5 18.Bf6
Again the wrong side of the board.
The top three moves are 18.Nb1, 18.Rfe1, and 18.Nc2. Seeing the trend here about activating worst pieces?
18...Rh7(?) 19.Ng5
This move is, at least, a removal of the guard tactic.
19...Reh8 20.Nxh7 Rxh7 21.Bxg7 Rxg7 22.Nc2
At last! Virtually tied for best move.
22...Nf5 23.Bxf5 exf5 24.f4
White is now ahead a rook so he just has to be careful not to allow the entire board to be blocked by pawns.
24...Rh7 25.Ne1 Qe7 26.Nf3 f6
Helping White by allowing a line to open, but Black’s position was hopeless anyway.
27.Rfe1 Kb8 28.exf6 Qxf6 29.Qe5+ Qxe5 30.Nxe5 Rg7 31.Re3 Kc7 32.Rg3 a5 33.bxa5 Kd6 34.Rxg6+ Rxg6 35.Nxg6 c5 36.a3 cxd4 37.cxd4 Ke6 38.Re1+ Kf6 39.Ne7 Ba8 40.Re5 1-0
Tip 113
IM Jeremy Silman suggests that for a plan to be strongly considered, it must be both feasible (doable) and effective.
I am always quick to give other instructors credit when they have a good idea, and if they are excellent instructors, most of their ideas are going to be good!
As IM John Watson has suggested in his advanced book
Secrets of Modern Chess Strategy
, most ‘plans’ in chess are not very long-term grandiose ones that formulate in the opening and carry you to victory 70 moves later. Rather, they are mini-plans that suggest how to get something done over the next several moves, like obtain a knight outpost, control and open files, or get a pawn majority rolling.
But whether the plan is long-term and grandiose, or whether it is short-term and mundane,
if the plan is seriously to be considered, it should meet Silman’s criteria: feasible and effective.
If it lacks one or the other, then it probably should be discarded and another idea found.
I use an old friend as an example. He was a weak player who loved to attack his opponent’s king. Therefore, he would spend several tempi marching his pieces over to the areas around his opponent’s king, no matter whether the position called for such a maneuver or not (one of Steinitz’s rules is that you must attack where you have an advantage, but if you attack where you do not have an advantage it will fail to proper defense). Take a typical Queen’s Gambit Declined position:
White to play
I am making this example up, but it is not farfetched, having seen many of his games in similar situations. My friend would think ‘If I can get my queen to g3 and put my bishop on h6, then I am threatening checkmate!’ Let’s apply Silman’s two criteria:
Is it feasible? If Black absolutely wants to stop this maneuver at some point, he could just play a move like ...Kg8-h8, so that Bg5-h6 would not be safe. There are other ways for Black to stop White from setting up this pattern, but it is possible that Black might even allow it, because...
Is it effective? No! Even if White achieves Qg3 and Bh6, Black can easily stop the mate and White has no effective follow-up.
Let’s make some innocuous moves for Black that allow White to play Qg3 and Bh6, and then let the engine defend:
1.Nh4 Bb7 2.Qf3 Rc8 3.Qg3 a6 4.Bh6
.
Black to play after 4.
B
h6
Black can now easily defend with 4...Ne8 and White’s pieces are looking a little silly, since there is no follow-up. But even better is
4...Nh5!
which stops the mate, attacks the queen, and discovers a threat on the knight on h4. So even if White’s plan was feasible, it would violate Steinitz’s rule that you cannot successfully attack where you do not have an advantage (Tip #115) and would, not surprisingly, be ineffective.
Therefore, any time you have a plan, make sure that it is both feasible and effective. That does not necessarily make it the best plan, but at least it makes it worth further consideration.
Tip 114
Don’t give up something for nothing.
When you watch or study master games, the games are not always balanced, but the players attempt to trade imbalances of at least roughly equal value to keep their chances alive. Examples of these imbalances might include:
• A lead in development.
• The advantage of the bishop-pair (possessing both bishops and the opponent does not).
• A superior pawn structure.
• A safer king.
• Possession of an open line, like an open file.
• Control of an outpost square.
• A superior minor piece.
So when offering to give up one or more of these (and other) imbalances, strong players attempt, and usually succeed, in getting in return one or more others. If they see they can’t get an equal imbalance in return, they are not going to give the opponent an imbalance advantage unless they are outplayed and forced to do so.
But amateurs often give up these advantages for little or nothing in return, so that makes their games look quite different. For example, they may not covet or understand the advantage of the bishop-pair and may give it to their opponent for no real compensation. Take the following example from a recent student game:
White to play after 12...
R
e8
In this position White has slightly more than a normal opening advantage. The engine prefers
13.Bb5
, e.g.
13...a6?! 14.Bxd7 Nxd7 15.Bxe7 Rxe7 16.dxc5 Nxc5
with play against the isolated queen’s pawn (IQP), or 13...Rf8 14.h3 c4 15.Ne5 with about a half-pawn advantage.
Instead, White played
13.Bxf6
, giving Black the bishop-pair after
13...Nxf6
. When I asked him why he did this, he replied that he wanted to capture on c5, but did not want the knight on d7 to recapture there and hit his d3-bishop. This is a reasonable concern, but with better understanding, this is not nearly sufficient reason to give up the valuable bishop-pair to his opponent.
On average the bishop-pair is worth about half a pawn
, and
Stockfish
drops White’s evaluation after 13.Bxf6 from about half a pawn ahead to about a tenth of a pawn behind, meaning White lost the full value of the bishop-pair without any compensation.
In the following position White, rated about 1500, was in a book position in the English Opening against a lower-rated player:
Position after 5...
B
e6
Normally White plays
6.Nf3
here with a normal white opening advantage.
But White wanted to drive away the centralized black knight and played the anti-positional
6.e4?
, blocking the g2-bishop and weakening the d3- and d4-squares. Thereupon, after the natural
6...Nb4
, White has given Black targets on those severely weakened squares for ‘negative’ compensation and has no defense to the coming ...Nb4-d3+. The engine says White is already lost. He was so shocked that he quickly played the unsafe
7.d3?
and after
7...Nxd3+
he lost a pawn and the right to castle. A few moves later he blundered again and resigned. When I later asked White about this game, he said it was the worst game he ever played...
Tip 115
Steinitz said you cannot win a fight where you do not already have some advantage.
World champion (1886-1894) Wilhelm Steinitz is the father of much positional theory and strategy. Most of Steinitz’s theories are based on common sense – and even later, game theory – and have held up through the years.
One of his general ideas is that you cannot make something out of nothing; in other words, in order to attack in some area of the board, you need to have an advantage of some type in that area. The advantage can come in the form of more material/power in that area (often due to a lead in development), or opponent’s weaknesses like a wrecked pawn structure, weak squares, loose pieces, an over-exposed king, or really any of the Seeds of Tactical Destruction (Tip #100).
Steinitz also implied that if you have a lead in time/development and do nothing, then that lead in development will dissipate as the opponent has time to catch up in activation and mobility. It therefore follows that when you have some lead in time, you need to ‘attack’ (that is, take some positive action; it does not have to be an ‘attack’ in the sense of ‘attack a king’), to try to win material, checkmate, or possibly transfer your advantage to another more permanent type, like converting a lead in development into a superior pawn structure. This transferring of advantage was a central idea in GM Larry Evans book
New Ideas in Chess
.
If you must ‘attack’ when you have an advantage, then it follows that if you do not have the advantage, an attack is not called for, for it must fail against proper defense. This leads to the saying
a premature attack is doomed to failure
. We could extend this to, say, any attack where there is no advantage is doomed to failure against proper defense.
Jeff Coakley is one of the best chess authors and he summarizes a basic description of Steinitz’s theory in his section ‘Logic of Chess’ on page 202 of his excellent introduction to strategy book
Winning Chess Strategy for Kids
. Like many chess books that have in the title “for Kids”, it is
not
just for kids, but helpful for any improving player who needs to learn good, fundamental material. You can’t always judge a book by its cover – or its title.
Tip 116
When castled on opposite sides with queens on the board, the initiative is usually the most important factor and the strategy is to ‘throw the kitchen sink’ at the opponent’s king, and whoever gets there first usually wins.
A corollary to this tip is that material does not matter as much, at least less than the initiative, when both kings are targets (Tip #117). In fact, sometimes winning a pawn in front of your own king can be helpful to the opponent if it opens lines for his/her rooks and/or queen toward your king.
In castled on opposite sides positions, sacrificing a pawn in front of the opponent’s king when there are queens and/or rooks to take advantage is a standard device:
Position after 10.0-0-0(?)
Even though Counting tells us that the pawn break
10...b5!
is not safe since it is attacked three times and defended only once, whether White captures or not, Black can thematically use that move as a sacrifice to force open lines on the queenside and get the initiative there first, e.g.
11.axb5?
(better is 11.Ba2, but after 11...Be6 12.Bxe6 Rxe6 13.Nf3 Bb4 Black is still much better, possibly winning)
11...axb5 12.Bxb5
(12.Nxb5?? Ra1#)
12...Ra1+ 13.Nb1 Bd7 14.Ne2 Nb4
, threatening the smothered mate 15...Na2# and Black has a winning attack.
A good guideline for these opposite-side castling positions is:
If the overwhelming majority of the moves are being played on one side (kingside or queenside) of the board, the player whose king is on that side will very likely lose
(or be losing).
Game 2 of the well-known book
My 60 Memorable Games is
Fischer-Larsen, Portoroz 1958. After White’s 16th move, Fischer writes about his Yugoslav Dragon Sicilian position, which featured the typical opposite sides castling:
Black to play after 16.
B
b3
“I’d won dozens of skittles games in analogous positions and had it down to a science: pry open the KR-file [king’s rook = h-file] sac, sac...mate!”
This one sentence tells you three important things:
• One of the best ways to learn openings is to play many, many speed/skittles games to get experience (Tip #134).
• When castling on opposite sides, open lines and go after the opponent’s king as fast as possible.
• In castled on opposite sides positions, material is not as important as the initiative; Fischer’s willingness to sacrifice shows that he understood that purposeful material loss in these types of positions is not only acceptable, but a standard way of ‘opening up’ the opposing king for mating attacks.
The game continued:
16...Rc7 17.h4 Qb5 18.h5 Rfc8 19.hxg6 hxg6 20.g4 a5 21.g5 Nh5 22.Rxh5 gxh5? 23.g6
, with a decisive attack. In his book Fischer gives the variation 22...Bxd4 23.Qxd4 gxh5 24.g6 and now he writes that both 24...Qe5 and 24...e6 fail, which is true. However, Fischer therefore concludes that 22...Bxd4 is “no better”, which is not so because modern engines have found that Black can improve on the 24th move of that variation with 22...Bxd4 23.Qxd4 gxh5 24.g6 Rc4! when White’s advantage is only minimal.
Castling opposite sides often leads to critical positions with both sides attacking, and it is easy to make mistakes!
Tip 117
If both kings are heavily exposed, the initiative is often more important than the material balance.
Mate outweighs material, so if both kings are exposed and both sides have mating possibilities, stopping to pick up material and, in doing so, letting your opponent get a strong initiative by allowing him/her to check first can be dangerous, to put it mildly. Sometimes when both kings are exposed, a mating sequence that starts with a series of checks, even if sacrifices are required, may be the best alternative.
Here is an excellent example; I think it may have been from an old game by a student.
Black to play
In this position Black is already ahead the exchange and a pawn, but if he gets materialistic and picks up that ‘free’ bishop, his exposed king will prove fatal, e.g. 1...Qxh4? 2.Bc4+ (best to get another piece into the attack; Tip #108) 2...Kg7 3.Rg1+ Bg2 4.Rxg2+ (the rook on h2 is overworked) 4...Rxg2 5.Qxh4 and if Black tries to equalize the material with 5...Rxc4 then 6.Qe7+ Kh8 (moving the king to the sixth rank loses the rook on c4 to 7.Qe6+, as does 6...Kg8) 7.Qf8+ Rg8 (7...Kh7 8.Qf7+ again wins the rook on c4) 8.Qh6#.
So rather than win material, give some. It is better to give than receive – well, sometimes. Black wins with:
1...Rxf2+! 2.Kd1
(if 2.Bxf2 Qxh5+ or 2.Kxf2 Qg2#)
2...Qxd3+
(of course 2...Bf3+ wins too)
3.cxd3 Ba4#
.
Also winning easily is the sneaky pin (Tip #105) 1...Qe3+ 2.Kd1 (if 2.Kf1 Rh1#) 2...Bf3+ 3.Be2 Qxe2+ (3...Bxh5 wins too) 4.Rxe2 Bxh5.
Tip 118
The most common way to win a game is to use a tactic to get ahead on material and then win by attrition (using ‘technique’).
Studying checkmate problems is obviously very important because if you miss a checkmate, either on offense or defense, that can easily cost you the game. Therefore, you get a lot of bang for your study buck from studying them.
However, in anyone’s career the percentage of games they win by checkmate when the material is even (or they are behind) is very small, especially beyond the beginner stage. The overwhelming majority of games are won when one player wins material and the other player eventually resigns, or the player ahead on material increases his/her advantage by attrition (making fair trades = simplifying) until the advantage is so great he/she can either checkmate, or promote a pawn to a queen and then use the queen to checkmate.
The fact that most games are won by first winning material, even a pawn, is one reason I like to initially emphasize tactical problem sets that contain mostly (but not all!) problems that win material over ones that have a high percentage of checkmate problems. Sacrificing your queen for a pretty checkmate gets high marks for fun and helps sell a considerable number of tactics books and tactical software. However, the number of serious games you will win in your lifetime with a spectacular queen sacrifice from an otherwise even-material position is quite small compared to the number of games you will (or should) win once you use a tactic to get ahead on material.
We call winning a won game ‘technique’. The better technique you have and the more your opponents perceive your technique, the earlier they will resign. While some of the tips in this book (#67, 85, 86, and 119) deal directly with how to play when way ahead (a well-known principle is ‘When ahead in material, make fair trades of pieces, but not necessarily pawns’), a host of other tips and principles also indirectly help, as does both inexact and exact endgame knowledge.
An ‘exact’ endgame is one with only a few pieces on the board where a standard technique is used to achieve an aim like win or draw. Examples would be how to checkmate with a king and queen versus a king or whether a king and pawn versus king position is a win or draw, and how to achieve that result.
Two of the tactics books that I think have (i.) the proper balance of problem for winning material to those that pursue checkmate, (ii.) a consistent level for post-beginner study, and (iii.) positions that commonly occur in games, and are thus more effective to study are Giannatos’s
Everyone’s First Chess Workbook
and Bain’s
Chess Tactics for Students
. Of course, there are others, but those are the first two I recommend.
Tip 119
When you are way ahead in a game, the ‘trade or retreat’ idea is helpful.
For example, if you are ahead a pawn and an opponent’s rook controls an open file, you can often safely put a rook on that file and offer a trade. He either must trade (good for you), or give you the file (retreating; also good).
The following position illustrates this:
White to play
I think most players would realize they can successfully play
1.Rc1
, putting Black between a rock – moving the rook off the c-file and ceding entry to White – and a hard place, trading rooks into a clearly lost king and pawn endgame.
Although this example is easy to understand, similar logic could have been applied if there were more pieces on the board and, when White challenged the file, Black was not facing a lost king and pawn endgame. So unless White was offering a trade into, say, a bishop of opposite colors endgame, with most material advantages a move similar to 1.Rc1 above would still be the right idea.
Although opposing on a file is a typical and easy example, many other trade offers when ahead on material illustrate this ‘trade or retreat’ idea. The key is that the opponent has no wonderful place to move when avoiding the trade; if he/she did, it would no longer constitute a retreat!
Here is an example with a centralizing queen instead of a rook:
Black to play
White is double-attacking the pawns on c4 and a7, but the ‘trade or retreat’ answer
1...Qc5
not only protects all the pawns, but either forces White’s queen to a less advantageous position or a trade into a rather futile bishop of the same color endgame down a pawn.
Now an example where the option is not a retreat, so the idea does not work:
Black to play
Black is winning easily and White is triple-attacking a7, c3 and e5. Black can centralize and save all with 1...Qd4??, but then 2.Qh6 threatening an unstoppable mate is not a retreat!
Instead, Black should play 1...Kh8,
1...Nd5
or 1...Qf5.
Tip 120
The value of a tempo varies greatly. At the start of a game, it could be worth around one-third of a pawn. But in complicated positions it may be worth a queen!
Suppose you gave someone odds that at any point in the game, they get an extra tempo; they could insert one extra move. How much would that handicap be worth? At least a queen? In many positions one could just use a knight or bishop, attack the queen, and then capture it with the extra tempo. So, in those cases the extra tempo is worth at least a queen minus a minor piece, if not more.
Compare that with giving White an extra tempo but only on the first move, when the pieces are not in conflict. Then White could start the handicapped game with both moves 1.e4 and 2.d4. That is a nice advantage, but nothing like the handicap of taking the extra move at
any
point in the game.
The question of an early tempo’s value is an issue at the start of the game when one player, usually White, offers a gambit. The gambit is often in the form of offering a pawn in return for some gain in development/time. White often gets about two tempi and open lines and, in many, the engine evaluates that as about even. Normally White is ahead around 0.3 pawns, so even is not quite enough compensation, hence the approximate one-third pawn value of a tempo at the start.
In the Morra Gambit Accepted
1.e4 c5 2.d4 cxd4 3.c3 dxc3 4.Nxc3
...
Black to play after 4.
N
xc3
...White has about two tempi for the pawn with some open lines. The engine evaluates this as about -0.2 in Black’s favor, instead of White’s normal +0.3.
Now let’s take the other extreme, an example which also is an excellent demonstration of the difference between static and dynamic evaluation:
Flip a coin to determine whose move it is
When I ask students who is winning this position, they all reasonably answer either “The side whose move it is” or “Whose move is it?”
In response, I reply, “We don’t know. We are going to flip a fair coin to determine whose move it is.” Then students correctly conclude that the chances are even; we could bet equally on either side as the odds are 50-50.
Therefore,
statically
the chances are the same, but
dynamically
one side or the other must be winning – a little like Schrödinger’s Cat; it is either alive or dead, but we don’t know which.
In the previous example, whoever had the move was winning. The opposite case, where whoever has the move is losing (i.e. in a sense that tempo is worth absolute negative value), occurs in a mutual zugzwang position like the Trebuchet:
Trebuchet: the tempo has absolute negative value – whoever is to play loses
We have shown cases where having the move loses and having the wins. Obviously we could also show many dead drawn endgame positions where it doesn’t matter whose move it is; the tempo has no positive or negative value, such as this one with bishops of the opposite color:
Either side to move: draw
No matter who is to move, any safe move draws easily. In this case, unlike the others, the tempo has no value.
Tip 121
A piece is usually worth more than three pawns except in the endgame.
One way to test this is to offer the following challenge: from the starting position, let Black take off whatever three white pawns he/she feels is most advantageous to him/her, but he/she should remove the knight or bishop he/she feels is the most advantageous (for Black) to give up. For example, suppose Black says White should remove his c-, f-, and g-pawns and he will remove his queen’s knight on b8.
A normal white advantage is (using
Stockfish
) about 0.2-0.3 pawns for White. If the three pawns are actually worth the queen’s knight and we have removed the pawns that help Black the most, White’s advantage should be 0.2 or less, but let’s run it for a minute and see...
White to play: evaluation?
At depth 33, White’s advantage is +1.21, about a pawn more than normal and on the verge of a forced win.
Why is the piece worth so much more than the three pawns at the start of a game?
• The ‘Reinfeld’ average value of a knight or bishop as three pawns (Tip #98) is a little conservative; it is fractionally higher.
• Early in the game, an extra piece has the entire game to help gang up with the other pieces on the superior piece side to win pawns.
• Pawns gain in power in the endgame when they can threaten to promote. In the opening they can control space, but an extra piece more than compensates for that, in most cases.
There are not too many ‘book’ openings where one side ‘sacrifices’ a piece for three pawns, and they usually only do so if they can get some other compensation, such as a lead in development, a massive center, better piece coordination, etc. It happens occasionally in the Sicilian (e.g. the Najdorf variation) when Black plays ...b7-b5 and White sacrifices on b5 and then wins the d6-pawn. Giving up the piece for three pawns is even more advantageous in lines such as
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 d6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 Nf6 5.Nc3 a6 6.Bc4 e6 7.Bb3 Nbd7 8.0-0 Be7??
.
White to play after 8...
B
e7??
9.Bxe6!
A stock sacrifice. Taking with the knight is much weaker.
9...fxe6
This isn’t even best! Black’s position is so poor he should just concede the pawn with the meek 9...0-0.
10.Nxe6 Qb6 11.Nxg7+ Kf7 12.Nf5
Black has the extra piece for the three pawns, but the kingside is wrecked and the king will have nowhere to hide for the middlegame. With perfect play, White is already winning.
Here is a piece for three pawns line from the French Defense:
1.e4 e6 2.d4 d5 3.Nc3 Nf6 4.e5 Nfd7 5.f4 c5 6.Nf3 Nc6 7.Be3 Qb6 8.Na4 Qa5+ 9.c3 cxd4?!
(probably better is 9...b6 with some advantage to White)
10.b4! Nxb4 11.cxb4 Bxb4+ 12.Bd2 Bxd2+ 13.Nxd2
.
Black to play after 13.
N
xd2
Black has three pawns for the piece, but White’s advantage is about a full pawn, much more than a normal opening plus.
In summary, a random three pawns are rarely as good as an extra piece early in the game; in the deep endgame it could be quite the other way around, as promotion tactics become predominant. It is rarely true that an interesting trade balance is
always
good for one side or the other – the evaluation is position dependent. In this case the side with the three pawns can have the advantage when supported with additional compensation, but that is more the exception than the rule. In general, if it is not an endgame, take the extra piece!
Tip 122
Knights often have two primary ideas: to support squares (tactics, control, etc) from their current position, or to move toward favorable destination square(s) through a feasible path. That is why I call knights ‘path pieces’ because, unlike the fast pieces which can zoom through squares, knights need a viable path to get places.
‘You can’t get there from here’ is often a humorous idea involving travel or destination planning, but it is no joke when it applies to knight maneuvers. Sometimes it is possible for a knight to get from its current position to a more desirable one, and sometimes it is not, for various reasons. The path may literally be blocked, possibly by pawns, or it may take so long to get there that the purpose of going to the square would then be lost.
IM Silman once wrote that for a plan to be strong enough that you might want to execute it, it should meet two criteria: it has to be both feasible and effective (Tip #113). This would also apply to knight maneuvers: is it possible for a knight to get somewhere and, if so, would it be worth the tempi it takes for that to happen? Let’s consider the Botvinnik System in the English,
1.c4 c5 2.g3 g6 3.Bg2 Bg7 4.Nc3 Nc6 5.e4 d6 6.N1e2 Nf6 7.0-0 0-0 8.d3
:
Black to play
If Black plays 8...Nd4, then simply 9.Nxd4 cxd4 10.Nd5 and White is fine, as the weak d4-square can no longer be occupied by black pieces. So sometimes Black tries to get a second knight to cover d4! How to do this? If White falls asleep it will be ...Nf6-e8-c7-e6-d4! How can White counter? If immediately 8...Ne8 then White plays 9.Be3 and d3-d4. Therefore, that Black maneuver is only somewhat feasible since White can interfere by getting in a timely d3-d4. If not, it can be effective!
If you cannot see these knight maneuvers easily, you might try a knight ‘maze’. In the following position, keep moving the knight (to empty squares, of course!) until it captures the king:
Move only the knight to capture the king
The solution is: N
f3-g1-h3-f2-h1-g3-h5-f6-h7-f8-g6-h8-f7-d8-b7-c5-a6-b4-a2-c3-b1-a3-c4-xe3!
Or you can Google ‘chess knight mazes’ and find some different types of mazes online to improve your ‘knight vision’.
Tip 123
Except in some deep endgame positions, two minor pieces are almost always worth more than a rook and a pawn
.
This is a special case within Tip #98, which stated that the average values of the pieces are not exactly 1-3-3-5-9.
I once read that Michael Tal had said that he sometimes spent time looking for positions where ‘sacrificing; two pieces for a rook and a pawn was good. Why? Because it was so well known among strong players that two pieces were better than a rook and a pawn, that his opponent would almost always dismiss that possibility and let him give up the two pieces for the rook and pawn on the rare cases when that trade was favorable for the rook and pawn!
I like to show beginners the sequence
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bc4 Bc5 4.0-0 Nf6 5.Ng5? 0-0 6.Nxf7? Rxf7 7.Bxf7+ Kxf7
...
White to play after 7...
K
xf7
... and ask the questions “How would you evaluate this position?” and “Which king is safer?”
Not surprisingly, many students think White is better since, in their estimation – using Reinfeld integer values – the material is ‘even’ and, to them, White’s king is clearly safer.
Neither of these is correct; the engine evaluates Black’s position as winning(!) by about 3.2 pawns because Black has a knight and bishop for the rook and pawn (not equal!), the bishop-pair (see the next Tip #124), a lead in development, plus Black’s (!) king is much safer, since Black’s piece activity makes it much easier for him to harass White’s king in the middlegame.
Another experiment I do to illustrate the relative value of a rook and pawn versus two piece trade is to ask a student to evaluate a starting position where they could play White, but they had to take off the knight and bishop they were most willing to lose, while removing the black rook and pawn they thought would help them (White) the most. One recent student picked removing White’s b1-knight plus c1-bishop, and Black’s h8-rook and f7-pawn:
White to play after removing two pieces vs rook and pawn
This student guessed that instead of White’s normal (roughly) 0.3 pawn advantage, due to removing the ‘equal value’ but most advantageous-for-White pieces, that White might now be ahead much more, maybe about 2-3 pawns.
He was completely surprised when the engine evaluated this position in favor of Black by about -1.3! That is enough of advantage to be on the borderline of ‘forced win for Black if both sides play perfectly’, I think seeing just some of these engine evaluations of this imbalance should be enough to convince almost any astute student that, in general, two pieces are usually worth quite a bit more than a rook and pawn.
The main exceptions occur in endgames where either:
• The extra pawn gives promotion possibilities.
• The rook has extra access to attack the opponent’s pawns quickly through open lines (an engine usually quickly sees the future win of material).
But these are more the exception; otherwise, the two pieces are usually superior.
Tip 124
The bishop-pair does not mean having two bishops; it is short for ‘the advantage of the bishop-pair’ which means one side has both bishops and the other does not.
When I ask new students if they know what ‘the bishop-pair’ means, almost everyone says yes, but when I ask their definition, most get it incorrect. The most common incorrect answers I receive are:
• “I have two bishops” (to this I respond “Yes, that’s what it means in English, but that’s not the meaning in chess”).
• “My two bishops are strong/active/well placed.”
Then I tell them ‘the bishop-pair’ is short for ‘the advantage of the two bishops’ and this usually gets them on the right track to the correct idea.
In his award-winning article ‘The Evaluation of Material Imbalances’, first published in the March 1999
Chess Life
, GM Larry Kaufman measured the average value of the bishop-pair as about a +0.5 pawns ‘bonus’ to the material balance.
My coach, Donald Byrne, used to emphasize to me how important the bishop-pair was. While its average value is ‘only’ 0.5 pawns, in some positions the bishop-pair may be sufficient all by itself to provide a winning advantage. I was reminded of this when I reviewed a recent classical time control game from the Norway tournament:
Dommaraju Gukesh – Hikaru Nakamura
Stavanger 2023
Tarrasch Defense
1.d4 d5 2.c4 e6 3.Nc3 c5 4.e3 Nf6 5.Nf3 Nc6 6.a3 dxc4 7.Bxc4 a6 8.0-0 b5 9.Bd3 Bb7 10.Re1 Qc7 11.Qe2 Rd8 12.Rd1 cxd4 13.exd4 Be7 14.a4 b4 15.Ne4 Nxe4 16.Bxe4 0-0 17.Be3 Na5 18.Rac1 Qb8 19.Bb1 Bd5 20.Ne5 Qb7 21.Bd3 Ra8 22.Bf4 f5
Black creates a weak e-pawn and a weakened e5-square, but he is trying to create some imbalance in the position. The scoring system for the event rewarded wins as worth somewhat more than twice a draw!
23.Nf3 Bd8 24.Re1 Nb3 25.Rc2 Bb6 26.Qe5 Rac8 27.Rxc8 Rxc8 28.Qe2 Ra8 29.Qe3 Qc6 30.Be5 Qd7 31.Ng5 Na5 32.h4 Nc6 33.Rc1
Black to play after 33.
R
c1
33...Nxe5
Grabbing the bishop-pair.
34.Qxe5 h6 35.Nh3 Rc8 36.Re1?
36.Rxc8+ Qxc8 37.Qe3 holds the balance.
36...a5 37.Bb5 Qc7 38.Qe3 Qd6 39.Bd3 Ba2!
The d-pawn now falls too.
40.Nf4 Bxd4 41.Qd2 Be5 42.Ng6 Bf6 43.Qe2 Qd4 44.Rd1 Bb3 45.Rd2 Rd8 46.h5 Qg4
Offering to trade queens when ahead, even if it messes the pawn structure (Tip #86). Greedy would be 46...Bc4? 47.Bxc4 Qxd2 48.Qxe6+ Kh7 49.Qxf5 with a winning attack.
47.Qe3 Bd4 48.Be2 Qe4 49.Qxe4 fxe4 50.Kf1 Bf6 51.Rxd8+ Bxd8
A winning endgame; the bishops can ‘do their thing’. If you are one of those that don’t know how to use the bishop-pair, study endgames like these.
52.Ne5 Bxa4 53.Bc4 Kf8 54.Bxe6 Bf6 55.Ng6+ Ke8 56.b3 Bb5+ 57.Ke1 Bc3+ 58.Kd1 Bd4 59.Bd5 Bxf2 60.Bxe4 Bd4 61.Bd5 Kd7 62.Nh4 Kd6 63.Bg8 Bd7 64.Bf7 Bf6 65.Nf3 Bc6 66.Bg6 Bd5
The one thing you must not do in positions like this would be to trade into a bishops of opposite colors endgame. So the principle ‘Make fair trades of pieces, not necessarily pawns when ahead’ would
no
apply here. 66...Bxf3?? would be a monstrous beginner mistake.
67.Kc2 Bc3 68.Bf5 Ke7 69.Bg6 Kf6 70.Bd3 Bf7 71.g4 Be6
0-1
Even if you do not know how to best use the bishop-pair, in many positions its power is there! In general, you do not want to give your opponent the bishop-pair unless you get something substantial in return: a lead in development, a better pawn structure, an outpost knight, etc. If you play lower-rated players, they often give you the bishop-pair for no compensation, so it is almost like getting free material – look for that whenever you can.
Tip 125
Openings begin with closed lines and then often develop ‘fixed’ pawns. A common part of strategy is identifying possible pawn breaks which could potentially open lines to increase piece activity (or other benefits), and determining which breaks are most feasible, and how/when they can best be achieved.
A pawn break is often called a ‘break move’. This is a pawn move that attacks an opposing pawn that cannot easily/legally/safely just move past the attacking pawn. For example, after 1.e4 e5, the e-pawns become fixed and White’s break moves are d2-d4 and f2-f4; similarly, Black’s break moves are ...d7-d5 and ...f7-f5.
Similarly, in the Open Sicilian after 1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 d6 (or 2...e6 or 2...Nc6) 3.d4 is a break move because although 3...c4 is legal, it is not safe due to 4.Bxc4.
Break moves are used to open lines for pieces since the attacked pawn cannot easily move, so the attacking pawn will have the future option of capturing and opening lines, assuming the attacked pawn does not already capture and already open lines. When break move opportunities occur, it makes sense to try to coordinate piece development and activity with the achievement of beneficial break moves.
For example, if the game opens with a Symmetric English 1.c4 c5, White can try to engineer an eventual d2-d4 with moves like 2.Nf3 or maybe an eventual e2-e3, since the immediate 2.d4 can be met by 2...cxd4 3.Qxd4 Nc6 (AWL; see tip 56), winning a tempo. Alternatively, White could prepare his other break move, b2-b4, with preparatory moves like Rb1 and/or a2-a3.
I often say: “Break moves are the only non-beginner strategy that needs to be taught to (near-) beginners once they learn basic safety and activity principles.” The reason is that break moves are so important that it is very difficult to play a reasonable opening sequence without using them. Players who either don’t know about break moves, or use them poorly or not at all, often complain to me “I keep getting cramped positions out of the opening!”
There is a type of break move error I frequently see in lower-rated players’ games. Since almost everyone understands that after 1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 that the defending knight move 2...Nc6 is a reasonable (and, in fact best and most common) move,
they ‘naturally’ feel the mirror image idea of 1.d4 d5 2.Nf3 should be met by 2...Nc6(?).
White to move after 2...
N
c6
While not an outright mistake, 2...Nc6, at least when played by low-rated players, is misguided because it violates the principle ‘Do not develop your pieces where they unnecessarily block your most likely and/or effective break moves’.
In 1.d4 d5 openings, White’s break moves are e2-e4 and c2-c4. Black’s break moves are ...c7-c5 and ...e7-e5. But breaking with the e-pawns is more problematic; it is easier to make the break moves with the c-pawns safely, plus it introduces the other beneficial possibility of trading a ‘side’ pawn for a central pawn. It is not a coincidence that White’s most popular second move is the Queen’s Gambit, 2.c4. However, after White’s second most popular move 2.Nf3, which doesn’t block a break move, the ‘natural’ response 2...Nc6 does block Black’s most effective break pawn.
Similarly in the French Defense, the sequence 1.e4 e6 2.d4 d5 3.e5 Nc6(?) blocking the natural break ...c7-c5 is usually only played by beginners or strong players playing a very rare strategy (though rarely the latter). The reason is that 3...Nc6 so blatantly violates the ‘don’t block break moves’ principle that only someone who knows almost nothing or is so strong they can blatantly ignore principles would make this move!
When I review games with students who block important break moves, I often interject “Let me guess, you had problems later in this game because you got a cramped position?” and the most common reply I get is “I did! How did you know?”
Importantly, effective break moves do not just occur in the opening; they can occur in the middlegame and endgame. Therefore, just understanding this one (almost) basic strategy can greatly improve both your understanding of strategic play and help alleviate that ‘I didn’t know what to do’ feeling.
Tip 126
In closed positions (those without open or semi-open files), pieces are often better placed behind the pawns, not in front.
This principle, like all principles (which are not rules), has exceptions, but I often see amateur players get into trouble by putting too many pieces in front of their pawns in closed positions only to see opponent’s pawns push those pieces all over the board. I had an amusing game as White several years ago that illustrated this point. I could not quickly locate it, but it went something very similar to:
1.c4 c5 2.g3 g6 3.Bg2 Bg7 4.Nc3 Nc6 5.e4 d6 6.Nge2 Nf6 7.0-0 0-0 8.d3 Bd7 9.a3 Rb8 10.Rb1 a6 11.b4 Qc8 12.b5 Ne5?? 13.f4 Neg4 14.h3 Nh6 15.g4
Black to play after 15.g4
Here I am winning a piece due to the dual threat of 16.g5 with a fork and also trapping the knight on h6. If 15...Ne8 16.g5 anyway. So in this position my opponent sacrificed on g4, possibly
15...Nhxg4 16.hxg4 Bxg4
, but I won easily.
Notice in this game Black only put two pieces – his knights – in front of his pawns, but I had none. Moreover, it is the only game I can remember where I moved all eight pawns so early in the game!
Next let’s consider the main line in the King’s Indian Defense:
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 g6 3.Nc3 Bg7 4.e4 d6 5.Be2 0-0 6.Nf3 e5 7.0-0 Nc6 8.d5 Ne7
.
White to play after 8...
N
e7
Not only is the position closed (no open or semi-open files), but the center is fixed, or locked, as the d- and e-pawns are blocked up against each other. Both sides have only their king’s knight in front of their pawns, but one of the main lines now runs
9.Ne1 Nd7
, so that now all 16 non-pawns are behind pawns! This creates somewhat the opposite effect of the previous example, where Black’s knights were in front of his pawns and were being harassed by the white pawns.
Finally, the famous miniature Borochow-Fine, Pasadena 1932:
1.e4 Nf6 2.e5 Nd5 3.c4 Nb6 4.d4 Nc6?? 5.d5! Nxe5 6.c5 Nbc4 7.Qd4
when one of the harassed knights is lost.
Position after 7.
Q
d4
Tip 127
If one side is weak on a certain square complex (e.g. weak dark squares on the kingside), and the other side has that color bishop and the side with the weak squares does not, and queens are on the board, that is usually worth at least an extra pawn in evaluation for the side with that color bishop.
This tip is a ‘Dan’ specialty. A good example is a line in the Kan Sicilian where Black plays ...Be7-b4 and then ...Bb4xc3 to double White’s pawns on the semi-open c-file. In this example, White can even offer an extra pawn!
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 e6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 a6 5.Nc3 Qc7 6.Be2 Bb4 7.0-0 Bxc3+?
This is not a good move, but I have seen many lower-rated players play moves like this in analogous situations.
8.bxc3
Black to play after 8.bxc3
All the conditions here are met:
• Black is weak on the central and queenside dark squares.
• White has a dark-squared bishop and Black does not.
• Queens are still on the board.
Although Black can now win a pawn with 8...Qxc3 (not one of Black’s three best moves), no matter what Black plays, Black is already lost! If you do not believe me, set up this position with your friendly local high-level engine (or a grandmaster if you have one available), and try to defend Black’s position.
The combination of Black’s weakness on the dark squares and the power of White’s bishop-pair (especially his unopposed dark-squared bishop) can very quickly create unstoppable threats. Just for the fun of it, I will let
Stockfish
play itself in this position (I will give it maybe 20-30 seconds per move). Here is the result:
8...Nf6 9.Ba3 Qxc3 10.Nb3 b5 11.e5 b4 12.exf6 bxa3 13.fxg7 Rg8 14.Bf3 Nc6 15.Re1 Ra7 16.Bxc6 dxc6 17.Qh5 Qxg7 18.g3 h6 19.h4 Re7 20.Rab1 Qg4 21.Qxh6 Rd7 22.Nc5 Rd4 23.Qe3 Qxh4 24.Rb8 Kd8 25.Nb7+ Kd7 26.Nc5+ Kd8 27.Nd3 c5 28.Nxc5 Qg4 29.Qc3 Rd1 30.Nxe6+ Ke7 31.Nf4+ Kd7 32.Rb4 Rxe1+ 33.Qxe1 Rg5 34.Qd2+ Ke7 35.Re4+ Kf8 36.Qd4 Rg8 37.Qc5+ Kg7 38.Nh5+ 1-0
To paraphrase what Fischer once wrote: Of course, Black can play can play differently, but then Black would lose differently!
Another example is Radjabov-Bortnyk, World Rapid Championship, Doha 2016:
White to play
White could save his attacked rook on c1, but instead he offered an exchange sacrifice with
11.d5!
. After
11...Bxc1
(not best, but White is doing very well either way)
12.Qxc1
White has the bishop-pair, the only dark-squared bishop, and weak dark squares around the black king. With the queens on the board, the principle is fully satisfied.
Despite being down the exchange, the engine rates White’s position as +3! Play continued:
12...Nd4 13.g4 Bd7 14.Qh6 Nxf3+ 15.Bxf3 Ne8 16.Ne4 f6 17.h4 Bxg4 18.h5 gxh5 19.Ng5 fxg5?
Only 19...Rf7 gives Black a chance.
20.Qxg5+ Kf7 21.Qxh5+!! Bxh5 22.Bxh5+ Kg8 23.Rg1+ Ng7 24.Rxg7+ Kh8 25.Rxe7+ Rf6 26.Bxf6+ Kg8 27.Rg7+ Kf8 28.Rxh7 Qf5 29.Rh8# 1-0
For a full annotation of this instructive game, I suggest Cyrus Lakdawala’s recent work
Irrational Chess
.
There is a well-known line in the Grünfeld where White sacrifices the exchange to get rid of Black’s kingside fianchettoed bishop; it is called the Sokolsky Attack:
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 g6 3.Nc3 d5 4.cxd5 Nxd5 5.e4 Nxc3 6.bxc3 Bg7 7.Bc4 0-0 8.Ne2 c5 9.0-0 Nc6 10.Be3 Bg4 11.f3 Na5 12.Bd3 cxd4 13.cxd4 Be6 14.d5!?
.
Position after 14.d5!?
On the popular chess website Lichess, someone let this position run for 52 ply (those long ‘think’ results gets saved to the Cloud) and 14.d5 was calculated as the equally best move for White (along with 14.Rc1 and 14.Bf2) and all three moves were evaluated as 0.00! After
14...Bxa1
(best)
15.Qxa1 f6
the chances are still evaluated as equal, despite Black’s material advantage.
So, once again, we see that getting your opponent’s dark-squared bishop while retaining yours while meeting all the other conditions of this tip were worth at least the entire pawn – but in this ‘book line’ not nearly as much more as in the previous examples.
Tip 128
Chess is not checkers: you do not have to capture, and putting all your pawns on the same color to guard each other is often a bad idea.
Consider the sequence
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.d4 exd4 4.Nxd4
...
Black to play after 4.
N
xd4
...and then
4...Nxd4(?) 5.Qxd4.
In the Lichess master database, 4...Nxd4(?) was played in 0.6% of the games, probably mostly when an inferior player was playing Black. In their ‘everyone’ database 4...Nxd4(?) was played 32% of the games, more than any other move! The engine does not rate 4...Nxd4(?) among its top five moves!
So why is the inferior 4...Nxd4 so popular with amateurs? They believe either/or:
• It prematurely lures the queen to the center, causing White to violate ‘Don’t bring your queen out too early’.
• That once a capturing sequence is started, it pretty much commits them to all the ‘safe’ captures on that square.
But chess is not checkers, where captures are mandatory. You should only make a capture if you think it is the best (or equally best) move, not because it is possible, legal, or continues a previous capturing sequence.
The other ‘checker problem’ that is commonly seen with amateurs is to set up a pawn chain where as many pawns as possible guard other pawns, by putting all the pawns on the same color squares, e.g.
1.d4 d5 2.e3 c5 3.f4 Nf6 4.c3 e6 5.g3(?)
5...Be7 6.a3(?)
.
Black to play after 6.a3(?)
Stockfish
rates Black’s position as better by about one pawn, already on the borderline of possibly winning.
Why are beginners and near-beginners attracted to similar pawn structures? They feel they are safe, with maximum pawn coverage of adjacent pawns, and seemingly setting up a ‘rock solid’ pawn wedge.
Of course, as these players improve, they experience the pain of being punished repeatedly for creating such clearly weak square complexes. In the example above, White is massively weak on the light squares, which can be occupied by enemy forces. But for an amateur used to checkers (and many who are not!), where only the 32 squares of one color can be occupied, the pawns instead seem to form a fortress. Once their ‘board vision’ gets more experienced with the chess board, where all 64 squares should be utilized, such ‘ugly’ pawn structures now are not only not formidable, but can actually be seen as the opposite, i.e. as weak.
Tip 129
More pawns in the middle are good.
We have already seen in Tip #126 that pieces generally belong behind pawns in closed positions. For analogous reasons – pieces generally lose fights to pawns in battles over square control – it is usually true that more pawns toward the center are good, especially before the endgame. Because this is such a common and powerful idea, I am surprised this tip does not have a wider audience.
There is a well-known principle that says ‘When two pawns can capture, generally capture toward the middle.’ This is especially true when the captures can be made on the knight files (b- and g-files), so that the centrally capturing pawn converts a rook’s pawn into a knight’s pawn, so that now it is attacking two squares instead of one. Consider
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bc4 Bc5 4.d3 Nf6 5.Be3 Bb6 6.Bxb6
:
Black to play after 6.
B
xb6
The engine rates the ‘natural’
6...axb6
as about half a pawn better than the ugly 6...cxb6.
But our tip takes this idea one step further. It says that even if other pieces can capture instead of a pawn, if you can get another pawn toward the center, that is often correct. Let’s examine the famous ‘Every Russian Schoolboy Knows’ recapture for Black in many variations of the main line King’s Indian Defense, e.g.
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 g6 3.Nc3 Bg7 4.e4 d6 5.Be2 0-0 6.Nf3 e5 7.0-0 Nc6 8.d5 Ne7 9.Nd2
(9.Ne1 is currently the engine’s choice)
9...Ne8 10.f3 f5
and now 11.b4 is the preferred move, but what if White plays
11.exf5
:
Black to play after 11.exf5
Black has four ways to recapture and we can see the appeal of capturing with a piece:
• 11...Bxf5 develops a formerly undeveloped piece.
• 11...Rxf5 keeps the rook unblocked on a semi-open file.
• 11...Nxf5 activates the knight with the winning fork threat of 12...Ne3.
• But, as ‘every Russian schoolboy knows’, the engine rates the ‘more pawns in the center are good’
11...gxf5
as better than all of them by at least one full pawn!
This is a surprising result to many. Once, when I was a young man who had just won the prestigious Greater Philadelphia Invitational Championship, I was teaching this idea to a class of night school adults. One of the adults told me I was wrong: Black’s king was getting too exposed, and Black was sure to get checkmated. At the time there were no engines to back up my claim. But then – and now – we know Black’s immediate and strong control of the center after
11...gxf5
makes that the best move.
This evaluation is also consistent with Tip #118 about immediate benefits often outweighing nebulous long-term possibilities. In this case, the nebulous possibility is Black’s king being in danger. Instead, Black’s king is surrounded by black pieces and the central control granted by his pawns also further indicates that with proper play, the white king is likely in more danger than the black one!
Tip 130
The ‘Pointing Rule’ is a helpful but often misunderstood principle indicating the direction of break moves and possible attack.
When I ask new students if they know the pointing rule and they answer “Yes!” and I ask them to tell it to me, they usually say something like: “Attack the way your pawns are pointing” or “Your pawns point to the side of the board you want to play.”
While they are on the right track, following the ‘rule’ the way they state it can often lead to both confusion or outright misdirection. I think the best wording to indicate the right idea for the Pointing Rule is:
When all four d- and e-pawns are locked up against each other so none can move, place your hand across your d- and e-pawns and the direction your hand points is the side you want to attack, and the side you almost always want to look for a break move (usually with the c- or f-pawns).
Good examples are the Advance French and the main line of the King’s Indian Defense. The French structure is seen after
1.e4 e6 2.d4 d5 3.e5
:
Black to play after 3.e5
All four d- and e-pawns are locked against each other; the white pawns point to the kingside with a possible f2-f4-f5 pawn break (Tip #125), and the black pawns point to the queenside and 3...c5 is the indicated immediate pawn break.
The King’s Indian Defense main line is
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 g6 3.Nc3 Bg7 4.e4 d6 5.Be2 0-0 6.Nf3 e5 7.0-0 Nc6 8.d5 Ne7
:
White to play after 8...
N
e7
All four d- and e-pawns are again locked against each other; the white pawns point to the queenside with a possible c4-c5 pawn break and the black pawns point to the kingside with an eventual ...f7-f5 pawn break.
The pointing rule only works well with the d- and e-pawns and only when they are locked. For example, if the central pawns were on d3 and e4 for White, and the black pawns on d5 and e6, as often happens in the Queen’s Gambit Declined or London...
Don’t apply the ‘Pointing Rule’ here!
...here both sets of pawns would point to the queenside, indicating both could have the advantage to attack there! That does not make a lot of sense.
The further away the pawns are from the center the less it works, too:
Don’t apply the ‘Pointing Rule’ here either!
If you tried to apply a Pointing Rule here, it would indicate Black should attack in the center and White should attack on the a-file. That might make some sense for Black, but it would make no sense at all for White!
In summary, the Pointing Rule should primarily apply only for four locked, central d- and e-pawns. If you try to apply this principle in other situations, it may or may not indicate a correct plan of action. If the ‘way your pawns point’ in other situations makes sense, then it is acceptable to follow your instincts, but don’t
blindly
apply this rule to situations other than those properly indicated with locked d- and e-pawns.
Tip 131
In the opening or early middlegame, if you can capture a center (d- or e-) pawn with a side pawn (c- or f-pawn), and the recapture cannot be with a pawn, it is almost always correct to do so.
It is important to add:
• If the recapture can be made with a pawn, this principle does not apply: sometimes the initial capture of the central pawn is still very good, and sometimes not so good. The principle does not imply that if the recapture can be made with a pawn that the initial capture of the central pawn is not indicated.
• After the early middlegame, positional factors like the one in this principle start to yield to tactical and endgame pawn structure considerations, so it is less likely (but not impossible) to be applied.
For our first example, consider the Queen’s Gambit,
1.d4 d5 2.c4
:
Black to play
White is threatening 3.cxd5 Qxd5 4.Nc3, winning a tempo, so Black’s main replies are
2...e6
(Queen’s Gambit Declined), 2...c6 (Slav Defense), and 2...dxc4 (Queen’s Gambit Accepted). If Black makes any other move, such as the inferior Marshall Defense (2...Nf6?!) then this principle applies and 3.cxd5 is, in almost all cases, the right move.
Let’s consider the Open Sicilian,
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 d6
(or 2...Nc6 or 2...e6)
3.d4
:
Black to play
White ‘threatens’ 4.dxc5 dxc5 5.Qxd8+, but that’s somewhat irrelevant since one of the ideas of the Sicilian is to offer the trade of the c-pawn for the white d-pawn. Moreover, 3.d4 is a pawn break because Black cannot safely play 3...c4. So Black almost always follows the principle and plays
3...cxd4
.
Likewise, in the Advance French,
1.e4 e6 2.d4 d5 3.e5 c5
...
White to play after 3...c5
... if White plays any move other than the normal 4.c3 or the strange 4.dxc5 (also allowing Black to trade a side pawn for a center pawn), Black should follow the principle and play 4...cxd4, making White recapture on d4 with a piece. Of course, if White does play the normal 4.c3, then 4...cxd4 is
not
indicated by the principle. Instead, Black should develop with 4...Nc6, 4...Qb6 or 4...Bd7.
Tip 132
It is usually better if your weak pawns are on the defending (minority) side of the board. Weak pawns are often not that much of a detriment in defending versus a majority than they are when they on the offensive side, crippling a majority.
Consider just the pawn structure in the following variation of the Exchange Ruy Lopez,
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5 a6 4.Bxc6 dxc6 5.d4 exd4 6.Qxd4
:
Diagram 1: Exchange Ruy Lopez pawn structure
Now compare this with the pawn structure if we shift Black’s doubled pawn from c6 to f6.
Is the theoretical harm of the doubled pawns for Black about equal in each diagram, or is one structure more helpful than the other? On the surface, this may seem like a silly question, but it illustrates a very helpful bit of chess knowledge.
Diagram 2: doubled pawn shifted from c6 to f6
The answer is that the first diagram is better for White than the second (and thus the first diagram is worse for Black). Why? Because in the first diagram, White has a healthy kingside majority that, with proper play, can create a passed pawn in the endgame (if I recall correctly, engines have proven that if Diagram 1 was a king and pawn endgame, it is a win for White). Moreover, in that same Diagram 1, Black’s queenside majority is crippled by the passed pawn and Black cannot forcibly create a passed pawn, which is why White can win the endgame.
But in the second diagram neither side has a pawn majority on either side of the board. Black’s doubled pawns are not as much of a detriment, as White cannot use his slight advantage in the pawn structure to potentially create a passed pawn.
Diagram 3
In our final example Black has weak pawns on both sides of the board. But his queenside problems are more detrimental; Black has a majority, but it is badly crippled and the extra pawn is only a minor benefit; without help those pawns cannot create a passed pawn. On the other hand, the black doubled pawns on the kingside may be vulnerable, but if only pawn play is allowed, those doubled isolated g-pawns can prevent the two white pawns from creating a passed pawn.
Understanding when weak pawns and crippled majorities can or cannot create passed pawns or, conversely, prevent or not prevent the creation of passed pawns, is a big step toward understanding endgame possibilities.
Tip 133
Doubled pawns are not always bad.
I remember a student game where my student made some important decisions, primarily based on his feeling the necessity to avoid doubled pawns. But to worry about doubled pawns when there was also the possibility he might lose a rook in a relatively straightforward position was getting his ‘chess perspective’ quite backwards.
In the road to chess improvement, one must be able to consistently make the big safety decisions correctly before spending time worrying about the smaller ones.
Strategy is usually the tiebreak of equally safe moves
. My math analogy would be worrying about something in geometry before you know the next integer after 15 when counting to 20. I see this problem in lessons quite frequently, and it often comes from inexperienced players reading advanced grandmaster analysis and arriving at the conclusion that they, too, need to implement those fine positional details if they are to improve. Better to work on the big issues like safety/tactics, playing consistently slowly in long time control games, and using all your pieces all the time (activity) – see Tip #1.
With that in mind I use as illustration the beginning of the following game, which was played several years ago. White was a friend of mine rated 1500, who liked to play in the U2200 section of tournaments. In this game, which I think can be considered as a ‘poster game for positions where doubled pawns aren’t always bad’, he was paired with an Expert, who had Black.
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bc4 Bc5 4.Nc3 Nf6 5.d3 d6 6.0-0 Be6 7.Bxe6 fxe6 8.Qe2 0-0 9.Be3 Bb6 10.Bxb6 axb6 11.Rfe1 Qe8 12.Rad1 Qg6 13.a3 Rf7 14.Kh1 Raf8 15.Rd2 Nh5
White to play after 15...
N
h5
Around this point I walked away. When I returned a little later, I found the game was over, so I approached my friend. Our conversation went something like this:
Me: “How did you do?”
1500 Friend: “I got crushed.”
Me: “Yes, I saw – things didn’t look too good when I left.”
1500 Friend: “But you know what was funny?”
Me: “No, what?”
1500 Friend: “I did everything right and he did everything wrong!”
Me: “Really? Do you mean his two sets of doubled pawns?”
1500 Friend: “Yes!”
Me: “You really think that those doubled pawns were bad for him and good for you?”
1500 Friend: “Of course!”
Me: “Maybe I should get you to read my book
Elements of Positional Evaluation
where I discuss when doubled pawns can be harmful and helpful!”
In this game the expert understood that the capture 7...fxe6 gave him the only semi-open file on the kingside, providing important mobility/activity gains for his rooks, while simultaneously giving him an extra pawn in the center to guard the important d5-square. Moreover, his doubled pawns on the b-file were also not harmful, as that gave him the second semi-open file and moved his a-pawn on to the more influential b-file. That leads to a *Tip Alert!*
If one side has the only semi-open file on the board for his rooks and the other side has no open or semi-open files, that can in many circumstances be a big advantage for the side possessing the only semi-open file
.
I think the first book I read where it emphasized that doubled pawns are not always harmful was
Point Count Chess
by Horowitz and Mott-Smith. This book has been criticized for its point count system as being over-simplified, which may be so, but I thought their section on when to count doubled pawns as harmful was incisive, eye-opening, and very instructive.
Chapter Six
Openings
The tips in this chapter, not surprisingly, deal with the opening stage of the game. This includes not only tips to make good moves during the initial part of the game, but how to choose openings, and choosing/playing specific openings and opening structures. There is even a tip about how to best manage your time during the opening phase.
Tip 134
Learning openings and the two extremes of either: (i.) not studying anything about openings, or (ii.) locking yourself in a closet trying to memorize entire openings... are both clearly incorrect. Moderation, as usual, is optimal.
Therefore, the following are four ‘moderate’ things you can do to efficiently study openings:
• Learn first just the main lines (‘tabiyas’) of the openings you intend to play, starting with one for White, one with Black versus 1.e4, and one with Black versus 1.d4.
• After each game, review the game and ask ‘If I had to play this game again, on which move would I improve?’ You can use any media such as a database, book, engine, video, etc. This will both extend your tabiya knowledge further (to extra moves), and widen it when opponents vary from the first tabiya moves you learned.
• Learn the basic traps in your openings, not just to spring them on your opponents, but also to avoid falling into them yourself.
• Play many speed games with your opening before using it in more serious games, again reviewing your openings after each set of speed games.
I have been quoted on social media that I think studying openings is not important. I have never written that and it is clearly not true. Sometimes I do write: “One argument that the opening is the most important phase of the game is that it’s the only phase you are guaranteed to play each and every game!” Of course, that is a little tongue-in-cheek, as I make arguments about each phase possibly being the most important.
Moreover, the tools today to help players learn the openings are far superior to those I had. Like Beth Harmon in
The Queen’s Gambit
, I learned many of my openings in the 1960s and, whereas Beth perused
Modern Chess Openings
#9
, I had the latter 60s benefit of
MCO-10
. In those pre-Batsford years, there were basically no books in English on specific openings, so I had to purchase the German Schwarcz series (I didn’t know German...).
But today players have databases, books, videos, engines, and specific opening tools like ChessBase, Chess Openings Wizard, and many more. You want to follow the important advice (*Tip Alert!*):
After each game, fast or slow, look up your opening in a database, book, video, and/or engine and determine ‘If I had to play this opening again and my opponent make the same moves, on which move would I improve, what move would I play instead, and how can I remember this?
’
If you are reviewing the game and cannot find what move to play in a database or book, the engine’s recommendation is now so accurate that you can be pretty sure its top move is a good one.
I religiously looked up my games in
MCO-10
(or anywhere), not because I wanted to be a better player (that was a goal!), but because I felt it would be embarrassing and unbecoming of an intelligent person to make an opening mistake and then not try to learn from it when there was a ready resource that would help correct mistakes. Players who do not look up their openings after a game are likely to either repeat the same mistakes forever or make a misguided attempt to do it themselves, which is a hit-or-miss proposition, whereas engine or grandmaster corrections are conveniently available.
Moreover, today it is easier than ever to look up speed game openings because much of speed play is done online, and the same servers that have the openings databases preserve all your games, fast or slow, for later analysis.
Yes, emphasis on opening study (especially memorization) is not
as
important, especially for lower-rated players, as many amateurs make it out to be. But it is still important! Memorizing is all right, but understanding is better, and learning and applying principles to apply when you are ‘out of book’ is almost essential.
Tip 135
Grandmaster Andrew Soltis suggests that in many cases it might be more beneficial for class players to play openings that are not the most popular ones at the super-grandmaster level.
In his book
Grandmaster Secrets: Openings
, GM Andrew Soltis gives some reasons why class players might want to consider playing openings that are not the most popular among the top grandmasters. One obvious reason was that these were the openings that other amateurs were playing, so to also use those openings would be to play lines that the opponents would most likely know well.
Another reason to play less popular openings is that the reasons why grandmasters are less likely to play those openings does not necessarily hold at the lower levels. I believe he mentioned the Riga variation in the Ruy Lopez as an example. The Riga is not played because of the forcing line White has that was first illustrated by Capablanca:
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5 a6 4.Ba4 Nf6 5.0-0 Nxe4 6.d4 exd4?!
This is the Riga.
7.Re1 d5 8.Nxd4 Bd6! 9.Nxc6 Bxh2+ 10.Kh1
10.Kxh2 Qh4+ 11.Kg1 Qxf2+ is a draw.
10...Qh4 11.Rxe4+! dxe4 12.Qd8+ Qxd8 13.Nxd8+ Kxd8 14.Kxh2
Black to play after 14.
K
xh2
Black has a rook and two pawns for the two pieces, so an amateur using the ‘Reinfeld’ integer scale for piece value (1-3-3-5-9) would think that Black has ‘more material’. But grandmasters – and now engines – know better and White, with his bishop-pair, has all the winning chances. Therefore, once this line was known, it was avoided like the plague by all strong players with the black pieces. In fact, Soltis suggested that even if your opponent playing White might get this far, he not only might not have the technique that would give him a win, but he might evaluate the position as better for Black!
But what are the chances that an amateur level player would happen to know this rare line for White? Not good, you might surmise. Thanks to my student, radio personality Howard Stern, who took my advice and tried playing the Riga against other amateurs (and even amateur level practice engines), we have a good idea of the answer. Howard played this line for Black about 100 times and
not once
did anyone play the Capablanca ‘refutation’.
In other words, in about 100 amateur games, Black had at least an equal (or in many cases winning) position out of the opening in every game.
I think this experiment greatly backed up GM Soltis’s claim – which I had agreed with from the start (otherwise I would not have included this tip nor recommended it to Mr. Stern). Plus, although the Riga is an excellent example of Soltis’s suggestion, there are many more openings or lines that would benefit from a similar argument – that they are not popular for GMs, but OK for class players.
I sometimes hear students rated in the low 1000s say something like “I can’t play the King’s Indian Defense; didn’t Kasparov give it up because it was no good?”, or “The engines think it is good for White.”
In these instances, I try to explain to them what Soltis also had argued: what makes sense at the super-grandmaster level doesn’t always translate into what works or helps at the improving amateur level. Of course, there are some questionable opening lines that grandmasters avoid and amateurs should too, but things are not always so black and white (no pun intended).
There is much ‘grey’ room available for opening choice – just consider all the clever ways former world champion Magnus Carlsen uses in his openings to avoid the most popular lines, but still get his opponents to just ‘play chess’. Carlsen is often willing to give up his opening advantage with White and play discarded or ignored lines which do not promise White much, but take his opponent out of their engine ‘book’ analysis.
Amateur players do not need to worry as much about their opponent being armed with engine analysis 23 moves deep. This freedom gives them much greater leeway in choosing and playing opening lines that are fun and interesting, no matter their reputation among high-level international players.
Tip 136
A tip for helping you find a ‘good’ opening.
Sometimes I get asked, out of context, “What’s a good opening you recommend?”, “What’s a good opening for White?”, or “What a good opening against 1.e4?”. The problem is with the word ‘good’. If by good you mean the more winning chances, the better, then no legitimate opening is that good!
Assume there was an opening, the Yadeeda variation of the HumptyUmp opening, which won all the time for White. Then that opening sounds really good, doesn’t it? But if the Yadeeda variation wins all the time by force, then why would any good black player who likes to play the HumptyUmp opening go into the Yadeeda variation? He wouldn’t. So that line wins for White, but it is not such a ‘good’ opening since it amounts to an opening trap – no one of any knowledge would play it for Black.
Similarly, suppose a line leads to absolute equality (or better) for Black in every variation. Then no good player would likely play it for White (unless they really like that type of position or are playing for a draw), because White is supposed to try for an advantage, not play openings where Black easily equalizes (or better).
Therefore, all ‘good’ openings, at least those played regularly at the grandmaster level, have the same characteristics: if played correctly by both players, White is fighting for a slight advantage and Black is struggling for equality. If the evaluation of the opening is any less for White, then good players will not play it consistently as White, and any poorer evaluation for Black and good players won’t play that side consistently, either.
In that sense all ‘good’ openings theoretically yield a slightly advantage to White. However, if by ‘good’ you mean:
• Gives me good winning chances if my opponent plays it wrong.
• Is fun to play.
• Helps me learn something.
• Contains a lot of rich strategic and tactical possibilities.
Then you are talking!
As I noted in the previous tip, GM Soltis wrote in
Grandmaster Secrets: Openings
that it makes sense for the average player to at least sometimes try openings that are
not
popular at the grandmaster level, and I agree. However, many players erroneously tend to think that if a grandmaster doesn’t play an opening there’s something wrong with it.
For example, when former World Champion Garry Kasparov gave up the King’s Indian Defense, thousands of class players did too, as if their winning chances in that opening were gravely affected by Kasparov’s decision. Needless to say, I thought they were being just as illogical as they might have been to pick up the King’s Indian Defense again a couple of years later just because GM Teimour Radjabov starting having success with it at the top level. The value of the opening did not change from Kasparov to Radjabov; just some players secondary perception of its effectiveness.
There are lots of ‘good’ openings for intermediate players that grandmasters don’t play and, as computers, and former world champion Magnus Carlsen, have shown us, even some very rare openings are playable if handled correctly. My suggestion: pick out openings that are both fun to play and help you learn something (assuming you want to improve). Then, every once in a while, rotate to something completely different and learn how to play different pawn structures (Tip #140).
Finally, no opening is good if you keep making the same mistake over and over, so it is almost necessary to review each game, fast or slow, with an opening database (strong players, books, and engines are helpful, too). The goal is to find out what you would do differently next time if your opponent played the same moves against you.
Tip 137
Start your opening preparation by identifying the most common opening moves you are likely to encounter. Then find a ‘tabiya’ (standard opening sequence) that addresses those lines and learn them first.
The next Tip, #138 will deal with how to learn opening lines. This tip discusses what a tabiya is and how to find one.
Tabiya: An Arabic word originally meaning the opening set-up of pieces, but now more commonly used to mean the standard moves of any opening, i.e. “the real game starts after the players play the tabiya.” For example, we could say the tabiya for the Closed Ruy Lopez is 1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5 a6 4.Ba4 Nf6 5.0-0 Be7 6.Re1 b5 7.Bb3 0-0. At this point White could allow the Marshall Attack with 8.c3 d5 or after 8.c3 Black could opt for the regular Closed lines with 8...d6 9.h3, or White could play an Anti-Marshall line with another 8th move such as 8.a4 or 8.d3 or 8.h3.
When you choose your opening repertoire, you should prepare tabiyas as the ‘trunks’ of your learning tree against all common replies. For example, suppose you decide to open 1.d4 and 2.c4
when playing White. You might then prepare a tabiya – a set of standard moves for each side – for
