each
of the following common defenses (you can include more or fewer lines if you wish!):
• Queen’s Gambit Declined, 1.d4 d5 2.c4 e6.
• Queen’s Gambit Accepted, 1.d4 d5 2.c4 dxc4.
• Semi-Slav Defense, 1.d4 d5 2.c4 c6 3.Nf3 Nf6 4.Nc3 e6.
• King’s Indian Defense, 1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 g6 3.Nc3 Bg7.
• Grünfeld Defense, 1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 g6 3.Nc3 d5.
• Nimzo-Indian Defense, 1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 e6 3.Nc3 Bb4 (or, if you prefer to play 3.Nf3, the Queen’s Indian, 3...b6, and Bogo-Indian, 3...Bb4+).
• Modern Benoni, 1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 c5 3.d5 e6.
• Benko Gambit, 1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 c5 3.d5 b5.
• Dutch Defense, 1.d4 f5.
As you expand your repertoire, you can add tabiyas to meet lesser played lines like the Albin Counter-Gambit, 1.d4 d5 2.c4 e5,
the Budapest Gambit, 1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 e5,
the Modern Defense, 1.d4 g6,
the Old Benoni, 1.d4 c5, or any other variations for which you think it wise to prepare.
How do you find these tabiyas? In the old days, players like myself or the fictional Beth Harmon would use a book like
Modern Chess Openings
. In the layout of that book, the longer lines that are ‘starter’ moves at the top of each page could often form a complete tabiya. Today there are even entire opening series which primarily offer explanations of tabiyas such as New in Chess’s
Comprehensive Chess Openings
or John Watson’s
Mastering the Chess Openings
.
How do you pick out a tabiya from among various lines you can play? There is not one answer to that. You can pick out the line that the database says gets the best results or one that is the most popular. I did not usually use either of those criteria; I picked out the variation that seemed like it led to the position(s) I found the most fun.
One of the common questions I get asked is: “How do I know when the tabiya has ended?” The most common answer is when the main lines branch out to multiple reasonable choices. However, this is an individual question that does not have a just this one black and white answer, but depends on other factors such as how proficient a player you are, what your opponents know and are playing, how much you can remember, or how the information is presented in the source material you are referencing.
In any case, you want to come to the board armed with a repertoire of possible tabiyas that can at least get you started if someone plays the most popular replies to your opening. This will represent the trunk of your tree and, as you get more experience with your lines and look up your openings after each game (Tip #134), your repertoire will both deepen and widen as you branch out from your tabiyas and fill in your opening knowledge ‘tree’.
Tip 138
When learning a sequence (opening, endgame etc), practice it repeatedly, best from both sides, with a friend/engine, to try to move it from short-term into long-term memory (i.e. ‘understand it better’).
In my experience, the stronger the student, the more likely they are to remember a taught sequence without too much repetition. This is not because stronger players have better memories in general (psychological experiments have shown this is not usually true), but because they are more familiar with the chess board and thus have better ‘board vision’.
What often happens when I show something to a lower-rated student is they see and understand when I am teaching, but if the same pattern/sequence occurs again in a future lesson, they frequently don’t recognize/remember it. One way they can improve their ‘memory’ is to practice repeating the pattern/sequence as soon as possible after the lesson, until they feel comfortable it is something they understand and can repeat.
When I was a beginning tournament player, I would pick out my ‘tabiyas’ (standard opening sequences) from
MCO-10
, and then play each out for both sides several times until I felt comfortable I could repeat it at a later time. Usually learning a sequence that well took about 20 minutes. For example, I learned the main line of the Closed Ruy Lopez:
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5 a6 4.Ba4 Nf6 5.0-0 Be7 6.Re1 b5 7.Bb3 d6
(7...0-0 has become more common, but then it signaled the ‘Marshall Attack’)
8.c3 0-0 9.h3
.
Black to play after 9.h3
Once I mastered this sequence, I was ready to extend it and/or branch out with earlier variations:
• Extend with Black variations, 9...Nb8 (Breyer), 9...Na5 (Classical), 9...Bb7 (Zaitsev), etc.
• Branch with earlier lines 5...Nxe4 (Open), 7...0-0 8.c3 d5 (Marshall Attack), etc.
If I had a study-buddy available (a friend who would help me study), I would play out the repetitions with him/her. My study buddy could have a book to aid them, if needed. After several repetitions we would switch sides. Today there are opening study software tools like Chess Openings Wizard, ChessBase, and tools within chess server websites which allow players to store their repertoires and practice them.
There is nothing unique about learning opening sequences in this manner; the subject could have been ‘In the endgame, how to draw in a king and pawn versus king position when the side with the king can’t get in front of the pawn.’ When learning these ideas, a comparable repetition of the sequence, preferably playing both sides, will aid both understanding and remembrance.
Tip 139
If you are an aspiring to improve player, it is helpful to rotate your openings periodically to learn more about different pawn structures. Also, it helps greatly when you must transpose out of your opening (or are tricked into it), or play versus irregular openings.
Some instructors suggest to their students that they stick to openings that fit their ‘style’. While that is often good advice for the short run, if you want to improve, you will run into more general situations that will help you in many openings if you play different pawn structures that arise from various openings.
There is also the benefit of avoiding boredom and exposing yourself to different types of games to better decide not only which ones you enjoy playing the most, but also to better expose your weaknesses so that you can become a better player.
As will be discussed further in Tip #140, learning various openings also helps when facing possible transpositions from one opening to another, or when facing irregular openings that require you to play your side like one type of position or another.
For example, if you have played the black side of the Queen’s Gambit Declined with 1.d4 d5 2.c4 e6,
you will probably be comfortable meeting the English Opening, 1.c4, with 1...e6 and 2...d5.
Or if you have enjoyed the Caro-Kann and you are trying to play the Slav against 1.d4 with 1...d5 (getting ready to answer 2.c4 with 2...c6), but White instead plays 2.e4, offering a Blackmar-Diemer Gambit, you can safely slide into the comfort of the Caro with 2...c6.
Let’s address some of the common questions about rotating openings. For the purpose of this discussion, we’ll assume your repertoire has three main parts: what you play as White, how you defend 1.d4, and how you defend 1.e4:
• How often should you rotate? This depends on many factors, such as how much fun/boring your current opening choice is, how many times you have played it (much more via lots of speed games accelerates learning, assuming you are looking up your opening after each game(s); Tip #134), what your success rate is, and how many different times you have previously rotated.
• Should you rotate all three parts of your opening at the same time? Not usually; it is too much work in a short period, although you could do two if they ‘pair’ (like adopting The Modern, 1...g6, against both 1.e4 and 1.d4)
• What openings should you choose? Refer to Tip #136, but in general ones that are fun/challenging to play, lead to positions where you can learn, and ones with pawn structures that you have not previously practiced.
Tip 140
If you have never tried the King’s Indian Defense or the French Defense, those openings are not only good for understanding fixed centers, but also plausible defense set-ups against many irregular openings too. Therefore, it is helpful to adopt each for at least a while (not necessarily at the same time).
I call this my ‘French and Indian’ opening suggestion. One advantage of having taught for so long is I get meaningful data from the amount of feedback from my suggestions. This tip/suggestion has been very successful; my students who have adopted it have overwhelmingly reported it to be effective.
Playing different openings helps you learn how to play in different pawn structures. It is fairly easy not only for some openings to evolve into French or King’s Indian Defense pawn structures, if not outright transpose into those openings. In addition, Black can often set up some of the common pawn structures in those openings against irregular early play. For example, if White opens with moves like 1.b3 or 1.g3 or even 1.f4 Black can often safely choose to set up the King’s Indian set-up with 1...Nf6, 2...g6, 3...Bg7, 4...0-0, 5...d6 (maybe in some other order, depending on what White does).
Here is an example of how a c3 Sicilian can easily become a French Defense: 1.e4 c5 2.c3 e6 3.d4 d5 4.e5 is the same position as
1.e4 e6 2.d4 d5 3.e5 c5 4.c3.
Or the Blackmar-Diemer Gambit, a queen’s pawn opening, becomes a French when declined with 1.d4 d5 2.e4 e6. Many such transpositions are possible and common.
Tips #125 and #130 discussed pawn breaks; many lines of the French and King’s Indian lead to possible fixed centers with important breaks. In the French the center can be white d4/e5 versus black d5/e6, where Black breaks with ...c7-c5, and in the King’s Indian Defense, it may be the ‘mirror’ of that with white d5/e4 versus black d6/e5, and Black breaks with ...f7-f5.
You don’t have to play the French or King’s Indian very long if you play many speed games to learn the opening ideas well. It is quite possible to play hundreds of speed games in a couple of months. My guess is that if you try each opening for a reasonable period, you will get quite a bit out of each – even if you didn’t like them as much as some other openings! You can, and should, switch back or rotate on to other openings to learn more, and maybe get to positions you enjoy better.
Tip 141
Sometimes when you are out of your opening book, the process is as simple as ‘Does either side have a tactic? No? Then which is next piece I need to bring out and where should it go? Next man up!’
Many players play the opening like it is the middlegame. They look for a quick attack and tactics on every move after the first few. While these are sometimes possible, much more likely – and effective – is a quick mobilization of all the forces. Let’s consider a typical example:
Black to play
In the above position, Black to move, the first step is to make sure there are no tactics for either side. So far, so good, although just because the opponent does not currently have a tactic or a threat doesn’t mean that a proposed candidate move does not give him a new one: ‘Is my move safe?’
With no tactic in sight, quick development is the focus. Moving a second piece twice like 4...Ne4(?) ‘to occupy a key central square’ is just too early. Players who spend time analyzing ‘attacking’ moves like this often get into unnecessary time trouble later.
The double d-pawn set-up without White’s c4 dictates Black should probably play the break move ...c7-c5 sooner or sooner (think of this as a Queen’s Gambit pawn structure reversed). But Black also should castle as soon as possible, as White is already to castle and Black’s king’s bishop is in the way.
Therefore, the initial candidate moves should be 4...c5
or 4...Be7.
If you are not familiar with these positions and want to play 4...Bd6, that is not bad and the difference between that move and 4...Be7 is not worth too much time comparing the pros and cons (time management!).
However, 4...Bb4+ is questionable due to the AWL (Tip #94; Attack with something Worth Less) 5.c3. The engine thinks 4...Bb4+ is reasonable, but in most similar positions moves that allow these free AWLs are just a waste of time (or worse), and lower-rated players should probably not spend too much time considering them.
The engine also likes the prophylactic 4...b5, hindering White’s break move c2-c4. Again, if you are not a strong player it is just much better in many cases to get ready to castle. In quiet normal positions like this, both sides should be strongly influenced by the important principle:
Move every piece once before you move any piece twice unless there’s a tactic
(Tip #144).
I chose this ‘dull’ position because it is in just such positions where players who play too slowly try to do too much, which has a doubly bad effect: not only do they likely produce second-best moves, but also they use unnecessary time, which could be saved and used much more efficiently on future critical moves.
Tip 142
If you get taken out of your opening ‘book’, do not panic! Start taking your time, analyze carefully, and follow good opening principles.
On many occasions I hear from my students, “My opponent took me out of my book, so I did not know what to do and I panicked.” I often reply, “But how many games do you expect to play where your opponent doesn’t eventually make a move that takes you out of your book?”
The answer, of course, is that in almost every game you play, your opponent is eventually going to make a move which takes you out of your book knowledge! The rare exception might be if someone falls into an opening trap you know and resigns! But if something is going to happen almost every game, you should get familiar with that situation; panicking every game would become very tiresome.
In their book
Chess Rules of Thumb
, Alburt and Lawrence write that if your opponent makes a move where you are not 100% sure what the book reply is, you must treat it as if you are now relying on your analysis, so you should take a deep breath, slam on the breaks, and start thinking. Moving too quickly because ‘it’s the opening’ or ‘I think I know what the book move is’ can range from acceptable to disastrous, but, in any case, is dangerous.
Just for the fun of it, let’s put ourselves in the shoes of a typical amateur, take an opening sequence and pretend that we are out of our book – even if there actually is a substantial amount of book knowledge about that position. Suppose we are studying 1.e4 openings and after 1...e5, play 2.Nf3. Further, we have learned what to do if someone replies 2...Nc6 or 2...Nf6, but have not studied other moves.
Then let’s assume you are playing a slow game and someone answers
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3
with
2...d6
and you think ‘Oh no! What is that? I only studied 2...Nc6 and 2...Nf6. I am in trouble. What do I do now?’
Black is playing the Philidor Defense, common among amateurs but rare at the grandmaster level, and so not as heavily covered in opening books or videos.
White to play after 2..d6
First, you are not ‘in trouble’. Is Black’s move 2...d6 a dangerous check, capture, or threat? Hardly. Is 3...Bg4, pinning the knight to the queen, a threat? No, in fact it would violate Lasker’s Principle: ‘Do not pin the adverse king’s knight before your opponent has castled’ (this mainly applies to the kind of double king’s pawn opening that were played in Lasker’s day, and to which Philidor’s Defense belongs). Therefore, stopping the potential pin with 3.h3 is a big waste of time.
In this position the ‘book’ move is
3.d4!
, but we are pretending we don’t know that. What
do
we know from opening principles?
•
Move every piece once before you move any piece twice, unless there is a tactic
. That would suggest moves like 3.Bc4 or maybe the less active 3.Be2 to get out the king’s bishop and be ready to castle. It could suggest 3.Nc3. Or it might suggest 3.d3 or 3.d4 to allow the queenside bishop to develop, although 3.d3 unnecessarily blocks in the king’s bishop.
•
Develop the pieces on the side you are going to castle first
. It looks like White is going to castle kingside, so 3.Bc4 or 3.Be2 would do that. 3.Bb5+ looks aggressive, but it fails to the ‘Attack with something Worth Less’ (AWL) 3...c6 and the bishop must retreat.
•
Try to get some control of the center
. For this the best moves are 3.Bc4, 3.Nc3, and 3.d4.
Most amateurs play 3.Bc4, which is considered the second best move. But the main idea is to stop and think before deciding on any move, just as we did here.
Tip 143
In the opening if you win material, but it costs you development, then you want to catch up in development, not try to win a little more material (like more pawns).
A student was Black in a recent example game:
1.e4 e6 2.e5 d5
I prefer 2...d6.
3.d4 c5 4.Nf3
À la Nimzowitsch, but preserving the center with 4.c3 is normal.
4...Qb6
4...cxd4 would follow Tip #131.
5.Be3? Qxb2 6.Nbd2 Nc6
Better is 6...c4 to calm things down and facilitate further development.
7.Be2
Better 7.dxc5 or 7.Rb1 when White has some compensation. Notice how both players had trouble finding the right idea once the game became more abnormal and unbalanced.
7...Nxd4
7...c4 is still best.
8.0-0?
8.Nxd4! cxd4 9.Rb1 and White is actually better, despite his material deficit, as 10.Bb5+ is looming.
8...Nxe2+ 9.Qxe2
Now Black can take the pawn that is en prise on c2. Should he capture it?
Black to play after 9.
Q
xe2
9...Bd7(?)
Black correctly does not take the pawn, but does not find the most accurate continuation, instead unguarding his b-pawn and giving White skewer possibilities. Even worse was 9...Qxc2? 10.Qb5+ Bd7 (10...Kd8? 11.Rfc1 Qf5 12.Bxc5 wins) 11.Qxb7 Rc8 12.Nb3, with full compensation for the lost material.
Correct was the consolidating 9...Qb6, when 10.Rab1 Qc6 calms the board and allows Black time to finish his development.
10.Rfb1
The wrong rook. Correct was 10.Rab1 Qxc2 11.Rxb7 when Black needs to find 11...Qa4! to keep the advantage.
10...Qxc2(?)
Missing the opportunity to ‘freeze’ the b1-rook with 10...Qc3! and save the b7-pawn. Instead, the trade of the b7-pawn for the c2-pawn greatly favors White.
11.Rxb7
Now Black is far behind in development and there is only one move to keep the advantage.
11...Be7??
This is not the one. The only move to keep the advantage was 11...Qa4. Second best is 11...c4 with equal chances. What would you play here for White?
White to play after 11...
B
e7
The correct move is the sacrifice 12.Rxd7! I saw this possibility immediately. This represents the kind of move players rated 1200-1800 find in a combination book or software that holds up a sign that says ‘White to play and win!’ But in a game those same players make quiescent errors (Tip #44), and think ‘I can’t take the bishop; that would be a trade of a rook (Reinfeld value = 5) for a bishop (value 3) and I would lose the equivalent of two pawns (or points)’
But it’s not too difficult to see that after 12.Rxd7 Kxd7 13.Qb5+ Black not only cannot castle, but White’s lead in development is going to generate a winning attack even if he plays slightly inaccurate moves, e.g. 13...Kc7 14.Qa5+ Kc6 (other moves loses similarly) 15.Qa6+ (15.Nb3! is actually better) 15...Kc7 16.Rb1 and White wins the house.
I do not think players over 1800 would have to calculate too far or too exactly to realize that 12.Rxd7 does not give up too much material for the type of attack it generates. They don’t think ‘I can’t take the bishop; I lose material’, but rather ‘If I take the bishop and sacrifice the exchange, is it worth further investigation?’... and the answer would be ‘Yes!’
Instead, White played the developing
12.Rab1
when White is ‘only’ much better after the best replies 12...a6 or 12...Qa4. Black played
12...Nh6?
, again allowing 13.Rxd7!, but White played
13.Rb8+ Rxb8 14.Rxb8+ Bd8 15.Bg5
and Black has nothing better than
15...0-0
. White netted a piece with
16.Rxd8
and eventually won on move 31, although he gave Black some chances along the way.
Tip 144
The most important principle in the opening is: ‘Move every piece once before you move any piece twice, unless there is a tactic.’
This is not the
strongest
principle (Tip #29); there are several circumstances in a variety of openings that merit exceptions. But it is important because it can be applied to so many moves in every opening. How important is this principle? I like to tell the following story to my students rated under 1600:
About 30 years ago I was giving lessons to John Keir’s son, Clayton. John was an active member of our scholastic club and decided to become a United States Chess Federation (USCF) tournament director. After one event as a club director, he decided he wanted to move up to be a regular ‘local’ director, but to do so he needed to be a USCF-rated player.
For that reason, John played in one event and got an over-the-board rating in the 1100s.
His second event was the large National Chess Congress, at that time held at the Adam’s Mark Hotel in Philadelphia. John entered the Under-1200 section, which that year featured many promising juniors.
Despite these obstacles, John amazingly swept the section 6-0. He won about $580, which is more than I have won, in total, in my entire tournament chess career (the most I ever won in one event was for winning the Greater Philadelphia Closed Championship in 1973, which was $200; that is not as bad as it sounds if you adjust for inflation).
So, as soon as the final game ended, I asked John, “How did you do it? How did you manage to sweep only your second event, winning every game?”
John smiled and replied, “Simple. You know how you go over Clayton’s games and you are always saying ‘Move every piece once, before you move any piece twice, unless there’s a tactic?’ You must have told him 500 times.”
He went on, “So, I resolved that you would not be able to tell me that about any of my games. In every game I moved every piece once without moving any piece twice and castled, getting seven pieces into the game. My opponents, on the other hand, always managed to move several pieces more than once, so that in every game I had seven pieces out to my opponent’s three or four. Naturally with such a big advantage in development, I automatically got a strong attack. Then all I had to do was to be careful and not make any big tactical mistakes to give them free pieces. And thankfully, I did not, so I ended up winning all my games easily!”
I was startled that John had successfully implemented this important concept so readily – it seemed that all my lower-rated students (before and after!) had such a difficult time following this powerful idea. As a consequence of these experiences, my answer came quickly: “If
we could bottle that and sell it, we could make a fortune!”
This story is powerful, but it can be augmented with another amazing fact about
‘Move every piece once, before you move any piece twice, unless there is a tactic.’
In my coaching career, I have had private one-on-one lessons with over 700 players whose playing strength was low enough that they either did not know that principle or were violating it regularly to their detriment.
Of the first 300 I taught, upon hearing the principle none(!) were able to immediately incorporate it regularly and correctly into their play. Instead, they continued to have ‘better ideas’ and delayed their development, often with disastrous results. Of course, many were able to do it eventually. Then one day, about 15 years ago, I was instructing a young professor from Temple University. I taught him this principle and, amazingly, he was able to reasonably follow it in every game I reviewed afterwards. I told him he was the first student who was able to follow the principle immediately without further painful losses as lessons; he was quite surprised – he said he thought the advice was straightforward and easy to follow.
In the intervening 15 years there have been about 400 additional students who needed to learn this principle. Unfortunately, none have been able to duplicate the professor’s feat and follow it without further missteps; the lure of ‘having a better idea’ continued to attract them, at least for a while. The good news is that my students who do finally learn their lesson and follow this advice whenever it is reasonable to do so, reap clear improvement benefits.
Tip 145
Early in the game, in each non-tactical position, ask about each piece ‘What is this piece’s role in the early middlegame? Where should it go so it becomes active, and how can I feasibly, efficiently, and safely get it there?’
This is yet another aspect of ‘Use all your pieces all the time’ (Tip #111), ‘Make sure every piece has a future’, and ‘Which is next piece I need to bring out and where should it go?’ (Tip #141). You don’t want to make or have even one piece unnecessarily useless early in the game.
Some openings make it harder than others for certain pieces. For example, the French Defense 1.e4 e6 is notorious for Black’s bad queen’s bishop, leading to interesting lines like 2.d4 d5 3.e5 c5 4.c3 Qb6 5.Nf3 Bd7, intending on trading Black’s ‘bad’ bishop for White’s ‘good’ bishop with 6...Bb5. Is it worth the extra tempos to do this? Can White stop it? Those are the kind of questions that make openings fun and challenging for both sides.
Another frequent problem piece is Black’s queen’s bishop in some double d-pawn openings. Bringing it out too early to prevent this with lines like 1.d4 d5 2.c4 Bf5?! often runs into ideas like early Qd1-b3, attacking the light squares d5 and b7 on the queenside.
My students playing Black often run into the London System, and those that play the black side of lines such as
1.d4 d5 2.Bf4 Nf6 3.e3 e6 4.Nf3 c5 5.c3 sometimes wonder how to successfully activate their queen’s bishop.
Black to play
If Black now plays 5...Nc6, play might continue something like 6.Be2 Be7 7.h3 0-0 8.0-0 when 8...b6 gives Black the opportunity to support a later ...Bc8-Bb7 (to hinder White’s e3-e4 or support a possible Black ...Nf6-e4 once the knight on c6 moves), or even possibly ...a7-a5 and Bc8-a6.
But if Black leaves the knight on b8 and develops the kingside first with 5...Be7 6.h3 0-0 7.Be2 he has the possibility of playing 7...b6, and if 8.0-0 Ba6; like in the French, he is trying to trade his ‘bad’ light-squared bishop for White’s good one. This maneuver is not possible before castling since 5...b6 6.Be2 Ba6?? loses a piece to 7.Bxa6 (7.Qa4+? Qd7) 7...Nxa6 8.Qa4+, picking up the knight. For more on this tactic, see Tip #101.
The fight for making sure all the pieces have a role is obviously more important once you are out of your opening ‘book’ knowledge.
If at the point you get out of your ‘book; all your pieces are not yet active, playing carefully to make sure they can all become and remain so is a major goal of the opening
.
Tip 146
Do not play the opening like it is the middlegame.
Many of my students, who are representative of others in their situation, are tempted to ‘have a better idea’ in the opening and play it like the middlegame. For example, they want to threaten (no matter how inane or easily parried), and ‘attack’ at every chance they get, even if their forces are not yet in position to do so. I call this particular ‘better idea’: playing the opening like the middlegame.
Let’s review the three main goals of the opening:
• Activate
all
your pieces quickly, safely, efficiently, and effectively.
• Get some control over the center.
• Find a safe place for the king to spend the middlegame, preferably castling.
Steinitz suggested a premature attack is doomed to failure. Therefore, unless the opponent makes a tactical error or falls far behind in development, trying to attack in the opening is not only not a goal, it is likely harmful.
It is fairly easy to get examples for this tip! Here is one:
White to play after 7...
N
c6
In this recent amateur game, White is playing a Stonewall Attack and Black is defending with the reasonable King’s Indian set-up. The engine suggests White has equality with a move like 8.dxc5.
Instead, White played the incomprehensible (at least to a strong player)
8.Ng5?
. If we can read the amateur’s mind, perhaps he envisioned a mating attack where he follows with Qd1-e1-h4 and then tries to remove the guard of h7 by attacking the knight on f6. That’s pretty far-fetched and definitely violates IM Silman’s advice that for a plan to be seriously considered it should be both feasible and effective (Tip #113). But this is the opening; instead of attack White should be concentrating on how to finish developing his pieces.
Here’s a second example:
White to play after 9...
R
e8
The black rook has just placed itself on the same file as the white king, so White needs to continue his development with a safe move like 10.Nge2
(10.0-0-0?! b5! is a typical strong pawn sacrifice that’s good to recognize and understand; this position was discussed in Tip #116).
Instead White ‘attacks’ with
10.Bg5(?)
moving the bishop twice unnecessarily and after
10...h6
, putting the question to the bishop, goes
11.Bh4?
, “keeping up the pressure”, but losing material if Black finds the common discovery 11...Nxe4!. White should have cut his losses with 11.Bxf6 Qxf6 12.0-0-0 when Black is better, but there’s ample play.
White went on to get a completely lost position, down a piece. To complete the story Black, instead of keeping it simple when way ahead (Tip #85), had a later ill-advised counter-attack that instead lost the game!
Tip 147
If your opponent is not contesting the center, it is probably correct to advance more than just two pawns.
Suppose you are White and your opponent does not advance any pawns past the sixth rank, say 1...e6 and 2...b6 (but it could be many possibilities of doing this). After
1.e4 e6 2.d4 b6?!
developing the king’s knight to get ready to castle with
3.Nf3
is good, but suppose Black continues not challenging with
3...h6(?)
:
White to play after 3...h6
In similar positions I see many of my student play the reasonable 4.Nc3, but
Stockfish
has this as only the fifth best move at 37 ply, behind even the strange 4.a3. The best move is
4.Bd3
, following the principle ‘In closed positions, pieces are best placed behind the pawns.’ Not only does 4.Bd3 get White ready for quick castling, it leaves the flexibility of pushing the c-pawn before the queen’s knight is developed. This is illustrated by the engine’s best moves for both sides:
4...d6 5.Be3 a6 6.c4
, using the c-pawn as a third pawn to gain space in the center, without the queen’s knight blocking it:
Black to play – Position after 6.c4
We could have chosen different Black first moves, e.g.
1.e4 Nc6 2.d4 e6 3.Nf3 a6
when White’s highest-rated move is
4.c3!
Position after 4.c3
That extra central pawn is useful! While in many analogous positions it is acceptable to put both knights on f3 and c3, it is often more flexible and even aggressive to move either the c-pawn and/or the f-pawn early to gain more space, and/or support the central d- and e-pawns.
Tip 148
When you fianchetto g2-g3, b2-b3, ...g7-g6, or ...b7-b6, the pawn moves that go with them is often the d-pawn (with g2-g3 or ...g7-g6), and the e-pawn (with b2-b3 or ...b7-b6).
I sometimes see beginners open a game with 1.g3 and then 2.e3 (or vice-versa). When I ask them why they did that they sometimes tell me they want to give White’s king’s bishop on f1 extra mobility. This is a ‘noble’ answer, but it turns out the extra mobility is irrelevant because:
• The bishop on f1 is not going to stay there and enjoy the extra mobility; it is quickly going to develop to only one of those diagonals. So whichever diagonal you are going to use should determine the one pawn you are going to push, e.g. if you want the bishop on the long diagonal then 1.g3 and 2.Bg2 puts it there and playing 2.e3 just wastes time.
• 1.g3 and 2.e3 weaken the light squares on the kingside, notably f3, for little or no gain.
• Playing 2.e3 after 1.g3 puts two pawns on the c1-h6 diagonal of the other bishop, making White’s undeveloped dark-squared bishop on c1 more difficult to activate.
In the opening both bishops need to be developed so, for the king bishop, this requires moving the e- or g-pawn and for the queen bishop the b- or d-pawns. From the above we can deduce that all good openings require pushing at least two pawns, one of each ‘set’ that allow both bishops to activate.
Therefore, when White plays g2-g3, he will need to move either the b-pawn or the d-pawn at some point to activate the other bishop. The double fianchetto with b2-b3 (or even b2-b4) is certainly possible and does occur, but in most kingside fianchetto openings the other bishop is developed via moving the d-pawn.
Let’s consider the initial opening moves in the main lines of some popular openings where this would apply as one side or the other has an early fianchetto: the Queen’s Indian Defense, the King’s Indian Defense, the Grünfeld Defense, and the King’s Indian Attack.
The Queen’s Indian Defense starts out
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 e6 3.Nf3 b6
. With this last move we can see that Black is one about to fianchetto here (or play ...Bc8-a6 in some lines) to develop his queen’s bishop. What is Black’s other pawn move? It is with the e-pawn so the other bishop can also be efficiently developed.
Queen’s Indian Defense: Black uses the b- and e-pawns to open lines for the bishops
Not only do Black’s b- and e-pawns open lines for the bishops, they avoid any weak square complexes as all the central squares either currently are, or can be, attacked by pawns. White has moved the d-pawn for his queen’s bishop, so naturally he will soon move either the e-pawn or the g-pawn to allow the other bishop to activate.
There are similar ideas for Black in the King’s Indian Defense, where the main line is
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 g6 3.Nc3 Bg7 4.e4 d6
. Instead of the b- and e-pawns, Black is using the d- and g-pawns to open lines for the bishops. Obviously if White moves both his central pawns (d- and e-pawns), that will open lines for both bishops and fianchettoing is not necessary. That is considered a more ‘classical’ development.
Looking at this same development “reversed” for White would be the King’s Indian Attack where White’s first five moves are often 1.Nf3 1...--, 2.g3 2...--, 3.Bg2 3...--, 4.0-0 4...-- and 5.d3. Again the d-pawn is moved after the fianchetto with the g-pawn.
In the Grünfeld Black uses the d-pawn, but pushes it do d5:
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 g6 3.Nc3 d5
. Still, it is that same combination of the d-pawns and g-pawn development to open lines for both bishops.
Hopefully next time you see a beginner open 1.g3 and 2.e3, or 1...g6 and 2...e6 for Black, you will wince a little. It is not disastrous, but if not played carefully, it could lead to problems fairly quickly.
Tip 149
In fianchetto openings, if the opponent plays
B
g5 (or against White, ...
B
g4) to attack a knight on f6 (f3) and, in addition,
B
xf6 (...
B
xf3) is not a threat, then it is usually correct to play ...h7-h6 (or h2-h3) immediately to ‘put the question’ to the bishop.
For example, after
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 g6 3.Nc3 Bg7 4.e4 d6 5.Bg5
(this is called the Accelerated Averbakh System):
Black to play after 5.
B
g5
I call the bishop on g5 a
two-way bishop
because it is strong on both diagonals:
• On the h4-d8 diagonal the bishop exerts pressure on the knight and it becomes a pin if Black ever tries the center thrust ...e7-e5, as is typical in many King’s Indian lines.
• On the c1-h6 diagonal where it can pressure the weak square h6 and, if needed, return to support the center with a move like Bg5-e3 later.
Black does not want White to have this flexibility of the two-way bishop. For example, suppose Black continues ‘naturally’ with 5...0-0. Then White can play a move like 6.Qd2:
Position after 6.
Q
d2
In the above position it will be difficult for Black to displace the g5-bishop. But instead of castling, Black first inserts
5...h7-h6 (5...h6)
:
White to play after 5...h6
White must declare which diagonal he wants his bishop to occupy and this information will help Black decide how to proceed. If the bishop retreats to h4, it has less influence on the center and cannot form a battery with the queen along the c1-h6 diagonal. If Bxf6, Black will get the advantage of the bishop-pair; on other bishop moves, the pressure on the h4-d8 diagonal is relieved.
Tip 150
When one side fianchettoes, for example, Black with ...g6 and ...
B
g7, the plan to counter that with
B
e3,
Q
d2 and
B
h6 to trade off the bishops is often key in opposite-side-castling openings like the Sicilian Dragon Yugoslav Attack, the 150 Attack in the Pirc, or the Sämisch Variation of the King’s Indian. However, many amateur players feel like playing
B
e3,
Q
d2, and
B
h6 is
de rigueur
and try it every time when the opponent fianchettoes, and doing so in many situations is lukewarm at best and a waste of time in many.
Let’s first consider the 150 Attack in the Pirc where White can generate a strong attack quickly if Black plays too conventionally:
1.e4 d6 2.d4 Nf6 3.Nc3 g6 4.Be3 Bg7 5.Qd2
(White can also play a similar system with f2-f3 or h2-h3 to protect the dark-squared bishop from ...Ng4)
5...0-0(?)
(this move is usually not played; instead, Black often starts developing the queenside in anticipation White will castle there)
6.0-0-0 Nbd7
(normal looking and a common move in similar positions at the amateur level, but again quicker central and queenside action is required)
7.f3!
(White is already getting ready to push his kingside pawns and get the much faster attack):
Black to play after 7.f3!
White’s attack will be faster and the engine already rates this as +1.3 for White. So this is an example where the plan of Be3, Qd2, and later possibly Bh6 is justified, although even here White could have played 7.Bh6, but getting ready for the pawn-storm first was better.
Now let’s consider a line in the Closed Sicilian:
1.e4 c5 2.Nc3 Nc6 3.g3 g6 4.Bg2 Bg7 5.d3 d6 6.Nf3 Nf6 7.Be3 0-0 8.0-0 Rb8
:
White to play after 8...
R
b8
This is the type of positions where many amateurs automatically feel that White’s best plan is to ‘trade off the strong black bishop on g7 with Qd2 and Bh6’. Moreover, when playing Black, they will fear this threat, and meet 9.Qd2 with the ‘obvious’ 9...Re8, so as to preserve their bishop after 10.Bh6 with 10...Bh8.
But Black need not fear 9.Qd2. He could play 9...Ng4!, guarding h6 and also threatening to win the bishop-pair with 10...Nxe3. Suppose White plays
9.h3
(best is likely 9.a4)
9...b5 10.Qd2 e5 11.Bh6 b4 12.Bxg7 Kxg7 13.Nd1 Nd4
:
White to play after 13...
N
d4
Black is evaluated as better by about a third of pawn. The Be3/Qd2/Bh6/Bxg7 plan for White was nothing special and Black did not need to fear it or stop it.
Tip 151
In many e-pawn openings, if both d2-d3 and d2-d4 are equally safe, often d2-d4 is better/more aggressive. This includes most ‘irregular’ defenses, but some modern grandmaster lines in the Ruy Lopez and Italian are notable exceptions.
Over the years I’ve noticed in my lower-rated students’ games that they tamely play d2-d3 in many positions where d2-d4 is much more forceful. Let’s consider a couple of examples.
When Black plays the Philidor Defense, many lower-rated students meet
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 d6
with 3.Bc4 followed by a later d2-d3. This is solid, but White sometimes suffers through less space in the middlegame. Instead, the main line (going all the way back to Morphy versus the Duke of Brunswick and Count Isouard) is
3.d4
:
Black to play after 3.d4
3.d4
puts pressure on e5, thus ‘activating’ the knight on f3. It also sets the table for giving White more space in the middlegame. It is a more dynamic move.
A mid-level engine on the Internet Chess Club sometimes defends 1.e4 with the Center Counter Game (the Elephant Gambit in some lines), with
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 d5?! 3.exd5 Qxd5
(better is 3...e4)
4.Nc3 Qd6
.
White to play after 4...
Q
d6
In this and analogous positions I have seen multiple students continue their development with the solid 5.Bc4 followed by d2-d3 (possibly preceded by 6.0-0 depending on what Black plays on move 5). While this way of playing certainly is advantageous for White, it is not very dynamic and doesn’t attempt to fully punish Black’s insipid play. After 5.Bc4 Be6 White needs to find the tricky 6.Nb5! to maintain a strong advantage.
But the straightforward
5.d4
is even better. If Black tries to trade with 5...exd4 White can just play 6.Nxd4, when the open lines and excellent position of the white knights promise White a nice advantage. The engine slightly prefers the tricky 6.Nb5.
White can even finesse on move 5 with the strange-looking 5.Bb5+ when encouraging 5...c6 6.Bc4 is actually somewhat better than the immediate 5.Bc4. If Black develops a piece instead on move 5 with, say, 5...Bd7, then 6.0-0 gives White a large lead in development with, unsurprisingly, d2-d4 in many lines to open up the position, following the strong principle:
If you are castled and your opponent is not, then open the center to initiate a fight
.
There are many more examples where d2-d4 is much more aggressive than d2-d3. That does not mean, of course, that there are not many main lines in 1.e4 openings where d2-d3 is not only acceptable, but common grandmaster lines. I don’t wish to generalize too much, but I have seen enough amateur games where an aggressive d2-d4 is
not
played that putting that idea into a tip seemed like a good inclusion for this book!
Tip 152
When playing White against the French Defense, avoid the Advance Variation 1.e4 e6 2.d4 d5 3.e5 unless you are well-versed in that line.
Black to play after 3.e5
In the Lichess databases, 3.e5 is played 10% of the opportunities in master play (the classical 3.Nc3 is first with a full 50% of the games), but 3.e5 is played 29% in the amateur database, about three times as often. In general, the lower rated the player, the more often 3.e5 is played.
I believe that to the less experienced eye this move seems the ‘natural’ move. For weaker players 3.e5 to gain space is the likely way to ‘punish’ Black for his/her passive 1...e6 move.
From having played the French myself and watching tens of thousands of amateur games throughout the years, I can state that 3.e5, while a very respectable move if White is well-versed in the line, is also one of the fastest ways to get into trouble for players who don’t know much about openings. Usually, Black knows at least to play the break move
3...c5
and attack the d4-square. What often happens is either:
• The pawn on d4 falls to attacks from ...Nb8-c6, ...Qd8-b6 and ...Ng8-h6(e7)-f5.
• The e5-pawn falls when White does not play c2-c3 and Black plays ...Nb8-c6 and then ...c5xd4, removing the guard on the e5-pawn, or some combination of the two.
Here is a line from the Milner-Barry Gambit where an inexperienced White, as often the case, does not understand that Bf1-d3 is an implicit pawn gambit offer:
1.e4 e6 2.d4 d5 3.e5 c5 4.c3 Nc6 5.Nf3 Bd7
(5...Qb6 first may transpose when 6.a3 is now the most popular reply)
6.Bd3
(6.a3 or 6.Be2 are better)
6...Qb6
.
White to play after 6...
Q
b6
I see this position in amateur games quite a bit, and most of the time White does not understand he is best served by offering the Milner-Barry pawn sacrifice with 7.0-0. Therefore, in many cases amateur white players often panic, or at least fall into a deep think, seeing that 7.Be3 to guard d4 fails to 7...Qxb2.
One of the moves we sometimes see in this position is
7.Bc2?, to allow the queen to guard d4. This move is more often played at the amateur level than 7.Be2, which psychologically is difficult because the bishop could have been developed there earlier, so 7.Be2 would be admitting a mistake. But after 7.Bc2 cxd4 8.cxd4 Nb4, White should just give up the bishop-pair with 9.0-0 when Black may have a small advantage after 9...Nxc2 10.Qxc2 Ne7. However, if White insists of saving the bishop with 9.Bb3 (or 9.Ba4 Qa6! 10.Bxd7+ Kxd7 when White cannot castle and ...Nd3+ is threatened in many lines) 9...Qa6! gets a grip on the important f1-a6 diagonal, stopping White from castling.
Even when White plays the more normal 6.Be2 to allow the queen to continue to guard d4, 6...Qb6 7.0-0 Nge7 often confuses White, who wants to continue to guard d4 and b2.
White to play after 7...
N
ge7
White should play 8.Na3 or 8.dxc5! but those are often a little unnatural so he might try something innocuous like 8.Re1? when Black can cash in typical manner, with 8...cxd4 9.cxd4 Nf5 when d4 or b2 falls.
Another typical amateur line is 4.Bb5+ when after 4...Nc6 5.Nf3 Bd7 Black is threatening one of radio personality Howard Stern’s favorite tactics, 6...Nxe5 winning a pawn (6.c3? Nxe5 7.dxe5 Bxb5 or 7.Bxd7+ Nxd7)
There are many ways for White to go wrong and Black does not need much knowledge to find ways to take advantage of White mistakes. 3.e5 is a good move, but avoid it if you are not familiar with at least one main tabiya.
Tip 153
Botvinnik’s Rule is a good time management rule to use in the opening
.
Botvinnik’s Rules states:
In a ‘normal’ opening for a long time control game, try not to use more than about 20% of your time for the first 15 moves.
A ‘normal’ opening is one where the main idea is to activate all the pieces effectively, efficiently, and safely. In this respect ‘safely’ usually implies a minimum of complicated tactics that normally occur later in the game.
In the days when world champion Mikhail Botvinnik was active, there were not increments (or time delays) so the calculation was very easy. For example, if the time control was 40 moves in the first 2 hours (that and 40 moves in two and a half hours were common then for grandmaster play), then 20% of 120 minutes is 24 minutes. Therefore, we should subtract 24 minutes from 120 and say that a player should have about 96 minutes remaining on his clock for a 40 in two-hour time limit.
When would this not be wise or true? Suppose the opening was a Marshall Gambit and it goes ‘book’ for more than 15 moves. Then clearly you want to play all the book moves fairly rapidly to leave more time to think about moves in unfamiliar positions, so Botvinnik’s Rule would not apply.
The other clear exception would be a crazy opening like the Traxler Defense to the Two Knight’s Defense. Suppose you only know this for nine moves, but then the next six moves might be super critical (a piece is often sacrificed), and decide the game. If the game is possibly being decided within 15 moves, that is again an exception to Botvinnik’s Rule and obviously more than 20% of the time may be spent on the first 15 moves.
The math gets a little more complicated when there is a large increment. In that case just taking 20% of the ‘minutes’ portion of the time control and not including the increment will yield a wrong and misleading answer. Let’s take the common online slow time control of 45 minutes for a game with a 45-second increment.
If we wrongly ignore the increment, the rule would imply that we should take 20% (of 45) or nine minutes for the first 15 moves, leaving us with about 36 minutes after move 15. But that incorrectly ignores the fact than we have used 15 moves and received 45 extra seconds on each move.
So, let’s assume a 40 move game and see how many seconds we actually have. We have 45 minutes, which is 45 x 60 or 2700 seconds before adding the increment. Then we have 40 x 45 or 1800 extra seconds of increment (not insubstantial!). That gives us a total time of 2700+1800 = 4500 seconds for the 40-move games (obviously more if the game goes more moves, due to the increment).
20% of 4500 seconds is 900 seconds. But the first 15 moves yields 15 x 45 seconds = 675 seconds of increment. Therefore, the remaining time consumed that should be taken off the clock is 900 – 675 = 225 seconds of non-increment, or 3 minutes and 45 seconds. If we subtract that from the initial 45 minutes, Botvinnik’s Rule implies that we should have about 41 minutes and 15 seconds remaining on the clock after move 15 (that’s much more than the non-increment time of 36 minutes remaining).
The good news is that you don’t have to do all that calculation each time you play with a large increment. Simply add extra time to the ‘20% of the minutes’ to guesstimate how much time you should have left. For example, suppose the game is 30 minutes with a 30-second increment. Then 20% of 30 is 6 minutes, 30 minutes – 6 minutes is 24 minutes without an increment. Therefore, in this situation add a couple of minutes for a large increment and aim for about 26-27 minutes remaining on your clock; that’s close enough.
What do you do if you play too slowly and have less time remaining at move 15 than you should? I suggest students should ‘asymptotically’ speed up for a few moves to get back on track on how much time should be spent.
Another common error I see in students who understand and try to follow Botvinnik’s Rule is they feel so relieved on move 15 that they made it, that they slow down tremendously for moves 16-20, and ruin the cushion they have built up by not playing too slowly on the first 15 moves. Then they get into unnecessary time trouble and throw away the game on later moves, the very problem that Botvinnik’s Rule was designed to help prevent!
One last important point, which I was taught by watching Senior Master Ken Potts’s games. When playing rapid chess (say 30 0 or 30 5) with a low time increment, Botvinnik’s Rule is much too liberal on the optimum time to spend on the opening. For example, in 30 0 it would suggest having 24 minutes left on move 15. But Potts played much faster than that whenever he could, with the idea that ‘the shorter the time limit, the more you want to compress non-critical (opening) moves and leave time for critical later moves’.
Therefore, Ken would blitz the opening, often taking only 1-3 minutes for his first 15 moves instead of 6 minutes! This would leave him with more time for critical moves later, and often a decisive time advantage over opponents who would play the opening more slowly. I often saw Ken have an advantage on time of about 15 minutes to 5 minutes when the game got complicated around move 25-30. Not too many masters could give Ken that type of odds.
So, when the US Chess Federation made sudden death (all the moves in a certain amount of time) legal for slow rated games around 1980, a proliferation of 30 0 tournaments were held, and Ken Potts went up and down the East Coast with his time management ‘secret’, beating masters and raising his rating into the Senior Master (above 2400) level.
Chapter Seven
Endgames
This shorter chapter provides tips that address playing and learning the endgame, the phase with not only fewer pieces on the board, but the one that importantly requires the king to play actively. Because the endgame is not only critical, but has different principles than the remainder of the game, it is important to understand how these tips/principles differ than dealing with situations earlier in the game.
Tip 154
After learning how to checkmate with king and queen vs king and king and rook vs king, best is to learn well king and one pawn vs king because that forms the basis of many more complex endgames.
I think almost everyone understands that endgame study starts with basic checkmates: king and queen versus king (you cannot easily finish games without that one), and king and rook versus king. But after those, there is divergence of opinion. Some coaches progress to king and two bishops versus king and then the rare king and bishop and knight versus king. I don’t suggest that path, partly because these mates are relatively rare, plus you don’t need them to build into more common and important, endgames. Others go to well-known ‘exact’ endgames like the standard Lucena or Philidor rook and pawn endgames. A third approach might be to use non-exact practical endgames with several pawns and pieces.
My suggestion for the next endgame to study is
king and one pawn versus king. This endgame is extremely important not just because it occurs so frequently, but that pretty much all more complex endgames that involve pawns require some knowledge of king and pawn versus king. Why?
Because you cannot play an endgame well without having a good understanding if a trade is good or bad for you. In other words,
if you make a trade to a ‘simpler’ endgame, does that make the win (or draw, depending on your goal) easier, more difficult, or perhaps impossible?
Without this information, the decision to trade would have to be made on calculation (which could be both slow and error prone), or by a quick guess, which could be completely wrong and cost the game.
I like to use positions comparable to the following to illustrate the idea that knowledge of simpler (fewer piece) endgames is instrumental, if not totally a prerequisite, for studying endgames with more pieces:
Black to play
Black’s king is cut off from the defense to White’s d-pawn by the white rook controlling the e-file. Knowledge of the ‘Philidor’ endgame is not necessary to understand the following; it is just common sense that the black king would be better off if it could help stop the white pawn. But the only way for the black king to cross the e-file would be to use the black rook as a shield, say, after a move like 1...Re7. Unfortunately, this offers White the chance the trade rooks and go into a king and pawn versus king endgame.
But if Black knows the endgame is drawn after
1...Re7 2.Rxe7 Kxe7
, then 1...Re7 is not only a strong candidate move, it may be the only idea to save the game (it is not, but it is the simplest). However, if 1...Re7 leads to a trivial loss (it does not, but Black must know this), then it must be discarded and other attempts at defense need to be found. Finally, if Black does not know whether or not 1...Re7 works and has to calculate the lines, that could be inordinately slow and he might make an error in his evaluation.
The bottom line is clear. It is very difficult to study, and essentially understand, endgames which can eventually trade down into king and pawn versus king without studying those endgames first!
Tip 155
The king is worth about 4¼ pawns fighting value in the endgame
.
Everyone understands the king is ‘infinite’ trade value – losing it (i.e., being checkmated) is worth the entire game. But what is less commonly known is the king’s fighting value, which has been estimated at about 4¼ pawns. What does this mean?
Suppose we change the rules to state ‘the first side to capture all the opponent’s pieces wins’, and we offer as a starting position king and six pawns versus a piece (knight or bishop) and six pawns:
White to play
Which side would you rather have? If a king’s fighting value is much less than 4¼, then perhaps you would prefer playing Black and have the piece instead. But, when shown this example, almost everyone chooses the king’s side, whether it is against a knight or a bishop. Choosing the king is consistent with both pieces’ estimated average value.
One reason a king has more fighting value than the faster moving knight and bishop is that half the pieces on the board are pawns and a king can simultaneously attack a pawn and the square in front of it (the pawn’s potential move). Therefore, it is easier for a king to approach and win a pawn than it is for a knight or bishop. This ‘anti-pawn’ capability is powerful, especially in the endgame when pawn promotion becomes a major aspect and kings are safer to roam around, helping their own pawns and attacking opposing pawns.
Many of my lower-rated students are slow to activate their king once it is safe in the early endgame, especially when they are ahead. If they are ahead enough (let’s say a rook), then it is likely they can win the game without activating their king. But if they are ahead less than a rook, sometimes winning without using the king is difficult or even impossible, lacking those 4¼ pawns worth of fighting value. When a student is ahead a piece (knight or bishop), they often feel they can just move that piece around the board and win. But if it is just that piece versus the opposing king, they are often in for a rude shock until they realize they need their king, too.
Here is a sample position where Black can more easily activate his king, so his king has more ‘fighting value’:
Black to play
Black can use his king to attack the blockaded white d-pawn, while White’s king is far away from the action. Due to Black’s superior king activity, he won the pawn and the game. More on this example in the next tip...
Tip 156
In the endgame, the king’s average fighting value diminishes if you delay activating it and is greater if your king becomes very active.
The corollary is that a king should be activated as soon as it is safe to do so. In his book
The Wisest Things Ever Said About Chess
, GM Soltis suggests “it is usually safe to activate the king when the opponent has the equivalent of queen plus minor piece or less.”
As we discussed in the previous tip, the king’s average fighting value in the endgame is about 4¼ pawns. In my experience, one of the most common endgame errors of amateurs is simply waiting too long to activate their king. Instead, they often have ‘a better idea’ which turns out to be less effective.
Let’s take a more detailed look at the final example in the Tip 155. A few moves earlier, the position was:
White to play after 33...
N
d7
Black’s pawn structure is solid, but White has a dangerous passed pawn and Black’s king is a little exposed. If White would now play 34.h4! that alleviates back rank mates, guards the key g5-square, and gives White extra space. The engine rates that position as about +1.8 pawns for White. Instead White gives a meaningless check:
34.Qd5+? Qf7
Black would love to trade queens and get his king centralized. Once the queens are traded the d-pawn would look more like an isolated liability than a passed asset!
35.Qxf7+?
It is tough for anyone to admit their mistake, but I find that the lower rated the player, the less likely they are to ‘take their move back’. White should boldly retreat with 35.Qd2 and be content with a now small advantage.
35...Kxf7
The black king enters the fray near the action around the isolated d-pawn and now
Black
has a clear advantage. In this type of position, you can think of Black’s king as worth more than the average fighting value and White’s king as worth less!
36.Ng5+ Kg7 37.Kf1?
Possibly the losing move. Better was 37.f3 to allow the knight to support the d6-pawn with Ng5-e4 in some lines.
37...h6 38.Nf3
We have now arrived at the position from the previous tip.
38...Kf6 39.Nd2 Re6
Even more to the point is 39...Ke6 40.Nc4 Kd5.
40.Nc4 Re4 41.Rc1 Ke6 42.Re1 Rxe1+ 43.Kxe1 Kd5
Anyway. The double attack by the king nets the pawn and
Black won on move 62.
Sometimes in the endgame the king can be used as a valuable tempo machine, but is there a position where we can ‘freeze’ him out of even that role?
White to play
Black’s king is frozen so its fighting value is currently very small; any king move will allow the a-pawn to promote. But he has three(!) connected passed pawns versus the white king. In this well-known puzzle, can someone win or is it a draw?
After
1.Kg2 f6 2.Kg3 f5 3.Kf4 g6 4.Kg3 g5 5.Kg2 h5
(5...h6 6.Kg3 h5 7.Kg2 f4 8.Kf3 h4 9.Kg4 wins)
6.Kg3 g4
(6...h4+ 7.Kh3 also wins)
7.Kg2 h4 8.Kh2 f4 9.Kg1!
(the only move to win)
9...f3 10.Kf2 h3 11.Kg3 h2 12.Kxh2 f2 13.Kg2 g3 14.Kf1
White wins as Black will soon have to move his king and allow the a-pawn to promote.
Therefore, in this admittedly very special case, the active king is able to beat the inactive king even when the inactive king has three connected passed pawns.
Tip 157
If you have the option, do not trade into a king and pawn endgame unless you are almost certain you are going to get the result you want (win or draw).
GM Andrew Soltis once wrote that a grandmaster would never go into a king and pawn endgame against a 1900 player unless he was 100% certain to win. The reason is that all the GM’s advantages in knowledge would dissipate and it would simply become a pure analytical battle, and 1900 players might likely be able to calculate well enough to hold equal positions. But in other types of endgames there is much more knowledge and strategy involved, and much more chance for the lower-rated player to err.
Let’s give a couple of examples from students’ games. In the first game, my student was White and was down a pawn in a rook and pawn endgame.
White to play
If Black plays accurately, White is probably lost no matter what he plays, but the general principles are (*Tip Alert!*):
• King and pawn endgames are usually the easiest endgames to win with a slight advantage, and to lose with even a slight disadvantage. They are also the most calculatable.
• Single rook and pawn endgames are the second easiest to draw at a disadvantage, like being down a pawn. By far the easiest to draw with a disadvantage are bishops of opposite colors.
Therefore, if White was unable to calculate the possible trade of rooks as possibly leading to a draw (it doesn’t), on general principles he should avoid the trade. Instead he played
1.Rd2?
, which would have earned two question marks if he had not already likely been lost.
When I asked White after the game why he traded rooks, he said “I looked at the queenside and thought I could get my king over there faster than he could and attack his pawns.” Making moves on general ideas like this without doing any analysis to back it up I call ‘hand-waving’.
I don’t remember the exact continuation, but play went something like:
1...Rxd2+ 2.Kxd2 Kf6 3.Kc3 Ke5
(even 3...b5 should have been enough to convince White his idea of quicker king penetration was incorrect)
4.Kc4 Kd6 5.Kb5 Kc7
and White is losing. For example,
6.a3 Kb7 7.Kc4 Kc6 8.a4 b5+
(a cute tactical trick that’s good to know)
9.axb5+ Kb6
zugzwang!
10.Kc3 Kxb5 11.Kc2 Kb4 12.Kb2 c4 13.bxc4 Kxc4 14.Ka3 Kd3
and the white pawns fall.
In the second example my student was Black and in a bind:
White to play
White can trade off into a winning king and pawn endgame with 1.Qxe8+ Qxe8 2.Bxe8 Kxe8 3.h5! (3.Kg3? g5 is OK for Black) 3...Ke7 4.Kg3 Kd6 5.Kf4 Kxd5 6.Kxf5 and Black’s kingside pawns fall first.
Instead he took the less forcing – and wrong – way
1.Bxe8+?
, which allows Black a perpetual with 1...Qxh4+ 2.Kg1 Qe1+, etc.
But now it was Black’s turn to make a mistake with the ‘automatic’ recapture
1...Qxe8?
which would transpose into the losing line above if White would play 2.Qxe8+. The mistakes continued as White instead played
2.Qd6+? Qe7 3.Qf4
(here the trade of queens no longer works as Black’s king is one square closer: 3.Qxe7+?? Kxe7 and 4.Kg3 g5 or 4.h5 Kd6 wins).
Now Black erroneously offer a king and pawn endgame with
3...Qe4??
(equal is 3...Qf6 or 3...Ke8). White can now win with the straightforward 4.Qxe4 fxe4 5.Kg3 when the e-pawn falls. However, White fell for another common low-rated fallacy: ‘If my opponent offers a trade, then it must be good for him and bad for me, so I won’t do it.’ This logic is often incorrect for two reasons:
• The opponent may have made a mistake and the trade is bad for them.
• The trade may be good for the opponent, but it is very possible not trading is even better for them!
Therefore, White continued the parade of misconceptions with
4.Qd6+? Qe7 5.Qb8+ Kf7 6.Qf4 Kf6
, which should be equal after moves like 7.Kh3 or 7.Qf2. Instead, it was ‘Patzer sees a check, gives a check’ with
7.Qd4+??
,
a Hope Chess move allowing the obvious trade of queens with
7...Qe5+ 8.Qxe5 Kxe5
and Black’s remaining moves are easy:
9.Kg3 g6 10.d6 Kxd6 11.Kf4 Ke6 12.a4 Kf6 13.Ke3 Ke5 14.Kf3 g5 15.hxg5 hxg5 16.g3 Kd4
and
White resigned.
Tip 158
Zugzwang is not a rare endgame ploy used by high-rated players; rather it is a fairly common way to make progress in many endgame positions.
Sometimes when I instruct lower-rated players, they treat zugzwang (‘move-bound’ pronounced ‘tsug-tsvang’) as some exotic strategy, perhaps partly because the German name zugzwang sounds exotic to English-speaking newcomers. But understanding what zugzwang is, is not only fairly easy, but also essential to many basic endgame positions and ideas. Probably the easiest one is:
White to play and win
Even beginners can stumble upon the fact that the only two ways for White to continue to guard the pawn both win!
1.Kc6 Ke7 2.Kc7
followed by
3.d8
=Q
or
1.Ke6 Kc7 2.Ke7
followed by
3.d8
=Q.
What is more educational is explaining why this works: The rules of chess don’t allow Black to ‘pass; on his move, so because he is required to move, but doesn’t want to, he is forced to abandon his blockade of the passed pawn. This idea that Black is compelled to move and ruin his position is the basic idea of zugzwang.
Once students understand positions like this, it makes it easier for them to understand how similar situations with a variety of pieces can use zugzwang. For example, when I teach how to checkmate with a rook and king versus a king (which would not be a win without zugzwang, if the defender could pass!) and I use the ‘box’ method, it often arrives at a position identical (assuming various symmetries) to the following:
White to play
White wants Black’s king to go into the corner to he can mate him. Therefore, he simply wants to continue to guard the exit square h6 with his rook by moving it to any safe square on the sixth rank. I call this the ‘slidey rook maneuver’ because you can just slide the rook left to any square, e.g. 1.Rc6 Kh8 2.Rh6#.
White to play
A higher-rated student reached this position as White and a low-rated spectator whispered, “Draw!” and I replied “Watch!”
1.Ke3 Ke5 2.Be8
(zugzwang)
2...Kd5 3.Kf4 Kd6 4.Kf5 Ke7 5.Bd7 Kd8
(5...Kd6 6.Kf6 wins)
6.Ke5 Ke7 7.Kd5 Kd8 8.Kc5 Ke7 9.Kb5 Kd8 10.Ka6 Ke7 11.Kb7 Kd8 12.Kb8 1-0
It’s a further zugzwang.
Tip 159
If you are far enough ahead on material, sometimes ‘sacrifices’ are possible which make the win even easier.
White to play
I often use this position as an example during my lessons. I ask my students what they would play. Almost everyone sees the threat of a back-rank mate, and the most common answers are: 1.Qa1+,
1.Qb2+
and 1.h3.
I personally prefer the microscopically better 1.h4 to 1.h3, since the pawn is perfectly safe, and 1.h4 is both a defensive move (to stop the mate) and an offensive move (to cover g5 when the black king gets attacked). Moreover, without even giving the position to an engine, I am sure it would play a move like 1.Qb2+ and win quickly.
However, I have been in vaguely analogous overwhelming positions in speed games my entire life, and I would play none of those moves! Without hesitation in similar positions I always play
1.Qxd5
, eliminating my opponent’s final attacking piece. This usually elicits either wonder or a resignation, but, if they don’t resign, there’s nothing much for Black to continue playing. It doesn’t bother me that my queen is worth much more than the rook; I am following the principle (*Tip Alert!*):
When you make a trade of pieces, it often doesn’t matter what you are trading so much as how that affects the pieces left on the board.
In this case, leaving White with ahead a rook for a pawn makes the technique very simple with little counterplay.
If they continue with
1...exd5
then I realize a rook (worth about 5 pawns) does not fight well versus a king (4+ pawns) and the d-pawn (1 pawn for a total of 5+ for Black to fight in the middle), so I simply play
2.Kf1
with the plan of placing my king on the d-file, where it blockades Black’s passed pawn and neither the black king nor any of the black pawns can check it off the file. Plus, with the only rook, White’s king also cannot be put in zugzwang. That means Black now has absolutely no counterplay – a worthy goal when trying to ensure that only one outcome is possible!
1.
Q
xd5
also follows another worthwhile tip:
‘Permanent’ solutions are usually better than ‘temporary’ solutions
.
In this example a move like 1.Qa1+ is only a temporary solution because the queen has to continue to watch the back-rank mate. However, a move like
1.
Q
xd5
or even 1.h4 creates a more permanent solution such that it becomes difficult for Black to construct a possible future mating plan after those moves, while after 1.Qa1+ he can mate on the very next move if White absent-mindedly vacates the back-rank.
When you make a ‘sacrifice’ like
1.
Q
xd5
in the above position, you must be sure that the resultant position is an easy, if not easier, win. When my son was a weaker player he understood this principle, but he sometimes misapplied it, sacrificed material and made the win more difficult! I have occasionally seen a student make the same mistake – their intentions were good, but the result was detrimental. Of course, if you are in doubt as to whether the sacrifice makes the win easier, don’t do it!
Tip 160
If you reach a position, usually in the endgame, where you can’t lose unless you literally give away pieces, but you have the only chance to win, you don’t offer or accept a draw, no matter who you are playing.
Are there any exceptions to this tip? Sure; suppose you are playing without an increment (it could be a speed game) and you won’t have time to checkmate, then taking a draw could be wise. Or suppose you are having some personal crisis like a bad headache when you can’t play comfortably. But otherwise, if you can’t lose, have any advantage, and are not in a ‘dead drawn’ position where you have no chance of winning, then you should play for a win against anyone.
I tell the following story: when I was an expert, I was playing in one of the first World Opens and was paired with a 1900 player. As normal for me, I was a little rusty and got outplayed to the point where I had to sacrifice a piece for a bunch of checks, chasing my opponent’s king all over the board with just my queen, trying to get a perpetual check. I wasn’t sure I could, but the best I could do was draw, so I said to my lower-rated opponent “Obviously, I offer you a draw”. My wise opponent correctly replied, “Obviously, I decline” since the worst he could do was draw. I gave several checks, but eventually the draw was not there and I resigned.
One young promising player at our club demonstrated his endgame determination the night before I wrote this tip. Against a higher-rated opponent, he entered an endgame in the following position:
White to play
This is exactly the type of position to which this tip refers. Unless White falls asleep or puts his rook
en prise, he can’t lose but he has winning chances. Instead of timidly offering his higher-rated opponent a draw, the young man boldly and correctly chose to try to win. A little while later they reached this position:
White to play
White has cleverly and literally maneuvered Black into a corner. The engine says White has multiple ways of winning, but I liked best the move White played:
1.Rd8!
. I would have given the young man an ovation for finding an idea many his age would find daunting – he is simply running Black out of moves by taking away the bishop’s only safe square – but an ovation would be quite illegal, not to mention discourteous to his opponent.
All of Black’s replies are self-destructive. If 1...c3 the rook returns with 2.Rc8 and first the pawn falls and soon the whole black position. If 1...Kh8 then 2.Kg6 is one way to quickly win. So Black tried
1...h4!?
and White quickly played
2.gxh4
(after the game I suggested this was not such an obvious move, since getting only rook’s pawns makes sacrificing the exchange impossible in many lines; the engine thinks 2.Rd4! is the quickest win), and the game concluded:
2...c3 3.Rc8 Bd5 4.Rxc3 Bf3 5.h5 Be2
(Black is forlornly hoping for some type of drawing fortress against the rook pawns)
6.Rc8 Bf3 7.h6 Be2 8.Kg5 Bf3 9.Rc7+ Kh8 10.h7 Be2 11.Kh6
and Black resigned a move or two later.
Whenever you get a position with similar chances to our example, even if you do not know how to win, in the words of Bobby Fischer, “Try stuff!” Sometimes you are not winning but just better, but in the ensuing play your opponent might make a mistake (common at the lower level where tough defense is psychologically difficult) and you may win anyway. You have very little to lose by playing on, no matter how much higher-rated your opponent may be.
Tip 161
In the endgame if you have a king and a non-rook’s pawn and anything else (pawn, knight, bishop...) vs a king, you can almost always win just by advancing your king and pawn, using the extra piece at the end to put opponent in zugzwang, then promote
.
White to play
For example,
1.Kf1 Kf4 2.Kf2
. A few instructors call this ‘the opposition’, but I reserve that designation for positions where the offensive king is in front of the pawn because, in this position – with or without the knight on a1 – it doesn’t matter whose move it is, so the idea of having ‘the opposition’ should not apply (Tip #164).
White wins as follows:
2...Ke4 3.e3 Ke5 4.Kf3 Kf5 5.e4+ Ke5 6.Ke3 Kd6 7.Kd4 Ke6 8.e5 Ke7 9.Kd5 Kd7 10.e6+ Ke7 11.Ke5 Ke8
(without the knight, this retreat along the same file as the pawn would be the only move to draw once the pawn is on the sixth rank)
12.Kd6 Kd8 13.e7+ Ke8 14.Nb3
(here
any move by any piece
in a similar position that does not cover d7 and stalemate wins the crucial tempo)
14...Kf7 15.Kd7
and
16.e8
=Q wins.
The important point is that the ‘tempo wasting’ knight move on move 14 could be by any piece – it could be a second pawn or a bishop (a rook would not need promotion). I have seen many players with two connected pawns push both pawns and then had to be careful to avoid stalemate. My suggested method of just pushing one pawn is an easy way to win most endgames with any non-rook’s pawn and anything else against a lone king. This method also often works if both sides have equal extra pawns that are fixed and cannot create a passed pawn (e.g. add a white pawn on a4 and a black pawn on a5 in the example above).
Tip 162
In rook and pawn endgames, it is often better to be down a pawn with an active rook than be even on material with a passive, tied-down rook.
Here is a well-known example:
Black to play
The only way for Black to save the pawn is the ‘obvious’ 1...Ra8, a move that most lower-rated players might choose fairly quickly. But the engine does not rate 1...Ra8 among the top six moves!
After
1...Rc3!
(activating the rook and cutting off the white king)
2.Rxa7 Ra3
(rooks belong behind passed pawns!) the engine rates the position as dead even despite White’s extra pawn.
But suppose Black passively defends the pawn with 1...Ra8(?), giving the white king free reign of the board. Further assume that Black will now just keep his rook on a8 guarding the pawn and daring White to make progress. Play might continue 2.Kf3 g6? (2...Re8! is still possible with equality) 3.Ke4. Now White’s king is heading for b7 and there is no defense, e.g. 3...Kg7 4.Kd5 Kf6 5.Kc6 Rb8 Too late 6.Rxa7 Rb2 7.Rc7 Rxf2 8.a7 Ra2 9.Kb7 Rb2+ 10.Kc8 Ra2 11.Rc6+ Ke5 12.Kb7 Rb2+ 13.Rb6 and White wins.
From the above example we can see that having a passive rook and giving the opponent free rein of the board is often disastrous. Hence the idea that an active counter-attacking rook might be superior in many positions to a rook tied down to inactivity in protection of a pawn. Of course, there are exceptions so, as with many endgame decisions, choose your plan and moves very carefully.
Tip 163
In endgame positions where one side has an ‘outside’ passed pawn – and thus logically the opponent has the ‘inside’ passed pawn – the player with the outside passed pawn should push it as a decoy only so far as to be able to capture the inside passed pawn.
Pushing the outside passed pawn further than needed to deflect the defending king is rarely helpful and often harmful.
White to play
Many authors call the a-pawn in similar positions without the black d-pawn the ‘outside passed pawn’, but I only call it that if there is another passed pawn to compare it to – in this position represented by the black d-pawn – which I guess we could call the ‘inner passed pawn’.
White should naturally push the a-pawn to deflect the black king away from the d-pawn:
1.a6 Kc6 2.a7 Kb7
. But here is where I have seen a multitude of amateur players misplay similar positions:
White to play after 2...
K
b7
Using our tip, we know the correct move is
3.
K
xd5
. Let’s see what happens if White instead plays the tempting 3.a8=Q??, which proponents defend by suggestion it “lures the black king further away”: 3...Kxa8 4.Kxd5 Kb7 5.Ke5 Kc7 6.Kf6 Kd7 7.Kg6 Ke7 8.Kxh6 Kf7 9.Kh7 (or 9.Kg5 Kg7) 9...Kf8 10.h6 Kf7 is a draw.
But after the correct
3.Kxd5
we see that
3...Kxa7 4.Ke6 Kb7 5.Kf6 Kc7 6.Kg6 Kd7 7.Kxh6 Ke7 8.Kg7
wins.
The reason 3.a8=Q?? is detrimental is that the distance from a7 to the drawing key squares, f7 and f8, is identical to the distance from a8 to f7 and f8 (both five king moves). Therefore, the tempo spent on 3.a8=Q is completely wasted as it does not push the black king further away from where it will need to go next. White needed that tempo to capture the pawn on d5; in all cases the black king needs to move to the a-file anyway even if White does not push the a-pawn any further.
At worst (as in this example) pushing the outside pawn when the inside pawn is vulnerable costs a tempo that throws away the win; at best (if the remaining pawns were situated otherwise), it would not be helpful. From this analysis we can conclude that the tip to only push the outside passed pawn far enough to deflect the defending king away from defending the ‘inside passed pawn’ makes sense in almost all similar positions.
Tip 164
The term ‘opposition’ is often used inconsistently in many king and pawn endgame explanations, so beware of the distinctions.
Showing the king and non-rook’s pawn plus anything versus king example in Tip #161 did raise a relevant, interesting question I have talked about quite frequently (the situation occurs often in actual play), which is the differing use of the term ‘opposition’. I think everyone uses that term to describe what is happening when you have a king directly in front of a pawn that is on the fourth rank or lower, with the opposing king across (Diagram 1):
Diagram 1- Either side to play determines ‘the opposition’
As any endgame text will tell you, in Diagram 1 White to play gives Black the opposition and, with correct play, Black can draw:
1.Ke4 Ke6 2.d4 Kd6 3.d5 Kc7
(any move but 3...Kc5?? draws)
4.Ke5 Kd7 5.d6 Kd8! 6.Ke6 Ke8 7.d7+ Kd8 8.Kd6
stalemate.
But, if it is Black’s move,
White
has the opposition and can win:
1...Ke6 2.Kc5 Kd7 3.Kd5 Ke7 4.Kc6 Ke6 5.d4 Ke7 6.d5 Kd8 7.Kd6 Ke8 8.Kc7
followed by d6-d7-d8=Q.
However, if the pawn is not far advanced and the offensive king is not in front of the pawn (Diagram 2) then, even though the kings are separated by the same ‘one square in-between’ that marks close opposition, I think all authors realize it does not matter who is to move, and generally agree on
not
using the term ‘opposition’ to apply to this situation:
Diagram 2
–
Either side to play: no opposition
Diagram 3
–
Black to play
The third diagram shows a standard drawn position if Black knows to play directly back along the file with 1...Kc8.
However, suppose Black blunders by moving back diagonally
1...Kd8??
and White responds
2.Kd6
:
Diagram 4 – Black to play: does White have the opposition?
Authors and instructors will correctly say that Black’s move is an error, but my point involves the nomenclature some use to describe this error. Many, if not most, will say 1...Kd8?? is wrong because “2.Kd6 gives White the opposition” as Black loses after 2...Kc8 3.c7 (zugzwang) 3...Kb7 4.Kd7.
However, I feel calling the situation in Diagram 4 ‘the opposition’ is questionable, if not incorrect, since those same authors, faced with Diagram 2 and a visually comparable situation (offensive king next to the pawn) with the pawn lower than the sixth rank, don’t call it the opposition! This discrepancy is attributable to the requirement in Diagram 4 that it matters who is to move, while in Diagram 2 it does not.
I think calling the situation in Diagram 4 ‘getting the opposition’ is confusing and inconsistent. When the position has just king and one pawn versus king and the kings are close to each other (not the ‘distant opposition’), I would prefer to reserve the term ‘opposition’ to only cases where the offensive king is in front of the pawn.
In positions like Diagram 4 where it matters who is to play, but the king is not in front of the pawn, then it is a matter of tempi, but not (in my humble opinion) ‘the opposition’ since then we would have to extend the definition of ‘opposition’ to include cases where the king is beside a pawn, but it matters whose move it is – and possibly others as well.
In cases where there is more material on the board, I can understand ‘opposition’ being used in a variety of situations. I admit it is all a matter of semantics, but, whenever I see someone explain Diagram 4 as Black “giving up the opposition”, I grimace a little since they would not say that if Black had safely moved back diagonally when the pawn was on a lower rank. I think my use of ‘opposition’ is more consistent for the reasons stated above. Show these positions to your local author and/or master and see what he/she says...
Epilogue
Final Tip:
All the tips in the world will not help you if you do not do the work.
Just as all the chess knowledge in the world will not help you much if you don’t know how to analyze, evaluate and manage your time, these chess tips just help provide direction. But you should take these suggestions and do positive things with them in order to get any real benefit.
For example, if you know you should master basic tactics before you get the full benefit of intermediate tactics, it won’t help you if you don’t do repetitious easy tactics almost on a daily basis until you can recognize a high percentage of them quickly and accurately.
Similarly, if you know that you need to activate your king as soon as it is safe in the endgame but you ‘have a better idea’ and delay it, that knowledge will not help much.
If you know to ‘move every piece in the opening once before you move any piece twice unless there is a tactic’, but again have better ideas and consistently delay getting all your pieces activated, the knowledge of that principle is rather moot.
We can go on and on with examples, but you get the idea: the knowledge of the tips in this book is helpful, but only to the extent that you believe them and use them actively and wisely. I use the term ‘have a better idea’ when a student makes a mistake, learns what they should do next time, and then makes a similar mistake again even if they knew what to do the second time because of some reason (‘better idea’) that almost always was less important than just following the advice they learned.
The willingness to follow the advice you have been given is called ‘coachability’. I have seen coachability listed as an important trait in many sports; not just chess. Some players are very talented, but either will not listen to advice or are stubborn and only do ‘their work’ instead. In either case, they are either less likely to reach their potential.
Some of my students wait for me to give them an epiphany that will make them a much stronger player. Occasionally I can give them one that gives them that ‘Aha!’ moment that breaks a rut or propels them to the next level. But much more often the epiphanies have a limited half-life and what propels them to the next level is them doing a great amount of useful work! Hopefully you can do it!
